<!-- Start poem -->
# üíÆ Don Juan by *George Gordon, Lord Byron*

<p>
    FRAGMENT ON THE BACK OF THE MS. OF CANTO I.<br/><br/>I WOULD to Heaven that I were so much clay,<br/>  As I am blood, bone, marrow, passion, feeling--<br/>Because at least the past were passed away,<br/>  And for the future--(but I write this reeling,<br/>Having got drunk exceedingly to-day,<br/>  So that I seem to stand upon the ceiling)<br/>I say--the future is a serious matter--<br/>And so--for God's sake--hock and soda-water!<br/><br/>DEDICATION.<br/><br/>BOB SOUTHEY! You're a poet--Poet-laureate,<br/>  And representative of all the race;<br/>Although 't is true that you turned out a Tory at<br/>  Last,--yours has lately been a common case;<br/>And now, my Epic Renegade! what are ye at?<br/>  With all the Lakers, in and out of place?<br/>A nest of tuneful persons, to my eye<br/>Like "four and twenty Blackbirds in a pye;<br/><br/>"Which pye being opened they began to sing,"<br/>  (This old song and new simile holds good),<br/>"A dainty dish to set before the King,"<br/>  Or Regent, who admires such kind of food;--<br/>And Coleridge, too, has lately taken wing,<br/>  But like a hawk encumbered with his hood,--<br/>Explaining Metaphysics to the nation--<br/>I wish he would explain his Explanation.<br/><br/>You, Bob! are rather insolent, you know,<br/>  At being disappointed in your wish<br/>To supersede all warblers here below,<br/>  And be the only Blackbird in the dish;<br/>And then you overstrain yourself, or so,<br/>  And tumble downward like the flying fish<br/>Gasping on deck, because you soar too high, Bob,<br/>And fall, for lack of moisture, quite a-dry, Bob!<br/><br/>And Wordsworth, in a rather long "Excursion,"<br/>  (I think the quarto holds five hundred pages),<br/>Has given a sample from the vasty version<br/>  Of his new system to perplex the sages;<br/>'T is poetry-at least by his assertion,<br/>  And may appear so when the dog-star rages--<br/>And he who understands it would be able<br/>To add a story to the Tower of Babel.<br/><br/>You--Gentlemen! by dint of long seclusion<br/>  From better company, have kept your own<br/>At Keswick, and, through still continued fusion<br/>  Of one another's minds, at last have grown<br/>To deem as a most logical conclusion,<br/>  That Poesy has wreaths for you alone:<br/>There is a narrowness in such a notion,<br/>Which makes me wish you'd change your lakes for Ocean.<br/><br/>I would not imitate the petty thought,<br/>  Nor coin my self-love to so base a vice,<br/>For all the glory your conversion brought,<br/>  Since gold alone should not have been its price.<br/>You have your salary; was 't for that you wrought?<br/>  And Wordsworth has his place in the Excise.<br/>You're shabby fellows--true--but poets still,<br/>And duly seated on the Immortal Hill.<br/><br/>Your bays may hide the baldness of your brows--<br/>  Perhaps some virtuous blushes;--let them go--<br/>To you I envy neither fruit nor boughs--<br/>  And for the fame you would engross below,<br/>The field is universal, and allows<br/>  Scope to all such as feel the inherent glow:<br/>Scott, Rogers, Campbell, Moore, and Crabbe, will try<br/>'Gainst you the question with posterity.<br/><br/>For me, who, wandering with pedestrian Muses,<br/>  Contend not with you on the wing√©d steed,<br/>I wish your fate may yield ye, when she chooses,<br/>  The fame you envy, and the skill you need;<br/>And, recollect, a poet nothing loses<br/>  In giving to his brethren their full meed<br/>Of merit--and complaint of present days<br/>Is not the certain path to future praise.<br/><br/>He that reserves his laurels for posterity<br/>  (Who does not often claim the bright reversion)<br/>Has generally no great crop to spare it, he<br/>  Being only injured by his own assertion;<br/>And although here and there some glorious rarity<br/>  Arise like Titan from the sea's immersion,<br/>The major part of such appellants go<br/>To--God knows where--for no one else can know.<br/><br/>If, fallen in evil days on evil tongues,<br/>  Milton appealed to the Avenger, Time,<br/>If Time, the Avenger, execrates his wrongs,<br/>  And makes the word "Miltonic" mean "_Sublime_,"<br/>_He_ deigned not to belie his soul in songs,<br/>  Nor turn his very talent to a crime;<br/>_He_ did not loathe the Sire to laud the Son,<br/>But closed the tyrant-hater he begun.<br/><br/>Think'st thou, could he--the blind Old Man--arise<br/>  Like Samuel from the grave, to freeze once more<br/>The blood of monarchs with his prophecies,<br/>  Or be alive again--again all hoar<br/>With time and trials, and those helpless eyes,<br/>  And heartless daughters--worn--and pale--and poor;<br/>Would _he_ adore a sultan? _he_ obey<br/>The intellectual eunuch Castlereagh?<br/><br/>Cold-blooded, smooth-faced, placid miscreant!<br/>  Dabbling its sleek young hands in Erin's gore,<br/>And thus for wider carnage taught to pant,<br/>  Transferred to gorge upon a sister shore,<br/>The vulgarest tool that Tyranny could want,<br/>  With just enough of talent, and no more,<br/>To lengthen fetters by another fixed,<br/>And offer poison long already mixed.<br/><br/>An orator of such set trash of phrase<br/>  Ineffably--legitimately vile,<br/>That even its grossest flatterers dare not praise,<br/>  Nor foes--all nations--condescend to smile,--<br/>Nor even a sprightly blunder's spark can blaze<br/>  From that Ixion grindstone's ceaseless toil,<br/>That turns and turns to give the world a notion<br/>Of endless torments and perpetual motion.<br/><br/>A bungler even in its disgusting trade,<br/>  And botching, patching, leaving still behind<br/>Something of which its masters are afraid--<br/>  States to be curbed, and thoughts to be confined,<br/>Conspiracy or Congress to be made--<br/>  Cobbling at manacles for all mankind--<br/>A tinkering slave-maker, who mends old chains,<br/>With God and Man's abhorrence for its gains.<br/><br/>If we may judge of matter by the mind,<br/>  Emasculated to the marrow _It_<br/>Hath but two objects, how to serve, and bind,<br/>  Deeming the chain it wears even men may fit,<br/>Eutropius of its many masters,--blind<br/>  To worth as freedom, wisdom as to wit,<br/>Fearless--because _no_ feeling dwells in ice,<br/>Its very courage stagnates to a vice.<br/><br/>Where shall I turn me not to _view_ its bonds,<br/>  For I will never _feel_ them?--Italy!<br/>Thy late reviving Roman soul desponds<br/>  Beneath the lie this State-thing breathed o'er thee--<br/>Thy clanking chain, and Erin's yet green wounds,<br/>  Have voices--tongues to cry aloud for me.<br/>Europe has slaves--allies--kings--armies still--<br/>And Southey lives to sing them very ill.<br/><br/>Meantime, Sir Laureate, I proceed to dedicate,<br/>  In honest simple verse, this song to you.<br/>And, if in flattering strains I do not predicate,<br/>  'T is that I still retain my "buff and blue;"<br/>My politics as yet are all to educate:<br/>  Apostasy's so fashionable, too,<br/>To keep _one_ creed's a task grown quite Herculean;<br/>Is it not so, my Tory, ultra-Julian?<br/><br/>DON JUAN<br/><br/>CANTO THE FIRST.<br/><br/>I WANT a hero: an uncommon want,<br/>  When every year and month sends forth a new one,<br/>Till, after cloying the gazettes with cant,<br/>  The age discovers he is not the true one;<br/>Of such as these I should not care to vaunt,<br/>  I'll therefore take our ancient friend Don Juan--<br/>We all have seen him, in the pantomime,<br/>Sent to the Devil somewhat ere his time.<br/><br/>Vernon, the butcher Cumberland, Wolfe, Hawke,<br/>  Prince Ferdinand, Granby, Burgoyne, Keppel, Howe,<br/>Evil and good, have had their tithe of talk,<br/>  And filled their sign-posts then, like Wellesley now;<br/>Each in their turn like Banquo's monarchs stalk,<br/>  Followers of Fame, "nine farrow" of that sow:<br/>France, too, had Buonapart√© and Dumourier<br/>Recorded in the Moniteur and Courier.<br/><br/>Barnave, Brissot, Condorcet, Mirabeau,<br/>  Petion, Clootz, Danton, Marat, La Fayette<br/>Were French, and famous people, as we know;<br/>  And there were others, scarce forgotten yet,<br/>Joubert, Hoche, Marceau, Lannes, Desaix, Moreau,<br/>  With many of the military set,<br/>Exceedingly remarkable at times,<br/>But not at all adapted to my rhymes.<br/><br/>Nelson was once Britannia's god of War,<br/>  And still should be so, but the tide is turned;<br/>There's no more to be said of Trafalgar,<br/>  'T is with our hero quietly inurned;<br/>Because the army's grown more popular,<br/>  At which the naval people are concerned;<br/>Besides, the Prince is all for the land-service.<br/>Forgetting Duncan, Nelson, Howe, and Jervis.<br/><br/>Brave men were living before Agamemnon<br/>  And since, exceeding valorous and sage,<br/>A good deal like him too, though quite the same none;<br/>  But then they shone not on the poet's page,<br/>And so have been forgotten:--I condemn none,<br/>  But can't find any in the present age<br/>Fit for my poem (that is, for my new one);<br/>So, as I said, I'll take my friend Don Juan.<br/><br/>Most epic poets plunge _"in medias res"_<br/>  (Horace makes this the heroic turnpike road),<br/>And then your hero tells, whene'er you please,<br/>  What went before--by way of episode,<br/>While seated after dinner at his ease,<br/>  Beside his mistress in some soft abode,<br/>Palace, or garden, paradise, or cavern,<br/>Which serves the happy couple for a tavern.<br/><br/>That is the usual method, but not mine--<br/>  My way is to begin with the beginning;<br/>The regularity of my design<br/>  Forbids all wandering as the worst of sinning,<br/>And therefore I shall open with a line<br/>  (Although it cost me half an hour in spinning),<br/>Narrating somewhat of Don Juan's father,<br/>And also of his mother, if you'd rather.<br/><br/>In Seville was he born, a pleasant city,<br/>  Famous for oranges and women,--he<br/>Who has not seen it will be much to pity,<br/>  So says the proverb--and I quite agree;<br/>Of all the Spanish towns is none more pretty,<br/>  Cadiz perhaps--but that you soon may see;--<br/>Don Juan's parents lived beside the river,<br/>A noble stream, and called the Guadalquivir.<br/><br/>His father's name was Jos√©-_Don_, of course,--<br/>  A true Hidalgo, free from every stain<br/>Of Moor or Hebrew blood, he traced his source<br/>  Through the most Gothic gentlemen of Spain;<br/>A better cavalier ne'er mounted horse,<br/>  Or, being mounted, e'er got down again,<br/>Than Jos√©, who begot our hero, who<br/>Begot--but that's to come----Well, to renew:<br/><br/>His mother was a learn√©d lady, famed<br/>  For every branch of every science known--<br/>In every Christian language ever named,<br/>  With virtues equalled by her wit alone:<br/>She made the cleverest people quite ashamed,<br/>  And even the good with inward envy groan,<br/>Finding themselves so very much exceeded,<br/>In their own way, by all the things that she did.<br/><br/>Her memory was a mine: she knew by heart<br/>  All Calderon and greater part of Lop√©;<br/>So, that if any actor missed his part,<br/>  She could have served him for the prompter's copy;<br/>For her Feinagle's were an useless art,<br/>  And he himself obliged to shut up shop--he<br/>Could never make a memory so fine as<br/>That which adorned the brain of Donna Inez.<br/><br/>Her favourite science was the mathematical,<br/>  Her noblest virtue was her magnanimity,<br/>Her wit (she sometimes tried at wit) was Attic all,<br/>  Her serious sayings darkened to sublimity;<br/>In short, in all things she was fairly what I call<br/>  A prodigy--her morning dress was dimity,<br/>Her evening silk, or, in the summer, muslin,<br/>And other stuffs, with which I won't stay puzzling.<br/><br/>She knew the Latin--that is, "the Lord's prayer,"<br/>  And Greek--the alphabet--I'm nearly sure;<br/>She read some French romances here and there,<br/>  Although her mode of speaking was not pure;<br/>For native Spanish she had no great care,<br/>  At least her conversation was obscure;<br/>Her thoughts were theorems, her words a problem,<br/>As if she deemed that mystery would ennoble 'em.<br/><br/>She liked the English and the Hebrew tongue,<br/>  And said there was analogy between 'em;<br/>She proved it somehow out of sacred song,<br/>  But I must leave the proofs to those who've seen 'em;<br/>But this I heard her say, and can't be wrong,<br/>  And all may think which way their judgments lean 'em,<br/>"'T is strange--the Hebrew noun which means 'I am,'<br/>The English always use to govern d--n."<br/><br/>Some women use their tongues--she _looked_ a lecture,<br/>  Each eye a sermon, and her brow a homily,<br/>An all-in-all sufficient self-director,<br/>  Like the lamented late Sir Samuel Romilly,<br/>The Law's expounder, and the State's corrector,<br/>  Whose suicide was almost an anomaly--<br/>One sad example more, that "All is vanity,"--<br/>(The jury brought their verdict in "Insanity!")<br/><br/>In short, she was a walking calculation,<br/>  Miss Edgeworth's novels stepping from their covers,<br/>Or Mrs. Trimmer's books on education,<br/>  Or "Coelebs' Wife" set out in quest of lovers,<br/>Morality's prim personification,<br/>  In which not Envy's self a flaw discovers;<br/>To others' share let "female errors fall,"<br/>For she had not even one--the worst of all.<br/><br/>Oh! she was perfect past all parallel--<br/>  Of any modern female saint's comparison;<br/>So far above the cunning powers of Hell,<br/>  Her Guardian Angel had given up his garrison;<br/>Even her minutest motions went as well<br/>  As those of the best time-piece made by Harrison:<br/>In virtues nothing earthly could surpass her,<br/>Save thine "incomparable oil," Macassar!<br/><br/>Perfect she was, but as perfection is<br/>  Insipid in this naughty world of ours,<br/>Where our first parents never learned to kiss<br/>  Till they were exiled from their earlier bowers,<br/>Where all was peace, and innocence, and bliss,<br/>  (I wonder how they got through the twelve hours),<br/>Don Jos√©, like a lineal son of Eve,<br/>Went plucking various fruit without her leave.<br/><br/>He was a mortal of the careless kind,<br/>  With no great love for learning, or the learned,<br/>Who chose to go where'er he had a mind,<br/>  And never dreamed his lady was concerned;<br/>The world, as usual, wickedly inclined<br/>  To see a kingdom or a house o'erturned,<br/>Whispered he had a mistress, some said _two_.<br/>But for domestic quarrels _one_ will do.<br/><br/>Now Donna Inez had, with all her merit,<br/>  A great opinion of her own good qualities;<br/>Neglect, indeed, requires a saint to bear it,<br/>  And such, indeed, she was in her moralities;<br/>But then she had a devil of a spirit,<br/>  And sometimes mixed up fancies with realities,<br/>And let few opportunities escape<br/>Of getting her liege lord into a scrape.<br/><br/>This was an easy matter with a man<br/>  Oft in the wrong, and never on his guard;<br/>And even the wisest, do the best they can,<br/>  Have moments, hours, and days, so unprepared,<br/>That you might "brain them with their lady's fan;"<br/>  And sometimes ladies hit exceeding hard,<br/>And fans turn into falchions in fair hands,<br/>And why and wherefore no one understands.<br/><br/>'T is pity learn√©d virgins ever wed<br/>  With persons of no sort of education,<br/>Or gentlemen, who, though well born and bred,<br/>  Grow tired of scientific conversation:<br/>I don't choose to say much upon this head,<br/>  I'm a plain man, and in a single station,<br/>But--Oh! ye lords of ladies intellectual,<br/>Inform us truly, have they not hen-pecked you all?<br/><br/>Don Jos√© and his lady quarrelled--_why_,<br/>  Not any of the many could divine,<br/>Though several thousand people chose to try,<br/> 'T was surely no concern of theirs nor mine;<br/>I loathe that low vice--curiosity;<br/>  But if there's anything in which I shine,<br/>'T is in arranging all my friends' affairs,<br/>Not having, of my own, domestic cares.<br/><br/>And so I interfered, and with the best<br/>  Intentions, but their treatment was not kind;<br/>I think the foolish people were possessed,<br/>  For neither of them could I ever find,<br/>Although their porter afterwards confessed--<br/>  But that's no matter, and the worst's behind,<br/>For little Juan o'er me threw, down stairs,<br/>A pail of housemaid's water unawares.<br/><br/>A little curly-headed, good-for-nothing,<br/>  And mischief-making monkey from his birth;<br/>His parents ne'er agreed except in doting<br/>  Upon the most unquiet imp on earth;<br/>Instead of quarrelling, had they been but both in<br/>  Their senses, they'd have sent young master forth<br/>To school, or had him soundly whipped at home,<br/>To teach him manners for the time to come.<br/><br/>Don Jos√© and the Donna Inez led<br/>  For some time an unhappy sort of life,<br/>Wishing each other, not divorced, but dead;<br/>  They lived respectably as man and wife,<br/>Their conduct was exceedingly well-bred,<br/>  And gave no outward signs of inward strife,<br/>Until at length the smothered fire broke out,<br/>And put the business past all kind of doubt.<br/><br/>For Inez called some druggists and physicians,<br/>  And tried to prove her loving lord was _mad_,<br/>But as he had some lucid intermissions,<br/>  She next decided he was only _bad_;<br/>Yet when they asked her for her depositions,<br/>  No sort of explanation could be had,<br/>Save that her duty both to man and God<br/>Required this conduct--which seemed very odd.<br/><br/>She kept a journal, where his faults were noted,<br/>  And opened certain trunks of books and letters,<br/>All which might, if occasion served, be quoted;<br/>  And then she had all Seville for abettors,<br/>Besides her good old grandmother (who doted);<br/>  The hearers of her case became repeaters,<br/>Then advocates, inquisitors, and judges,<br/>Some for amusement, others for old grudges.<br/><br/>And then this best and meekest woman bore<br/>  With such serenity her husband's woes,<br/>Just as the Spartan ladies did of yore,<br/>  Who saw their spouses killed, and nobly chose<br/>Never to say a word about them more--<br/>  Calmly she heard each calumny that rose,<br/>And saw _his_ agonies with such sublimity,<br/>That all the world exclaimed, "What magnanimity!"<br/><br/>No doubt this patience, when the world is damning us,<br/>  Is philosophic in our former friends;<br/>'T is also pleasant to be deemed magnanimous,<br/>  The more so in obtaining our own ends;<br/>And what the lawyers call a _"malus animus"_<br/>  Conduct like this by no means comprehends:<br/>Revenge in person's certainly no virtue,<br/>But then 't is not _my_ fault, if _others_ hurt you.<br/><br/>And if our quarrels should rip up old stories,<br/>  And help them with a lie or two additional,<br/>_I_'m not to blame, as you well know--no more is<br/>  Any one else--they were become traditional;<br/>Besides, their resurrection aids our glories<br/>  By contrast, which is what we just were wishing all:<br/>And Science profits by this resurrection--<br/>Dead scandals form good subjects for dissection.<br/><br/>Their friends had tried at reconciliation,<br/>  Then their relations, who made matters worse.<br/>('T were hard to tell upon a like occasion<br/>  To whom it may be best to have recourse--<br/>I can't say much for friend or yet relation)<br/>  The lawyers did their utmost for divorce,<br/>But scarce a fee was paid on either side<br/>Before, unluckily, Don Jos√© died.<br/><br/>He died: and most unluckily, because,<br/>  According to all hints I could collect<br/>From Counsel learn√©d in those kinds of laws,<br/>  (Although their talk's obscure and circumspect)<br/>His death contrived to spoil a charming cause;<br/>  A thousand pities also with respect<br/>To public feeling, which on this occasion<br/>Was manifested in a great sensation.<br/><br/>But ah! he died; and buried with him lay<br/>  The public feeling and the lawyers' fees:<br/>His house was sold, his servants sent away,<br/>  A Jew took one of his two mistresses,<br/>A priest the other--at least so they say:<br/>  I asked the doctors after his disease--<br/>He died of the slow fever called the tertian,<br/>And left his widow to her own aversion.<br/><br/>Yet Jos√© was an honourable man,<br/>  That I must say, who knew him very well;<br/>Therefore his frailties I'll no further scan,<br/>  Indeed there were not many more to tell:<br/>And if his passions now and then outran<br/>  Discretion, and were not so peaceable<br/>As Numa's (who was also named Pompilius),<br/>He had been ill brought up, and was born bilious.<br/><br/>Whate'er might be his worthlessness or worth,<br/>  Poor fellow! he had many things to wound him.<br/>Let's own--since it can do no good on earth--<br/>  It was a trying moment that which found him<br/>Standing alone beside his desolate hearth,<br/>  Where all his household gods lay shivered round him:<br/>No choice was left his feelings or his pride,<br/>Save Death or Doctors' Commons--so he died.<br/><br/>Dying intestate, Juan was sole heir<br/>  To a chancery suit, and messuages, and lands,<br/>Which, with a long minority and care,<br/>  Promised to turn out well in proper hands:<br/>Inez became sole guardian, which was fair,<br/>  And answered but to Nature's just demands;<br/>An only son left with an only mother<br/>Is brought up much more wisely than another.<br/><br/>Sagest of women, even of widows, she<br/>  Resolved that Juan should be quite a paragon,<br/>And worthy of the noblest pedigree,<br/>  (His Sire was of Castile, his Dam from Aragon)<br/>Then, for accomplishments of chivalry,<br/>  In case our Lord the King should go to war again,<br/>He learned the arts of riding, fencing, gunnery,<br/>And how to scale a fortress--or a nunnery.<br/><br/>But that which Donna Inez most desired,<br/>  And saw into herself each day before all<br/>The learn√©d tutors whom for him she hired,<br/>  Was, that his breeding should be strictly moral:<br/>Much into all his studies she inquired,<br/>  And so they were submitted first to her, all,<br/>Arts, sciences--no branch was made a mystery<br/>To Juan's eyes, excepting natural history.<br/><br/>The languages, especially the dead,<br/>  The sciences, and most of all the abstruse,<br/>The arts, at least all such as could be said<br/>  To be the most remote from common use,<br/>In all these he was much and deeply read:<br/>  But not a page of anything that's loose,<br/>Or hints continuation of the species,<br/>Was ever suffered, lest he should grow vicious.<br/><br/>His classic studies made a little puzzle,<br/>  Because of filthy loves of gods and goddesses,<br/>Who in the earlier ages raised a bustle,<br/>  But never put on pantaloons or bodices;<br/>His reverend tutors had at times a tussle,<br/>  And for their √Üneids, Iliads, and Odysseys,<br/>Were forced to make an odd sort of apology,<br/>For Donna Inez dreaded the Mythology.<br/><br/>Ovid's a rake, as half his verses show him,<br/>  Anacreon's morals are a still worse sample,<br/>Catullus scarcely has a decent poem,<br/>  I don't think Sappho's Ode a good example,<br/>Although Longinus tells us there is no hymn<br/>  Where the Sublime soars forth on wings more ample;<br/>But Virgil's songs are pure, except that horrid one<br/>Beginning with _"Formosum Pastor Corydon."_<br/><br/>Lucretius' irreligion is too strong<br/>  For early stomachs, to prove wholesome food;<br/>I can't help thinking Juvenal was wrong,<br/>  Although no doubt his real intent was good,<br/>For speaking out so plainly in his song,<br/>  So much indeed as to be downright rude;<br/>And then what proper person can be partial<br/>To all those nauseous epigrams of Martial?<br/><br/>Juan was taught from out the best edition,<br/>  Expurgated by learned men, who place,<br/>Judiciously, from out the schoolboy's vision,<br/>  The grosser parts; but, fearful to deface<br/>Too much their modest bard by this omission,<br/>  And pitying sore his mutilated case,<br/>They only add them all in an appendix,<br/>Which saves, in fact, the trouble of an index;<br/><br/>For there we have them all "at one fell swoop,"<br/>  Instead of being scattered through the pages;<br/>They stand forth marshalled in a handsome troop,<br/>  To meet the ingenuous youth of future ages,<br/>Till some less rigid editor shall stoop<br/>  To call them back into their separate cages,<br/>Instead of standing staring all together,<br/>Like garden gods--and not so decent either.<br/><br/>The Missal too (it was the family Missal)<br/>  Was ornamented in a sort of way<br/>Which ancient mass-books often are, and this all<br/>  Kinds of grotesques illumined; and how they,<br/>Who saw those figures on the margin kiss all,<br/>  Could turn their optics to the text and pray,<br/>Is more than I know--But Don Juan's mother<br/>Kept this herself, and gave her son another.<br/><br/>Sermons he read, and lectures he endured,<br/>  And homilies, and lives of all the saints;<br/>To Jerome and to Chrysostom inured,<br/>  He did not take such studies for restraints;<br/>But how Faith is acquired, and then insured,<br/>  So well not one of the aforesaid paints<br/>As Saint Augustine in his fine Confessions,<br/>Which make the reader envy his transgressions.<br/><br/>This, too, was a sealed book to little Juan--<br/>  I can't but say that his mamma was right,<br/>If such an education was the true one.<br/>  She scarcely trusted him from out her sight;<br/>Her maids were old, and if she took a new one,<br/>  You might be sure she was a perfect fright;<br/>She did this during even her husband's life--<br/>I recommend as much to every wife.<br/><br/>Young Juan waxed in goodliness and grace;<br/>  At six a charming child, and at eleven<br/>With all the promise of as fine a face<br/>  As e'er to Man's maturer growth was given:<br/>He studied steadily, and grew apace,<br/>  And seemed, at least, in the right road to Heaven,<br/>For half his days were passed at church, the other<br/>Between his tutors, confessor, and mother.<br/><br/>At six, I said, he was a charming child,<br/>  At twelve he was a fine, but quiet boy;<br/>Although in infancy a little wild,<br/>  They tamed him down amongst them: to destroy<br/>His natural spirit not in vain they toiled,<br/>  At least it seemed so; and his mother's joy<br/>Was to declare how sage, and still, and steady,<br/>Her young philosopher was grown already.<br/><br/>I had my doubts, perhaps I have them still,<br/> But what I say is neither here nor there:<br/>I knew his father well, and have some skill<br/>  In character--but it would not be fair<br/>From sire to son to augur good or ill:<br/>  He and his wife were an ill-sorted pair--<br/>But scandal's my aversion--I protest<br/>Against all evil speaking, even in jest.<br/><br/>For my part I say nothing--nothing--but<br/>  _This_ I will say--my reasons are my own--<br/>That if I had an only son to put<br/>  To school (as God be praised that I have none),<br/>'T is not with Donna Inez I would shut<br/>  Him up to learn his catechism alone,<br/>No--no--I'd send him out betimes to college,<br/>For there it was I picked up my own knowledge.<br/><br/>For there one learns--'t is not for me to boast,<br/>  Though I acquired--but I pass over _that_,<br/>As well as all the Greek I since have lost:<br/>  I say that there's the place--but "_Verbum sat_,"<br/>I think I picked up too, as well as most,<br/>  Knowledge of matters--but no matter _what_--<br/>I never married--but, I think, I know<br/>That sons should not be educated so.<br/><br/>Young Juan now was sixteen years of age,<br/>  Tall, handsome, slender, but well knit: he seemed<br/>Active, though not so sprightly, as a page;<br/>  And everybody but his mother deemed<br/>Him almost man; but she flew in a rage<br/>  And bit her lips (for else she might have screamed)<br/>If any said so--for to be precocious<br/>Was in her eyes a thing the most atrocious.<br/><br/>Amongst her numerous acquaintance, all<br/>  Selected for discretion and devotion,<br/>There was the Donna Julia, whom to call<br/>  Pretty were but to give a feeble notion<br/>Of many charms in her as natural<br/>  As sweetness to the flower, or salt to Ocean,<br/>Her zone to Venus, or his bow to Cupid,<br/>(But this last simile is trite and stupid.)<br/><br/>The darkness of her Oriental eye<br/>  Accorded with her Moorish origin;<br/>(Her blood was not all Spanish; by the by,<br/>  In Spain, you know, this is a sort of sin;)<br/>When proud Granada fell, and, forced to fly,<br/>  Boabdil wept: of Donna Julia's kin<br/>Some went to Africa, some stayed in Spain--<br/>Her great great grandmamma chose to remain.<br/><br/>She married (I forget the pedigree)<br/>  With an Hidalgo, who transmitted down<br/>His blood less noble than such blood should be;<br/>  At such alliances his sires would frown,<br/>In that point so precise in each degree<br/>  That they bred _in and in_, as might be shown,<br/>Marrying their cousins--nay, their aunts, and nieces,<br/>Which always spoils the breed, if it increases.<br/><br/>This heathenish cross restored the breed again,<br/>  Ruined its blood, but much improved its flesh;<br/>For from a root the ugliest in Old Spain<br/>  Sprung up a branch as beautiful as fresh;<br/>The sons no more were short, the daughters plain:<br/>  But there's a rumour which I fain would hush,<br/>'T is said that Donna Julia's grandmamma<br/>Produced her Don more heirs at love than law.<br/><br/>However this might be, the race went on<br/>  Improving still through every generation,<br/>Until it centred in an only son,<br/>  Who left an only daughter; my narration<br/>May have suggested that this single one<br/>  Could be but Julia (whom on this occasion<br/>I shall have much to speak about), and she<br/>Was married, charming, chaste, and twenty-three.<br/><br/>Her eye (I'm very fond of handsome eyes)<br/>  Was large and dark, suppressing half its fire<br/>Until she spoke, then through its soft disguise<br/>  Flashed an expression more of pride than ire,<br/>And love than either; and there would arise<br/>  A something in them which was not desire,<br/>But would have been, perhaps, but for the soul<br/>Which struggled through and chastened down the whole.<br/><br/>Her glossy hair was clustered o'er a brow<br/>  Bright with intelligence, and fair, and smooth;<br/>Her eyebrow's shape was like the a√´rial bow,<br/>  Her cheek all purple with the beam of youth,<br/>Mounting, at times, to a transparent glow,<br/>  As if her veins ran lightning; she, in sooth,<br/>Possessed an air and grace by no means common:<br/>Her stature tall--I hate a dumpy woman.<br/><br/>Wedded she was some years, and to a man<br/>  Of fifty, and such husbands are in plenty;<br/>And yet, I think, instead of such a ONE<br/>  'T were better to have TWO of five-and-twenty,<br/>Especially in countries near the sun:<br/>  And now I think on 't, "_mi vien in mente_",<br/>Ladies even of the most uneasy virtue<br/>Prefer a spouse whose age is short of thirty.<br/><br/>'T is a sad thing, I cannot choose but say,<br/>  And all the fault of that indecent sun,<br/>Who cannot leave alone our helpless clay,<br/>  But will keep baking, broiling, burning on,<br/>That howsoever people fast and pray,<br/>  The flesh is frail, and so the soul undone:<br/>What men call gallantry, and gods adultery,<br/>Is much more common where the climate's sultry,<br/><br/>Happy the nations of the moral North!<br/>  Where all is virtue, and the winter season<br/>Sends sin, without a rag on, shivering forth<br/>  ('T was snow that brought St. Anthony to reason);<br/>Where juries cast up what a wife is worth,<br/>  By laying whate'er sum, in mulct, they please on<br/>The lover, who must pay a handsome price,<br/>Because it is a marketable vice.<br/><br/>Alfonso was the name of Julia's lord,<br/>  A man well looking for his years, and who<br/>Was neither much beloved nor yet abhorred:<br/>  They lived together as most people do,<br/>Suffering each other's foibles by accord,<br/>  And not exactly either _one_ or _two_;<br/>Yet he was jealous, though he did not show it,<br/>For Jealousy dislikes the world to know it.<br/><br/>Julia was--yet I never could see why--<br/>  With Donna Inez quite a favourite friend;<br/>Between their tastes there was small sympathy,<br/>  For not a line had Julia ever penned:<br/>Some people whisper (but, no doubt, they lie,<br/>  For Malice still imputes some private end)<br/>That Inez had, ere Don Alfonso's marriage,<br/>Forgot with him her very prudent carriage;<br/><br/>And that still keeping up the old connection,<br/>  Which Time had lately rendered much more chaste,<br/>She took his lady also in affection,<br/>  And certainly this course was much the best:<br/>She flattered Julia with her sage protection,<br/>  And complimented Don Alfonso's taste;<br/>And if she could not (who can?) silence scandal,<br/>At least she left it a more slender handle.<br/><br/>I can't tell whether Julia saw the affair<br/>  With other people's eyes, or if her own<br/>Discoveries made, but none could be aware<br/>  Of this, at least no symptom e'er was shown;<br/>Perhaps she did not know, or did not care,<br/>  Indifferent from the first, or callous grown:<br/>I'm really puzzled what to think or say,<br/>She kept her counsel in so close a way.<br/><br/>Juan she saw, and, as a pretty child,<br/>  Caressed him often--such a thing might be<br/>Quite innocently done, and harmless styled,<br/>  When she had twenty years, and thirteen he;<br/>But I am not so sure I should have smiled<br/>  When he was sixteen, Julia twenty-three;<br/>These few short years make wondrous alterations,<br/>Particularly amongst sun-burnt nations.<br/><br/>Whate'er the cause might be, they had become<br/>  Changed; for the dame grew distant, the youth shy,<br/>Their looks cast down, their greetings almost dumb,<br/>  And much embarrassment in either eye;<br/>There surely will be little doubt with some<br/>  That Donna Julia knew the reason why,<br/>But as for Juan, he had no more notion<br/>Than he who never saw the sea of Ocean.<br/><br/>Yet Julia's very coldness still was kind,<br/>  And tremulously gentle her small hand<br/>Withdrew itself from his, but left behind<br/>  A little pressure, thrilling, and so bland<br/>And slight, so very slight, that to the mind<br/>  'T was but a doubt; but ne'er magician's wand<br/>Wrought change with all Armida's fairy art<br/>Like what this light touch left on Juan's heart.<br/><br/>And if she met him, though she smiled no more,<br/>  She looked a sadness sweeter than her smile,<br/>As if her heart had deeper thoughts in store<br/>  She must not own, but cherished more the while<br/>For that compression in its burning core;<br/>  Even Innocence itself has many a wile,<br/>And will not dare to trust itself with truth,<br/>And Love is taught hypocrisy from youth.<br/><br/>But Passion most dissembles, yet betrays<br/>  Even by its darkness; as the blackest sky<br/>Foretells the heaviest tempest, it displays<br/>  Its workings through the vainly guarded eye,<br/>And in whatever aspect it arrays<br/>  Itself, 't is still the same hypocrisy;<br/>Coldness or Anger, even Disdain or Hate,<br/>Are masks it often wears, and still too late.<br/><br/>Then there were sighs, the deeper for suppression,<br/>  And stolen glances, sweeter for the theft,<br/>And burning blushes, though for no transgression,<br/>  Tremblings when met, and restlessness when left;<br/>All these are little preludes to possession,<br/>  Of which young Passion cannot be bereft,<br/>And merely tend to show how greatly Love is<br/>Embarrassed at first starting with a novice.<br/><br/>Poor Julia's heart was in an awkward state;<br/>  She felt it going, and resolved to make<br/>The noblest efforts for herself and mate,<br/>  For Honour's, Pride's, Religion's, Virtue's sake:<br/>Her resolutions were most truly great,<br/>  And almost might have made a Tarquin quake:<br/>She prayed the Virgin Mary for her grace,<br/>As being the best judge of a lady's case.<br/><br/>She vowed she never would see Juan more,<br/>  And next day paid a visit to his mother,<br/>And looked extremely at the opening door,<br/>  Which, by the Virgin's grace, let in another;<br/>Grateful she was, and yet a little sore--<br/>  Again it opens, it can be no other,<br/>'T is surely Juan now--No! I'm afraid<br/>That night the Virgin was no further prayed.<br/><br/>She now determined that a virtuous woman<br/>  Should rather face and overcome temptation,<br/>That flight was base and dastardly, and no man<br/>  Should ever give her heart the least sensation,<br/>That is to say, a thought beyond the common<br/>  Preference, that we must feel, upon occasion,<br/>For people who are pleasanter than others,<br/>But then they only seem so many brothers.<br/><br/>And even if by chance--and who can tell?<br/>  The Devil's so very sly--she should discover<br/>That all within was not so very well,<br/>  And, if still free, that such or such a lover<br/>Might please perhaps, a virtuous wife can quell<br/>  Such thoughts, and be the better when they're over;<br/>And if the man should ask, 't is but denial:<br/>I recommend young ladies to make trial.<br/><br/>And, then, there are such things as Love divine,<br/>  Bright and immaculate, unmixed and pure,<br/>Such as the angels think so very fine,<br/>  And matrons, who would be no less secure,<br/>Platonic, perfect, "just such love as mine;"<br/>  Thus Julia said--and thought so, to be sure;<br/>And so I'd have her think, were _I_ the man<br/>On whom her reveries celestial ran.<br/><br/>Such love is innocent, and may exist<br/>  Between young persons without any danger.<br/>A hand may first, and then a lip be kissed;<br/>  For my part, to such doings I'm a stranger,<br/>But _hear_ these freedoms form the utmost list<br/>  Of all o'er which such love may be a ranger:<br/>If people go beyond, 't is quite a crime,<br/>But not my fault--I tell them all in time.<br/><br/>Love, then, but Love within its proper limits,<br/>  Was Julia's innocent determination<br/>In young Don Juan's favour, and to him its<br/>  Exertion might be useful on occasion;<br/>And, lighted at too pure a shrine to dim its<br/>  Ethereal lustre, with what sweet persuasion<br/>He might be taught, by Love and her together--<br/>I really don't know what, nor Julia either.<br/><br/>Fraught with this fine intention, and well fenced<br/>  In mail of proof--her purity of soul--<br/>She, for the future, of her strength convinced,<br/>  And that her honour was a rock, or mole,<br/>Exceeding sagely from that hour dispensed<br/>  With any kind of troublesome control;<br/>But whether Julia to the task was equal<br/>Is that which must be mentioned in the sequel.<br/><br/>Her plan she deemed both innocent and feasible,<br/>  And, surely, with a stripling of sixteen<br/>Not Scandal's fangs could fix on much that's seizable,<br/>  Or if they did so, satisfied to mean<br/>Nothing but what was good, her breast was peaceable--<br/>  A quiet conscience makes one so serene!<br/>Christians have burnt each other, quite persuaded<br/>That all the Apostles would have done as they did.<br/><br/>And if in the mean time her husband died,<br/>  But Heaven forbid that such a thought should cross<br/>Her brain, though in a dream! (and then she sighed)<br/>  Never could she survive that common loss;<br/>But just suppose that moment should betide,<br/>  I only say suppose it--_inter nos_:<br/>(This should be _entre nous_, for Julia thought<br/>In French, but then the rhyme would go for nought.)<br/><br/>I only say, suppose this supposition:<br/>  Juan being then grown up to man's estate<br/>Would fully suit a widow of condition,<br/>  Even seven years hence it would not be too late;<br/>And in the interim (to pursue this vision)<br/>  The mischief, after all, could not be great,<br/>For he would learn the rudiments of Love,<br/>I mean the _seraph_ way of those above.<br/><br/>So much for Julia! Now we'll turn to Juan.<br/>  Poor little fellow! he had no idea<br/>Of his own case, and never hit the true one;<br/>  In feelings quick as Ovid's Miss Medea,<br/>He puzzled over what he found a new one,<br/>  But not as yet imagined it could be a<br/>Thing quite in course, and not at all alarming,<br/>Which, with a little patience, might grow charming.<br/><br/>Silent and pensive, idle, restless, slow,<br/>  His home deserted for the lonely wood,<br/>Tormented with a wound he could not know,<br/>  His, like all deep grief, plunged in solitude:<br/>I'm fond myself of solitude or so,<br/>  But then, I beg it may be understood,<br/>By solitude I mean a Sultan's (not<br/>A Hermit's), with a haram for a grot.<br/><br/>"Oh Love! in such a wilderness as this,<br/>  Where Transport and Security entwine,<br/>Here is the Empire of thy perfect bliss,<br/>  And here thou art a God indeed divine."<br/>The bard I quote from does not sing amiss,<br/>  With the exception of the second line,<br/>For that same twining "Transport and Security"<br/>Are twisted to a phrase of some obscurity.<br/><br/>The Poet meant, no doubt, and thus appeals<br/>  To the good sense and senses of mankind,<br/>The very thing which everybody feels,<br/>  As all have found on trial, or may find,<br/>That no one likes to be disturbed at meals<br/>  Or love.--I won't say more about "entwined"<br/>Or "Transport," as we knew all that before,<br/>But beg "Security" will bolt the door.<br/><br/>Young Juan wandered by the glassy brooks,<br/>  Thinking unutterable things; he threw<br/>Himself at length within the leafy nooks<br/>  Where the wild branch of the cork forest grew;<br/>There poets find materials for their books,<br/>  And every now and then we read them through,<br/>So that their plan and prosody are eligible,<br/>Unless, like Wordsworth, they prove unintelligible.<br/><br/>He, Juan (and not Wordsworth), so pursued<br/>  His self-communion with his own high soul,<br/>Until his mighty heart, in its great mood,<br/>  Had mitigated part, though not the whole<br/>Of its disease; he did the best he could<br/>  With things not very subject to control,<br/>And turned, without perceiving his condition,<br/>Like Coleridge, into a metaphysician.<br/><br/>He thought about himself, and the whole earth,<br/>  Of man the wonderful, and of the stars,<br/>And how the deuce they ever could have birth:<br/>  And then he thought of earthquakes, and of wars,<br/>How many miles the moon might have in girth,<br/>  Of air-balloons, and of the many bars<br/>To perfect knowledge of the boundless skies;--<br/>And then he thought of Donna Julia's eyes.<br/><br/>In thoughts like these true Wisdom may discern<br/>  Longings sublime, and aspirations high,<br/>Which some are born with, but the most part learn<br/>  To plague themselves withal, they know not why:<br/>'T was strange that one so young should thus concern<br/>  His brain about the action of the sky;<br/>If _you_ think 't was Philosophy that this did,<br/>I can't help thinking puberty assisted.<br/><br/>He pored upon the leaves, and on the flowers,<br/>  And heard a voice in all the winds; and then<br/>He thought of wood-nymphs and immortal bowers,<br/>  And how the goddesses came down to men:<br/>He missed the pathway, he forgot the hours,<br/>  And when he looked upon his watch again,<br/>He found how much old Time had been a winner--<br/>He also found that he had lost his dinner.<br/><br/>Sometimes he turned to gaze upon his book,<br/>  Boscan, or Garcilasso;--by the wind<br/>Even as the page is rustled while we look,<br/>  So by the poesy of his own mind<br/>Over the mystic leaf his soul was shook,<br/>  As if 't were one whereon magicians bind<br/>Their spells, and give them to the passing gale,<br/>According to some good old woman's tale.<br/><br/>Thus would he while his lonely hours away<br/>  Dissatisfied, not knowing what he wanted;<br/>Nor glowing reverie, nor poet's lay,<br/>  Could yield his spirit that for which it panted,<br/>A bosom whereon he his head might lay,<br/>  And hear the heart beat with the love it granted,<br/>With----several other things, which I forget,<br/>Or which, at least, I need not mention yet.<br/><br/>Those lonely walks, and lengthening reveries,<br/>  Could not escape the gentle Julia's eyes;<br/>She saw that Juan was not at his ease;<br/>  But that which chiefly may, and must surprise,<br/>Is, that the Donna Inez did not tease<br/>  Her only son with question or surmise;<br/>Whether it was she did not see, or would not,<br/>Or, like all very clever people, could not.<br/><br/>This may seem strange, but yet 't is very common;<br/>  For instance--gentlemen, whose ladies take<br/>Leave to o'erstep the written rights of Woman,<br/>  And break the----Which commandment is 't they break?<br/>(I have forgot the number, and think no man<br/>  Should rashly quote, for fear of a mistake;)<br/>I say, when these same gentlemen are jealous,<br/>They make some blunder, which their ladies tell us.<br/><br/>A real husband always is suspicious,<br/>  But still no less suspects in the wrong place,<br/>Jealous of some one who had no such wishes,<br/>  Or pandering blindly to his own disgrace,<br/>By harbouring some dear friend extremely vicious;<br/>  The last indeed's infallibly the case:<br/>And when the spouse and friend are gone off wholly,<br/>He wonders at their vice, and not his folly.<br/><br/>Thus parents also are at times short-sighted:<br/>  Though watchful as the lynx, they ne'er discover,<br/>The while the wicked world beholds delighted,<br/>  Young Hopeful's mistress, or Miss Fanny's lover,<br/>Till some confounded escapade has blighted<br/>  The plan of twenty years, and all is over;<br/>And then the mother cries, the father swears,<br/>And wonders why the devil he got heirs.<br/><br/>But Inez was so anxious, and so clear<br/>  Of sight, that I must think, on this occasion,<br/>She had some other motive much more near<br/>  For leaving Juan to this new temptation,<br/>But what that motive was, I sha'n't say here;<br/>  Perhaps to finish Juan's education,<br/>Perhaps to open Don Alfonso's eyes,<br/>In case he thought his wife too great a prize.<br/><br/>It was upon a day, a summer's day;--<br/>  Summer's indeed a very dangerous season,<br/>And so is spring about the end of May;<br/>  The sun, no doubt, is the prevailing reason;<br/>But whatsoe'er the cause is, one may say,<br/>  And stand convicted of more truth than treason,<br/>That there are months which nature grows more merry in,--<br/>March has its hares, and May must have its heroine.<br/><br/>'T was on a summer's day--the sixth of June:<br/>  I like to be particular in dates,<br/>Not only of the age, and year, but moon;<br/>  They are a sort of post-house, where the Fates<br/>Change horses, making History change its tune,<br/>  Then spur away o'er empires and o'er states,<br/>Leaving at last not much besides chronology,<br/>Excepting the post-obits of theology.<br/><br/>'T was on the sixth of June, about the hour<br/>  Of half-past six--perhaps still nearer seven--<br/>When Julia sate within as pretty a bower<br/>  As e'er held houri in that heathenish heaven<br/>Described by Mahomet, and Anacreon Moore,<br/>  To whom the lyre and laurels have been given,<br/>With all the trophies of triumphant song--<br/>He won them well, and may he wear them long!<br/><br/>She sate, but not alone; I know not well<br/>  How this same interview had taken place,<br/>And even if I knew, I should not tell--<br/>  People should hold their tongues in any case;<br/>No matter how or why the thing befell,<br/>  But there were she and Juan, face to face--<br/>When two such faces are so, 't would be wise,<br/>But very difficult, to shut their eyes.<br/><br/>How beautiful she looked! her conscious heart<br/>  Glowed in her cheek, and yet she felt no wrong:<br/>Oh Love! how perfect is thy mystic art,<br/>  Strengthening the weak, and trampling on the strong!<br/>How self-deceitful is the sagest part<br/>  Of mortals whom thy lure hath led along!--<br/>The precipice she stood on was immense,<br/>So was her creed in her own innocence.<br/><br/>She thought of her own strength, and Juan's youth,<br/>  And of the folly of all prudish fears,<br/>Victorious Virtue, and domestic Truth,<br/>  And then of Don Alfonso's fifty years:<br/>I wish these last had not occurred, in sooth,<br/>  Because that number rarely much endears,<br/>And through all climes, the snowy and the sunny,<br/>Sounds ill in love, whate'er it may in money.<br/><br/>When people say, "I've told you _fifty_ times,"<br/>  They mean to scold, and very often do;<br/>When poets say, "I've written _fifty_ rhymes,"<br/>  They make you dread that they 'll recite them too;<br/>In gangs of _fifty_, thieves commit their crimes;<br/>  At _fifty_ love for love is rare, 't is true,<br/>But then, no doubt, it equally as true is,<br/>A good deal may be bought for _fifty_ Louis.<br/><br/>Julia had honour, virtue, truth, and love<br/>  For Don Alfonso; and she inly swore,<br/>By all the vows below to Powers above,<br/>  She never would disgrace the ring she wore,<br/>Nor leave a wish which wisdom might reprove;<br/>  And while she pondered this, besides much more,<br/>One hand on Juan's carelessly was thrown,<br/>Quite by mistake--she thought it was her own;<br/><br/>Unconsciously she leaned upon the other,<br/>  Which played within the tangles of her hair;<br/>And to contend with thoughts she could not smother<br/>  She seemed by the distraction of her air.<br/>'T was surely very wrong in Juan's mother<br/>  To leave together this imprudent pair,<br/>She who for many years had watched her son so--<br/>I'm very certain _mine_ would not have done so.<br/><br/>The hand which still held Juan's, by degrees<br/>  Gently, but palpably confirmed its grasp,<br/>As if it said, "Detain me, if you please;"<br/>  Yet there's no doubt she only meant to clasp<br/>His fingers with a pure Platonic squeeze;<br/>  She would have shrunk as from a toad, or asp,<br/>Had she imagined such a thing could rouse<br/>A feeling dangerous to a prudent spouse.<br/><br/>I cannot know what Juan thought of this,<br/>  But what he did, is much what you would do;<br/>His young lip thanked it with a grateful kiss,<br/>  And then, abashed at its own joy, withdrew<br/>In deep despair, lest he had done amiss,--<br/>  Love is so very timid when 't is new:<br/>She blushed, and frowned not, but she strove to speak,<br/>And held her tongue, her voice was grown so weak.<br/><br/>The sun set, and up rose the yellow moon:<br/>  The Devil's in the moon for mischief; they<br/>Who called her CHASTE, methinks, began too soon<br/>  Their nomenclature; there is not a day,<br/>The longest, not the twenty-first of June,<br/>  Sees half the business in a wicked way,<br/>On which three single hours of moonshine smile--<br/>And then she looks so modest all the while!<br/><br/>There is a dangerous silence in that hour,<br/>  A stillness, which leaves room for the full soul<br/>To open all itself, without the power<br/>  Of calling wholly back its self-control;<br/>The silver light which, hallowing tree and tower,<br/>  Sheds beauty and deep softness o'er the whole,<br/>Breathes also to the heart, and o'er it throws<br/>A loving languor, which is not repose.<br/><br/>And Julia sate with Juan, half embraced<br/>  And half retiring from the glowing arm,<br/>Which trembled like the bosom where 't was placed;<br/>  Yet still she must have thought there was no harm,<br/>Or else 't were easy to withdraw her waist;<br/>  But then the situation had its charm,<br/>And then--God knows what next--I can't go on;<br/>I'm almost sorry that I e'er begun.<br/><br/>Oh Plato! Plato! you have paved the way,<br/>  With your confounded fantasies, to more<br/>Immoral conduct by the fancied sway<br/>  Your system feigns o'er the controlless core<br/>Of human hearts, than all the long array<br/>  Of poets and romancers:--You're a bore,<br/>A charlatan, a coxcomb--and have been,<br/>At best, no better than a go-between.<br/><br/>And Julia's voice was lost, except in sighs,<br/>  Until too late for useful conversation;<br/>The tears were gushing from her gentle eyes,<br/>  I wish, indeed, they had not had occasion;<br/>But who, alas! can love, and then be wise?<br/>  Not that Remorse did not oppose Temptation;<br/>A little still she strove, and much repented,<br/>And whispering "I will ne'er consent"--consented.<br/><br/>'T is said that Xerxes offered a reward<br/>  To those who could invent him a new pleasure:<br/>Methinks the requisition's rather hard,<br/>  And must have cost his Majesty a treasure:<br/>For my part, I'm a moderate-minded bard,<br/>  Fond of a little love (which I call leisure);<br/>I care not for new pleasures, as the old<br/>Are quite enough for me, so they but hold.<br/><br/>Oh Pleasure! you're indeed a pleasant thing,<br/>  Although one must be damned for you, no doubt:<br/>I make a resolution every spring<br/>  Of reformation, ere the year run out,<br/>But somehow, this my vestal vow takes wing,<br/>  Yet still, I trust, it may be kept throughout:<br/>I'm very sorry, very much ashamed,<br/>And mean, next winter, to be quite reclaimed.<br/><br/>Here my chaste Muse a liberty must take--<br/>  Start not! still chaster reader--she'll be nice hence-<br/>Forward, and there is no great cause to quake;<br/>  This liberty is a poetic licence,<br/>Which some irregularity may make<br/>  In the design, and as I have a high sense<br/>Of Aristotle and the Rules, 't is fit<br/>To beg his pardon when I err a bit.<br/><br/>This licence is to hope the reader will<br/>  Suppose from June the sixth (the fatal day,<br/>Without whose epoch my poetic skill<br/>  For want of facts would all be thrown away),<br/>But keeping Julia and Don Juan still<br/>  In sight, that several months have passed; we'll say<br/>'T was in November, but I'm not so sure<br/>About the day--the era's more obscure.<br/><br/>We'll talk of that anon.--'T is sweet to hear<br/>  At midnight on the blue and moonlit deep<br/>The song and oar of Adria's gondolier,<br/>  By distance mellowed, o'er the waters sweep;<br/>'T is sweet to see the evening star appear;<br/>  'T is sweet to listen as the night-winds creep<br/>From leaf to leaf; 't is sweet to view on high<br/>The rainbow, based on ocean, span the sky.<br/><br/>'T is sweet to hear the watch-dog's honest bark<br/>  Bay deep-mouthed welcome as we draw near home;<br/>'T is sweet to know there is an eye will mark<br/>  Our coming, and look brighter when we come;<br/>'T is sweet to be awakened by the lark,<br/>  Or lulled by falling waters; sweet the hum<br/>Of bees, the voice of girls, the song of birds,<br/>The lisp of children, and their earliest words.<br/><br/>Sweet is the vintage, when the showering grapes<br/>  In Bacchanal profusion reel to earth,<br/>Purple and gushing: sweet are our escapes<br/>  From civic revelry to rural mirth;<br/>Sweet to the miser are his glittering heaps,<br/>  Sweet to the father is his first-born's birth,<br/>Sweet is revenge--especially to women--<br/>Pillage to soldiers, prize-money to seamen.<br/><br/>Sweet is a legacy, and passing sweet<br/>  The unexpected death of some old lady,<br/>Or gentleman of seventy years complete,<br/>  Who've made "us youth" wait too--too long already,<br/>For an estate, or cash, or country seat,<br/>  Still breaking, but with stamina so steady,<br/>That all the Israelites are fit to mob its<br/>Next owner for their double-damned post-obits.<br/><br/>'T is sweet to win, no matter how, one's laurels,<br/>  By blood or ink; 't is sweet to put an end<br/>To strife; 't is sometimes sweet to have our quarrels,<br/>  Particularly with a tiresome friend:<br/>Sweet is old wine in bottles, ale in barrels;<br/>  Dear is the helpless creature we defend<br/>Against the world; and dear the schoolboy spot<br/>We ne'er forget, though there we are forgot.<br/><br/>But sweeter still than this, than these, than all,<br/>  Is first and passionate Love--it stands alone,<br/>Like Adam's recollection of his fall;<br/>  The Tree of Knowledge has been plucked--all 's known--<br/>And Life yields nothing further to recall<br/>  Worthy of this ambrosial sin, so shown,<br/>No doubt in fable, as the unforgiven<br/>Fire which Prometheus filched for us from Heaven.<br/><br/>Man's a strange animal, and makes strange use<br/>  Of his own nature, and the various arts,<br/>And likes particularly to produce<br/>  Some new experiment to show his parts;<br/>This is the age of oddities let loose,<br/>  Where different talents find their different marts;<br/>You'd best begin with truth, and when you've lost your<br/>Labour, there's a sure market for imposture.<br/><br/>What opposite discoveries we have seen!<br/>  (Signs of true genius, and of empty pockets.)<br/>One makes new noses, one a guillotine,<br/>  One breaks your bones, one sets them in their sockets;<br/>But Vaccination certainly has been<br/>  A kind antithesis to Congreve's rockets,<br/>With which the Doctor paid off an old pox,<br/>By borrowing a new one from an ox.<br/><br/>Bread has been made (indifferent) from potatoes:<br/>  And Galvanism has set some corpses grinning,<br/>But has not answered like the apparatus<br/>  Of the Humane Society's beginning,<br/>By which men are unsuffocated gratis:<br/>  What wondrous new machines have late been spinning!<br/>I said the small-pox has gone out of late;<br/>Perhaps it may be followed by the great.<br/><br/>'T is said the great came from America;<br/>  Perhaps it may set out on its return,--<br/>The population there so spreads, they say<br/>  'T is grown high time to thin it in its turn,<br/>With war, or plague, or famine--any way,<br/>  So that civilisation they may learn;<br/>And which in ravage the more loathsome evil is--<br/>Their real _lues,_ or our pseudo-syphilis?<br/><br/>This is the patent age of new inventions<br/>  For killing bodies, and for saving souls,<br/>All propagated with the best intentions:<br/>  Sir Humphry Davy's lantern, by which coals<br/>Are safely mined for in the mode he mentions,<br/>  Tombuctoo travels, voyages to the Poles<br/>Are ways to benefit mankind, as true,<br/>Perhaps, as shooting them at Waterloo.<br/><br/>Man's a phenomenon, one knows not what,<br/>  And wonderful beyond all wondrous measure;<br/>'T is pity though, in this sublime world, that<br/>  Pleasure's a sin, and sometimes Sin's a pleasure;<br/>Few mortals know what end they would be at,<br/>  But whether Glory, Power, or Love, or Treasure,<br/>The path is through perplexing ways, and when<br/>The goal is gained, we die, you know--and then----<br/><br/>What then?--I do not know, no more do you--<br/>  And so good night.--Return we to our story:<br/>'T was in November, when fine days are few,<br/>  And the far mountains wax a little hoary,<br/>And clap a white cape on their mantles blue;<br/>  And the sea dashes round the promontory,<br/>And the loud breaker boils against the rock,<br/>And sober suns must set at five o'clock.<br/><br/>'T was, as the watchmen say, a cloudy night;<br/>  No moon, no stars, the wind was low or loud<br/>By gusts, and many a sparkling hearth was bright<br/>  With the piled wood, round which the family crowd;<br/>There's something cheerful in that sort of light,<br/>  Even as a summer sky's without a cloud:<br/>I'm fond of fire, and crickets, and all that,<br/>A lobster salad, and champagne, and chat.<br/><br/>'T was midnight--Donna Julia was in bed,<br/>  Sleeping, most probably,--when at her door<br/>Arose a clatter might awake the dead,<br/>  If they had never been awoke before,<br/>And that they have been so we all have read,<br/>  And are to be so, at the least, once more;--<br/>The door was fastened, but with voice and fist<br/>First knocks were heard, then "Madam--Madam--hist!<br/><br/>"For God's sake, Madam--Madam--here's my master,<br/>  With more than half the city at his back--Was<br/>ever heard of such a curst disaster!<br/>  'T is not my fault--I kept good watch--Alack!<br/>Do pray undo the bolt a little faster--<br/>  They're on the stair just now, and in a crack<br/>Will all be here; perhaps he yet may fly--<br/>Surely the window's not so _very_ high!"<br/><br/>By this time Don Alfonso was arrived,<br/>  With torches, friends, and servants in great number;<br/>The major part of them had long been wived,<br/>  And therefore paused not to disturb the slumber<br/>Of any wicked woman, who contrived<br/>  By stealth her husband's temples to encumber:<br/>Examples of this kind are so contagious,<br/>Were _one_ not punished, _all_ would be outrageous.<br/><br/>I can't tell how, or why, or what suspicion<br/>  Could enter into Don Alfonso's head;<br/>But for a cavalier of his condition<br/>  It surely was exceedingly ill-bred,<br/>Without a word of previous admonition,<br/>  To hold a levee round his lady's bed,<br/>And summon lackeys, armed with fire and sword,<br/>To prove himself the thing he most abhorred.<br/><br/>Poor Donna Julia! starting as from sleep,<br/>  (Mind--that I do not say--she had not slept),<br/>Began at once to scream, and yawn, and weep;<br/>  Her maid, Antonia, who was an adept,<br/>Contrived to fling the bed-clothes in a heap,<br/>  As if she had just now from out them crept:<br/>I can't tell why she should take all this trouble<br/>To prove her mistress had been sleeping double.<br/><br/>But Julia mistress, and Antonia maid,<br/>  Appeared like two poor harmless women, who<br/>Of goblins, but still more of men afraid,<br/>  Had thought one man might be deterred by two,<br/>And therefore side by side were gently laid,<br/>  Until the hours of absence should run through,<br/>And truant husband should return, and say,<br/>"My dear,--I was the first who came away."<br/><br/>Now Julia found at length a voice, and cried,<br/>  "In Heaven's name, Don Alfonso, what d' ye mean?<br/>Has madness seized you? would that I had died<br/>  Ere such a monster's victim I had been!<br/>What may this midnight violence betide,<br/>  A sudden fit of drunkenness or spleen?<br/>Dare you suspect me, whom the thought would kill?<br/>Search, then, the room!"--Alfonso said, "I will."<br/><br/>_He_ searched, _they_ searched, and rummaged everywhere,<br/>  Closet and clothes' press, chest and window-seat,<br/>And found much linen, lace, and several pair<br/>  Of stockings, slippers, brushes, combs, complete,<br/>With other articles of ladies fair,<br/>  To keep them beautiful, or leave them neat:<br/>Arras they pricked and curtains with their swords,<br/>And wounded several shutters, and some boards.<br/><br/>Under the bed they searched, and there they found--<br/>  No matter what--it was not that they sought;<br/>They opened windows, gazing if the ground<br/>  Had signs or footmarks, but the earth said nought;<br/>And then they stared each others' faces round:<br/>  'T is odd, not one of all these seekers thought,<br/>And seems to me almost a sort of blunder,<br/>Of looking _in_ the bed as well as under.<br/><br/>During this inquisition Julia's tongue<br/>  Was not asleep--"Yes, search and search," she cried,<br/>"Insult on insult heap, and wrong on wrong!<br/>  It was for this that I became a bride!<br/>For this in silence I have suffered long<br/>  A husband like Alfonso at my side;<br/>But now I'll bear no more, nor here remain,<br/>If there be law or lawyers in all Spain.<br/><br/>"Yes, Don Alfonso! husband now no more,<br/>  If ever you indeed deserved the name,<br/>Is 't worthy of your years?--you have threescore--<br/>  Fifty, or sixty, it is all the same--<br/>Is 't wise or fitting, causeless to explore<br/>  For facts against a virtuous woman's fame?<br/>Ungrateful, perjured, barbarous Don Alfonso,<br/>How dare you think your lady would go on so?<br/><br/>"Is it for this I have disdained to hold<br/>  The common privileges of my sex?<br/>That I have chosen a confessor so old<br/>  And deaf, that any other it would vex,<br/>And never once he has had cause to scold,<br/>  But found my very innocence perplex<br/>So much, he always doubted I was married--<br/>How sorry you will be when I've miscarried!<br/><br/>"Was it for this that no Cortejo e'er<br/>  I yet have chosen from out the youth of Seville?<br/>Is it for this I scarce went anywhere,<br/>  Except to bull-fights, mass, play, rout, and revel?<br/>Is it for this, whate'er my suitors were,<br/>  I favoured none--nay, was almost uncivil?<br/>Is it for this that General Count O'Reilly,<br/>Who took Algiers, declares I used him vilely?<br/><br/>"Did not the Italian _Musico_ Cazzani<br/>  Sing at my heart six months at least in vain?<br/>Did not his countryman, Count Corniani,<br/>  Call me the only virtuous wife in Spain?<br/>Were there not also Russians, English, many?<br/>  The Count Strongstroganoff I put in pain,<br/>And Lord Mount Coffeehouse, the Irish peer,<br/>Who killed himself for love (with wine) last year.<br/><br/>"Have I not had two bishops at my feet?<br/>  The Duke of Ichar, and Don Fernan Nunez;<br/>And is it thus a faithful wife you treat?<br/>  I wonder in what quarter now the moon is:<br/>I praise your vast forbearance not to beat<br/>  Me also, since the time so opportune is--<br/>Oh, valiant man! with sword drawn and cocked trigger,<br/>Now, tell me, don't you cut a pretty figure?<br/><br/>"Was it for this you took your sudden journey,<br/>  Under pretence of business indispensable<br/>With that sublime of rascals your attorney,<br/>  Whom I see standing there, and looking sensible<br/>Of having played the fool? though both I spurn, he<br/>  Deserves the worst, his conduct's less defensible,<br/>Because, no doubt, 't was for his dirty fee,<br/>And not from any love to you nor me.<br/><br/>"If he comes here to take a deposition,<br/>  By all means let the gentleman proceed;<br/>You've made the apartment in a fit condition:--<br/>  There's pen and ink for you, sir, when you need--<br/>Let everything be noted with precision,<br/>  I would not you for nothing should be fee'd--<br/>But, as my maid's undressed, pray turn your spies out."<br/>"Oh!" sobbed Antonia, "I could tear their eyes out."<br/><br/>"There is the closet, there the toilet, there<br/>  The antechamber--search them under, over;<br/>There is the sofa, there the great arm-chair,<br/>  The chimney--which would really hold a lover.<br/>I wish to sleep, and beg you will take care<br/>  And make no further noise, till you discover<br/>The secret cavern of this lurking treasure--<br/>And when 't is found, let me, too, have that pleasure.<br/><br/>"And now, Hidalgo! now that you have thrown<br/>  Doubt upon me, confusion over all,<br/>Pray have the courtesy to make it known<br/>  _Who_ is the man you search for? how d' ye call<br/>Him? what's his lineage? let him but be shown--<br/>  I hope he's young and handsome--is he tall?<br/>Tell me--and be assured, that since you stain<br/>My honour thus, it shall not be in vain.<br/><br/>"At least, perhaps, he has not sixty years,<br/>  At that age he would be too old for slaughter,<br/>Or for so young a husband's jealous fears--<br/>  (Antonia! let me have a glass of water.)<br/>I am ashamed of having shed these tears,<br/>  They are unworthy of my father's daughter;<br/>My mother dreamed not in my natal hour,<br/>That I should fall into a monster's power.<br/><br/>"Perhaps 't is of Antonia you are jealous,<br/>  You saw that she was sleeping by my side,<br/>When you broke in upon us with your fellows:<br/>  Look where you please--we've nothing, sir, to hide;<br/>Only another time, I trust, you'll tell us,<br/>  Or for the sake of decency abide<br/>A moment at the door, that we may be<br/>Dressed to receive so much good company.<br/><br/>"And now, sir, I have done, and say no more;<br/>  The little I have said may serve to show<br/>The guileless heart in silence may grieve o'er<br/>  The wrongs to whose exposure it is slow:--<br/>I leave you to your conscience as before,<br/>  'T will one day ask you _why_ you used me so?<br/>God grant you feel not then the bitterest grief!--<br/>Antonia! where's my pocket-handkerchief?"<br/><br/>She ceased, and turned upon her pillow; pale<br/>  She lay, her dark eyes flashing through their tears,<br/>Like skies that rain and lighten; as a veil,<br/>  Waved and o'ershading her wan cheek, appears<br/>Her streaming hair; the black curls strive, but fail<br/>  To hide the glossy shoulder, which uprears<br/>Its snow through all;--her soft lips lie apart,<br/>And louder than her breathing beats her heart.<br/><br/>The Senhor Don Alfonso stood confused;<br/>  Antonia bustled round the ransacked room,<br/>And, turning up her nose, with looks abused<br/>  Her master, and his myrmidons, of whom<br/>Not one, except the attorney, was amused;<br/>  He, like Achates, faithful to the tomb,<br/>So there were quarrels, cared not for the cause,<br/>Knowing they must be settled by the laws.<br/><br/>With prying snub-nose, and small eyes, he stood,<br/>  Following Antonia's motions here and there,<br/>With much suspicion in his attitude;<br/>  For reputations he had little care;<br/>So that a suit or action were made good,<br/>  Small pity had he for the young and fair,<br/>And ne'er believed in negatives, till these<br/>Were proved by competent false witnesses.<br/><br/>But Don Alfonso stood with downcast looks,<br/>  And, truth to say, he made a foolish figure;<br/>When, after searching in five hundred nooks,<br/>  And treating a young wife with so much rigour,<br/>He gained no point, except some self-rebukes,<br/>  Added to those his lady with such vigour<br/>Had poured upon him for the last half-hour,<br/>Quick, thick, and heavy--as a thunder-shower.<br/><br/>At first he tried to hammer an excuse,<br/>  To which the sole reply was tears, and sobs,<br/>And indications of hysterics, whose<br/>  Prologue is always certain throes, and throbs,<br/>Gasps, and whatever else the owners choose:<br/>  Alfonso saw his wife, and thought of Job's;<br/>He saw too, in perspective, her relations,<br/>And then he tried to muster all his patience.<br/><br/>He stood in act to speak, or rather stammer,<br/>  But sage Antonia cut him short before<br/>The anvil of his speech received the hammer,<br/>  With "Pray, sir, leave the room, and say no more,<br/>Or madam dies."--Alfonso muttered, "D--n her,"<br/>  But nothing else, the time of words was o'er;<br/>He cast a rueful look or two, and did,<br/>He knew not wherefore, that which he was bid.<br/><br/>With him retired his _"posse comitatus,"_<br/>  The attorney last, who lingered near the door<br/>Reluctantly, still tarrying there as late as<br/>  Antonia let him--not a little sore<br/>At this most strange and unexplained "_hiatus_"<br/>  In Don Alfonso's facts, which just now wore<br/>An awkward look; as he revolved the case,<br/>The door was fastened in his legal face.<br/><br/>No sooner was it bolted, than--Oh Shame!<br/>  Oh Sin! Oh Sorrow! and Oh Womankind!<br/>How can you do such things and keep your fame,<br/>  Unless this world, and t' other too, be blind?<br/>Nothing so dear as an unfilched good name!<br/>  But to proceed--for there is more behind:<br/>With much heartfelt reluctance be it said,<br/>Young Juan slipped, half-smothered, from the bed.<br/><br/>He had been hid--I don't pretend to say<br/>  How, nor can I indeed describe the where--<br/>Young, slender, and packed easily, he lay,<br/>  No doubt, in little compass, round or square;<br/>But pity him I neither must nor may<br/>  His suffocation by that pretty pair;<br/>'T were better, sure, to die so, than be shut<br/>With maudlin Clarence in his Malmsey butt.<br/><br/>And, secondly, I pity not, because<br/>  He had no business to commit a sin,<br/>Forbid by heavenly, fined by human laws;--<br/>  At least 't was rather early to begin,<br/>But at sixteen the conscience rarely gnaws<br/>  So much as when we call our old debts in<br/>At sixty years, and draw the accompts of evil,<br/>And find a deuced balance with the Devil.<br/><br/>Of his position I can give no notion:<br/>  'T is written in the Hebrew Chronicle,<br/>How the physicians, leaving pill and potion,<br/>  Prescribed, by way of blister, a young belle,<br/>When old King David's blood grew dull in motion,<br/>  And that the medicine answered very well;<br/>Perhaps 't was in a different way applied,<br/>For David lived, but Juan nearly died.<br/><br/>What's to be done? Alfonso will be back<br/>  The moment he has sent his fools away.<br/>Antonia's skill was put upon the rack,<br/>  But no device could be brought into play--<br/>And how to parry the renewed attack?<br/>  Besides, it wanted but few hours of day:<br/>Antonia puzzled; Julia did not speak,<br/>But pressed her bloodless lip to Juan's cheek.<br/><br/>He turned his lip to hers, and with his hand<br/>  Called back the tangles of her wandering hair;<br/>Even then their love they could not all command,<br/>  And half forgot their danger and despair:<br/>Antonia's patience now was at a stand--<br/>  "Come, come, 't is no time now for fooling there,"<br/>She whispered, in great wrath--"I must deposit<br/>This pretty gentleman within the closet:<br/><br/>"Pray, keep your nonsense for some luckier night--<br/>  _Who_ can have put my master in this mood?<br/>What will become on 't--I'm in such a fright,<br/>  The Devil's in the urchin, and no good--<br/>Is this a time for giggling? this a plight?<br/>  Why, don't you know that it may end in blood?<br/>You'll lose your life, and I shall lose my place,<br/>My mistress all, for that half-girlish face.<br/><br/>"Had it but been for a stout cavalier<br/>  Of twenty-five or thirty--(come, make haste)<br/>But for a child, what piece of work is here!<br/>  I really, madam, wonder at your taste--<br/>(Come, sir, get in)--my master must be near:<br/>  There, for the present, at the least, he's fast,<br/>And if we can but till the morning keep<br/>Our counsel--(Juan, mind, you must not sleep.)"<br/><br/>Now, Don Alfonso entering, but alone,<br/>  Closed the oration of the trusty maid:<br/>She loitered, and he told her to be gone,<br/>  An order somewhat sullenly obeyed;<br/>However, present remedy was none,<br/>  And no great good seemed answered if she staid:<br/>Regarding both with slow and sidelong view,<br/>She snuffed the candle, curtsied, and withdrew.<br/><br/>Alfonso paused a minute--then begun<br/>  Some strange excuses for his late proceeding;<br/>He would not justify what he had done,<br/>  To say the best, it was extreme ill-breeding;<br/>But there were ample reasons for it, none<br/>  Of which he specified in this his pleading:<br/>His speech was a fine sample, on the whole,<br/>Of rhetoric, which the learned call "_rigmarole._"<br/><br/>Julia said nought; though all the while there rose<br/>  A ready answer, which at once enables<br/>A matron, who her husband's foible knows,<br/>  By a few timely words to turn the tables,<br/>Which, if it does not silence, still must pose,--<br/>  Even if it should comprise a pack of fables;<br/>'T is to retort with firmness, and when he<br/>Suspects with _one_, do you reproach with _three_.<br/><br/>Julia, in fact, had tolerable grounds,--<br/>  Alfonso's loves with Inez were well known;<br/>But whether 't was that one's own guilt confounds--<br/>  But that can't be, as has been often shown,<br/>A lady with apologies abounds;--<br/>  It might be that her silence sprang alone<br/>From delicacy to Don Juan's ear,<br/>To whom she knew his mother's fame was dear.<br/><br/>There might be one more motive, which makes two;<br/>  Alfonso ne'er to Juan had alluded,--<br/>Mentioned his jealousy, but never who<br/>  Had been the happy lover, he concluded,<br/>Concealed amongst his premises; 't is true,<br/>  His mind the more o'er this its mystery brooded;<br/>To speak of Inez now were, one may say,<br/>Like throwing Juan in Alfonso's way.<br/><br/>A hint, in tender cases, is enough;<br/>  Silence is best: besides, there is a _tact_--<br/>(That modern phrase appears to me sad stuff,<br/>  But it will serve to keep my verse compact)--<br/>Which keeps, when pushed by questions rather rough,<br/>  A lady always distant from the fact:<br/>The charming creatures lie with such a grace,<br/>There's nothing so becoming to the face.<br/><br/>They blush, and we believe them; at least I<br/>  Have always done so; 't is of no great use,<br/>In any case, attempting a reply,<br/>  For then their eloquence grows quite profuse;<br/>And when at length they're out of breath, they sigh,<br/>  And cast their languid eyes down, and let loose<br/>A tear or two, and then we make it up;<br/>And then--and then--and then--sit down and sup.<br/><br/>Alfonso closed his speech, and begged her pardon,<br/>  Which Julia half withheld, and then half granted,<br/>And laid conditions he thought very hard on,<br/>  Denying several little things he wanted:<br/>He stood like Adam lingering near his garden,<br/>  With useless penitence perplexed and haunted;<br/>Beseeching she no further would refuse,<br/>When, lo! he stumbled o'er a pair of shoes.<br/><br/>A pair of shoes!--what then? not much, if they<br/>  Are such as fit with ladies' feet, but these<br/>(No one can tell how much I grieve to say)<br/>  Were masculine; to see them, and to seize,<br/>Was but a moment's act.--Ah! well-a-day!<br/>  My teeth begin to chatter, my veins freeze!<br/>Alfonso first examined well their fashion,<br/>And then flew out into another passion.<br/><br/>He left the room for his relinquished sword,<br/>  And Julia instant to the closet flew.<br/>"Fly, Juan, fly! for Heaven's sake--not a word--<br/>  The door is open--you may yet slip through<br/>The passage you so often have explored--<br/>  Here is the garden-key--Fly--fly--Adieu!<br/>Haste--haste! I hear Alfonso's hurrying feet--<br/>Day has not broke--there's no one in the street."<br/><br/>None can say that this was not good advice,<br/>  The only mischief was, it came too late;<br/>Of all experience 't is the usual price,<br/>  A sort of income-tax laid on by fate:<br/>Juan had reached the room-door in a trice,<br/>  And might have done so by the garden-gate,<br/>But met Alfonso in his dressing-gown,<br/>Who threatened death--so Juan knocked him down.<br/><br/>Dire was the scuffle, and out went the light;<br/>  Antonia cried out "Rape!" and Julia "Fire!"<br/>But not a servant stirred to aid the fight.<br/>  Alfonso, pommelled to his heart's desire,<br/>Swore lustily he'd be revenged this night;<br/>  And Juan, too, blasphemed an octave higher;<br/>His blood was up: though young, he was a Tartar,<br/>And not at all disposed to prove a martyr.<br/><br/>Alfonso's sword had dropped ere he could draw it,<br/>  And they continued battling hand to hand,<br/>For Juan very luckily ne'er saw it;<br/>  His temper not being under great command,<br/>If at that moment he had chanced to claw it,<br/>  Alfonso's days had not been in the land<br/>Much longer.--Think of husbands', lovers' lives!<br/>And how ye may be doubly widows--wives!<br/><br/>Alfonso grappled to detain the foe,<br/>  And Juan throttled him to get away,<br/>And blood ('t was from the nose) began to flow;<br/>  At last, as they more faintly wrestling lay,<br/>Juan contrived to give an awkward blow,<br/>  And then his only garment quite gave way;<br/>He fled, like Joseph, leaving it; but there,<br/>I doubt, all likeness ends between the pair.<br/><br/>Lights came at length, and men, and maids, who found<br/>  An awkward spectacle their eyes before;<br/>Antonia in hysterics, Julia swooned,<br/>  Alfonso leaning, breathless, by the door;<br/>Some half-torn drapery scattered on the ground,<br/>  Some blood, and several footsteps, but no more:<br/>Juan the gate gained, turned the
