key about,<br/>And liking not the inside, locked the out.<br/><br/>Here ends this canto.--Need I sing, or say,<br/>  How Juan, naked, favoured by the night,<br/>Who favours what she should not, found his way,<br/>  And reached his home in an unseemly plight?<br/>The pleasant scandal which arose next day,<br/>  The nine days' wonder which was brought to light,<br/>And how Alfonso sued for a divorce,<br/>Were in the English newspapers, of course.<br/><br/>If you would like to see the whole proceedings,<br/>  The depositions, and the Cause at full,<br/>The names of all the witnesses, the pleadings<br/>  Of Counsel to nonsuit, or to annul,<br/>There's more than one edition, and the readings<br/>  Are various, but they none of them are dull:<br/>The best is that in short-hand ta'en by Gurney,<br/>Who to Madrid on purpose made a journey.<br/><br/>But Donna Inez, to divert the train<br/>  Of one of the most circulating scandals<br/>That had for centuries been known in Spain,<br/>  At least since the retirement of the Vandals,<br/>First vowed (and never had she vowed in vain)<br/>  To Virgin Mary several pounds of candles;<br/>And then, by the advice of some old ladies,<br/>She sent her son to be shipped off from Cadiz.<br/><br/>She had resolved that he should travel through<br/>  All European climes, by land or sea,<br/>To mend his former morals, and get new,<br/>  Especially in France and Italy--<br/>(At least this is the thing most people do.)<br/>  Julia was sent into a convent--she<br/>Grieved--but, perhaps, her feelings may be better<br/>Shown in the following copy of her Letter:--<br/><br/>"They tell me 't is decided you depart:<br/>  'T is wise--'t is well, but not the less a pain;<br/>I have no further claim on your young heart,<br/>  Mine is the victim, and would be again:<br/>To love too much has been the only art<br/>  I used;--I write in haste, and if a stain<br/>Be on this sheet, 't is not what it appears;<br/>My eyeballs burn and throb, but have no tears.<br/><br/>"I loved, I love you, for this love have lost<br/>  State, station, Heaven, Mankind's, my own esteem,<br/>And yet can not regret what it hath cost,<br/>  So dear is still the memory of that dream;<br/>Yet, if I name my guilt, 't is not to boast,<br/>  None can deem harshlier of me than I deem:<br/>I trace this scrawl because I cannot rest--<br/>I've nothing to reproach, or to request.<br/><br/>"Man's love is of man's life a thing apart,<br/>  'T is a Woman's whole existence; Man may range<br/>The Court, Camp, Church, the Vessel, and the Mart;<br/>  Sword, Gown, Gain, Glory, offer in exchange<br/>Pride, Fame, Ambition, to fill up his heart,<br/>  And few there are whom these can not estrange;<br/>Men have all these resources, We but one,<br/>To love again, and be again undone."<br/><br/>"You will proceed in pleasure, and in pride,<br/>  Beloved and loving many; all is o'er<br/>For me on earth, except some years to hide<br/>  My shame and sorrow deep in my heart's core:<br/>These I could bear, but cannot cast aside<br/>  The passion which still rages as before,--<br/>And so farewell--forgive me, love me--No,<br/>That word is idle now--but let it go.<br/><br/>"My breast has been all weakness, is so yet;<br/>  But still I think I can collect my mind;<br/>My blood still rushes where my spirit's set,<br/>  As roll the waves before the settled wind;<br/>My heart is feminine, nor can forget--<br/>  To all, except one image, madly blind;<br/>So shakes the needle, and so stands the pole,<br/>As vibrates my fond heart to my fixed soul.<br/><br/>"I have no more to say, but linger still,<br/>  And dare not set my seal upon this sheet,<br/>And yet I may as well the task fulfil,<br/>  My misery can scarce be more complete;<br/>I had not lived till now, could sorrow kill;<br/>  Death shuns the wretch who fain the blow would meet,<br/>And I must even survive this last adieu,<br/>And bear with life, to love and pray for you!"<br/><br/>This note was written upon gilt-edged paper<br/>  With a neat little crow-quill, slight and new;<br/>Her small white hand could hardly reach the taper,<br/>  It trembled as magnetic needles do,<br/>And yet she did not let one tear escape her;<br/>  The seal a sun-flower; _"Elle vous suit partout,"_<br/>The motto cut upon a white cornelian;<br/>The wax was superfine, its hue vermilion.<br/><br/>This was Don Juan's earliest scrape; but whether<br/>  I shall proceed with his adventures is<br/>Dependent on the public altogether;<br/>  We'll see, however, what they say to this:<br/>Their favour in an author's cap's a feather,<br/>  And no great mischief's done by their caprice;<br/>And if their approbation we experience,<br/>Perhaps they'll have some more about a year hence.<br/><br/>My poem's epic, and is meant to be<br/>  Divided in twelve books; each book containing,<br/>With Love, and War, a heavy gale at sea,<br/>  A list of ships, and captains, and kings reigning,<br/>New characters; the episodes are three:<br/>  A panoramic view of Hell's in training,<br/>After the style of Virgil and of Homer,<br/>So that my name of Epic's no misnomer.<br/><br/>All these things will be specified in time,<br/>  With strict regard to Aristotle's rules,<br/>The _Vade Mecum_ of the true sublime,<br/>  Which makes so many poets, and some fools:<br/>Prose poets like blank-verse, I'm fond of rhyme,<br/>  Good workmen never quarrel with their tools;<br/>I've got new mythological machinery,<br/>And very handsome supernatural scenery.<br/><br/>There's only one slight difference between<br/>  Me and my epic brethren gone before,<br/>And here the advantage is my own, I ween<br/>  (Not that I have not several merits more,<br/>But this will more peculiarly be seen);<br/>  They so embellish, that 't is quite a bore<br/>Their labyrinth of fables to thread through,<br/>Whereas this story's actually true.<br/><br/>If any person doubt it, I appeal<br/>  To History, Tradition, and to Facts,<br/>To newspapers, whose truth all know and feel,<br/>  To plays in five, and operas in three acts;<br/>All these confirm my statement a good deal,<br/>  But that which more completely faith exacts<br/>Is, that myself, and several now in Seville,<br/>_Saw_ Juan's last elopement with the Devil.<br/><br/>If ever I should condescend to prose,<br/>  I'll write poetical commandments, which<br/>Shall supersede beyond all doubt all those<br/>  That went before; in these I shall enrich<br/>My text with many things that no one knows,<br/>  And carry precept to the highest pitch:<br/>I'll call the work "Longinus o'er a Bottle,<br/>Or, Every Poet his _own_ Aristotle."<br/><br/>Thou shalt believe in Milton, Dryden, Pope;<br/>  Thou shalt not set up Wordsworth, Coleridge, Southey;<br/>Because the first is crazed beyond all hope,<br/>  The second drunk, the third so quaint and mouthy:<br/>With Crabbe it may be difficult to cope,<br/>  And Campbell's Hippocrene is somewhat drouthy:<br/>Thou shalt not steal from Samuel Rogers, nor<br/>Commit--flirtation with the muse of Moore.<br/><br/>Thou shalt not covet Mr. Sotheby's Muse,<br/>  His Pegasus, nor anything that's his;<br/>Thou shalt not bear false witness like "the Blues"--<br/>  (There's _one_, at least, is very fond of this);<br/>Thou shalt not write, in short, but what I choose:<br/>  This is true criticism, and you may kiss--<br/>Exactly as you please, or not,--the rod;<br/>But if you don't, I'll lay it on, by G--d!<br/><br/>If any person should presume to assert<br/>  This story is not moral, first, I pray,<br/>That they will not cry out before they're hurt,<br/>  Then that they'll read it o'er again, and say<br/>(But, doubtless, nobody will be so pert)<br/>  That this is not a moral tale, though gay:<br/>Besides, in Canto Twelfth, I mean to show<br/>The very place where wicked people go.<br/><br/>If, after all, there should be some so blind<br/>  To their own good this warning to despise,<br/>Led by some tortuosity of mind,<br/>  Not to believe my verse and their own eyes,<br/>And cry that they "the moral cannot find,"<br/>  I tell him, if a clergyman, he lies;<br/>Should captains the remark, or critics, make,<br/>They also lie too--under a mistake.<br/><br/>The public approbation I expect,<br/>  And beg they'll take my word about the moral,<br/>Which I with their amusement will connect<br/>  (So children cutting teeth receive a coral);<br/>Meantime they'll doubtless please to recollect<br/>  My epical pretensions to the laurel:<br/>For fear some prudish readers should grow skittish,<br/>I've bribed my Grandmother's Review--the British.<br/><br/>I sent it in a letter to the Editor,<br/>  Who thanked me duly by return of post--<br/>I'm for a handsome article his creditor;<br/>  Yet, if my gentle Muse he please to roast,<br/>And break a promise after having made it her,<br/>  Denying the receipt of what it cost,<br/>And smear his page with gall instead of honey,<br/>All I can say is--that he had the money.<br/><br/>I think that with this holy _new_ alliance<br/>  I may ensure the public, and defy<br/>All other magazines of art or science,<br/>  Daily, or monthly, or three monthly; I<br/>Have not essayed to multiply their clients,<br/>  Because they tell me 't were in vain to try,<br/>And that the Edinburgh Review and Quarterly<br/>Treat a dissenting author very martyrly.<br/><br/>"_Non ego hoc ferrem calidus juvent√¢<br/>  Consule Planco_" Horace said, and so<br/>Say I; by which quotation there is meant a<br/>  Hint that some six or seven good years ago<br/>(Long ere I dreamt of dating from the Brenta)<br/>  I was most ready to return a blow,<br/>And would not brook at all this sort of thing<br/>In my hot youth--when George the Third was King.<br/><br/>But now at thirty years my hair is grey--<br/>  (I wonder what it will be like at forty?<br/>I thought of a peruke the other day--)<br/>  My heart is not much greener; and, in short, I<br/>Have squandered my whole summer while 't was May,<br/>  And feel no more the spirit to retort; I<br/>Have spent my life, both interest and principal,<br/>And deem not, what I deemed--my soul invincible.<br/><br/>No more--no more--Oh! never more on me<br/>  The freshness of the heart can fall like dew,<br/>Which out of all the lovely things we see<br/>  Extracts emotions beautiful and new,<br/>Hived in our bosoms like the bag o' the bee.<br/>  Think'st thou the honey with those objects grew?<br/>Alas! 't was not in them, but in thy power<br/>To double even the sweetness of a flower.<br/><br/>No more--no more--Oh! never more, my heart,<br/>  Canst thou be my sole world, my universe!<br/>Once all in all, but now a thing apart,<br/>  Thou canst not be my blessing or my curse:<br/>The illusion's gone for ever, and thou art<br/>  Insensible, I trust, but none the worse,<br/>And in thy stead I've got a deal of judgment,<br/>Though Heaven knows how it ever found a lodgment.<br/><br/>My days of love are over; me no more<br/>  The charms of maid, wife, and still less of widow,<br/>Can make the fool of which they made before,--<br/>  In short, I must not lead the life I did do;<br/>The credulous hope of mutual minds is o'er,<br/>  The copious use of claret is forbid too,<br/>So for a good old-gentlemanly vice,<br/>I think I must take up with avarice.<br/><br/>Ambition was my idol, which was broken<br/>  Before the shrines of Sorrow, and of Pleasure;<br/>And the two last have left me many a token<br/>  O'er which reflection may be made at leisure:<br/>Now, like Friar Bacon's Brazen Head, I've spoken,<br/>  "Time is, Time was, Time's past:"--a chymic treasure<br/>Is glittering Youth, which I have spent betimes--<br/>My heart in passion, and my head on rhymes.<br/><br/>What is the end of Fame? 't is but to fill<br/>  A certain portion of uncertain paper:<br/>Some liken it to climbing up a hill,<br/>  Whose summit, like all hills, is lost in vapour;<br/>For this men write, speak, preach, and heroes kill,<br/>  And bards burn what they call their "midnight taper,"<br/>To have, when the original is dust,<br/>A name, a wretched picture and worse bust.<br/><br/>What are the hopes of man? Old Egypt's King<br/>  Cheops erected the first Pyramid<br/>And largest, thinking it was just the thing<br/>  To keep his memory whole, and mummy hid;<br/>But somebody or other rummaging,<br/>  Burglariously broke his coffin's lid:<br/>Let not a monument give you or me hopes,<br/>Since not a pinch of dust remains of Cheops.<br/><br/>But I, being fond of true philosophy,<br/>  Say very often to myself, "Alas!<br/>All things that have been born were born to die,<br/>  And flesh (which Death mows down to hay) is grass;<br/>You've passed your youth not so unpleasantly,<br/>  And if you had it o'er again--'t would pass--<br/>So thank your stars that matters are no worse,<br/>And read your Bible, sir, and mind your purse."<br/><br/>But for the present, gentle reader! and<br/>  Still gentler purchaser! the Bard--that's I--<br/>Must, with permission, shake you by the hand,<br/>  And so--"your humble servant, and Good-bye!"<br/>We meet again, if we should understand<br/>  Each other; and if not, I shall not try<br/>Your patience further than by this short sample--<br/>'T were well if others followed my example.<br/><br/>"Go, little Book, from this my solitude!<br/>  I cast thee on the waters--go thy ways!<br/>And if, as I believe, thy vein be good,<br/>  The World will find thee after many days."<br/>When Southey's read, and Wordsworth understood,<br/>  I can't help putting in my claim to praise--<br/>The four first rhymes are Southey's every line:<br/>For God's sake, reader! take them not for mine.<br/><br/>CANTO THE SECOND.<br/><br/>OH ye! who teach the ingenuous youth of nations,<br/>  Holland, France, England, Germany, or Spain,<br/>I pray ye flog them upon all occasions--<br/>  It mends their morals, never mind the pain:<br/>The best of mothers and of educations<br/>  In Juan's case were but employed in vain,<br/>Since, in a way that's rather of the oddest, he<br/>Became divested of his native modesty.<br/><br/>Had he but been placed at a public school,<br/>  In the third form, or even in the fourth,<br/>His daily task had kept his fancy cool,<br/>  At least, had he been nurtured in the North;<br/>Spain may prove an exception to the rule,<br/>  But then exceptions always prove its worth--<br/>A lad of sixteen causing a divorce<br/>Puzzled his tutors very much, of course.<br/><br/>I can't say that it puzzles me at all,<br/>  If all things be considered: first, there was<br/>His lady-mother, mathematical,<br/>  A----never mind;--his tutor, an old ass;<br/>A pretty woman--(that's quite natural,<br/>  Or else the thing had hardly come to pass)<br/>A husband rather old, not much in unity<br/>With his young wife--a time, and opportunity.<br/><br/>Well--well; the World must turn upon its axis,<br/>  And all Mankind turn with it, heads or tails,<br/>And live and die, make love and pay our taxes,<br/>  And as the veering wind shifts, shift our sails;<br/>The King commands us, and the Doctor quacks us,<br/>  The Priest instructs, and so our life exhales,<br/>A little breath, love, wine, ambition, fame,<br/>Fighting, devotion, dust,--perhaps a name.<br/><br/>I said that Juan had been sent to Cadiz--<br/>  A pretty town, I recollect it well--<br/>'T is there the mart of the colonial trade is,<br/>  (Or was, before Peru learned to rebel),<br/>And such sweet girls!--I mean, such graceful ladies,<br/>  Their very walk would make your bosom swell;<br/>I can't describe it, though so much it strike,<br/>Nor liken it--I never saw the like:<br/><br/>An Arab horse, a stately stag, a barb<br/>  New broke, a camelopard, a gazelle,<br/>No--none of these will do;--and then their garb,<br/>  Their veil and petticoat--Alas! to dwell<br/>Upon such things would very near absorb<br/>  A canto--then their feet and ankles,--well,<br/>Thank Heaven I've got no metaphor quite ready,<br/>(And so, my sober Muse--come, let's be steady--<br/><br/>Chaste Muse!--well,--if you must, you must)--the veil<br/>  Thrown back a moment with the glancing hand,<br/>While the o'erpowering eye, that turns you pale,<br/>  Flashes into the heart:--All sunny land<br/>Of Love! when I forget you, may I fail<br/>  To----say my prayers--but never was there planned<br/>A dress through which the eyes give such a volley,<br/>Excepting the Venetian Fazzioli.<br/><br/>But to our tale: the Donna Inez sent<br/>  Her son to Cadiz only to embark;<br/>To stay there had not answered her intent,<br/>  But why?--we leave the reader in the dark--<br/>'T was for a voyage the young man was meant,<br/>  As if a Spanish ship were Noah's ark,<br/>To wean him from the wickedness of earth,<br/>And send him like a Dove of Promise forth.<br/><br/>Don Juan bade his valet pack his things<br/>  According to direction, then received<br/>A lecture and some money: for four springs<br/>  He was to travel; and though Inez grieved<br/>(As every kind of parting has its stings),<br/>  She hoped he would improve--perhaps believed:<br/>A letter, too, she gave (he never read it)<br/>Of good advice--and two or three of credit.<br/><br/>In the mean time, to pass her hours away,<br/>  Brave Inez now set up a Sunday school<br/>For naughty children, who would rather play<br/>  (Like truant rogues) the devil, or the fool;<br/>Infants of three years old were taught that day,<br/>  Dunces were whipped, or set upon a stool:<br/>The great success of Juan's education<br/>Spurred her to teach another generation.<br/><br/>Juan embarked--the ship got under way,<br/>  The wind was fair, the water passing rough;<br/>A devil of a sea rolls in that bay,<br/>  As I, who've crossed it oft, know well enough;<br/>And, standing on the deck, the dashing spray<br/>  Flies in one's face, and makes it weather-tough:<br/>And there he stood to take, and take again,<br/>His first--perhaps his last--farewell of Spain.<br/><br/>I can't but say it is an awkward sight<br/>  To see one's native land receding through<br/>The growing waters; it unmans one quite,<br/>  Especially when life is rather new:<br/>I recollect Great Britain's coast looks white,<br/>  But almost every other country's blue,<br/>When gazing on them, mystified by distance,<br/>We enter on our nautical existence.<br/><br/>So Juan stood, bewildered on the deck:<br/>  The wind sung, cordage strained, and sailors swore,<br/>And the ship creaked, the town became a speck,<br/>  From which away so fair and fast they bore.<br/>The best of remedies is a beef-steak<br/>  Against sea-sickness: try it, Sir, before<br/>You sneer, and I assure you this is true,<br/>For I have found it answer--so may you.<br/><br/>Don Juan stood, and, gazing from the stern,<br/>  Beheld his native Spain receding far:<br/>First partings form a lesson hard to learn,<br/>  Even nations feel this when they go to war;<br/>There is a sort of unexpressed concern,<br/>  A kind of shock that sets one's heart ajar,<br/>At leaving even the most unpleasant people<br/>And places--one keeps looking at the steeple.<br/><br/>But Juan had got many things to leave,<br/>  His mother, and a mistress, and no wife,<br/>So that he had much better cause to grieve<br/>  Than many persons more advanced in life:<br/>And if we now and then a sigh must heave<br/>  At quitting even those we quit in strife,<br/>No doubt we weep for those the heart endears--<br/>That is, till deeper griefs congeal our tears.<br/><br/>So Juan wept, as wept the captive Jews<br/>  By Babel's waters, still remembering Sion:<br/>I'd weep,--but mine is not a weeping Muse,<br/>  And such light griefs are not a thing to die on;<br/>Young men should travel, if but to amuse<br/>  Themselves; and the next time their servants tie on<br/>Behind their carriages their new portmanteau,<br/>Perhaps it may be lined with this my canto.<br/><br/>And Juan wept, and much he sighed and thought,<br/>  While his salt tears dropped into the salt sea,<br/>"Sweets to the sweet;" (I like so much to quote;<br/>  You must excuse this extract,--'t is where she,<br/>The Queen of Denmark, for Ophelia brought<br/>  Flowers to the grave;) and, sobbing often, he<br/>Reflected on his present situation,<br/>And seriously resolved on reformation.<br/><br/>"Farewell, my Spain! a long farewell!" he cried,<br/>  "Perhaps I may revisit thee no more,<br/>But die, as many an exiled heart hath died,<br/>  Of its own thirst to see again thy shore:<br/>Farewell, where Guadalquivir's waters glide!<br/>  Farewell, my mother! and, since all is o'er,<br/>Farewell, too, dearest Julia!--(here he drew<br/>Her letter out again, and read it through.)<br/><br/>"And oh! if e'er I should forget, I swear--<br/>  But that's impossible, and cannot be--<br/>Sooner shall this blue Ocean melt to air,<br/>  Sooner shall Earth resolve itself to sea,<br/>Than I resign thine image, oh, my fair!<br/>  Or think of anything, excepting thee;<br/>A mind diseased no remedy can physic--<br/>(Here the ship gave a lurch, and he grew sea-sick.)<br/><br/>"Sooner shall Heaven kiss earth--(here he fell sicker)<br/>  Oh, Julia! what is every other woe?--<br/>(For God's sake let me have a glass of liquor;<br/>  Pedro, Battista, help me down below.)<br/>Julia, my love!--(you rascal, Pedro, quicker)--<br/>  Oh, Julia!--(this curst vessel pitches so)--<br/>Belov√©d Julia, hear me still beseeching!"<br/>(Here he grew inarticulate with retching.)<br/><br/>He felt that chilling heaviness of heart,<br/>  Or rather stomach, which, alas! attends,<br/>Beyond the best apothecary's art,<br/>  The loss of Love, the treachery of friends,<br/>Or death of those we dote on, when a part<br/>  Of us dies with them as each fond hope ends:<br/>No doubt he would have been much more pathetic,<br/>But the sea acted as a strong emetic.<br/><br/>Love's a capricious power: I've known it hold<br/>  Out through a fever caused by its own heat,<br/>But be much puzzled by a cough and cold,<br/>  And find a quinsy very hard to treat;<br/>Against all noble maladies he's bold,<br/>  But vulgar illnesses don't like to meet,<br/>Nor that a sneeze should interrupt his sigh,<br/>Nor inflammations redden his blind eye.<br/><br/>But worst of all is nausea, or a pain<br/>  About the lower region of the bowels;<br/>Love, who heroically breathes a vein,<br/>  Shrinks from the application of hot towels,<br/>And purgatives are dangerous to his reign,<br/>  Sea-sickness death: his love was perfect, how else<br/>Could Juan's passion, while the billows roar,<br/>Resist his stomach, ne'er at sea before?<br/><br/>The ship, called the most holy "Trinidada,"<br/>  Was steering duly for the port Leghorn;<br/>For there the Spanish family Moncada<br/>  Were settled long ere Juan's sire was born:<br/>They were relations, and for them he had a<br/>  Letter of introduction, which the morn<br/>Of his departure had been sent him by<br/>His Spanish friends for those in Italy.<br/><br/>His suite consisted of three servants and<br/>  A tutor, the licentiate Pedrillo,<br/>Who several languages did understand,<br/>  But now lay sick and speechless on his pillow<br/>And, rocking in his hammock, longed for land,<br/>  His headache being increased by every billow;<br/>And the waves oozing through the port-hole made<br/>His berth a little damp, and him afraid.<br/><br/>'T was not without some reason, for the wind<br/>  Increased at night, until it blew a gale;<br/>And though 't was not much to a naval mind,<br/>  Some landsmen would have looked a little pale,<br/>For sailors are, in fact, a different kind:<br/>  At sunset they began to take in sail,<br/>For the sky showed it would come on to blow,<br/>And carry away, perhaps, a mast or so.<br/><br/>At one o'clock the wind with sudden shift<br/>  Threw the ship right into the trough of the sea,<br/>Which struck her aft, and made an awkward rift,<br/>  Started the stern-post, also shattered the<br/>Whole of her stern-frame, and, ere she could lift<br/>  Herself from out her present jeopardy,<br/>The rudder tore away: 't was time to sound<br/>The pumps, and there were four feet water found.<br/><br/>One gang of people instantly was put<br/>  Upon the pumps, and the remainder set<br/>To get up part of the cargo, and what not;<br/>  But they could not come at the leak as yet;<br/>At last they did get at it really, but<br/>  Still their salvation was an even bet:<br/>The water rushed through in a way quite puzzling,<br/>While they thrust sheets, shirts, jackets, bales of muslin,<br/><br/>Into the opening; but all such ingredients<br/>  Would have been vain, and they must have gone down,<br/>Despite of all their efforts and expedients,<br/>  But for the pumps: I'm glad to make them known<br/>To all the brother tars who may have need hence,<br/>  For fifty tons of water were upthrown<br/>By them per hour, and they had all been undone,<br/>But for the maker, Mr. Mann, of London.<br/><br/>As day advanced the weather seemed to abate,<br/>  And then the leak they reckoned to reduce,<br/>And keep the ship afloat, though three feet yet<br/>  Kept two hand--and one chain-pump still in use.<br/>The wind blew fresh again: as it grew late<br/>  A squall came on, and while some guns broke loose,<br/>A gust--which all descriptive power transcends--<br/>Laid with one blast the ship on her beam ends.<br/><br/>There she lay, motionless, and seemed upset;<br/>  The water left the hold, and washed the decks,<br/>And made a scene men do not soon forget;<br/>  For they remember battles, fires, and wrecks,<br/>Or any other thing that brings regret<br/>  Or breaks their hopes, or hearts, or heads, or necks:<br/>Thus drownings are much talked of by the divers,<br/>And swimmers, who may chance to be survivors.<br/><br/>Immediately the masts were cut away,<br/>  Both main and mizen; first the mizen went,<br/>The main-mast followed: but the ship still lay<br/>  Like a mere log, and baffled our intent.<br/>Foremast and bowsprit were cut down, and they<br/>  Eased her at last (although we never meant<br/>To part with all till every hope was blighted),<br/>And then with violence the old ship righted.<br/><br/>It may be easily supposed, while this<br/>  Was going on, some people were unquiet,<br/>That passengers would find it much amiss<br/>  To lose their lives, as well as spoil their diet;<br/>That even the able seaman, deeming his<br/>  Days nearly o'er, might be disposed to riot,<br/>As upon such occasions tars will ask<br/>For grog, and sometimes drink rum from the cask.<br/><br/>There's nought, no doubt, so much the spirit calms<br/>  As rum and true religion: thus it was,<br/>Some plundered, some drank spirits, some sung psalms,<br/>  The high wind made the treble, and as bass<br/>The hoarse harsh waves kept time; fright cured the qualms<br/>  Of all the luckless landsmen's sea-sick maws:<br/>Strange sounds of wailing, blasphemy, devotion,<br/>Clamoured in chorus to the roaring Ocean.<br/><br/>Perhaps more mischief had been done, but for<br/>  Our Juan, who, with sense beyond his years,<br/>Got to the spirit-room, and stood before<br/>  It with a pair of pistols; and their fears,<br/>As if Death were more dreadful by his door<br/>  Of fire than water, spite of oaths and tears,<br/>Kept still aloof the crew, who, ere they sunk,<br/>Thought it would be becoming to die drunk.<br/><br/>"Give us more grog," they cried, "for it will be<br/>  All one an hour hence." Juan answered, "No!<br/>'T is true that Death awaits both you and me,<br/>  But let us die like men, not sink below<br/>Like brutes:"--and thus his dangerous post kept he,<br/>  And none liked to anticipate the blow;<br/>And even Pedrillo, his most reverend tutor,<br/>Was for some rum a disappointed suitor.<br/><br/>The good old gentleman was quite aghast,<br/>  And made a loud and pious lamentation;<br/>Repented all his sins, and made a last<br/>  Irrevocable vow of reformation;<br/>Nothing should tempt him more (this peril past)<br/>  To quit his academic occupation,<br/>In cloisters of the classic Salamanca,<br/>To follow Juan's wake, like Sancho Panca.<br/><br/>But now there came a flash of hope once more;<br/>  Day broke, and the wind lulled: the masts were gone<br/>The leak increased; shoals round her, but no shore,<br/>  The vessel swam, yet still she held her own.<br/>They tried the pumps again, and though before<br/>  Their desperate efforts seemed all useless grown,<br/>A glimpse of sunshine set some hands to bale--<br/>The stronger pumped, the weaker thrummed a sail.<br/><br/>Under the vessel's keel the sail was passed,<br/>  And for the moment it had some effect;<br/>But with a leak, and not a stick of mast,<br/>  Nor rag of canvas, what could they expect?<br/>But still 't is best to struggle to the last,<br/>  'T is never too late to be wholly wrecked:<br/>And though 't is true that man can only die once,<br/>'T is not so pleasant in the Gulf of Lyons.<br/><br/>There winds and waves had hurled them, and from thence,<br/>  Without their will, they carried them away;<br/>For they were forced with steering to dispense,<br/>  And never had as yet a quiet day<br/>On which they might repose, or even commence<br/>  A jurymast or rudder, or could say<br/>The ship would swim an hour, which, by good luck,<br/>Still swam--though not exactly like a duck.<br/><br/>The wind, in fact, perhaps, was rather less,<br/>  But the ship laboured so, they scarce could hope<br/>To weather out much longer; the distress<br/>  Was also great with which they had to cope<br/>For want of water, and their solid mess<br/>  Was scant enough: in vain the telescope<br/>Was used--nor sail nor shore appeared in sight,<br/>Nought but the heavy sea, and coming night.<br/><br/>Again the weather threatened,--again blew<br/>  A gale, and in the fore and after hold<br/>Water appeared; yet, though the people knew<br/>  All this, the most were patient, and some bold,<br/>Until the chains and leathers were worn through<br/>  Of all our pumps:--a wreck complete she rolled,<br/>At mercy of the waves, whose mercies are<br/>Like human beings during civil war.<br/><br/>Then came the carpenter, at last, with tears<br/>  In his rough eyes, and told the captain, he<br/>Could do no more: he was a man in years,<br/>  And long had voyaged through many a stormy sea,<br/>And if he wept at length they were not fears<br/>  That made his eyelids as a woman's be,<br/>But he, poor fellow, had a wife and children,--<br/>Two things for dying people quite bewildering.<br/><br/>The ship was evidently settling now<br/>  Fast by the head; and, all distinction gone,<br/>Some went to prayers again, and made a vow<br/>  Of candles to their saints--but there were none<br/>To pay them with; and some looked o'er the bow;<br/>  Some hoisted out the boats; and there was one<br/>That begged Pedrillo for an absolution,<br/>Who told him to be damned--in his confusion.<br/><br/>Some lashed them in their hammocks; some put on<br/>  Their best clothes, as if going to a fair;<br/>Some cursed the day on which they saw the Sun,<br/>  And gnashed their teeth, and, howling, tore their hair;<br/>And others went on as they had begun,<br/>  Getting the boats out, being well aware<br/>That a tight boat will live in a rough sea,<br/>Unless with breakers close beneath her lee.<br/><br/>The worst of all was, that in their condition,<br/>  Having been several days in great distress,<br/>'T was difficult to get out such provision<br/>  As now might render their long suffering less:<br/>Men, even when dying, dislike inanition;<br/>  Their stock was damaged by the weather's stress:<br/>Two casks of biscuit, and a keg of butter,<br/>Were all that could be thrown into the cutter.<br/><br/>But in the long-boat they contrived to stow<br/>  Some pounds of bread, though injured by the wet;<br/>Water, a twenty-gallon cask or so;<br/>  Six flasks of wine; and they contrived to get<br/>A portion of their beef up from below,<br/>  And with a piece of pork, moreover, met,<br/>But scarce enough to serve them for a luncheon--<br/>Then there was rum, eight gallons in a puncheon.<br/><br/>The other boats, the yawl and pinnace, had<br/>  Been stove in the beginning of the gale;<br/>And the long-boat's condition was but bad,<br/>  As there were but two blankets for a sail,<br/>And one oar for a mast, which a young lad<br/>  Threw in by good luck over the ship's rail;<br/>And two boats could not hold, far less be stored,<br/>To save one half the people then on board.<br/><br/>'T was twilight, and the sunless day went down<br/>  Over the waste of waters; like a veil,<br/>Which, if withdrawn, would but disclose the frown<br/>  Of one whose hate is masked but to assail.<br/>Thus to their hopeless eyes the night was shown,<br/>  And grimly darkled o'er the faces pale,<br/>And the dim desolate deep: twelve days had Fear<br/>Been their familiar, and now Death was here.<br/><br/>Some trial had been making at a raft,<br/>  With little hope in such a rolling sea,<br/>A sort of thing at which one would have laughed,<br/>  If any laughter at such times could be,<br/>Unless with people who too much have quaffed,<br/>  And have a kind of wild and horrid glee,<br/>Half epileptical, and half hysterical:--<br/>Their preservation would have been a miracle.<br/><br/>At half-past eight o'clock, booms, hencoops, spars,<br/>  And all things, for a chance, had been cast loose,<br/>That still could keep afloat the struggling tars,<br/>  For yet they strove, although of no great use:<br/>There was no light in heaven but a few stars,<br/>  The boats put off o'ercrowded with their crews;<br/>She gave a heel, and then a lurch to port,<br/>And, going down head foremost--sunk, in short.<br/><br/>Then rose from sea to sky the wild farewell--<br/>  Then shrieked the timid, and stood still the brave,--<br/>Then some leaped overboard with dreadful yell,<br/>  As eager to anticipate their grave;<br/>And the sea yawned around her like a hell,<br/>  And down she sucked with her the whirling wave,<br/>Like one who grapples with his enemy,<br/>And strives to strangle him before he die.<br/><br/>And first one universal shriek there rushed,<br/>  Louder than the loud Ocean, like a crash<br/>Of echoing thunder; and then all was hushed,<br/>  Save the wild wind and the remorseless dash<br/>Of billows; but at intervals there gushed,<br/>  Accompanied by a convulsive splash,<br/>A solitary shriek, the bubbling cry<br/>Of some strong swimmer in his agony.<br/><br/>The boats, as stated, had got off before,<br/>  And in them crowded several of the crew;<br/>And yet their present hope was hardly more<br/>  Than what it had been, for so strong it blew<br/>There was slight chance of reaching any shore;<br/>  And then they were too many, though so few--<br/>Nine in the cutter, thirty in the boat,<br/>Were counted in them when they got afloat.<br/><br/>All the rest perished; near two hundred souls<br/>  Had left their bodies; and what's worse, alas!<br/>When over Catholics the Ocean rolls,<br/>  They must wait several weeks before a mass<br/>Takes off one peck of purgatorial coals,<br/>  Because, till people know what's come to pass,<br/>They won't lay out their money on the dead--<br/>It costs three francs for every mass that's said.<br/><br/>Juan got into the long-boat, and there<br/>  Contrived to help Pedrillo to a place;<br/>It seemed as if they had exchanged their care,<br/>  For Juan wore the magisterial face<br/>Which courage gives, while poor Pedrillo's pair<br/>  Of eyes were crying for their owner's case:<br/>Battista, though, (a name called shortly Tita),<br/>Was lost by getting at some aqua-vita.<br/><br/>Pedro, his valet, too, he tried to save,<br/>  But the same cause, conducive to his loss,<br/>Left him so drunk, he jumped into the wave,<br/>  As o'er the cutter's edge he tried to cross,<br/>And so he found a wine-and-watery grave;<br/>  They could not rescue him although so close,<br/>Because the sea ran higher every minute,<br/>And for the boat--the crew kept crowding in it.<br/><br/>A small old spaniel,--which had been Don Jos√©'s,<br/>  His father's, whom he loved, as ye may think,<br/>For on such things the memory reposes<br/>  With tenderness--stood howling on the brink,<br/>Knowing, (dogs have such intellectual noses!)<br/>  No doubt, the vessel was about to sink;<br/>And Juan caught him up, and ere he stepped<br/>Off threw him in, then after him he leaped.<br/><br/>He also stuffed his money where he could<br/>  About his person, and Pedrillo's too,<br/>Who let him do, in fact, whate'er he would,<br/>  Not knowing what himself to say, or do,<br/>As every rising wave his dread renewed;<br/>  But Juan, trusting they might still get through,<br/>And deeming there were remedies for any ill,<br/>Thus re-embarked his tutor and his spaniel.<br/><br/>'T was a rough night, and blew so stiffly yet,<br/>  That the sail was becalmed between the seas,<br/>Though on the wave's high top too much to set,<br/>  They dared not take it in for all the breeze:<br/>Each sea curled o'er the stern, and kept them wet,<br/>  And made them bale without a moment's ease,<br/>So that themselves as well as hopes were damped,<br/>And the poor little cutter quickly swamped.<br/><br/>Nine souls more went in her: the long-boat still<br/>  Kept above water, with an oar for mast,<br/>Two blankets stitched together, answering ill<br/>  Instead of sail, were to the oar made fast;<br/>Though every wave rolled menacing to fill,<br/>  And present peril all before surpassed,<br/>They grieved for those who perished with the cutter,<br/>And also for the biscuit-casks and butter.<br/><br/>The sun rose red and fiery, a sure sign<br/>  Of the continuance of the gale: to run<br/>Before the sea until it should grow fine,<br/>  Was all that for the present could be done:<br/>A few tea-spoonfuls of their rum and wine<br/>  Were served out to the people, who begun<br/>To faint, and damaged bread wet through the bags,<br/>And most of them had little clothes but rags.<br/><br/>They counted thirty, crowded in a space<br/>  Which left scarce room for motion or exertion;<br/>They did their best to modify their case,<br/>  One half sate up, though numbed with the immersion,<br/>While t' other half were laid down in their place,<br/>  At watch and watch; thus, shivering like the tertian<br/>Ague in its cold fit, they filled their boat,<br/>With nothing but the sky for a great coat.<br/><br/>'T is very certain the desire of life<br/>  Prolongs it: this is obvious to physicians,<br/>When patients, neither plagued with friends nor wife,<br/>  Survive through very desperate conditions,<br/>Because they still can hope, nor shines the knife<br/>  Nor shears of Atropos before their visions:<br/>Despair of all recovery spoils longevity,<br/>And makes men's misery of alarming brevity.<br/><br/>'T is said that persons living on annuities<br/>  Are longer lived than others,--God knows why,<br/>Unless to plague the grantors,--yet so true it is,<br/>  That some, I really think, _do_ never die:<br/>Of any creditors the worst a Jew it is,<br/>  And _that's_ their mode of furnishing supply:<br/>In my young days they lent me cash that way,<br/>Which I found very troublesome to pay.<br/><br/>'T is thus with people in an open boat,<br/>  They live upon the love of Life, and bear<br/>More than can be believed, or even thought,<br/>  And stand like rocks the tempest's wear and tear;<br/>And hardship still has been the sailor's lot,<br/>  Since Noah's ark went cruising here and there;<br/>She had a curious crew as well as cargo,<br/>Like the first old Greek privateer, the Argo.<br/><br/>But man is a carnivorous production,<br/>  And must have meals, at least one meal a day;<br/>He cannot live, like woodcocks, upon suction,<br/>  But, like the shark and tiger, must have prey;<br/>Although his anatomical construction<br/>  Bears vegetables, in a grumbling way,<br/>Your labouring people think, beyond all question,<br/>Beef, veal, and mutton, better for digestion.<br/><br/>And thus it was with this our hapless crew;<br/>  For on the third day there came on a calm,<br/>And though at first their strength it might renew,<br/>  And lying on their weariness like balm,<br/>Lulled them like turtles sleeping on the blue<br/>  Of Ocean, when they woke they felt a qualm,<br/>And fell all ravenously on their provision,<br/>Instead of hoarding it with due precision.<br/><br/>The consequence was easily foreseen--<br/>  They ate up all they had, and drank their wine,<br/>In spite of all remonstrances, and then<br/>  On what, in fact, next day were they to dine?<br/>They hoped the wind would rise, these foolish men!<br/>  And carry them to shore; these hopes were fine,<br/>But as they had but one oar, and that brittle,<br/>It would have been more wise to save their victual.<br/><br/>The fourth day came, but not a breath of air,<br/>  And Ocean slumbered like an unweaned child:<br/>The fifth day, and their boat lay floating there,<br/>  The sea and sky were blue, and clear, and mild--<br/>With their one oar (I wish they had had a pair)<br/>  What could they do? and Hunger's rage grew wild:<br/>So Juan's spaniel, spite of his entreating,<br/>Was killed, and portioned out for present eating.<br/><br/>On the sixth day they fed upon his hide,<br/>  And Juan, who had still refused, because<br/>The creature was his father's dog that died,<br/>  Now feeling all the vulture in his jaws,<br/>With some remorse received (though first denied)<br/>  As a great favour one of the fore-paws,<br/>Which he divided with Pedrillo, who<br/>Devoured it, longing for the other too.<br/><br/>The seventh day, and no wind--the burning sun<br/>  Blistered and scorched, and, stagnant on the sea,<br/>They lay like carcasses; and hope was none,<br/>  Save in the breeze that came not: savagely<br/>They glared upon each other--all was done,<br/>  Water, and wine, and food,--and you might see<br/>The longings of the cannibal arise<br/>(Although they spoke not) in their wolfish eyes.<br/><br/>At length one whispered his companion, who<br/>  Whispered another, and thus it went round,<br/>And then into a hoarser murmur grew,<br/>  An ominous, and wild, and desperate sound;<br/>And when his comrade's thought each sufferer knew,<br/>  'T was but his own, suppressed till now, he found:<br/>And out they spoke of lots for flesh and blood,<br/>And who should die to be his fellow's food.<br/><br/>But ere they came to this, they that day shared<br/>  Some leathern caps, and what remained of shoes;<br/>And then they looked around them, and despaired,<br/>  And none to be the sacrifice would choose;<br/>At length the lots were torn up, and prepared,<br/>  But of materials that must shock the Muse--<br/>Having no paper, for the want of better,<br/>They took by force from Juan Julia's letter.<br/><br/>The lots were made, and marked, and mixed, and handed,<br/>  In silent horror, and their distribution<br/>Lulled even the savage hunger which demanded,<br/>  Like the Promethean vulture, this pollution;<br/>None in particular had sought or planned it,<br/>  'T was Nature gnawed them to this resolution,<br/>By which none were permitted to be neuter--<br/>And the lot fell on Juan's luckless tutor.<br/><br/>He but requested to be bled to death:<br/>  The surgeon had his instruments, and bled<br/>Pedrillo, and so gently ebbed his breath,<br/>  You hardly could perceive when he was dead.<br/>He died as born, a Catholic in faith,<br/>  Like most in the belief in which they're bred,<br/>And first a little crucifix he kissed,<br/>And then held out his jugular and wrist.<br/><br/>The surgeon, as there was no other fee,<br/>  Had his first choice of morsels for his pains;<br/>But being thirstiest at the moment, he<br/>  Preferred a draught from the fast-flowing veins:<br/>Part was divided, part thrown in the sea,<br/>  And such things as the entrails and the brains<br/>Regaled two sharks, who followed o'er the billow--<br/>The sailors ate the rest of poor Pedrillo.<br/><br/>The sailors ate him, all save three or four,<br/>  Who were not quite so fond of animal food;<br/>To these was added Juan, who, before<br/>  Refusing his own spaniel, hardly could<br/>Feel now his appetite increased much more;<br/>  'T was not to be expected that he should,<br/>Even in extremity of their disaster,<br/>Dine with them on his pastor and his master.<br/><br/>'T was better that he did not; for, in fact,<br/>  The consequence was awful in the extreme;<br/>For they, who were most ravenous in the act,<br/>  Went raging mad--Lord! how they did blaspheme!<br/>And foam, and roll, with strange convulsions racked,<br/>  Drinking salt-water like a mountain-stream,<br/>Tearing, and grinning, howling, screeching, swearing,<br/>And, with hy√¶na-laughter, died despairing.<br/><br/>Their numbers were much thinned by this infliction,<br/>  And all the rest were thin enough, Heaven knows;<br/>And some of them had lost their recollection,<br/>  Happier than they who still perceived their woes;<br/>But others pondered on a new dissection,<br/>  As if not warned sufficiently by those<br/>Who had already perished, suffering madly,<br/>For having used their appetites so sadly.<br/><br/>And next they thought upon the master's mate,<br/>  As fattest; but he saved himself, because,<br/>Besides being much averse from such a fate,<br/>  There were some other reasons: the first was,<br/>He had been rather indisposed of late;<br/>  And--that which chiefly proved his saving clause--<br/>Was a small present made to him at Cadiz,<br/>By general subscription of the ladies.<br/><br/>Of poor Pedrillo something still remained,<br/>  But was used sparingly,--some were afraid,<br/>And others still their appetites constrained,<br/>  Or but at times a little supper made;<br/>All except Juan, who throughout abstained,<br/>  Chewing a piece of bamboo, and some lead:<br/>At length they caught two Boobies, and a Noddy,<br/>And then they left off eating the dead body.<br/><br/>And if Pedrillo's fate should shocking be,<br/>  Remember Ugolino condescends<br/>To eat the head of his arch-enemy<br/>  The moment after he politely ends<br/>His tale: if foes be food in Hell, at sea<br/>  'T is surely fair to dine upon our friends,<br/>When Shipwreck's short allowance grows too scanty,<br/>Without being much more horrible than Dante.<br/><br/>And the same night there fell a shower of rain,<br/>  For which their mouths gaped, like the cracks of earth<br/>When dried to summer dust; till taught by pain,<br/>  Men really know not what good water's worth;<br/>If you had been in Turkey or in Spain,<br/>  Or with a famished boat's-crew had your berth,<br/>Or in the desert heard the camel's bell,<br/>You'd wish yourself where Truth is--in a well.<br/><br/>It poured down torrents, but they were no richer<br/>  Until they found a ragged piece of sheet,<br/>Which served them as a sort of spongy pitcher,<br/>  And when they deemed its moisture was complete,<br/>They wrung it out, and though a thirsty ditcher<br/>  Might not have thought the scanty draught so sweet<br/>As a full pot of porter, to their thinking<br/>They ne'er till now had known the joys of drinking.<br/><br/>And their baked lips, with many a bloody crack,<br/>  Sucked in the moisture, which like nectar streamed;<br/>Their throats were ovens, their swoln tongues were black,<br/>  As the rich man's in Hell, who vainly screamed<br/>To beg the beggar, who could not rain back<br/>A drop of dew, when every drop had seemed<br/>To taste of Heaven--If this be true, indeed,<br/>Some Christians have a comfortable creed.<br/><br/>There were two fathers in this ghastly crew,<br/>  And with them their two sons, of whom the one<br/>Was more robust and hardy to the view,<br/>  But he died early; and when he was gone,<br/>His nearest messmate told his sire, who threw<br/>  One glance at him, and said, "Heaven's will be done!<br/>I can do nothing," and he saw him thrown<br/>Into the deep without a tear or groan.<br/><br/>The other father had a weaklier child,<br/>  Of a soft cheek, and aspect delicate;<br/>But the boy bore up long, and with a mild<br/>  And patient spirit held aloof his fate;<br/>Little he said, and now and then he smiled,<br/>  As if to win a part from off the weight<br/>He saw increasing on his father's heart,<br/>With the deep deadly thought, that they must part.<br/><br/>And o'er him bent his sire, and never raised<br/>  His eyes from off his face, but wiped the foam<br/>From his pale lips, and ever on him gazed,<br/>  And when the wished-for shower at length was come,<br/>And the boy's eyes, which the dull film half glazed,<br/>  Brightened, and for a moment seemed to roam,<br/>He squeezed from out a rag some drops of rain<br/>Into his dying child's mouth--but in vain.<br/><br/>The boy expired--the father held the clay,<br/>  And looked upon it long, and when at last<br/>Death left no doubt, and the dead burthen lay<br/>  Stiff on his heart, and pulse and hope were past,<br/>He watched it wistfully, until away<br/>  'T was borne by the rude wave wherein't was cast;<br/>Then he himself sunk down all dumb and shivering,<br/>And gave no sign of life, save his limbs quivering.<br/><br/>Now overhead a rainbow, bursting through<br/>  The scattering clouds, shone, spanning the dark sea,<br/>Resting its bright base on the quivering blue;<br/>  And all within its arch appeared to be<br/>Clearer than that without, and its wide hue<br/>  Waxed broad and waving, like a banner free,<br/>Then changed like to a bow that's bent, and then<br/>Forsook the dim eyes of these shipwrecked men.<br/><br/>It changed, of course; a heavenly Chameleon,<br/>  The airy child of vapour and the sun,<br/>Brought forth in purple, cradled in vermilion,<br/>  Baptized in molten gold, and swathed in dun,<br/>Glittering like crescents o'er a Turk's pavilion,<br/>  And blending every colour into one,<br/>Just like a black eye in a recent scuffle<br/>(For sometimes we must box without the muffle).<br/><br/>Our shipwrecked seamen thought it a good omen--<br/>  It is as well to think so, now and then;<br/>'T was an old custom of the Greek and Roman,<br/>  And may become of great advantage when<br/>Folks are discouraged; and most surely no men<br/>  Had greater need to nerve themselves again<br/>Than these, and so this rainbow looked like Hope--<br/>Quite a celestial Kaleidoscope.<br/><br/>About this time a beautiful white bird,<br/>  Webfooted, not unlike a dove in size<br/>And plumage (probably it might have erred<br/>  Upon its course), passed oft before their eyes,<br/>And tried to perch, although it saw and heard<br/>  The men within the boat, and in this guise<br/>It came and went, and fluttered round them till<br/>Night fell:--this seemed a better omen still.<br/><br/>But in this case I also must remark,<br/>  'T was well this bird of promise did not perch,<br/>Because the tackle of our shattered bark<br/>  Was not so safe for roosting as a church;<br/>And had it been the dove from Noah's ark,<br/>  Returning there from her successful search,<br/>Which in their way that moment chanced to fall,<br/>They would have eat her, olive-branch and all.<br/><br/>With twilight it again came on to blow,<br/>  But not with violence; the stars shone out,<br/>The boat made way; yet now they were so low,<br/>  They knew not where nor what they were about;<br/>Some fancied they saw land, and some said "No!"<br/>  The frequent fog-banks gave them cause to doubt--<br/>Some swore that they heard breakers, others guns,<br/>And all mistook about the latter once.<br/><br/>As morning broke, the light wind died away,<br/>  When he who had the watch sung out and swore,<br/>If 't was not land that rose with the Sun's ray,<br/>  He wished that land he never might see more;<br/>And the rest rubbed their eyes and saw a bay,<br/>  Or thought they saw, and shaped their course for shore;<br/>For shore it was, and gradually grew<br/>Distinct, and high, and palpable to view.<br/><br/>And then of these some part burst into tears,<br/>  And others, looking with a stupid stare,<br/>Could not yet separate their hopes from fears,<br/>  And seemed as if they had no further care;<br/>While a few prayed--(the first time for some years)--<br/>  And at the bottom of the boat three were<br/>Asleep: they shook them by the hand and head,<br/>And tried to awaken them, but found them dead.<br/><br/>The day before, fast sleeping on the water,<br/>  They found a turtle of the hawk's-bill kind,<br/>And by good fortune, gliding softly, caught her,<br/>Which yielded a day's life, and to their mind<br/>Proved even still a more nutritious matter,<br/>  Because it left encouragement behind:<br/>They thought that in such perils, more than chance<br/>Had sent them this for their deliverance.<br/><br/>The land appeared a high and rocky coast,<br/>  And higher grew the mountains as they drew,<br/>Set by a current, toward it: they were lost<br/>  In various conjectures, for none knew<br/>To what part of the earth they had been tost,<br/>  So changeable had been the winds that blew;<br/>Some thought it was Mount √Ütna, some the highlands<br/>Of Candia, Cyprus, Rhodes, or other islands.<br/><br/>Meantime the current, with a rising gale,<br/>  Still set them onwards to the welcome shore,<br/>Like Charon's bark of spectres, dull and pale:<br/>  Their living freight was now reduced to four,<br/>And three dead, whom their strength could not avail<br/>  To heave into the deep with those before,<br/>Though the two sharks still followed them, and dashed<br/>The spray into their faces as they splashed.<br/><br/>Famine--despair--cold--thirst and heat, had done<br/>  Their work on them by turns, and thinned them to<br/>Such things a mother had not known her son<br/>  Amidst the skeletons of that gaunt crew;<br/>By night chilled, by day scorched, thus one by one<br/>  They perished, until withered to these few,<br/>But chiefly by a species of self-slaughter,<br/>In washing down Pedrillo with salt water.<br/><br/>As they drew nigh the land, which now was seen<br/>  Unequal in its aspect here and there,<br/>They felt the freshness of its growing green,<br/>  That waved in forest-tops, and smoothed the air,<br/>And fell upon their glazed eyes like a screen<br/>  From glistening waves, and skies so hot and bare--<br/>Lovely seemed any object that should sweep<br/>Away the vast--salt--dread--eternal Deep.<br/><br/>The shore looked wild, without a trace of man,<br/> And girt by formidable waves; but they<br/>Were mad for land, and thus their course they ran,<br/>  Though right ahead the roaring breakers lay:<br/>A reef between them also now began<br/>  To show its boiling surf and bounding spray,<br/>But finding no place for their landing better,<br/>They ran the boat for shore,--and overset her.<br/><br/>But in his native stream, the Guadalquivir,<br/>  Juan to lave his youthful limbs was wont;<br/>And having learnt to swim in that sweet river,<br/>  Had often turned the art to some account:<br/>A better swimmer you could scarce see ever,<br/>  He could, perhaps, have passed the Hellespont,<br/>As once (a feat on which ourselves we prided)<br/>Leander, Mr. Ekenhead, and I did.<br/><br/>So here, though faint, emaciated, and stark,<br/>  He buoyed his boyish limbs, and strove to ply<br/>With the quick wave, and gain, ere it was dark,<br/>  The beach which lay before him, high and dry:<br/>The greatest danger here was from a shark,<br/>  That carried off his neighbour by the thigh;<br/>As for the other two, they could not swim,<br/>So nobody arrived on shore but him.<br/><br/>Nor yet had he arrived but for the oar,<br/>  Which, providentially for him, was washed<br/>Just as his feeble arms could strike no more,<br/>  And the hard wave o'erwhelmed him as 't was dashed<br/>Within his grasp; he clung to it, and sore<br/>  The waters beat while he thereto was lashed;<br/>At last, with swimming, wading, scrambling, he<br/>Rolled on the beach, half-senseless, from the sea:<br/><br/>There, breathless, with his digging nails he clung<br/>  Fast to the sand, lest the returning wave,<br/>From whose reluctant roar his life he wrung,<br/>  Should suck him back to her insatiate grave:<br/>And there he lay, full length, where he was flung,<br/>  Before the entrance of a cliff-worn cave,<br/>With just enough of life to feel its pain,<br/>And deem that it was saved, perhaps, in vain.<br/><br/>With slow and staggering effort he arose,<br/>  But sunk again upon his bleeding knee<br/>And quivering hand; and then he looked for those<br/>  Who long had been his mates upon the sea;<br/>But none of them appeared to share his woes,<br/>  Save one, a corpse, from out the famished three,<br/>Who died two days before, and now had found<br/>An unknown barren beach for burial ground.<br/><br/>And as he gazed, his dizzy brain spun fast,<br/>  And down he sunk; and as he sunk, the sand<br/>Swam round and round, and all his senses passed:<br/>  He fell upon his side, and his stretched hand<br/>Drooped dripping on the oar (their jury-mast),<br/>  And, like a withered lily, on the land<br/>His slender frame and pallid aspect lay,<br/>As fair a thing as e'er was formed of clay.<br/><br/>How long in his damp trance young Juan lay<br/>  He knew not, for the earth was gone for him,<br/>And Time had nothing more of night nor day<br/>  For his congealing blood, and senses dim;<br/>And how this heavy faintness passed away<br/>  He knew not, till each painful pulse and limb,<br/>And tingling vein, seemed throbbing back to life,<br/>For Death, though vanquished, still retired with strife.<br/><br/>His eyes he opened, shut, again unclosed,<br/>  For all was doubt and dizziness; he thought<br/>He still was in the boat, and had but dozed,<br/>  And felt again with his despair o'erwrought,<br/>And wished it Death in which he had reposed,<br/>  And then once more his feelings back were brought,<br/>And slowly by his swimming eyes was seen<br/>A lovely female face of seventeen.<br/><br/>'T was bending close o'er his, and the small mouth<br/>  Seemed almost prying into his for breath;<br/>And chafing him, the soft warm hand of youth<br/>  Recalled his answering spirits back from Death:<br/>And, bathing his chill temples, tried to soothe<br/>  Each pulse to animation, till beneath<br/>Its gentle touch and trembling care, a sigh<br/>To these kind efforts made a low reply.<br/><br/>Then was the cordial poured, and mantle flung<br/>  Around his scarce-clad limbs; and the fair arm<br/>Raised higher the faint head which o'er it hung;<br/>  And her transparent cheek, all pure and warm,<br/>Pillowed his death-like forehead; then she wrung<br/>  His dewy curls, long drenched by every storm;<br/>And watched with eagerness each throb that drew<br/>A sigh from his heaved bosom--and hers, too.<br/><br/>And lifting him with care into the cave,<br/>  The gentle girl, and her attendant,--one<br/>Young, yet her elder, and of brow less grave,<br/>  And more robust of figure,--then begun<br/>To kindle fire, and as the new flames gave<br/>  Light to the rocks that roofed them, which the sun<br/>Had never seen, the maid, or whatsoe'er<br/>She was, appeared distinct, and tall, and fair.<br/><br/>Her brow was overhung with coins of gold,<br/>  That sparkled o'er the auburn of her hair--<br/>Her clustering hair, whose longer locks were rolled<br/>  In braids behind; and though her stature were<br/>Even of the highest for a female mould,<br/>  They nearly reached her heel; and in her air<br/>There was a something which bespoke command,<br/>As one who was a Lady in the land.<br/><br/>Her hair, I said, was auburn; but her eyes<br/>  Were black as Death, their lashes the same hue,<br/>Of downcast length, in whose silk shadow lies<br/>  Deepest attraction; for when to the view<br/>Forth from its raven fringe the full glance flies,<br/>  Ne'er with such force the swiftest arrow flew;<br/>'T is as the snake late coiled, who pours his length,<br/>And hurls at once his venom and his strength.<br/><br/>Her brow was white and low, her cheek's pure dye<br/>  Like twilight rosy still with the set sun;<br/>Short upper lip--sweet lips! that make us sigh<br/>  Ever to have seen such; for she was one<br/>Fit for the model of a statuary<br/>  (A race of mere impostors, when all's done--<br/>I've seen much finer women, ripe and real,<br/>Than all the nonsense of their stone ideal).<br/><br/>I'll tell you why I say so, for 't is just<br/>  One should not rail without a decent cause:<br/>There was an Irish lady, to whose bust<br/>  I ne'er saw justice done, and yet she was<br/>A frequent model; and if e'er she must<br/>  Yield to stern Time and Nature's wrinkling laws,<br/>They will destroy a face which mortal thought<br/>Ne'er compassed, nor less mortal chisel wrought.<br/><br/>And such was she, the lady of the cave:<br/>  Her dress was very different from the Spanish,<br/>Simpler, and yet of colours not so grave;<br/>  For, as you know, the Spanish women banish<br/>Bright hues when out of doors, and yet, while wave<br/>  Around them (what I hope will never vanish)<br/>The basqui√±a and the mantilla, they<br/>Seem at the same time mystical and gay.<br/><br/>But with our damsel this was not the case:<br/>  Her dress was many-coloured, finely spun;<br/>Her locks curled negligently round her face,<br/>  But through them gold and gems profusely shone:<br/>Her girdle sparkled, and the richest lace<br/>  Flowed in her veil, and many a precious stone<br/>Flashed on her little hand; but, what was shocking,<br/>Her small snow feet had slippers, but no stocking.<br/><br/>The other female's dress was not unlike,<br/>  But of inferior materials: she<br/>Had not so many ornaments to strike,<br/>  Her hair had silver only, bound to be<br/>Her dowry; and her veil, in form alike,<br/>  Was coarser; and her air, though firm, less free;<br/>Her hair was thicker, but less long; her eyes<br/>As black, but quicker, and of smaller size.<br/><br/>And these two tended him, and cheered him both<br/>  With food and raiment, and those soft attentions,<br/>Which are--as I must own--of female growth,<br/>  And have ten thousand delicate inventions:<br/>They made a most superior mess of broth,<br/>  A thing which poesy but seldom mentions,<br/>But the best dish that e'er was cooked since Homer's<br/>Achilles ordered dinner for new comers.<br/><br/>I'll tell you who they were, this female pair,<br/>  Lest they should seem Princesses in disguise;<br/>Besides, I hate all mystery, and that air<br/>  Of clap-trap, which your recent poets prize;<br/>And so, in short, the girls they really were<br/>  They shall appear before your curious eyes,<br/>Mistress and maid; the first was only daughter<br/>Of an old man, who lived upon the water.<br/><br/>A fisherman he had been in his youth,<br/>  And still a sort of fisherman was he;<br/>But other speculations were, in sooth,<br/>  Added to his connection with the sea,<br/>Perhaps not so respectable, in truth:<br/>  A little smuggling, and some piracy,<br/>Left him, at last, the sole of many masters<br/>Of an ill-gotten million of piastres.<br/><br/>A fisher, therefore, was he,--though of men,<br/>  Like Peter the Apostle, and he fished<br/>For wandering merchant-vessels, now and then,<br/>  And sometimes caught as many as he wished;<br/>The cargoes he confiscated, and gain<br/>  He sought in the slave-market too, and dished<br/>Full many a morsel for that Turkish trade,<br/>By which, no doubt, a good deal may be made.<br/><br/>He was a Greek, and on his isle had built<br/>  (One of the wild and smaller Cyclades)<br/>A very handsome house from out his guilt,<br/>  And there he lived exceedingly at ease;<br/>Heaven knows what cash he got, or blood he spilt,<br/>  A sad old fellow was he, if you please;<br/>But this I know, it was a spacious building,<br/>Full of barbaric carving, paint, and gilding.<br/><br/>He had an only daughter, called Haid√©e,<br/>  The greatest heiress of the Eastern Isles;<br/>Besides, so very beautiful was she,<br/>  Her dowry was as nothing to her smiles:<br/>Still in her teens, and like a lovely tree<br/>  She grew to womanhood, and between whiles<br/>Rejected several suitors, just to learn<br/>How to accept a better in his turn.<br/><br/>And walking out upon the beach, below<br/>  The cliff, towards sunset, on that day she found,<br/>Insensible,--not dead, but nearly so,--<br/>  Don Juan, almost famished, and half drowned;<br/>But being naked, she was shocked, you know,<br/>  Yet deemed herself in common pity bound,<br/>As far as in her lay, "to take him in,<br/>A stranger" dying--with so white a skin.<br/><br/>But taking him into her father's house<br/>  Was not exactly the best way to save,<br/>But like conveying to the cat the mouse,<br/>  Or people in a trance into their grave;<br/>Because the good old man had so much "ŒΩŒøœÖœÇ",<br/>  Unlike the honest Arab thieves so brave,<br/>He would have hospitably cured the stranger,<br/>And sold him instantly when out of danger.<br/><br/>And therefore, with her maid, she thought it best<br/>  (A virgin always on her maid relies)<br/>To place him in the cave for present rest:<br/>  And when, at last, he opened his black eyes,<br/>Their charity increased about their guest;<br/>  And their compassion grew to such a size,<br/>It opened half the turnpike-gates to Heaven--<br/>(St. Paul says, 't is the toll which must be given).<br/><br/>They made a fire,--but such a fire as they<br/>  Upon the moment could contrive with such<br/>Materials as were cast up round the bay,--<br/>  Some broken planks, and oars, that to the touch<br/>Were nearly tinder, since, so long they lay,<br/>  A mast was almost crumbled to a crutch;<br/>But, by God's grace, here wrecks were in such plenty,<br/>That there was fuel to have furnished twenty.<br/><br/>He had a bed of furs, and a pelisse,<br/>  For Haid√©e stripped her sables off to make<br/>His couch; and, that he might be more at ease,<br/>  And warm, in case by chance he should awake,<br/>They also gave a petticoat apiece,<br/>  She and her maid,--and promised by daybreak<br/>To pay him a fresh visit, with a dish<br/>For breakfast, of eggs, coffee, bread, and fish.<br/><br/>And thus they left him to his lone repose:<br/>  Juan slept like a top, or like the dead,<br/>Who sleep at last, perhaps (God only knows),<br/>  Just for the present; and in his lulled head<br/>Not even a vision of his former woes<br/>  Throbbed in accurs√©d dreams, which sometimes spread<br/>Unwelcome visions of our former years,<br/>Till the eye, cheated, opens thick with tears.<br/><br/>Young Juan slept all dreamless:--but the maid,<br/>  Who smoothed his pillow, as she left the den<br/>Looked back upon him, and a moment stayed,<br/>  And turned, believing that he called again.<br/>He slumbered; yet she thought, at least she said<br/>  (The heart will slip, even as the tongue and pen),<br/>He had pronounced her name--but she forgot<br/>That at this moment Juan knew it not.<br/><br/>And pensive to her father's house she went,<br/>  Enjoining silence strict to Zoe, who<br/>Better than her knew what, in fact, she meant,<br/>  She being wiser by a year or two:<br/>A year or two's an age when rightly spent,<br/>  And Zoe spent hers, as most women do,<br/>In gaining all that useful sort of knowledge<br/>Which is acquired in Nature's good old college.<br/><br/>The morn broke, and found Juan slumbering still<br/>  Fast in his cave, and nothing clashed upon<br/>His rest; the rushing of the neighbouring rill,<br/>  And the young beams of the excluded Sun,<br/>Troubled him not, and he might sleep his fill;<br/>  And need he had of slumber yet, for none<br/>Had suffered more--his hardships were comparative<br/>To those related in my grand-dad's "Narrative."<br/><br/>Not so Haid√©e: she sadly tossed and tumbled,<br/>  And started from her sleep, and, turning o'er,<br/>Dreamed of a thousand wrecks, o'er which she stumbled,<br/>  And handsome corpses strewed upon the shore;<br/>And woke her maid so early that she grumbled,<br/>  And called her father's old slaves up, who swore<br/>In several oaths--Armenian, Turk, and Greek--<br/>They knew not what to think of such a freak.<br/><br/>But up she got, and up she made them get,<br/>  With some pretence about the Sun, that makes<br/>Sweet skies just when he rises, or is set;<br/>  And 't is, no doubt, a sight to see when breaks<br/>Bright Phoebus, while the mountains still are wet<br/>  With mist, and every bird with him awakes,<br/>And night is flung off like a mourning suit<br/>Worn for a husband,--or some other brute.<br/><br/>I say, the Sun is a most glorious sight,<br/>  I've seen him rise full oft, indeed of late<br/>I have sat up on purpose all the night,<br/>  Which hastens, as physicians say, one's fate;<br/>And so all ye, who would be in the right<br/>  In health and purse, begin your day to date<br/>From daybreak, and when coffined at fourscore,<br/>Engrave upon the plate, you rose at four.<br/><br/>And Haid√©e met the morning face to face;<br/>  Her own was freshest, though a feverish flush<br/>Had dyed it with the headlong blood, whose race<br/>  From heart to cheek is curbed into a blush,<br/>Like to a torrent which a mountain's base,<br/>  That overpowers some Alpine river's rush,<br/>Checks to a lake, whose waves in circles spread;<br/>Or the Red Sea--but the sea is not red.<br/><br/>And down the cliff the island virgin came,<br/>  And near the cave her quick light footsteps drew,<br/>While the Sun smiled on her with his first flame,<br/>  And young Aurora kissed her lips with dew,<br/>Taking her for a sister; just the same<br/>  Mistake you would have made on seeing the two,<br/>Although the mortal, quite as fresh and fair,<br/>Had all the advantage, too, of not being air.<br/><br/>And when into the cavern Haid√©e stepped<br/>  All timidly, yet rapidly, she saw<br/>That like an infant Juan sweetly slept;<br/>  And then she stopped, and stood as if in awe<br/>(For sleep is awful), and on tiptoe crept<br/>  And wrapped him closer, lest the air, too raw,<br/>Should reach his blood, then o'er him still as Death<br/>Bent, with hushed lips, that drank his scarce-drawn breath.<br/><br/>And thus like to an Angel o'er the dying<br/>  Who die in righteousness, she leaned; and there<br/>All tranquilly the shipwrecked boy was lying,<br/>  As o'er him lay the calm and stirless air:<br/>But Zoe the meantime some eggs was frying,<br/>  Since, after all, no doubt the youthful pair<br/>Must breakfast--and, betimes, lest they should ask it,<br/>She drew out her provision from the basket.<br/><br/>She knew that the best feelings must have victual,<br/>  And that a shipwrecked youth would hungry be;<br/>Besides, being less in love, she yawned a little,<br/>  And felt her veins chilled by the neighbouring sea;<br/>And so, she cooked their breakfast to a tittle;<br/>  I can't say that she gave them any tea,<br/>But there were eggs, fruit, coffee, bread, fish, honey,<br/>With Scio wine,--and all for love, not money.<br/><br/>And Zoe, when the eggs were ready, and<br/>  The coffee made, would fain have wakened Juan;<br/>But Haid√©e stopped her with her quick small hand,<br/>  And without word, a sign her finger drew on<br/>Her lip, which Zoe needs must understand;<br/>  And, the first breakfast spoilt, prepared a new one,<br/>Because her mistress would not let her break<br/>That sleep which seemed as it would ne'er awake.<br/><br/>For still he lay, and on his thin worn cheek<br/>  A purple hectic played like dying day<br/>On the snow-tops of distant hills; the streak<br/>  Of sufferance yet upon his forehead lay,<br/>Where the blue veins looked shadowy, shrunk, and weak;<br/>  And his black curls were dewy with the spray,<br/>Which weighed upon them yet, all damp and salt,<br/>Mixed with the stony vapours of the vault.<br/><br/>And she bent o'er him, and he lay beneath,<br/>  Hushed as the babe upon its mother's breast,<br/>Drooped as the willow when no winds can breathe,<br/>  Lulled like the depth of Ocean when at rest,<br/>Fair as the crowning rose of the whole wreath,<br/>  Soft as the callow cygnet in its nest;<br/>In short, he was a very pretty fellow,<br/>Although his woes had turned him rather yellow.<br/><br/>He woke and gazed, and would have slept again,<br/>  But the fair face which met his eyes forbade<br/>Those eyes to close, though weariness and pain<br/>  Had further sleep a further pleasure made:<br/>For Woman's face was never formed in vain<br/>  For Juan, so that even when he prayed<br/>He turned from grisly saints, and martyrs hairy,<br/>To the sweet portraits of the Virgin Mary.<br/><br/>And thus upon his elbow he arose,<br/>  And looked upon the lady, in whose cheek<br/>The pale contended with the purple rose,<br/>  As with an effort she began to speak;<br/>Her eyes were eloquent, her words would pose,<br/>  Although she told him, in good modern Greek,<br/>With an Ionian accent, low and sweet,<br/>That he was faint, and must not talk, but eat.<br/><br/>Now Juan could not understand a word,<br/>  Being no Grecian; but he had an ear,<br/>And her voice was the warble of a bird,<br/>  So soft, so sweet, so delicately clear,<br/>That finer, simpler music ne'er was heard;<br/>  The sort of sound we echo with a tear,<br/>Without knowing why--an overpowering tone,<br/>Whence Melody descends as from a throne.<br/><br/>And Juan gazed as one who is awoke<br/>  By a distant organ, doubting if he be<br/>Not yet a dreamer, till the spell is broke<br/>  By the watchman, or some such reality,<br/>Or by one's early valet's curs√©d knock;<br/>  At least it is a heavy sound to me,<br/>Who like a morning slumber--for the night<br/>Shows stars and women in a better light.<br/><br/>And Juan, too, was helped out from his dream,<br/>  Or sleep, or whatsoe'er it was, by feeling<br/>A most prodigious appetite; the steam<br/>  Of Zoe's cookery no doubt was stealing<br/>Upon his senses, and the kindling beam<br/>  Of the new fire, which Zoe kept up, kneeling,<br/>To stir her viands, made him quite awake<br/>And long for food, but chiefly a beef-steak.<br/><br/>But beef is rare within these oxless isles;<br/>  Goat's flesh there is, no doubt, and kid, and mutton,<br/>And, when a holiday upon them smiles,<br/>  A joint upon their barbarous spits they put on:<br/>But this occurs but seldom, between whiles,<br/>  For some of these are rocks with scarce a hut on;<br/>Others are fair and fertile, among which<br/>This, though not large, was one of the most rich.<br/><br/>I say that beef is rare, and can't help thinking<br/>  That the old fable of the Minotaur--From<br/>which our modern morals, rightly shrinking,<br/>  Condemn the royal lady's taste who wore<br/>A cow's shape for a mask--was only (sinking<br/>  The allegory) a mere type, no more,<br/>That Pasiphae promoted breeding cattle,<br/>To make the Cretans bloodier in battle.<br/><br/>For we all know that English people are<br/>  Fed upon beef--I won't say much of beer,<br/>Because 't is liquor only, and being far<br/>  From this my subject, has no business here;<br/>We know, too, they are very fond of war,<br/>  A pleasure--like all pleasures--rather dear;<br/>So were the Cretans--from which I infer,<br/>That beef and battles both were owing to her.<br/><br/>But to resume. The languid Juan raised<br/>  His head upon his elbow, and he saw<br/>A sight on which he had not lately gazed,<br/>  As all his latter meals had been quite raw,<br/>Three or four things, for which the Lord he praised,<br/>  And, feeling still the famished vulture gnaw,<br/>He fell upon whate'er was offered, like<br/>A priest, a shark, an alderman, or pike.<br/><br/>He ate, and he was well supplied; and she,<br/>  Who watched him like a mother, would have fed<br/>Him past all bounds, because she smiled to see<br/>  Such appetite in one she had deemed dead:<br/>But Zoe, being older than Haid√©e,<br/>  Knew (by tradition, for she ne'er had read)<br/>That famished people must be slowly nurst,<br/>And fed by spoonfuls, else they always burst.<br/><br/>And so she took the liberty to state,<br/>  Rather by deeds than words, because the case<br/>Was urgent, that the gentleman, whose fate<br/>  Had made her mistress quit her bed to trace<br/>The sea-shore at this hour, must leave his plate,<br/>  Unless he wished to die upon the place--<br/>She snatched it, and refused another morsel,<br/>Saying, he had gorged enough to make a horse ill.<br/><br/>Next they--he being naked, save a tattered<br/>  Pair of scarce decent trowsers--went to work,<br/>And in the fire his recent rags they scattered,<br/>  And dressed him, for the present, like a Turk,<br/>Or Greek--that is, although it not much mattered,<br/>  Omitting turban, slippers, pistol, dirk,--<br/>They furnished him, entire, except some stitches,<br/>With a clean shirt, and very spacious breeches.<br/><br/>And then fair Haid√©e tried her tongue at speaking,<br/>  But not a word could Juan comprehend,<br/>Although he listened so that the young Greek in<br/>  Her earnestness would ne'er have made an end;<br/>And, as he interrupted not, went eking<br/>  Her speech out to her prot√©g√© and friend,<br/>Till pausing at the last her breath to take,<br/>She saw he did not understand Romaic.<br/><br/>And then she had recourse to nods, and signs,<br/>  And smiles, and sparkles of the speaking eye,<br/>And read (the only book she could) the lines<br/>  Of his fair face, and found, by sympathy,<br/>The answer eloquent, where the Soul shines<br/>  And darts in one quick glance a long reply;<br/>And thus in every look she saw expressed<br/>A world of words, and things at which she guessed.<br/><br/>And now, by dint of fingers and of eyes,<br/>  And words repeated after her, he took<br/>A lesson in her tongue; but by surmise,<br/>  No doubt, less of her language than her look:<br/>As he who studies fervently the skies<br/>  Turns oftener to the stars than to his book,<br/>Thus Juan learned his _alpha beta_ better<br/>From Haid√©e's glance than any graven letter.<br/><br/>'T is pleasing to be schooled in a strange tongue<br/>  By female lips and eyes--that is, I mean,<br/>When both the teacher and the taught are young,<br/>  As was the case, at least, where I have been;<br/>They smile so when one's right, and when one's wrong<br/>  They smile still more, and then there intervene<br/>Pressure of hands, perhaps even a chaste kiss;--<br/>I learned the little that I know by this:<br/><br/>That is, some words of Spanish, Turk, and Greek,<br/>  Italian not at all, having no teachers;<br/>Much English I cannot pretend to speak,<br/>  Learning that language chiefly from its preachers,<br/>Barrow, South, Tillotson, whom every week<br/>  I study, also Blair--the highest reachers<br/>Of eloquence in piety and prose--<br/>I hate your poets, so read none of those.<br/><br/>As for the ladies, I have nought to say,<br/>  A wanderer from the British world of Fashion,<br/>Where I, like other "dogs, have had my day,"<br/>  Like other men, too, may have had my passion--<br/>But that, like other things, has passed away,<br/>  And all her fools whom I _could_ lay the lash on:<br/>Foes, friends, men, women, now are nought to me<br/>But dreams of what has been, no more to be.<br/><br/>Return we to Don Juan. He begun<br/>  To hear new words, and to repeat them; but<br/>Some feelings, universal as the Sun,<br/>  Were such as could not in his breast be shut<br/>More than within the bosom of a nun:<br/>  He was in love,--as you would be, no doubt,<br/>With a young benefactress,--so was she,<br/>Just in the way we very often see.<br/><br/>And every day by daybreak--rather early<br/>  For Juan, who was somewhat fond of rest--<br/>She came into the cave, but it was merely<br/>  To see her bird reposing in his nest;<br/>And she would softly stir his locks so curly,<br/>  Without disturbing her yet
