slumbering guest,<br/>Breathing all gently o'er his cheek and mouth,<br/>As o'er a bed of roses the sweet South.<br/><br/>And every morn his colour freshlier came,<br/>  And every day helped on his convalescence;<br/>'T was well, because health in the human frame<br/>  Is pleasant, besides being true Love's essence,<br/>For health and idleness to Passion's flame<br/>  Are oil and gunpowder; and some good lessons<br/>Are also learnt from Ceres and from Bacchus,<br/>Without whom Venus will not long attack us.<br/><br/>While Venus fills the heart, (without heart really<br/>  Love, though good always, is not quite so good,)<br/>Ceres presents a plate of vermicelli,--<br/>  For Love must be sustained like flesh and blood,--While<br/>Bacchus pours out wine, or hands a jelly:<br/>  Eggs, oysters, too, are amatory food;<br/>But who is their purveyor from above<br/>Heaven knows,--it may be Neptune, Pan, or Jove.<br/><br/>When Juan woke he found some good things ready,<br/>  A bath, a breakfast, and the finest eyes<br/>That ever made a youthful heart less steady,<br/>  Besides her maid's, as pretty for their size;<br/>But I have spoken of all this already--<br/>  A repetition's tiresome and unwise,--<br/>Well--Juan, after bathing in the sea,<br/>Came always back to coffee and Haid√©e.<br/><br/>Both were so young, and one so innocent,<br/>  That bathing passed for nothing; Juan seemed<br/>To her, as 't were, the kind of being sent,<br/>  Of whom these two years she had nightly dreamed,<br/>A something to be loved, a creature meant<br/>  To be her happiness, and whom she deemed<br/>To render happy; all who joy would win<br/>Must share it,--Happiness was born a Twin.<br/><br/>It was such pleasure to behold him, such<br/>  Enlargement of existence to partake<br/>Nature with him, to thrill beneath his touch,<br/>  To watch him slumbering, and to see him wake:<br/>To live with him for ever were too much;<br/>  But then the thought of parting made her quake;<br/>He was her own, her ocean-treasure, cast<br/>Like a rich wreck--her first love, and her last.<br/><br/>And thus a moon rolled on, and fair Haid√©e<br/>  Paid daily visits to her boy, and took<br/>Such plentiful precautions, that still he<br/>  Remained unknown within his craggy nook;<br/>At last her father's prows put out to sea,<br/>  For certain merchantmen upon the look,<br/>Not as of yore to carry off an Io,<br/>But three Ragusan vessels, bound for Scio.<br/><br/>Then came her freedom, for she had no mother,<br/>  So that, her father being at sea, she was<br/>Free as a married woman, or such other<br/>  Female, as where she likes may freely pass,<br/>Without even the encumbrance of a brother,<br/>  The freest she that ever gazed on glass:<br/>I speak of Christian lands in this comparison,<br/>Where wives, at least, are seldom kept in garrison.<br/><br/>Now she prolonged her visits and her talk<br/>  (For they must talk), and he had learnt to say<br/>So much as to propose to take a walk,--<br/>  For little had he wandered since the day<br/>On which, like a young flower snapped from the stalk,<br/>  Drooping and dewy on the beach he lay,--<br/>And thus they walked out in the afternoon,<br/>And saw the sun set opposite the moon.<br/><br/>It was a wild and breaker-beaten coast,<br/>  With cliffs above, and a broad sandy shore,<br/>Guarded by shoals and rocks as by an host,<br/>  With here and there a creek, whose aspect wore<br/>A better welcome to the tempest-tost;<br/>  And rarely ceased the haughty billow's roar,<br/>Save on the dead long summer days, which make<br/>The outstretched Ocean glitter like a lake.<br/><br/>And the small ripple spilt upon the beach<br/>  Scarcely o'erpassed the cream of your champagne,<br/>When o'er the brim the sparkling bumpers reach,<br/>  That spring-dew of the spirit! the heart's rain!<br/>Few things surpass old wine; and they may preach<br/>  Who please,--the more because they preach in vain,--<br/>Let us have Wine and Woman, Mirth and Laughter,<br/>Sermons and soda-water the day after.<br/><br/>Man, being reasonable, must get drunk;<br/>  The best of Life is but intoxication:<br/>Glory, the Grape, Love, Gold, in these are sunk<br/>  The hopes of all men, and of every nation;<br/>Without their sap, how branchless were the trunk<br/>  Of Life's strange tree, so fruitful on occasion!<br/>But to return,--Get very drunk, and when<br/>You wake with headache--you shall see what then!<br/><br/>Ring for your valet--bid him quickly bring<br/>  Some hock and soda-water, then you'll know<br/>A pleasure worthy Xerxes the great king;<br/>  For not the blest sherbet, sublimed with snow,<br/>Nor the first sparkle of the desert-spring,<br/>  Nor Burgundy in all its sunset glow,<br/>After long travel, Ennui, Love, or Slaughter,<br/>Vie with that draught of hock and soda-water!<br/><br/>The coast--I think it was the coast that I<br/>  Was just describing--Yes, it _was_ the coast--<br/>Lay at this period quiet as the sky,<br/>  The sands untumbled, the blue waves untossed,<br/>And all was stillness, save the sea-bird's cry,<br/>  And dolphin's leap, and little billow crossed<br/>By some low rock or shelve, that made it fret<br/>Against the boundary it scarcely wet.<br/><br/>And forth they wandered, her sire being gone,<br/>  As I have said, upon an expedition;<br/>And mother, brother, guardian, she had none,<br/>  Save Zoe, who, although with due precision<br/>She waited on her lady with the Sun,<br/>  Thought daily service was her only mission,<br/>Bringing warm water, wreathing her long tresses,<br/>And asking now and then for cast-off dresses.<br/><br/>It was the cooling hour, just when the rounded<br/>  Red sun sinks down behind the azure hill,<br/>Which then seems as if the whole earth it bounded,<br/>  Circling all Nature, hushed, and dim, and still,<br/>With the far mountain-crescent half surrounded<br/>  On one side, and the deep sea calm and chill<br/>Upon the other, and the rosy sky<br/>With one star sparkling through it like an eye.<br/><br/>And thus they wandered forth, and hand in hand,<br/>  Over the shining pebbles and the shells,<br/>Glided along the smooth and hardened sand,<br/>  And in the worn and wild receptacles<br/>Worked by the storms, yet worked as it were planned<br/>  In hollow halls, with sparry roofs and cells,<br/>They turned to rest; and, each clasped by an arm,<br/>Yielded to the deep Twilight's purple charm.<br/><br/>They looked up to the sky, whose floating glow<br/>  Spread like a rosy Ocean, vast and bright;<br/>They gazed upon the glittering sea below,<br/>  Whence the broad Moon rose circling into sight;<br/>They heard the waves' splash, and the wind so low,<br/>  And saw each other's dark eyes darting light<br/>Into each other--and, beholding this,<br/>Their lips drew near, and clung into a kiss;<br/><br/>A long, long kiss, a kiss of Youth, and Love,<br/>  And Beauty, all concentrating like rays<br/>Into one focus, kindled from above;<br/>  Such kisses as belong to early days,<br/>Where Heart, and Soul, and Sense, in concert move,<br/>  And the blood's lava, and the pulse a blaze,<br/>Each kiss a heart-quake,--for a kiss's strength,<br/>I think, it must be reckoned by its length.<br/><br/>By length I mean duration; theirs endured<br/>  Heaven knows how long--no doubt they never reckoned;<br/>And if they had, they could not have secured<br/>  The sum of their sensations to a second:<br/>They had not spoken, but they felt allured,<br/>  As if their souls and lips each other beckoned,<br/>Which, being joined, like swarming bees they clung--<br/>Their hearts the flowers from whence the honey sprung.<br/><br/>They were alone, but not alone as they<br/>  Who shut in chambers think it loneliness;<br/>The silent Ocean, and the starlight bay,<br/>  The twilight glow, which momently grew less,<br/>The voiceless sands, and dropping caves, that lay<br/>  Around them, made them to each other press,<br/>As if there were no life beneath the sky<br/>Save theirs, and that their life could never die.<br/><br/>They feared no eyes nor ears on that lone beach;<br/>  They felt no terrors from the night; they were<br/>All in all to each other: though their speech<br/>  Was broken words, they _thought_ a language there,--<br/>And all the burning tongues the Passions teach<br/>  Found in one sigh the best interpreter<br/>Of Nature's oracle--first love,--that all<br/>Which Eve has left her daughters since her fall.<br/><br/>Haid√©e spoke not of scruples, asked no vows,<br/>  Nor offered any; she had never heard<br/>Of plight and promises to be a spouse,<br/>  Or perils by a loving maid incurred;<br/>She was all which pure Ignorance allows,<br/>  And flew to her young mate like a young bird;<br/>And, never having dreamt of falsehood, she<br/>Had not one word to say of constancy.<br/><br/>She loved, and was belov√©d--she adored,<br/>  And she was worshipped after Nature's fashion--<br/>Their intense souls, into each other poured,<br/>  If souls could die, had perished in that passion,--<br/>But by degrees their senses were restored,<br/>  Again to be o'ercome, again to dash on;<br/>And, beating 'gainst _his_ bosom, Haid√©e's heart<br/>Felt as if never more to beat apart.<br/><br/>Alas! they were so young, so beautiful,<br/>  So lonely, loving, helpless, and the hour<br/>Was that in which the Heart is always full,<br/>  And, having o'er itself no further power,<br/>Prompts deeds Eternity can not annul,<br/>  But pays off moments in an endless shower<br/>Of hell-fire--all prepared for people giving<br/>Pleasure or pain to one another living.<br/><br/>Alas! for Juan and Haid√©e! they were<br/>  So loving and so lovely--till then never,<br/>Excepting our first parents, such a pair<br/>  Had run the risk of being damned for ever:<br/>And Haid√©e, being devout as well as fair,<br/>  Had, doubtless, heard about the Stygian river,<br/>And Hell and Purgatory--but forgot<br/>Just in the very crisis she should not.<br/><br/>They look upon each other, and their eyes<br/>  Gleam in the moonlight; and her white arm clasps<br/>Round Juan's head, and his around her lies<br/>  Half buried in the tresses which it grasps;<br/>She sits upon his knee, and drinks his sighs,<br/>  He hers, until they end in broken gasps;<br/>And thus they form a group that's quite antique,<br/>Half naked, loving, natural, and Greek.<br/><br/>And when those deep and burning moments passed,<br/>  And Juan sunk to sleep within her arms,<br/>She slept not, but all tenderly, though fast,<br/>  Sustained his head upon her bosom's charms;<br/>And now and then her eye to Heaven is cast,<br/>  And then on the pale cheek her breast now warms,<br/>Pillowed on her o'erflowing heart, which pants<br/>With all it granted, and with all it grants.<br/><br/>An infant when it gazes on a light,<br/>  A child the moment when it drains the breast,<br/>A devotee when soars the Host in sight,<br/>  An Arab with a stranger for a guest,<br/>A sailor when the prize has struck in fight,<br/>  A miser filling his most hoarded chest,<br/>Feel rapture; but not such true joy are reaping<br/>As they who watch o'er what they love while sleeping.<br/><br/>For there it lies so tranquil, so beloved,<br/>  All that it hath of Life with us is living;<br/>So gentle, stirless, helpless, and unmoved,<br/>  And all unconscious of the joy 't is giving;<br/>All it hath felt, inflicted, passed, and proved,<br/>  Hushed into depths beyond the watcher's diving:<br/>There lies the thing we love with all its errors<br/>And all its charms, like Death without its terrors.<br/><br/>The Lady watched her lover--and that hour<br/>  Of Love's, and Night's, and Ocean's solitude<br/>O'erflowed her soul with their united power;<br/>  Amidst the barren sand and rocks so rude<br/>She and her wave-worn love had made their bower,<br/>  Where nought upon their passion could intrude,<br/>And all the stars that crowded the blue space<br/>Saw nothing happier than her glowing face.<br/><br/>Alas! the love of Women! it is known<br/>  To be a lovely and a fearful thing;<br/>For all of theirs upon that die is thrown,<br/>  And if 't is lost, Life hath no more to bring<br/>To them but mockeries of the past alone,<br/>  And their revenge is as the tiger's spring,<br/>Deadly, and quick, and crushing; yet, as real<br/>Torture is theirs--what they inflict they feel.<br/><br/>They are right; for Man, to man so oft unjust,<br/>  Is always so to Women: one sole bond<br/>Awaits them--treachery is all their trust;<br/>  Taught to conceal their bursting hearts despond<br/>Over their idol, till some wealthier lust<br/>  Buys them in marriage--and what rests beyond?<br/>A thankless husband--next, a faithless lover--<br/>Then dressing, nursing, praying--and all's over.<br/><br/>Some take a lover, some take drams or prayers,<br/>  Some mind their household, others dissipation,<br/>Some run away, and but exchange their cares,<br/>  Losing the advantage of a virtuous station;<br/>Few changes e'er can better their affairs,<br/>  Theirs being an unnatural situation,<br/>From the dull palace to the dirty hovel:<br/>Some play the devil, and then write a novel.<br/><br/>Haid√©e was Nature's bride, and knew not this;<br/>  Haid√©e was Passion's child, born where the Sun<br/>Showers triple light, and scorches even the kiss<br/>  Of his gazelle-eyed daughters; she was one<br/>Made but to love, to feel that she was his<br/>  Who was her chosen: what was said or done<br/>Elsewhere was nothing. She had nought to fear,<br/>Hope, care, nor love, beyond,--her heart beat _here_.<br/><br/>And oh! that quickening of the heart, that beat!<br/>  How much it costs us! yet each rising throb<br/>Is in its cause as its effect so sweet,<br/>  That Wisdom, ever on the watch to rob<br/>Joy of its alchemy, and to repeat<br/>  Fine truths; even Conscience, too, has a tough job<br/>To make us understand each good old maxim,<br/>So good--I wonder Castlereagh don't tax 'em.<br/><br/>And now 't was done--on the lone shore were plighted<br/>  Their hearts; the stars, their nuptial torches, shed<br/>Beauty upon the beautiful they lighted:<br/>  Ocean their witness, and the cave their bed,<br/>By their own feelings hallowed and united,<br/>  Their priest was Solitude, and they were wed:<br/>And they were happy--for to their young eyes<br/>Each was an angel, and earth Paradise.<br/><br/>Oh, Love! of whom great C√¶sar was the suitor,<br/>  Titus the master, Antony the slave,<br/>Horace, Catullus, scholars--Ovid tutor--<br/>  Sappho the sage blue-stocking, in whose grave<br/>All those may leap who rather would be neuter--<br/>  (Leucadia's rock still overlooks the wave)--<br/>Oh, Love! thou art the very God of evil,<br/>For, after all, we cannot call thee Devil.<br/><br/>Thou mak'st the chaste connubial state precarious,<br/>  And jestest with the brows of mightiest men:<br/>C√¶sar and Pompey, Mahomet, Belisarius,<br/>  Have much employed the Muse of History's pen:<br/>Their lives and fortunes were extremely various,<br/>  Such worthies Time will never see again;<br/>Yet to these four in three things the same luck holds,<br/>They all were heroes, conquerors, and cuckolds.<br/><br/>Thou mak'st philosophers; there's Epicurus<br/>  And Aristippus, a material crew!<br/>Who to immoral courses would allure us<br/>  By theories quite practicable too;<br/>If only from the Devil they would insure us,<br/>  How pleasant were the maxim (not quite new),<br/>"Eat, drink, and love, what can the rest avail us?"<br/>So said the royal sage Sardanapalus.<br/><br/>But Juan! had he quite forgotten Julia?<br/>  And should he have forgotten her so soon?<br/>I can't but say it seems to me most truly a<br/>  Perplexing question; but, no doubt, the moon<br/>Does these things for us, and whenever newly a<br/>  Strong palpitation rises, 't is her boon,<br/>Else how the devil is it that fresh features<br/>Have such a charm for us poor human creatures?<br/><br/>I hate inconstancy--I loathe, detest,<br/>  Abhor, condemn, abjure the mortal made<br/>Of such quicksilver clay that in his breast<br/>  No permanent foundation can be laid;<br/>Love, constant love, has been my constant guest,<br/>  And yet last night, being at a masquerade,<br/>I saw the prettiest creature, fresh from Milan,<br/>Which gave me some sensations like a villain.<br/><br/>But soon Philosophy came to my aid,<br/>  And whispered, "Think of every sacred tie!"<br/>"I will, my dear Philosophy!" I said,<br/>  "But then her teeth, and then, oh, Heaven! her eye!<br/>I'll just inquire if she be wife or maid,<br/>  Or neither--out of curiosity."<br/>"Stop!" cried Philosophy, with air so Grecian,<br/>(Though she was masqued then as a fair Venetian;)<br/><br/>"Stop!" so I stopped.--But to return: that which<br/>  Men call inconstancy is nothing more<br/>Than admiration due where Nature's rich<br/>  Profusion with young beauty covers o'er<br/>Some favoured object; and as in the niche<br/>  A lovely statue we almost adore,<br/>This sort of adoration of the real<br/>Is but a heightening of the _beau ideal_.<br/><br/>'T is the perception of the Beautiful,<br/>  A fine extension of the faculties,<br/>Platonic, universal, wonderful,<br/>  Drawn from the stars, and filtered through the skies,<br/>Without which Life would be extremely dull;<br/>  In short, it is the use of our own eyes,<br/>With one or two small senses added, just<br/>To hint that flesh is formed of fiery dust.<br/><br/>Yet 't is a painful feeling, and unwilling,<br/>  For surely if we always could perceive<br/>In the same object graces quite as killing<br/>  As when she rose upon us like an Eve,<br/>'T would save us many a heartache, many a shilling,<br/>  (For we must get them anyhow, or grieve),<br/>Whereas if one sole lady pleased for ever,<br/>How pleasant for the heart, as well as liver!<br/><br/>The Heart is like the sky, a part of Heaven,<br/>  But changes night and day, too, like the sky;<br/>Now o'er it clouds and thunder must be driven,<br/>  And Darkness and Destruction as on high:<br/>But when it hath been scorched, and pierced, and riven,<br/>  Its storms expire in water-drops; the eye<br/>Pours forth at last the Heart's blood turned to tears,<br/>Which make the English climate of our years.<br/><br/>The liver is the lazaret of bile,<br/>  But very rarely executes its function,<br/>For the first passion stays there such a while,<br/>  That all the rest creep in and form a junction,<br/>Like knots of vipers on a dunghill's soil--<br/>  Rage, fear, hate, jealousy, revenge, compunction--<br/>So that all mischiefs spring up from this entrail,<br/>Like Earthquakes from the hidden fire called "central."<br/><br/>In the mean time, without proceeding more<br/>  In this anatomy, I've finished now<br/>Two hundred and odd stanzas as before,<br/>  That being about the number I'll allow<br/>Each canto of the twelve, or twenty-four;<br/>  And, laying down my pen, I make my bow,<br/>Leaving Don Juan and Haid√©e to plead<br/>For them and theirs with all who deign to read.<br/><br/>CANTO THE THIRD.<br/><br/>HAIL, Muse! _et cetera._--We left Juan sleeping,<br/>  Pillowed upon a fair and happy breast,<br/>And watched by eyes that never yet knew weeping,<br/>  And loved by a young heart, too deeply blest<br/>To feel the poison through her spirit creeping,<br/>  Or know who rested there, a foe to rest,<br/>Had soiled the current of her sinless years,<br/>And turned her pure heart's purest blood to tears!<br/><br/>Oh, Love! what is it in this world of ours<br/>  Which makes it fatal to be loved? Ah why<br/>With cypress branches hast thou wreathed thy bowers,<br/>  And made thy best interpreter a sigh?<br/>As those who dote on odours pluck the flowers,<br/>  And place them on their breast--but place to die--<br/>Thus the frail beings we would fondly cherish<br/>Are laid within our bosoms but to perish.<br/><br/>In her first passion Woman loves her lover,<br/>  In all the others all she loves is Love,<br/>Which grows a habit she can ne'er get over,<br/>  And fits her loosely--like an easy glove,<br/>As you may find, whene'er you like to prove her:<br/>  One man alone at first her heart can move;<br/>She then prefers him in the plural number,<br/>Not finding that the additions much encumber.<br/><br/>I know not if the fault be men's or theirs;<br/>  But one thing's pretty sure; a woman planted<br/>(Unless at once she plunge for life in prayers)--<br/>  After a decent time must be gallanted;<br/>Although, no doubt, her first of love affairs<br/>  Is that to which her heart is wholly granted;<br/>Yet there are some, they say, who have had _none_,<br/>But those who have ne'er end with only _one_.<br/><br/>'T is melancholy, and a fearful sign<br/>  Of human frailty, folly, also crime,<br/>That Love and Marriage rarely can combine,<br/>  Although they both are born in the same clime;<br/>Marriage from Love, like vinegar from wine--<br/>  A sad, sour, sober beverage--by Time<br/>Is sharpened from its high celestial flavour<br/>Down to a very homely household savour.<br/><br/>There's something of antipathy, as 't were,<br/>  Between their present and their future state;<br/>A kind of flattery that's hardly fair<br/>  Is used until the truth arrives too late--<br/>Yet what can people do, except despair?<br/>  The same things change their names at such a rate;<br/>For instance--Passion in a lover's glorious,<br/>But in a husband is pronounced uxorious.<br/><br/>Men grow ashamed of being so very fond;<br/>  They sometimes also get a little tired<br/>(But that, of course, is rare), and then despond:<br/>  The same things cannot always be admired,<br/>Yet 't is "so nominated in the bond,"<br/>  That both are tied till one shall have expired.<br/>Sad thought! to lose the spouse that was adorning<br/>Our days, and put one's servants into mourning.<br/><br/>There's doubtless something in domestic doings<br/>  Which forms, in fact, true Love's antithesis;<br/>Romances paint at full length people's wooings,<br/>  But only give a bust of marriages;<br/>For no one cares for matrimonial cooings,<br/>  There's nothing wrong in a connubial kiss:<br/>Think you, if Laura had been Petrarch's wife,<br/>He would have written sonnets all his life?<br/><br/>All tragedies are finished by a death,<br/>  All comedies are ended by a marriage;<br/>The future states of both are left to faith,<br/>  For authors fear description might disparage<br/>The worlds to come of both, or fall beneath,<br/>  And then both worlds would punish their miscarriage;<br/>So leaving each their priest and prayer-book ready,<br/>They say no more of Death or of the Lady.<br/><br/>The only two that in my recollection,<br/>  Have sung of Heaven and Hell, or marriage, are<br/>Dante and Milton, and of both the affection<br/>  Was hapless in their nuptials, for some bar<br/>Of fault or temper ruined the connection<br/>  (Such things, in fact, it don't ask much to mar);<br/>But Dante's Beatrice and Milton's Eve<br/>Were not drawn from their spouses, you conceive.<br/><br/>Some persons say that Dante meant Theology<br/>  By Beatrice, and not a mistress--I,<br/>Although my opinion may require apology,<br/>  Deem this a commentator's phantasy,<br/>Unless indeed it was from his own knowledge he<br/>  Decided thus, and showed good reason why;<br/>I think that Dante's more abstruse ecstatics<br/>Meant to personify the Mathematics.<br/><br/>Haid√©e and Juan were not married, but<br/>  The fault was theirs, not mine: it is not fair,<br/>Chaste reader, then, in any way to put<br/>  The blame on me, unless you wish they were;<br/>Then if you'd have them wedded, please to shut<br/>  The book which treats of this erroneous pair,<br/>Before the consequences grow too awful;<br/>'T is dangerous to read of loves unlawful.<br/><br/>Yet they were happy,--happy in the illicit<br/>  Indulgence of their innocent desires;<br/>But more imprudent grown with every visit,<br/>  Haid√©e forgot the island was her Sire's;<br/>When we have what we like 't is hard to miss it,<br/>  At least in the beginning, ere one tires;<br/>Thus she came often, not a moment losing,<br/>Whilst her piratical papa was cruising.<br/><br/>Let not his mode of raising cash seem strange,<br/>  Although he fleeced the flags of every nation,<br/>For into a Prime Minister but change<br/>  His title, and 't is nothing but taxation;<br/>But he, more modest, took an humbler range<br/>  Of Life, and in an honester vocation<br/>Pursued o'er the high seas his watery journey,<br/>And merely practised as a sea-attorney.<br/><br/>The good old gentleman had been detained<br/>  By winds and waves, and some important captures;<br/>And, in the hope of more, at sea remained,<br/>  Although a squall or two had damped his raptures,<br/>By swamping one of the prizes; he had chained<br/>  His prisoners, dividing them like chapters<br/>In numbered lots; they all had cuffs and collars,<br/>And averaged each from ten to a hundred dollars.<br/><br/>Some he disposed of off Cape Matapan,<br/>  Among his friends the Mainots; some he sold<br/>To his Tunis correspondents, save one man<br/>  Tossed overboard unsaleable (being old);<br/>The rest--save here and there some richer one,<br/>  Reserved for future ransom--in the hold,<br/>Were linked alike, as, for the common people, he<br/>Had a large order from the Dey of Tripoli.<br/><br/>The merchandise was served in the same way,<br/>  Pieced out for different marts in the Levant,<br/>Except some certain portions of the prey,<br/>  Light classic articles of female want,<br/>French stuffs, lace, tweezers, toothpicks, teapot, tray,<br/>  Guitars and castanets from Alicant,<br/>All which selected from the spoil he gathers,<br/>Robbed for his daughter by the best of fathers.<br/><br/>A monkey, a Dutch mastiff, a mackaw,<br/>  Two parrots, with a Persian cat and kittens,<br/>He chose from several animals he saw--<br/>  A terrier, too, which once had been a Briton's,<br/>Who dying on the coast of Ithaca,<br/>  The peasants gave the poor dumb thing a pittance:<br/>These to secure in this strong blowing weather,<br/>He caged in one huge hamper altogether.<br/><br/>Then, having settled his marine affairs,<br/>  Despatching single cruisers here and there,<br/>His vessel having need of some repairs,<br/>  He shaped his course to where his daughter fair<br/>Continued still her hospitable cares;<br/>  But that part of the coast being shoal and bare,<br/>And rough with reefs which ran out many a mile,<br/>His port lay on the other side o' the isle.<br/><br/>And there he went ashore without delay,<br/>  Having no custom-house nor quarantine<br/>To ask him awkward questions on the way,<br/>  About the time and place where he had been:<br/>He left his ship to be hove down next day,<br/>  With orders to the people to careen;<br/>So that all hands were busy beyond measure,<br/>In getting out goods, ballast, guns, and treasure.<br/><br/>Arriving at the summit of a hill<br/>  Which overlooked the white walls of his home,<br/>He stopped.--What singular emotions fill<br/>  Their bosoms who have been induced to roam!<br/>With fluttering doubts if all be well or ill--<br/>  With love for many, and with fears for some;<br/>All feelings which o'erleap the years long lost,<br/>And bring our hearts back to their starting-post.<br/><br/>The approach of home to husbands and to sires,<br/>  After long travelling by land or water,<br/>Most naturally some small doubt inspires--<br/>  A female family's a serious matter,<br/>(None trusts the sex more, or so much admires--<br/>  But they hate flattery, so I never flatter);<br/>Wives in their husbands' absences grow subtler,<br/>And daughters sometimes run off with the butler.<br/><br/>An honest gentleman at his return<br/>  May not have the good fortune of Ulysses;<br/>Not all lone matrons for their husbands mourn,<br/>  Or show the same dislike to suitors' kisses;<br/>The odds are that he finds a handsome urn<br/>  To his memory--and two or three young misses<br/>Born to some friend, who holds his wife and riches--<br/>And that _his_ Argus--bites him by the breeches.<br/><br/>If single, probably his plighted Fair<br/>  Has in his absence wedded some rich miser;<br/>But all the better, for the happy pair<br/>  May quarrel, and, the lady growing wiser,<br/>He may resume his amatory care<br/>  As cavalier servente, or despise her;<br/>And that his sorrow may not be a dumb one,<br/>Writes odes on the Inconstancy of Woman.<br/><br/>And oh! ye gentlemen who have already<br/>  Some chaste _liaison_ of the kind--I mean<br/>An honest friendship with a married lady--<br/>  The only thing of this sort ever seen<br/>To last--of all connections the most steady,<br/>  And the true Hymen, (the first's but a screen)--<br/>Yet, for all that, keep not too long away--<br/>I've known the absent wronged four times a day.<br/><br/>Lambro, our sea-solicitor, who had<br/>  Much less experience of dry land than Ocean,<br/>On seeing his own chimney-smoke, felt glad;<br/>  But not knowing metaphysics, had no notion<br/>Of the true reason of his not being sad,<br/>  Or that of any other strong emotion;<br/>He loved his child, and would have wept the loss of her,<br/>But knew the cause no more than a philosopher.<br/><br/>He saw his white walls shining in the sun,<br/>  His garden trees all shadowy and green;<br/>He heard his rivulet's light bubbling run,<br/>  The distant dog-bark; and perceived between<br/>The umbrage of the wood, so cool and dun,<br/>  The moving figures, and the sparkling sheen<br/>Of arms (in the East all arm)--and various dyes<br/>Of coloured garbs, as bright as butterflies.<br/><br/>And as the spot where they appear he nears,<br/>  Surprised at these unwonted signs of idling,<br/>He hears--alas! no music of the spheres,<br/>  But an unhallowed, earthly sound of fiddling!<br/>A melody which made him doubt his ears,<br/>  The cause being past his guessing or unriddling;<br/>A pipe, too, and a drum, and shortly after--<br/>A most unoriental roar of laughter.<br/><br/>And still more nearly to the place advancing,<br/>  Descending rather quickly the declivity,<br/>Through the waved branches o'er the greensward glancing,<br/>  'Midst other indications of festivity,<br/>Seeing a troop of his domestics dancing<br/>  Like Dervises, who turn as on a pivot, he<br/>Perceived it was the Pyrrhic dance so martial,<br/>To which the Levantines are very partial.<br/><br/>And further on a troop of Grecian girls,<br/>  The first and tallest her white kerchief waving,<br/>Were strung together like a row of pearls,<br/>  Linked hand in hand, and dancing; each too having<br/>Down her white neck long floating auburn curls--<br/>  (The least of which would set ten poets raving);<br/>Their leader sang--and bounded to her song<br/>With choral step and voice the virgin throng.<br/><br/>And here, assembled cross-legged round their trays,<br/>  Small social parties just begun to dine;<br/>Pilaus and meats of all sorts met the gaze,<br/>  And flasks of Samian and of Chian wine,<br/>And sherbet cooling in the porous vase;<br/>  Above them their dessert grew on its vine;--<br/>The orange and pomegranate nodding o'er,<br/>Dropped in their laps, scarce plucked, their mellow store.<br/><br/>A band of children, round a snow-white ram,<br/>  There wreathe his venerable horns with flowers;<br/>While peaceful as if still an unweaned lamb,<br/>  The patriarch of the flock all gently cowers<br/>His sober head, majestically tame,<br/>  Or eats from out the palm, or playful lowers<br/>His brow, as if in act to butt, and then<br/>Yielding to their small hands, draws back again.<br/><br/>Their classical profiles, and glittering dresses,<br/>  Their large black eyes, and soft seraphic cheeks,<br/>Crimson as cleft pomegranates, their long tresses,<br/>  The gesture which enchants, the eye that speaks,<br/>The innocence which happy childhood blesses,<br/>  Made quite a picture of these little Greeks;<br/>So that the philosophical beholder<br/>Sighed for their sakes--that they should e'er grow older.<br/><br/>Afar, a dwarf buffoon stood telling tales<br/>  To a sedate grey circle of old smokers,<br/>Of secret treasures found in hidden vales,<br/>  Of wonderful replies from Arab jokers,<br/>Of charms to make good gold and cure bad ails,<br/>  Of rocks bewitched that open to the knockers,<br/>Of magic ladies who, by one sole act,<br/>Transformed their lords to beasts (but that's a fact).<br/><br/>Here was no lack of innocent diversion<br/>  For the imagination or the senses,<br/>Song, dance, wine, music, stories from the Persian,<br/>  All pretty pastimes in which no offence is;<br/>But Lambro saw all these things with aversion,<br/>  Perceiving in his absence such expenses,<br/>Dreading that climax of all human ills,<br/>The inflammation of his weekly bills.<br/><br/>Ah! what is man? what perils still environ<br/>  The happiest mortals even after dinner!<br/>A day of gold from out an age of iron<br/>  Is all that Life allows the luckiest sinner;<br/>Pleasure (whene'er she sings, at least) 's a Siren,<br/>  That lures, to flay alive, the young beginner;<br/>Lambro's reception at his people's banquet<br/>Was such as fire accords to a wet blanket.<br/><br/>He--being a man who seldom used a word<br/>  Too much, and wishing gladly to surprise<br/>(In general he surprised men with the sword)<br/>  His daughter--had not sent before to advise<br/>Of his arrival, so that no one stirred;<br/>  And long he paused to re-assure his eyes,<br/>In fact much more astonished than delighted,<br/>To find so much good company invited.<br/><br/>He did not know (alas! how men will lie)<br/>  That a report (especially the Greeks)<br/>Avouched his death (such people never die),<br/>  And put his house in mourning several weeks,--<br/>But now their eyes and also lips were dry;<br/>  The bloom, too, had returned to Haid√©e's cheeks:<br/>Her tears, too, being returned into their fount,<br/>She now kept house upon her own account.<br/><br/>Hence all this rice, meat, dancing, wine, and fiddling,<br/>  Which turned the isle into a place of pleasure;<br/>The servants all were getting drunk or idling,<br/>  A life which made them happy beyond measure.<br/>Her father's hospitality seemed middling,<br/>  Compared with what Haid√©e did with his treasure;<br/>'T was wonderful how things went on improving,<br/>While she had not one hour to spare from loving.<br/><br/>Perhaps you think, in stumbling on this feast,<br/>  He flew into a passion, and in fact<br/>There was no mighty reason to be pleased;<br/>  Perhaps you prophesy some sudden act,<br/>The whip, the rack, or dungeon at the least,<br/>  To teach his people to be more exact,<br/>And that, proceeding at a very high rate,<br/>He showed the royal _penchants_ of a pirate.<br/><br/>You're wrong.--He was the mildest mannered man<br/>  That ever scuttled ship or cut a throat;<br/>With such true breeding of a gentleman,<br/>  You never could divine his real thought;<br/>No courtier could, and scarcely woman can<br/>  Gird more deceit within a petticoat;<br/>Pity he loved adventurous life's variety,<br/>He was so great a loss to good society.<br/><br/>Advancing to the nearest dinner tray,<br/>  Tapping the shoulder of the nighest guest,<br/>With a peculiar smile, which, by the way,<br/>  Boded no good, whatever it expressed,<br/>He asked the meaning of this holiday;<br/>  The vinous Greek to whom he had addressed<br/>His question, much too merry to divine<br/>The questioner, filled up a glass of wine,<br/><br/>And without turning his facetious head,<br/>  Over his shoulder, with a Bacchant air,<br/>Presented the o'erflowing cup, and said,<br/>  "Talking's dry work, I have no time to spare."<br/>A second hiccuped, "Our old Master's dead,<br/>  You'd better ask our Mistress who's his heir."<br/>"Our Mistress!" quoth a third: "Our Mistress!--pooh!--<br/>You mean our Master--not the old, but new."<br/><br/>These rascals, being new comers, knew not whom<br/>  They thus addressed--and Lambro's visage fell--<br/>And o'er his eye a momentary gloom<br/>  Passed, but he strove quite courteously to quell<br/>The expression, and endeavouring to resume<br/>  His smile, requested one of them to tell<br/>The name and quality of his new patron,<br/>Who seemed to have turned Haid√©e into a matron.<br/><br/>"I know not," quoth the fellow, "who or what<br/>  He is, nor whence he came--and little care;<br/>But this I know, that this roast capon's fat,<br/>  And that good wine ne'er washed down better fare;<br/>And if you are not satisfied with that,<br/>  Direct your questions to my neighbour there;<br/>He'll answer all for better or for worse,<br/>For none likes more to hear himself converse."<br/><br/>I said that Lambro was a man of patience,<br/>  And certainly he showed the best of breeding,<br/>Which scarce even France, the Paragon of nations,<br/>  E'er saw her most polite of sons exceeding;<br/>He bore these sneers against his near relations,<br/>  His own anxiety, his heart, too, bleeding,<br/>The insults, too, of every servile glutton,<br/>Who all the time was eating up his mutton.<br/><br/>Now in a person used to much command--<br/>  To bid men come, and go, and come again--<br/>To see his orders done, too, out of hand--<br/>  Whether the word was death, or but the chain--<br/>It may seem strange to find his manners bland;<br/>  Yet such things are, which I cannot explain,<br/>Though, doubtless, he who can command himself<br/>Is good to govern--almost as a Guelf.<br/><br/>Not that he was not sometimes rash or so,<br/>  But never in his real and serious mood;<br/>Then calm, concentrated, and still, and slow,<br/>  He lay coiled like the Boa in the wood;<br/>With him it never was a word and blow,<br/>  His angry word once o'er, he shed no blood,<br/>But in his silence there was much to rue,<br/>And his _one_ blow left little work for _two_.<br/><br/>He asked no further questions, and proceeded<br/>  On to the house, but by a private way,<br/>So that the few who met him hardly heeded,<br/>  So little they expected him that day;<br/>If love paternal in his bosom pleaded<br/>  For Haid√©e's sake, is more than I can say,<br/>But certainly to one deemed dead returning,<br/>This revel seemed a curious mode of mourning.<br/><br/>If all the dead could now return to life,<br/>  (Which God forbid!) or some, or a great many,<br/>For instance, if a husband or his wife<br/>  (Nuptial examples are as good as any),<br/>No doubt whate'er might be their former strife,<br/>  The present weather would be much more rainy--<br/>Tears shed into the grave of the connection<br/>Would share most probably its resurrection.<br/><br/>He entered in the house no more his home,<br/>  A thing to human feelings the most trying,<br/>And harder for the heart to overcome,<br/>  Perhaps, than even the mental pangs of dying;<br/>To find our hearthstone turned into a tomb,<br/>  And round its once warm precincts palely lying<br/>The ashes of our hopes, is a deep grief,<br/>Beyond a _single gentleman's_ belief.<br/><br/>He entered in the house--his home no more,<br/>  For without hearts there is no home;--and felt<br/>The solitude of passing his own door<br/>  Without a welcome: _there_ he long had dwelt,<br/>There his few peaceful days Time had swept o'er,<br/>  There his worn bosom and keen eye would melt<br/>Over the innocence of that sweet child,<br/>His only shrine of feelings undefiled.<br/><br/>He was a man of a strange temperament,<br/>  Of mild demeanour though of savage mood,<br/>Moderate in all his habits, and content<br/>  With temperance in pleasure, as in food,<br/>Quick to perceive, and strong to bear, and meant<br/>  For something better, if not wholly good;<br/>His Country's wrongs and his despair to save her<br/>Had stung him from a slave to an enslaver.<br/><br/>The love of power, and rapid gain of gold,<br/>  The hardness by long habitude produced,<br/>The dangerous life in which he had grown old,<br/>  The mercy he had granted oft abused,<br/>The sights he was accustomed to behold,<br/>  The wild seas, and wild men with whom he cruised,<br/>Had cost his enemies a long repentance,<br/>And made him a good friend, but bad acquaintance.<br/><br/>But something of the spirit of old Greece<br/>  Flashed o'er his soul a few heroic rays,<br/>Such as lit onward to the Golden Fleece<br/>  His predecessors in the Colchian days;<br/>'T is true he had no ardent love for peace--<br/>  Alas! his country showed no path to praise:<br/>Hate to the world and war with every nation<br/>He waged, in vengeance of her degradation.<br/><br/>Still o'er his mind the influence of the clime<br/>  Shed its Ionian elegance, which showed<br/>Its power unconsciously full many a time,--<br/>  A taste seen in the choice of his abode,<br/>A love of music and of scenes sublime,<br/>  A pleasure in the gentle stream that flowed<br/>Past him in crystal, and a joy in flowers,<br/>Bedewed his spirit in his calmer hours.<br/><br/>But whatsoe'er he had of love reposed<br/>  On that belov√©d daughter; she had been<br/>The only thing which kept his heart unclosed<br/>  Amidst the savage deeds he had done and seen,<br/>A lonely pure affection unopposed:<br/>  There wanted but the loss of this to wean<br/>His feelings from all milk of human kindness,<br/>And turn him like the Cyclops mad with blindness.<br/><br/>The cubless tigress in her jungle raging<br/>  Is dreadful to the shepherd and the flock;<br/>The Ocean when its yeasty war is waging<br/>  Is awful to the vessel near the rock;<br/>But violent things will sooner bear assuaging,<br/>  Their fury being spent by its own shock,<br/>Than the stern, single, deep, and wordless ire<br/>Of a strong human heart, and in a Sire.<br/><br/>It is a hard although a common case<br/>  To find our children running restive--they<br/>In whom our brightest days we would retrace,<br/>  Our little selves re-formed in finer clay,<br/>Just as old age is creeping on apace,<br/>  And clouds come o'er the sunset of our day,<br/>They kindly leave us, though not quite alone,<br/>But in good company--the gout or stone.<br/><br/>Yet a fine family is a fine thing<br/>  (Provided they don't come in after dinner);<br/>'T is beautiful to see a matron bring<br/>  Her children up (if nursing them don't thin her);<br/>Like cherubs round an altar-piece they cling<br/>  To the fire-side (a sight to touch a sinner).<br/>A lady with her daughters or her nieces<br/>Shine like a guinea and seven-shilling pieces.<br/><br/>Old Lambro passed unseen a private gate,<br/>  And stood within his hall at eventide;<br/>Meantime the lady and her lover sate<br/>  At wassail in their beauty and their pride:<br/>An ivory inlaid table spread with state<br/>  Before them, and fair slaves on every side;<br/>Gems, gold, and silver, formed the service mostly,<br/>Mother of pearl and coral the less costly.<br/><br/>The dinner made about a hundred dishes;<br/>  Lamb and pistachio nuts--in short, all meats,<br/>And saffron soups, and sweetbreads; and the fishes<br/>  Were of the finest that e'er flounced in nets,<br/>Dressed to a Sybarite's most pampered wishes;<br/>  The beverage was various sherbets<br/>Of raisin, orange, and pomegranate juice,<br/>Squeezed through the rind, which makes it best for use.<br/><br/>These were ranged round, each in its crystal ewer,<br/>  And fruits, and date-bread loaves closed the repast,<br/>And Mocha's berry, from Arabia pure,<br/>  In small fine China cups, came in at last;<br/>Gold cups of filigree, made to secure<br/>  The hand from burning, underneath them placed;<br/>Cloves, cinnamon, and saffron too were boiled<br/>Up with the coffee, which (I think) they spoiled.<br/><br/>The hangings of the room were tapestry, made<br/>  Of velvet panels, each of different hue,<br/>And thick with damask flowers of silk inlaid;<br/>  And round them ran a yellow border too;<br/>The upper border, richly wrought, displayed,<br/>  Embroidered delicately o'er with blue,<br/>Soft Persian sentences, in lilac letters,<br/>From poets, or the moralists their betters.<br/><br/>These Oriental writings on the wall,<br/>  Quite common in those countries, are a kind<br/>Of monitors adapted to recall,<br/>  Like skulls at Memphian banquets, to the mind,<br/>The words which shook Belshazzar in his hall,<br/>  And took his kingdom from him: You will find,<br/>Though sages may pour out their wisdom's treasure,<br/>There is no sterner moralist than Pleasure.<br/><br/>A Beauty at the season's close grown hectic,<br/>  A Genius who has drunk himself to death,<br/>A Rake turned methodistic, or Eclectic--<br/>  (For that's the name they like to pray beneath)--<br/>But most, an Alderman struck apoplectic,<br/>  Are things that really take away the breath,--<br/>And show that late hours, wine, and love are able<br/>To do not much less damage than the table.<br/><br/>Haid√©e and Juan carpeted their feet<br/>  On crimson satin, bordered with pale blue;<br/>Their sofa occupied three parts complete<br/>  Of the apartment--and appeared quite new;<br/>The velvet cushions (for a throne more meet)<br/>  Were scarlet, from whose glowing centre grew<br/>A sun embossed in gold, whose rays of tissue,<br/>Meridian-like, were seen all light to issue.<br/><br/>Crystal and marble, plate and porcelain,<br/>  Had done their work of splendour; Indian mats<br/>And Persian carpets, which the heart bled to stain,<br/>  Over the floors were spread; gazelles and cats,<br/>And dwarfs and blacks, and such like things, that gain<br/>  Their bread as ministers and favourites (that's<br/>To say, by degradation) mingled there<br/>As plentiful as in a court, or fair.<br/><br/>There was no want of lofty mirrors, and<br/>  The tables, most of ebony inlaid<br/>With mother of pearl or ivory, stood at hand,<br/>  Or were of tortoise-shell or rare woods made,<br/>Fretted with gold or silver:--by command<br/>  The greater part of these were ready spread<br/>With viands and sherbets in ice--and wine--<br/>Kept for all comers at all hours to dine.<br/><br/>Of all the dresses I select Haid√©e's:<br/>  She wore two jelicks--one was of pale yellow;<br/>Of azure, pink, and white was her chemise--<br/>  'Neath which her breast heaved like a little billow:<br/>With buttons formed of pearls as large as peas,<br/>  All gold and crimson shone her jelick's fellow,<br/>And the striped white gauze baracan that bound her,<br/>Like fleecy clouds about the moon, flowed round her.<br/><br/>One large gold bracelet clasped each lovely arm,<br/>  Lockless--so pliable from the pure gold<br/>That the hand stretched and shut it without harm,<br/>  The limb which it adorned its only mould;<br/>So beautiful--its very shape would charm,<br/>  And clinging, as if loath to lose its hold,<br/>The purest ore enclosed the whitest skin<br/>That e'er by precious metal was held in.<br/><br/>Around, as Princess of her father's land,<br/>  A like gold bar above her instep rolled<br/>Announced her rank; twelve rings were on her hand;<br/>  Her hair was starred with gems; her veil's fine fold<br/>Below her breast was fastened with a band<br/>  Of lavish pearls, whose worth could scarce be told;<br/>Her orange silk full Turkish trousers furled<br/>About the prettiest ankle in the world.<br/><br/>Her hair's long auburn waves down to her heel<br/>  Flowed like an Alpine torrent which the sun<br/>Dyes with his morning light,--and would conceal<br/>  Her person if allowed at large to run,<br/>And still they seemed resentfully to feel<br/>  The silken fillet's curb, and sought to shun<br/>Their bonds whene'er some Zephyr caught began<br/>To offer his young pinion as her fan.<br/><br/>Round her she made an atmosphere of life,<br/>  The very air seemed lighter from her eyes,<br/>They were so soft and beautiful, and rife<br/>  With all we can imagine of the skies,<br/>And pure as Psyche ere she grew a wife--<br/>  Too pure even for the purest human ties;<br/>Her overpowering presence made you feel<br/>It would not be idolatry to kneel.<br/><br/>Her eyelashes, though dark as night, were tinged<br/>  (It is the country's custom, but in vain),<br/>For those large black eyes were so blackly fringed,<br/>  The glossy rebels mocked the jetty stain,<br/>And in their native beauty stood avenged:<br/>  Her nails were touched with henna; but, again,<br/>The power of Art was turned to nothing, for<br/>They could not look more rosy than before.<br/><br/>The henna should be deeply dyed to make<br/>  The skin relieved appear more fairly fair;<br/>She had no need of this, day ne'er will break<br/>  On mountain tops more heavenly white than her:<br/>The eye might doubt if it were well awake,<br/>  She was so like a vision; I might err,<br/>But Shakespeare also says, 't is very silly<br/>"To gild refin√©d gold, or paint the lily."<br/><br/>Juan had on a shawl of black and gold,<br/>  But a white baracan, and so transparent<br/>The sparkling gems beneath you might behold,<br/>  Like small stars through the milky way apparent;<br/>His turban, furled in many a graceful fold,<br/>  An emerald aigrette, with Haid√©e's hair in 't,<br/>Surmounted as its clasp--a glowing crescent,<br/>Whose rays shone ever trembling, but incessant.<br/><br/>And now they were diverted by their suite,<br/>  Dwarfs, dancing girls, black eunuchs, and a poet,<br/>Which made their new establishment complete;<br/>  The last was of great fame, and liked to show it;<br/>His verses rarely wanted their due feet--<br/>  And for his theme--he seldom sung below it,<br/>He being paid to satirise or flatter,<br/>As the Psalm says, "inditing a good matter."<br/><br/>He praised the present, and abused the past,<br/>  Reversing the good custom of old days,<br/>An Eastern anti-jacobin at last<br/>  He turned, preferring pudding to _no_ praise--<br/>For some few years his lot had been o'ercast<br/>  By his seeming independent in his lays,<br/>But now he sung the Sultan and the Pacha--<br/>With truth like Southey, and with verse like Crashaw.<br/><br/>He was a man who had seen many changes,<br/>  And always changed as true as any needle;<br/>His Polar Star being one which rather ranges,<br/>  And not the fixed--he knew the way to wheedle:<br/>So vile he 'scaped the doom which oft avenges;<br/>  And being fluent (save indeed when fee'd ill),<br/>He lied with such a fervour of intention--<br/>There was no doubt he earned his laureate pension.<br/><br/>But _he_ had genius,--when a turncoat has it,<br/>  The _Vates irritabilis_ takes care<br/>That without notice few full moons shall pass it;<br/>  Even good men like to make the public stare:--<br/>But to my subject--let me see--what was it?--<br/>  Oh!--the third canto--and the pretty pair--<br/>Their loves, and feasts, and house, and dress, and mode<br/>Of living in their insular abode.<br/><br/>Their poet, a sad trimmer, but, no less,<br/>  In company a very pleasant fellow,<br/>Had been the favourite of full many a mess<br/>  Of men, and made them speeches when half mellow;<br/>And though his meaning they could rarely guess,<br/>  Yet still they deigned to hiccup or to bellow<br/>The glorious meed of popular applause,<br/>Of which the first ne'er knows the second cause.<br/><br/>But now being lifted into high society,<br/>  And having picked up several odds and ends<br/>Of free thoughts in his travels for variety,<br/>  He deemed, being in a lone isle, among friends,<br/>That, without any danger of a riot, he<br/>  Might for long lying make himself amends;<br/>And, singing as he sung in his warm youth,<br/>Agree to a short armistice with Truth.<br/><br/>He had travelled 'mongst the Arabs, Turks, and Franks,<br/>  And knew the self-loves of the different nations;<br/>And having lived with people of all ranks,<br/>  Had something ready upon most occasions--<br/>Which got him a few presents and some thanks.<br/>  He varied with some skill his adulations;<br/>To "do at Rome as Romans do," a piece<br/>Of conduct was which _he_ observed in Greece.<br/><br/>Thus, usually, when _he_ was asked to sing,<br/>  He gave the different nations something national;<br/>'T was all the same to him--"God save the King,"<br/>  Or "√áa ira," according to the fashion all:<br/>His Muse made increment of anything,<br/>  From the high lyric down to the low rational;<br/>If Pindar sang horse-races, what should hinder<br/>Himself from being as pliable as Pindar?<br/><br/>In France, for instance, he would write a chanson;<br/>  In England a six canto quarto tale;<br/>In Spain he'd make a ballad or romance on<br/>  The last war--much the same in Portugal;<br/>In Germany, the Pegasus he'd prance on<br/>  Would be old Goethe's--(see what says De Sta√´l);<br/>In Italy he'd ape the "Trecentisti;"<br/>In Greece, he'd sing some sort of hymn like this t' ye:<br/><br/>The Isles of Greece, the Isles of Greece!<br/>  Where burning Sappho loved and sung,<br/>Where grew the arts of War and Peace,<br/>  Where Delos rose, and Phoebus sprung!<br/>Eternal summer gilds them yet,<br/>But all, except their Sun, is set.<br/><br/>The Scian and the Teian muse,<br/>  The Hero's harp, the Lover's lute,<br/>Have found the fame your shores refuse:<br/>  Their place of birth alone is mute<br/>To sounds which echo further west<br/>Than your Sires' "Islands of the Blest."<br/><br/>The mountains look on Marathon--<br/>  And Marathon looks on the sea;<br/>And musing there an hour alone,<br/>  I dreamed that Greece might still be free;<br/>For standing on the Persians' grave,<br/>I could not deem myself a slave.<br/><br/>A King sate on the rocky brow<br/>  Which looks o'er sea-born Salamis;<br/>And ships, by thousands, lay below,<br/>  And men in nations;--all were his!<br/>He counted them at break of day--<br/>And, when the Sun set, where were they?<br/><br/>And where are they? and where art thou,<br/>  My Country? On thy voiceless shore<br/>The heroic lay is tuneless now--<br/>  The heroic bosom beats no more!<br/>And must thy Lyre, so long divine,<br/>Degenerate into hands like mine?<br/><br/>'T is something, in the dearth of Fame,<br/>  Though linked among a fettered race,<br/>To feel at least a patriot's shame,<br/>  Even as I sing, suffuse my face;<br/>For what is left the poet here?<br/>For Greeks a blush--for Greece a tear.<br/><br/>Must _we_ but weep o'er days more blest?<br/>  Must _we_ but blush?--Our fathers bled.<br/>Earth! render back from out thy breast<br/>  A remnant of our Spartan dead!<br/>Of the three hundred grant but three,<br/>To make a new Thermopyl√¶!<br/><br/>What, silent still? and silent all?<br/>  Ah! no;--the voices of the dead<br/>Sound like a distant torrent's fall,<br/>  And answer, "Let one living head,<br/>But one arise,--we come, we come!"<br/>'T is but the living who are dumb.<br/><br/>In vain--in vain: strike other chords;<br/>  Fill high the cup with Samian wine!<br/>Leave battles to the Turkish hordes,<br/>  And shed the blood of Scio's vine!<br/>Hark! rising to the ignoble call--<br/>How answers each bold Bacchanal!<br/><br/>You have the Pyrrhic dance as yet,<br/>  Where is the Pyrrhic phalanx gone?<br/>Of two such lessons, why forget<br/>  The nobler and the manlier one?<br/>You have the letters Cadmus gave--<br/>Think ye he meant them for a slave?<br/><br/>Fill high the bowl with Samian wine!<br/>  We will not think of themes like these!<br/>It made Anacreon's song divine:<br/>  He served--but served Polycrates--<br/>A Tyrant; but our masters then<br/>Were still, at least, our countrymen.<br/><br/>The Tyrant of the Chersonese<br/>  Was Freedom's best and bravest friend;<br/>_That_ tyrant was Miltiades!<br/>  Oh! that the present hour would lend<br/>Another despot of the kind!<br/>Such chains as his were sure to bind.<br/><br/>Fill high the bowl with Samian wine!<br/>  On Suli's rock, and Parga's shore,<br/>Exists the remnant of a line<br/>  Such as the Doric mothers bore;<br/>And there, perhaps, some seed is sown,<br/>The Heracleidan blood might own.<br/><br/>Trust not for freedom to the Franks--<br/>  They have a king who buys and sells;<br/>In native swords, and native ranks,<br/>  The only hope of courage dwells;<br/>But Turkish force, and Latin fraud,<br/>Would break your shield, however broad.<br/><br/>Fill high the bowl with Samian wine!<br/>  Our virgins dance beneath the shade--<br/>I see their glorious black eyes shine;<br/>  But gazing on each glowing maid,<br/>My own the burning tear-drop laves,<br/>To think such breasts must suckle slaves.<br/><br/>Place me on Sunium's marbled steep,<br/>  Where nothing, save the waves and I,<br/>May hear our mutual murmurs sweep;<br/>  There, swan-like, let me sing and die:<br/>A land of slaves shall ne'er be mine--<br/>Dash down yon cup of Samian wine!<br/><br/>Thus sung, or would, or could, or should have sung,<br/>  The modern Greek, in tolerable verse;<br/>If not like Orpheus quite, when Greece was young,<br/>  Yet in these times he might have done much worse:<br/>His strain displayed some feeling--right or wrong;<br/>  And feeling, in a poet, is the source<br/>Of others' feeling; but they are such liars,<br/>And take all colours--like the hands of dyers.<br/><br/>But words are things, and a small drop of ink,<br/>  Falling like dew, upon a thought, produces<br/>That which makes thousands, perhaps millions, think;<br/>  'T is strange, the shortest letter which man uses<br/>Instead of speech, may form a lasting link<br/>  Of ages; to what straits old Time reduces<br/>Frail man, when paper--even a rag like this,<br/>Survives himself, his tomb, and all that's his!<br/><br/>And when his bones are dust, his grave a blank,<br/>  His station, generation, even his nation,<br/>Become a thing, or nothing, save to rank<br/>  In chronological commemoration,<br/>Some dull MS. Oblivion long has sank,<br/>  Or graven stone found in a barrack's station<br/>In digging the foundation of a closet,<br/>May turn his name up, as a rare deposit.<br/><br/>And Glory long has made the sages smile;<br/>  'T is something, nothing, words, illusion, wind--<br/>Depending more upon the historian's style<br/>  Than on the name a person leaves behind:<br/>Troy owes to Homer what whist owes to Hoyle:<br/>  The present century was growing blind<br/>To the great Marlborough's skill in giving knocks,<br/>Until his late Life by Archdeacon Coxe.<br/><br/>Milton's the Prince of poets--so we say;<br/>  A little heavy, but no less divine:<br/>An independent being in his day--<br/>  Learned, pious, temperate in love and wine;<br/>But, his life falling into Johnson's way,<br/>  We're told this great High Priest of all the Nine<br/>Was whipped at college--a harsh sire--odd spouse,<br/>For the first Mrs. Milton left his house.<br/><br/>All these are, _certes_, entertaining facts,<br/>  Like Shakespeare's stealing deer, Lord Bacon's bribes;<br/>Like Titus' youth, and C√¶sar's earliest acts;<br/>  Like Burns (whom Doctor Currie well describes);<br/>Like Cromwell's pranks;--but although Truth exacts<br/>  These amiable descriptions from the scribes,<br/>As most essential to their Hero's story,<br/>They do not much contribute to his glory.<br/><br/>All are not moralists, like Southey, when<br/>  He prated to the world of "Pantisocracy;"<br/>Or Wordsworth unexcised, unhired, who then<br/>  Seasoned his pedlar poems with Democracy;<br/>Or Coleridge long before his flighty pen<br/>  Let to the Morning Post its aristocracy;<br/>When he and Southey, following the same path,<br/>Espoused two partners (milliners of Bath).<br/><br/>Such names at present cut a convict figure,<br/>  The very Botany Bay in moral geography;<br/>Their loyal treason, renegado rigour,<br/>  Are good manure for their more bare biography;<br/>Wordsworth's last quarto, by the way, is bigger<br/>  Than any since the birthday of typography;<br/>A drowsy, frowzy poem, called the "Excursion,"<br/>Writ in a manner which is my aversion.<br/><br/>He there builds up a formidable dyke<br/>  Between his own and others' intellect;<br/>But Wordsworth's poem, and his followers, like<br/>  Joanna Southcote's Shiloh and her sect,<br/>Are things which in this century don't strike<br/>  The public mind,--so few are the elect;<br/>And the new births of both their stale Virginities<br/>Have proved but Dropsies, taken for Divinities.<br/><br/>But let me to my story: I must own,<br/>  If I have any fault, it is digression,<br/>Leaving my people to proceed alone,<br/>  While I soliloquize beyond expression:<br/>But these are my addresses from the throne,<br/>  Which put off business to the ensuing session:<br/>Forgetting each omission is a loss to<br/>The world, not quite so great as Ariosto.<br/><br/>I know that what our neighbours call _"longueurs,"_<br/>  (We've not so good a _word_, but have the _thing_,<br/>In that complete perfection which insures<br/>  An epic from Bob Southey every spring--)<br/>Form not the true temptation which allures<br/>  The reader; but 't would not be hard to bring<br/>Some fine examples of the _Epop√©e_,<br/>To prove its grand ingredient is _Ennui_.<br/><br/>We learn from Horace, "Homer sometimes sleeps;"<br/>  We feel without him,--Wordsworth sometimes wakes,--<br/>To show with what complacency he creeps,<br/>  With his dear "_Waggoners_," around his lakes.<br/>He wishes for "a boat" to sail the deeps--<br/>  Of Ocean?--No, of air; and then he makes<br/>Another outcry for "a little boat,"<br/>And drivels seas to set it well afloat.<br/><br/>If he must fain sweep o'er the ethereal plain,<br/>  And Pegasus runs restive in his "Waggon,"<br/>Could he not beg the loan of Charles's Wain?<br/>  Or pray Medea for a single dragon?<br/>Or if, too classic for his vulgar brain,<br/>  He feared his neck to venture such a nag on,<br/>And he must needs mount nearer to the moon,<br/>Could not the blockhead ask for a balloon?<br/><br/>"Pedlars," and "Boats," and "Waggons!" Oh! ye shades<br/>  Of Pope and Dryden, are we come to this?<br/>That trash of such sort not alone evades<br/>  Contempt, but from the bathos' vast abyss<br/>Floats scumlike uppermost, and these Jack Cades<br/>  Of sense and song above your graves may hiss--<br/>The "little boatman" and his _Peter Bell_<br/>Can sneer at him who drew "Achitophel!"<br/><br/>T' our tale.--The feast was over, the slaves gone,<br/>  The dwarfs and dancing girls had all retired;<br/>The Arab lore and Poet's song were done,<br/>  And every sound of revelry expired;<br/>The lady and her lover, left alone,<br/>  The rosy flood of Twilight's sky admired;--<br/>Ave Maria! o'er the earth and sea,<br/>That heavenliest hour of Heaven is worthiest thee!<br/><br/>Ave Maria! bless√©d be the hour!<br/>  The time, the clime, the spot, where I so oft<br/>Have felt that moment in its fullest power<br/>  Sink o'er the earth--so beautiful and soft--<br/>While swung the deep bell in the distant tower,<br/>  Or the faint dying day-hymn stole aloft,<br/>And not a breath crept through the rosy air,<br/>And yet the forest leaves seemed stirred with prayer.<br/><br/>Ave Maria! 't is the hour of prayer!<br/>  Ave Maria! 't is the hour of Love!<br/>Ave Maria! may our spirits dare<br/>  Look up to thine and to thy Son's above!<br/>Ave Maria! oh that face so fair!<br/>  Those downcast eyes beneath the Almighty Dove--<br/>What though 't is but a pictured image?--strike--<br/>That painting is no idol,--'t is too like.<br/><br/>Some kinder casuists are pleased to say,<br/>  In nameless print--that I have no devotion;<br/>But set those persons down with me to pray,<br/>  And you shall see who has the properest notion<br/>Of getting into Heaven the shortest way;<br/>  My altars are the mountains and the Ocean,<br/>Earth--air--stars,--all that springs from the great Whole,<br/>Who hath produced, and will receive the Soul.<br/><br/>Sweet Hour of Twilight!--in the solitude<br/>  Of the pine forest, and the silent shore<br/>Which bounds Ravenna's immemorial wood,<br/>  Rooted where once the Adrian wave flowed o'er,<br/>To where the last C√¶sarean fortress stood,<br/>  Evergreen forest! which Boccaccio's lore<br/>And Dryden's lay made haunted ground to me,<br/>How have I loved the twilight hour and thee!<br/><br/>The shrill cicalas, people of the pine,<br/>  Making their summer lives one ceaseless song,<br/>Were the sole echoes, save my steed's and mine,<br/>  And Vesper bell's that rose the boughs along;<br/>The spectre huntsman of Onesti's line,<br/>  His hell-dogs, and their chase, and the fair throng<br/>Which learned from this example not to fly<br/>From a true lover,--shadowed my mind's eye.<br/><br/>Oh, Hesperus! thou bringest all good things--<br/>  Home to the weary, to the hungry cheer,<br/>To the young bird the parent's brooding wings,<br/>  The welcome stall to the o'erlaboured steer;<br/>Whate'er of peace about our hearthstone clings,<br/>  Whate'er our household gods protect of dear,<br/>Are gathered round us by thy look of rest;<br/>Thou bring'st the child, too, to the mother's breast.<br/><br/>Soft Hour! which wakes the wish and melts the heart<br/>  Of those who sail the seas, on the first day<br/>When they from their sweet friends are torn apart;<br/>  Or fills with love the pilgrim on his way<br/>As the far bell of Vesper makes him start,<br/>  Seeming to weep the dying day's decay;<br/>Is this a fancy which our reason scorns?<br/>Ah! surely Nothing dies but Something mourns!<br/><br/>When Nero perished by the justest doom<br/>  Which ever the Destroyer yet destroyed,<br/>Amidst the roar of liberated Rome,<br/>  Of nations freed, and the world overjoyed,<br/>Some hands unseen strewed flowers upon his tomb:<br/>  Perhaps the weakness of a heart not void<br/>Of feeling for some kindness done, when Power<br/>Had left the wretch an uncorrupted hour.<br/><br/>But I'm digressing; what on earth has Nero,<br/>  Or any such like sovereign buffoons,<br/>To do with the transactions of my hero,<br/>  More than such madmen's fellow man--the moon's?<br/>Sure my invention must be down at zero,<br/>  And I grown one of many "Wooden Spoons"<br/>Of verse, (the name with which we Cantabs please<br/>To dub the last of honours in degrees).<br/><br/>I feel this tediousness will never do--<br/>  T' is being _too_ epic, and I must cut down<br/>(In copying) this long canto into two;<br/>  They'll never find it out, unless I own<br/>The fact, excepting some experienced few;<br/>  And then as an improvement 't will be shown:<br/>I'll prove that such the opinion of the critic is<br/>From Aristotle _passim_.--See Œ†ŒüŒôŒóŒ§ŒôŒöŒóŒ£.<br/><br/>CANTO THE FOURTH.<br/><br/>NOTHING so difficult as a beginning<br/>  In poesy, unless perhaps the end;<br/>For oftentimes when Pegasus seems winning<br/>  The race, he sprains a wing, and down we tend,<br/>Like Lucifer when hurled from Heaven for sinning;<br/>  Our sin the same, and hard as his to mend,<br/>Being Pride, which leads the mind to soar too far,<br/>Till our own weakness shows us what we are.<br/><br/>But Time, which brings all beings to their level,<br/>  And sharp Adversity, will teach at last<br/>Man,--and, as we would hope,--perhaps the Devil,<br/>  That neither of their intellects are vast:<br/>While Youth's hot wishes in our red veins revel,<br/>  We know not this--the blood flows on too fast;<br/>But as the torrent widens towards the Ocean,<br/>We ponder deeply on each past emotion.<br/><br/>As boy, I thought myself a clever fellow,<br/>  And wished that others held the same opinion;<br/>They took it up when my days grew more mellow,<br/>  And other minds acknowledged my dominion:<br/>Now my sere Fancy "falls into the yellow<br/>  Leaf," and Imagination droops her pinion,<br/>And the sad truth which hovers o'er my desk<br/>Turns what was once romantic to burlesque.<br/><br/>And if I laugh at any mortal thing,<br/>  'T is that I may not weep; and if I weep,<br/>'T is that our nature cannot always bring<br/>  Itself to apathy, for we must steep<br/>Our hearts first in the depths of Lethe's spring,<br/>  Ere what we least wish to behold will sleep:<br/>Thetis baptized her mortal son in Styx;<br/>A mortal mother would on Lethe fix.<br/><br/>Some have accused me of a strange design<br/>  Against the creed and morals of the land,<br/>And trace it in this poem every line:<br/>  I don't pretend that I quite understand<br/>My own meaning when I would be _very_ fine;<br/>  But the fact is that I have nothing planned,<br/>Unless it were to be a moment merry--<br/>A novel word in my vocabulary.<br/><br/>To the kind reader of our sober clime<br/>  This way of writing will appear exotic;<br/>Pulci was sire of the half-serious rhyme,<br/>  Who sang when Chivalry was more quixotic,<br/>And revelled in the fancies of the time,<br/>  True Knights, chaste Dames, huge Giants, Kings despotic;<br/>But all these, save the last, being obsolete,<br/>I chose a modern subject as more meet.<br/><br/>How I have treated it, I do not know;<br/>  Perhaps no better than _they_ have treated me,<br/>Who have imputed such designs as show<br/>  Not what they saw, but what they wished to see:<br/>But if it gives them pleasure, be it so;<br/>  This is a liberal age, and thoughts are free:<br/>Meantime Apollo plucks me by the ear,<br/>And tells me to resume my story here.<br/><br/>Young Juan and his lady-love were left<br/>  To their own hearts' most sweet society;<br/>Even Time the pitiless in sorrow cleft<br/>  With his rude scythe such gentle bosoms; he<br/>Sighed to behold them of their hours bereft,<br/>  Though foe to Love; and yet they could not be<br/>Meant to grow old, but die in happy Spring,<br/>Before one charm or hope had taken wing.<br/><br/>Their faces were not made for wrinkles, their<br/>  Pure blood to stagnate, their great hearts to fail;<br/>The blank grey was not made to blast their hair,<br/>  But like the climes that know nor snow nor hail,<br/>They were all summer; lightning might assail<br/>  And shiver them to ashes, but to trail<br/>A long and snake-like life of dull decay<br/>Was not for them--they had too little clay.<br/><br/>They were alone once more; for them to be<br/>  Thus was another Eden; they were never<br/>Weary, unless when separate: the tree<br/>  Cut from its forest root of years--the river<br/>Dammed from its fountain--the child from the knee<br/>  And breast maternal weaned at once for ever,--<br/>Would wither less than these two torn apart;<br/>Alas! there is no instinct like the Heart--<br/><br/>The Heart--which may be broken: happy they!<br/>  Thrice fortunate! who of that fragile mould,<br/>The precious porcelain of human clay,<br/>  Break with the first fall: they can ne'er behold<br/>The long year linked with heavy day on day,<br/>  And all which must be borne, and never told;<br/>While Life's strange principle will often lie<br/>Deepest in those who long the most to die.<br/><br/>"Whom the gods love die young," was said of yore,<br/>  And many deaths do they escape by this:<br/>The death of friends, and that which slays even more--<br/>  The death of Friendship, Love, Youth, all that is,<br/>Except mere breath; and since the silent shore<br/>  Awaits at last even those who longest miss<br/>The old Archer's shafts, perhaps the early grave<br/>Which men weep over may be meant to save.<br/><br/>Haid√©e and Juan thought not of the dead--<br/>  The Heavens, and Earth, and Air, seemed made for them:<br/>They found no fault with Time, save that he fled;<br/>  They saw not in themselves aught to condemn:<br/>Each was the other's mirror, and but read<br/>  Joy sparkling in their dark eyes like a gem.<br/>And knew such brightness was but the reflection<br/>Of their exchanging glances of affection.<br/><br/>The gentle pressure, and the thrilling touch,<br/>  The least glance better understood than words,<br/>Which still said all, and ne'er could say too much;<br/>  A language, too, but like to that of birds,<br/>Known but to them, at least appearing such<br/>  As but to lovers a true sense affords;<br/>Sweet playful phrases, which would seem absurd<br/>To those who have ceased to hear such, or ne'er heard--<br/><br/>All these were theirs, for they were children still,<br/>  And children still they should have ever been;<br/>They were not made in the real world to fill<br/>  A busy character in the dull scene,<br/>But like two beings born from out a rill,<br/>  A Nymph and her belov√©d, all unseen<br/>To pass their lives in fountains and on flowers,<br/>And never know the weight of human hours.<br/><br/>Moons changing had rolled on, and changeless found<br/>  Those their bright rise had lighted to such joys<br/>As rarely they beheld throughout their round;<br/>  And these were not of the vain kind which cloys,<br/>For theirs were buoyant spirits, never bound<br/>  By the mere senses; and that which destroys<br/>Most love--possession--unto them appeared<br/>A thing which each endearment more endeared.<br/><br/>Oh beautiful! and rare as beautiful!<br/>  But theirs was Love in which the Mind delights<br/>To lose itself, when the old world grows dull,<br/>  And we are sick of its hack sounds and sights,<br/>Intrigues, adventures of the common school,<br/>  Its petty passions, marriages, and flights,<br/>Where Hymen's torch but brands one strumpet more,<br/>Whose husband only knows her not a whore.<br/><br/>Hard words--harsh truth! a truth which many know.<br/>  Enough.--The faithful and the fairy pair,<br/>Who never found a single hour too slow,<br/>  What was it made them thus exempt from care?<br/>Young innate feelings all have felt below,<br/>  Which perish in the rest, but in them were<br/>Inherent--what we mortals call romantic,<br/>And always envy, though we deem it frantic.<br/><br/>This is in others a factitious state,<br/>  An opium dream of too much youth and reading,<br/>But was in them their nature or their fate:<br/>  No novels e'er had set their young hearts bleeding,<br/>For Haid√©e's knowledge was by no means great,<br/>  And Juan was a boy of saintly breeding;<br/>So that there was no reason for their loves<br/>More than for those of nightingales or doves.<br/><br/>They gazed upon the sunset; 't is an hour<br/>  Dear unto all, but dearest to _their_ eyes,<br/>For it had made them what they were: the power<br/>  Of Love had first o'erwhelmed them from such skies,<br/>When Happiness had been their only dower,<br/>  And Twilight saw them linked in Passion's ties;<br/>Charmed with each other, all things charmed that brought<br/>The past still welcome as the present thought.<br/><br/>I know not why, but in that hour to-night,<br/>  Even as they gazed, a sudden tremor came,<br/>And swept, as 't were, across their hearts' delight,<br/>  Like the wind o'er a harp-string, or a flame,<br/>When one is shook in sound, and one in sight:<br/>  And thus some boding flashed through either frame,<br/>And called from Juan's breast a faint low sigh,<br/>While one new tear arose in Haid√©e's eye.<br/><br/>That large black prophet eye seemed to dilate<br/>  And follow far the disappearing sun,<br/>As if their last day of a happy date<br/>  With his broad, bright, and dropping orb were gone;<br/>Juan gazed on her as to ask his fate--<br/>  He felt a grief, but knowing cause for none,<br/>His glance inquired of hers for some excuse<br/>For feelings causeless, or at least abstruse.<br/><br/>She turned to him, and smiled, but in that sort<br/>  Which makes not others smile; then turned aside:<br/>Whatever feeling shook her, it seemed short,<br/>  And mastered by her wisdom or her pride;<br/>When Juan spoke, too--it might be in sport--<br/>  Of this their mutual feeling, she replied--<br/>"If it should be so,--but--it cannot be--<br/>Or I at least shall not survive to see."<br/><br/>Juan would question further, but she pressed<br/>  His lip to hers, and silenced him with this,<br/>And then dismissed the omen from her breast,<br/>  Defying augury with that fond kiss;<br/>And no doubt of all methods 't is the best:<br/>  Some people prefer wine--'t is not amiss;<br/>I have tried both--so those who would a part take<br/>May choose between the headache and the heartache.<br/><br/>One of the two, according to your choice,<br/>  Woman or wine, you'll have to undergo;<br/>Both maladies are taxes on our joys:<br/>  But which to choose, I really hardly know;<br/>And if I had to give a casting voice,<br/>  For both sides I could many reasons show,<br/>And then decide, without great wrong to either,<br/>It were much better to have both than neither.<br/><br/>Juan and Haid√©e gazed upon each other<br/>  With swimming looks of speechless tenderness,<br/>Which mixed all feelings--friend, child, lover, brother--<br/>  All that the best can mingle and express<br/>When two pure hearts are poured in one another,<br/>  And love too much, and yet can not love less;<br/>But almost sanctify the sweet excess<br/>By the immortal wish and power to bless.<br/><br/>Mixed in each other's arms, and heart in heart,<br/>  Why did they not then die?--they had lived too long<br/>Should an hour come to bid them breathe apart;<br/>  Years could but bring them cruel things or wrong;<br/>The World was not for them--nor the World's art<br/>  For beings passionate as Sappho's song;<br/>Love was born _with_ them, _in_ them, so intense,<br/>It was their very Spirit--not a sense.<br/><br/>They should have lived together deep in woods,<br/>  Unseen as sings the nightingale; they were<br/>Unfit to mix in these thick solitudes<br/>  Called social, haunts of Hate, and Vice, and Care:<br/>How lonely every freeborn creature broods!<br/>  The sweetest song-birds nestle in a pair;<br/>The eagle soars alone; the gull and crow<br/>Flock o'er their carrion, just like men below.<br/><br/>Now pillowed cheek to cheek, in loving sleep,<br/>  Haid√©e and Juan their siesta took,<br/>A gentle slumber, but it was not deep,<br/>  For ever and anon a something shook<br/>Juan, and shuddering o'er his frame would creep;<br/>  And Haid√©e's sweet lips murmured like a brook<br/>A wordless music, and her face so fair<br/>Stirred with her dream, as rose-leaves with the air.<br/><br/>Or as the stirring of a deep clear stream<br/>  Within an Alpine hollow, when the wind<br/>Walks o'er it, was she shaken by the dream,<br/>  The mystical Usurper of the mind--<br/>O'erpowering us to be whate'er may seem<br/>  Good to the soul which we no more can bind;<br/>Strange state of being! (for 't is still to be)<br/>Senseless to feel, and with sealed eyes to see.<br/><br/>She dreamed of being alone on the sea-shore,<br/>  Chained to a rock; she knew not how, but stir<br/>She could not from the spot, and the loud roar<br/>  Grew, and each wave rose roughly, threatening her;<br/>And o'er her upper lip they seemed to pour,<br/>  Until she sobbed for breath, and soon they were<br/>Foaming o'er her lone head, so fierce and high--<br/>Each broke to drown her, yet she could not die.<br/><br/>Anon--she was released, and then she strayed<br/>  O'er the sharp shingles with her bleeding feet,<br/>And stumbled almost every step she made:<br/>  And something rolled before her in a sheet,<br/>Which she must still pursue howe'er afraid:<br/>  'T was white and indistinct, nor stopped to meet<br/>Her glance nor grasp, for still she gazed and grasped,<br/>And ran, but it escaped her as she clasped.<br/><br/>The dream changed:--in a cave she stood, its walls<br/>  Were hung with marble icicles;
