shocked at tears,<br/>  For women shed and use them at their liking;<br/>But there is something when man's eye appears<br/>  Wet, still more disagreeable and striking:<br/>A woman's tear-drop melts, a man's half sears,<br/>  Like molten lead, as if you thrust a pike in<br/>His heart to force it out, for (to be shorter)<br/>To them 't is a relief, to us a torture.<br/><br/>And she would have consoled, but knew not how:<br/>  Having no equals, nothing which had e'er<br/>Infected her with sympathy till now,<br/>  And never having dreamt what 't was to bear<br/>Aught of a serious, sorrowing kind, although<br/>  There might arise some pouting petty care<br/>To cross her brow, she wondered how so near<br/>Her eyes another's eye could shed a tear.<br/><br/>But Nature teaches more than power can spoil,<br/>  And, when a strong although a strange sensation<br/>Moves--female hearts are such a genial soil<br/>  For kinder feelings, whatso'er their nation,<br/>They naturally pour the "wine and oil,"<br/>  Samaritans in every situation;<br/>And thus Gulbeyaz, though she knew not why,<br/>Felt an odd glistening moisture in her eye.<br/><br/>But tears must stop like all things else; and soon<br/>  Juan, who for an instant had been moved<br/>To such a sorrow by the intrusive tone<br/>  Of one who dared to ask if "he _had_ loved,"<br/>Called back the Stoic to his eyes, which shone<br/>  Bright with the very weakness he reproved;<br/>And although sensitive to beauty, he<br/>Felt most indignant still at not being free.<br/><br/>Gulbeyaz, for the first time in her days,<br/>  Was much embarrassed, never having met<br/>In all her life with aught save prayers and praise;<br/>  And as she also risked her life to get<br/>Him whom she meant to tutor in love's ways<br/>  Into a comfortable t√™te-√†-t√™te,<br/>To lose the hour would make her quite a martyr,<br/>And they had wasted now almost a quarter.<br/><br/>I also would suggest the fitting time<br/>  To gentlemen in any such like case,<br/>That is to say in a meridian clime--<br/>  With us there is more law given to the chase,<br/>But here a small delay forms a great crime:<br/>  So recollect that the extremest grace<br/>Is just two minutes for your declaration--<br/>A moment more would hurt your reputation.<br/><br/>Juan's was good; and might have been still better,<br/>  But he had got Haid√©e into his head:<br/>However strange, he could not yet forget her,<br/>  Which made him seem exceedingly ill-bred.<br/>Gulbeyaz, who looked on him as her debtor<br/>  For having had him to her palace led,<br/>Began to blush up to the eyes, and then<br/>Grow deadly pale, and then blush back again.<br/><br/>At length, in an imperial way, she laid<br/>  Her hand on his, and bending on him eyes<br/>Which needed not an empire to persuade,<br/>  Looked into his for love, where none replies:<br/>Her brow grew black, but she would not upbraid,<br/>  That being the last thing a proud woman tries;<br/>She rose, and pausing one chaste moment threw<br/>Herself upon his breast, and there she grew.<br/><br/>This was an awkward test, as Juan found,<br/>  But he was steeled by Sorrow, Wrath, and Pride:<br/>With gentle force her white arms he unwound,<br/>  And seated her all drooping by his side,<br/>Then rising haughtily he glanced around,<br/>  And looking coldly in her face he cried,<br/>"The prisoned eagle will not pair, nor I<br/>Serve a Sultana's sensual phantasy.<br/><br/>"Thou ask'st, if I can love? be this the proof<br/>  How much I _have_ loved--that I love not _thee!_<br/>In this vile garb, the distaff, web, and woof,<br/>  Were fitter for me: Love is for the free!<br/>I am not dazzled by this splendid roof;<br/>  Whate'er thy power, and great it seems to be,<br/>Heads bow, knees bend, eyes watch around a throne,<br/>And hands obey--our hearts are still our own."<br/><br/>This was a truth to us extremely trite;<br/>  Not so to her, who ne'er had heard such things:<br/>She deemed her least command must yield delight,<br/>  Earth being only made for Queens and Kings.<br/>If hearts lay on the left side or the right<br/>  She hardly knew, to such perfection brings<br/>Legitimacy its born votaries, when<br/>Aware of their due royal rights o'er men.<br/><br/>Besides, as has been said, she was so fair<br/>  As even in a much humbler lot had made<br/>A kingdom or confusion anywhere,<br/>  And also, as may be presumed, she laid<br/>Some stress on charms, which seldom are, if e'er,<br/>  By their possessors thrown into the shade:<br/>She thought hers gave a double "right divine;"<br/>And half of that opinion's also mine.<br/><br/>Remember, or (if you can not) imagine,<br/>  Ye! who have kept your chastity when young,<br/>While some more desperate dowager has been waging<br/>  Love with you, and been in the dog-days stung<br/>By your refusal, recollect her raging!<br/>  Or recollect all that was said or sung<br/>On such a subject; then suppose the face<br/>Of a young downright beauty in this case!<br/><br/>Suppose,--but you already have supposed,<br/>  The spouse of Potiphar, the Lady Booby,<br/>Phaedra, and all which story has disclosed<br/>  Of good examples; pity that so few by<br/>Poets and private tutors are exposed,<br/>  To educate--ye youth of Europe--you by!<br/>But when you have supposed the few we know,<br/>You can't suppose Gulbeyaz' angry brow.<br/><br/>A tigress robbed of young, a lioness,<br/>  Or any interesting beast of prey,<br/>Are similes at hand for the distress<br/>  Of ladies who can _not_ have their own way;<br/>But though my turn will not be served with less,<br/>  These don't express one half what I should say:<br/>For what is stealing young ones, few or many,<br/>To cutting short their hope of having _any?_<br/><br/>The love of offspring's Nature's general law,<br/>  From tigresses and cubs to ducks and ducklings;<br/>There's nothing whets the beak, or arms the claw<br/>  Like an invasion of their babes and sucklings;<br/>And all who have seen a human nursery, saw<br/>  How mothers love their children's squalls and chucklings:<br/>This strong extreme effect (to tire no longer<br/>Your patience) shows the cause must still be stronger.<br/><br/>If I said fire flashed from Gulbeyaz' eyes,<br/>  'T were nothing--for her eyes flashed always fire;<br/>Or said her cheeks assumed the deepest dyes,<br/>  I should but bring disgrace upon the dyer,<br/>So supernatural was her passion's rise;<br/>  For ne'er till now she knew a checked desire:<br/>Even ye who know what a checked woman is<br/>(Enough, God knows!) would much fall short of this.<br/><br/>Her rage was but a minute's, and 't was well--<br/>  A moment's more had slain her; but the while<br/>It lasted 't was like a short glimpse of Hell:<br/>  Nought's more sublime than energetic bile,<br/>Though horrible to see, yet grand to tell,<br/>  Like Ocean warring 'gainst a rocky isle;<br/>And the deep passions flashing through her form<br/>Made her a beautiful embodied storm.<br/><br/>A vulgar tempest 't were to a typhoon<br/>  To match a common fury with her rage,<br/>And yet she did not want to reach the moon,<br/>  Like moderate Hotspur on the immortal page;<br/>Her anger pitched into a lower tune,<br/>  Perhaps the fault of her soft sex and age--<br/>Her wish was but to "kill, kill, kill," like Lear's,<br/>And then her thirst of blood was quenched in tears.<br/><br/>A storm it raged, and like the storm it passed,<br/>  Passed without words--in fact she could not speak;<br/>And then her sex's shame broke in at last,<br/>  A sentiment till then in her but weak,<br/>But now it flowed in natural and fast,<br/>  As water through an unexpected leak;<br/>For she felt humbled--and humiliation<br/>Is sometimes good for people in her station.<br/><br/>It teaches them that they are flesh and blood,<br/>  It also gently hints to them that others,<br/>Although of clay, are yet not quite of mud;<br/>  That urns and pipkins are but fragile brothers,<br/>And works of the same pottery, bad or good,<br/>  Though not all born of the same sires and mothers;<br/>It teaches--Heaven knows only what it teaches,<br/>But sometimes it may mend, and often reaches.<br/><br/>Her first thought was to cut off Juan's head;<br/>  Her second, to cut only his--acquaintance;<br/>Her third, to ask him where he had been bred;<br/>  Her fourth, to rally him into repentance;<br/>Her fifth, to call her maids and go to bed;<br/>  Her sixth, to stab herself; her seventh, to sentence<br/>The lash to Baba:--but her grand resource<br/>Was to sit down again, and cry--of course.<br/><br/>She thought to stab herself, but then she had<br/>  The dagger close at hand, which made it awkward;<br/>For Eastern stays are little made to pad,<br/>  So that a poniard pierces if 't is struck hard:<br/>She thought of killing Juan--but, poor lad!<br/>  Though he deserved it well for being so backward,<br/>The cutting off his head was not the art<br/>Most likely to attain her aim--his heart.<br/><br/>Juan was moved: he had made up his mind<br/>  To be impaled, or quartered as a dish<br/>For dogs, or to be slain with pangs refined,<br/>  Or thrown to lions, or made baits for fish,<br/>And thus heroically stood resigned,<br/>  Rather than sin--except to his own wish:<br/>But all his great preparatives for dying<br/>Dissolved like snow before a woman crying.<br/><br/>As through his palms Bob Acres' valour oozed,<br/>  So Juan's virtue ebbed, I know not how;<br/>And first he wondered why he had refused;<br/>  And then, if matters could be made up now;<br/>And next his savage virtue he accused,<br/>  Just as a friar may accuse his vow,<br/>Or as a dame repents her of her oath,<br/>Which mostly ends in some small breach of both.<br/><br/>So he began to stammer some excuses;<br/>  But words are not enough in such a matter,<br/>Although you borrowed all that e'er the Muses<br/>  Have sung, or even a Dandy's dandiest chatter,<br/>Or all the figures Castlereagh abuses;<br/>  Just as a languid smile began to flatter<br/>His peace was making, but, before he ventured<br/>Further, old Baba rather briskly entered.<br/><br/>"Bride of the Sun! and Sister of the Moon!"<br/>  ('T was thus he spake,) "and Empress of the Earth!<br/>Whose frown would put the spheres all out of tune,<br/>  Whose smile makes all the planets dance with mirth,<br/>Your slave brings tidings--he hopes not too soon--<br/>  Which your sublime attention may be worth:<br/>The Sun himself has sent me like a ray,<br/>To hint that he is coming up this way."<br/><br/>"Is it," exclaimed Gulbeyaz, "as you say?<br/>  I wish to heaven he would not shine till morning!<br/>But bid my women form the milky way.<br/>  Hence, my old comet! give the stars due warning--<br/>And, Christian! mingle with them as you may,<br/>  And as you'd have me pardon your past scorning-----"<br/>Here they were interrupted by a humming<br/>Sound, and then by a cry, "The Sultan's coming!"<br/><br/>First came her damsels, a decorous file,<br/>  And then his Highness' eunuchs, black and white;<br/>The train might reach a quarter of a mile:<br/>  His Majesty was always so polite<br/>As to announce his visits a long while<br/>  Before he came, especially at night;<br/>For being the last wife of the Emperor,<br/>She was of course the favourite of the four.<br/><br/>His Highness was a man of solemn port,<br/>  Shawled to the nose, and bearded to the eyes,<br/>Snatched from a prison to preside at court,<br/>  His lately bowstrung brother caused his rise;<br/>He was as good a sovereign of the sort<br/>  As any mentioned in the histories<br/>Of Cantemir, or Kn≈çllƒõs, where few shine<br/>Save Solyman, the glory of their line.<br/><br/>He went to mosque in state, and said his prayers<br/>  With more than "Oriental scrupulosity;"<br/>He left to his vizier all state affairs,<br/>  And showed but little royal curiosity:<br/>I know not if he had domestic cares--<br/>  No process proved connubial animosity;<br/>Four wives and twice five hundred maids, unseen,<br/>Were ruled as calmly as a Christian queen.<br/><br/>If now and then there happened a slight slip,<br/>  Little was heard of criminal or crime;<br/>The story scarcely passed a single lip--<br/>  The sack and sea had settled all in time,<br/>From which the secret nobody could rip:<br/>  The public knew no more than does this rhyme;<br/>No scandals made the daily press a curse--<br/>Morals were better, and the fish no worse.<br/><br/>He saw with his own eyes the moon was round,<br/>  Was also certain that the earth was square,<br/>Because he had journeyed fifty miles, and found<br/>  No sign that it was circular anywhere;<br/>His empire also was without a bound:<br/>  'T is true, a little troubled here and there,<br/>By rebel pachas, and encroaching giaours,<br/>But then they never came to "the Seven Towers;"<br/><br/>Except in shape of envoys, who were sent<br/>  To lodge there when a war broke out, according<br/>To the true law of nations, which ne'er meant<br/>  Those scoundrels, who have never had a sword in<br/>Their dirty diplomatic hands, to vent<br/>  Their spleen in making strife, and safely wording<br/>Their lies, yclept despatches, without risk or<br/>The singeing of a single inky whisker.<br/><br/>He had fifty daughters and four dozen sons,<br/>  Of whom all such as came of age were stowed,<br/>The former in a palace, where like nuns<br/>  They lived till some Bashaw was sent abroad,<br/>When she, whose turn it was, was wed at once,<br/>  Sometimes at six years old--though this seems odd,<br/>'T is true; the reason is, that the Bashaw<br/>Must make a present to his sire-in-law.<br/><br/>His sons were kept in prison, till they grew<br/>  Of years to fill a bowstring or the throne,<br/>One or the other, but which of the two<br/>  Could yet be known unto the fates alone;<br/>Meantime the education they went through<br/>  Was princely, as the proofs have always shown;<br/>So that the heir apparent still was found<br/>No less deserving to be hanged than crowned.<br/><br/>His Majesty saluted his fourth spouse<br/>  With all the ceremonies of his rank,<br/>Who cleared her sparkling eyes and smoothed her brows,<br/>  As suits a matron who has played a prank;<br/>These must seem doubly mindful of their vows,<br/>  To save the credit of their breaking bank:<br/>To no men are such cordial greetings given<br/>As those whose wives have made them fit for Heaven.<br/><br/>His Highness cast around his great black eyes,<br/>  And looking, as he always looked, perceived<br/>Juan amongst the damsels in disguise,<br/>  At which he seemed no whit surprised nor grieved,<br/>But just remarked with air sedate and wise,<br/>  While still a fluttering sigh Gulbeyaz heaved,<br/>"I see you've bought another girl; 't is pity<br/>That a mere Christian should be half so pretty."<br/><br/>This compliment, which drew all eyes upon<br/>  The new-bought virgin, made her blush and shake.<br/>Her comrades, also, thought themselves undone:<br/>  Oh! Mahomet! that his Majesty should take<br/>Such notice of a giaour, while scarce to one<br/>  Of them his lips imperial ever spake!<br/>There was a general whisper, toss, and wriggle,<br/>But etiquette forbade them all to giggle.<br/><br/>The Turks do well to shut--at least, sometimes--<br/>  The women up--because, in sad reality,<br/>Their chastity in these unhappy climes<br/>  Is not a thing of that astringent quality<br/>Which in the North prevents precocious crimes,<br/>  And makes our snow less pure than our morality;<br/>The Sun, which yearly melts the polar ice,<br/>Has quite the contrary effect--on vice.<br/><br/>Thus in the East they are extremely strict,<br/>  And wedlock and a padlock mean the same:<br/>Excepting only when the former's picked<br/>  It ne'er can be replaced in proper frame;<br/>Spoilt, as a pipe of claret is when pricked:<br/>  But then their own polygamy's to blame;<br/>Why don't they knead two virtuous souls for life<br/>Into that moral centaur, man and wife?<br/><br/>Thus far our chronicle; and now we pause,<br/>  Though not for want of matter; but 't is time,<br/>According to the ancient epic laws,<br/>  To slacken sail, and anchor with our rhyme.<br/>Let this fifth canto meet with due applause,<br/>  The sixth shall have a touch of the sublime;<br/>Meanwhile, as Homer sometimes sleeps, perhaps<br/>You'll pardon to my muse a few short naps.<br/><br/>CANTO THE SIXTH.<br/><br/>"There is a tide in the affairs of men,<br/>  Which,--taken at the flood,"--you know the rest,<br/>And most of us have found it now and then:<br/>  At least we think so, though but few have guessed<br/>The moment, till too late to come again.<br/>  But no doubt everything is for the best--<br/>Of which the surest sign is in the end:<br/>When things are at the worst they sometimes mend.<br/><br/>There is a tide in the affairs of women,<br/>  Which, taken at the flood, leads--God knows where:<br/>Those navigators must be able seamen<br/>  Whose charts lay down its currents to a hair;<br/>Not all the reveries of Jacob Behmen<br/>  With its strange whirls and eddies can compare:<br/>Men with their heads reflect on this and that--<br/>But women with their hearts on Heaven knows what!<br/><br/>And yet a headlong, headstrong, downright She,<br/>  Young, beautiful, and daring--who would risk<br/>A throne--the world--the universe--to be<br/>  Beloved in her own way--and rather whisk<br/>The stars from out the sky, than not be free<br/>  As are the billows when the breeze is brisk--<br/>Though such a She's a devil (if there be one),<br/>Yet she would make full many a Manichean.<br/><br/>Thrones, worlds, _et cetera_, are so oft upset<br/>  By commonest ambition, that when Passion<br/>O'erthrows the same, we readily forget,<br/>  Or at the least forgive, the loving rash one.<br/>If Anthony be well remembered yet,<br/>  'T is not his conquests keep his name in fashion,<br/>But Actium, lost for Cleopatra's eyes,<br/>Outbalances all C√¶sar's victories.<br/><br/>He died at fifty for a queen of forty;<br/>  I wish their years had been fifteen and twenty,<br/>For then wealth, kingdoms, worlds are but a sport--I<br/>  Remember when, though I had no great plenty<br/>Of worlds to lose, yet still, to pay my court, I<br/>  Gave what I had--a heart; as the world went, I<br/>Gave what was worth a world; for worlds could never<br/>Restore me those pure feelings, gone for ever.<br/><br/>'T was the boy's "mite," and, like the "widow's," may<br/>  Perhaps be weighed hereafter, if not now;<br/>But whether such things do or do not weigh,<br/>  All who have loved, or love, will still allow<br/>Life has nought like it. God is Love, they say,<br/>  And Love's a god, or was before the brow<br/>Of Earth was wrinkled by the sins and tears<br/>Of--but Chronology best knows the years.<br/><br/>We left our hero and third heroine in<br/>  A kind of state more awkward than uncommon,<br/>For gentlemen must sometimes risk their skin<br/>  For that sad tempter, a forbidden woman:<br/>Sultans too much abhor this sort of sin,<br/>  And don't agree at all with the wise Roman,<br/>Heroic, stoic Cato, the sententious,<br/>Who lent his lady to his friend Hortensius.<br/><br/>I know Gulbeyaz was extremely wrong;<br/>  I own it, I deplore it, I condemn it;<br/>But I detest all fiction even in song,<br/>  And so must tell the truth, howe'er you blame it.<br/>Her reason being weak, her passions strong,<br/>  She thought that her Lord's heart (even could she claim it)<br/>Was scarce enough; for he had fifty-nine<br/>Years, and a fifteen-hundredth concubine.<br/><br/>I am not, like Cassio, "an arithmetician,"<br/>  But by "the bookish theoric" it appears,<br/>If 't is summed up with feminine precision,<br/>  That, adding to the account his Highness' years,<br/>The fair Sultana erred from inanition;<br/>  For, were the Sultan just to all his dears,<br/>She could but claim the fifteen-hundredth part<br/>Of what should be monopoly--the heart.<br/><br/>It is observed that ladies are litigious<br/>  Upon all legal objects of possession,<br/>And not the least so when they are religious,<br/>  Which doubles what they think of the transgression:<br/>With suits and prosecutions they besiege us,<br/>  As the tribunals show through many a session,<br/>When they suspect that any one goes shares<br/>In that to which the law makes them sole heirs.<br/><br/>Now, if this holds good in a Christian land,<br/>  The heathen also, though with lesser latitude,<br/>Are apt to carry things with a high hand,<br/>  And take, what Kings call "an imposing attitude;"<br/>And for their rights connubial make a stand,<br/>  When their liege husbands treat them with ingratitude;<br/>And as four wives must have quadruple claims,<br/>The Tigris hath its jealousies like Thames.<br/><br/>Gulbeyaz was the fourth, and (as I said)<br/>  The favourite; but what's favour amongst four?<br/>Polygamy may well be held in dread,<br/>  Not only as a sin, but as a bore:<br/>Most wise men with one moderate woman wed,<br/>  Will scarcely find philosophy for more;<br/>And all (except Mahometans) forbear<br/>To make the nuptial couch a "Bed of Ware."<br/><br/>His Highness, the sublimest of mankind,--<br/>  So styled according to the usual forms<br/>Of every monarch, till they are consigned<br/>  To those sad hungry Jacobins the worms,<br/>Who on the very loftiest kings have dined,--<br/>  His Highness gazed upon Gulbeyaz' charms,<br/>Expecting all the welcome of a lover<br/>(A "Highland welcome" all the wide world over).<br/><br/>Now here we should distinguish; for howe'er<br/>  Kisses, sweet words, embraces, and all that,<br/>May look like what it is--neither here nor there,<br/>  They are put on as easily as a hat,<br/>Or rather bonnet, which the fair sex wear,<br/>  Trimmed either heads or hearts to decorate,<br/>Which form an ornament, but no more part<br/>Of heads, than their caresses of the heart.<br/><br/>A slight blush, a soft tremor, a calm kind<br/>  Of gentle feminine delight, and shown<br/>More in the eyelids than the eyes, resigned<br/>  Rather to hide what pleases most unknown,<br/>Are the best tokens (to a modest mind)<br/>  Of Love, when seated on his loveliest throne,<br/>A sincere woman's breast,--for over-_warm_<br/>Or over-_cold_ annihilates the charm.<br/><br/>For over-warmth, if false, is worse than truth;<br/>  If true, 't is no great lease of its own fire;<br/>For no one, save in very early youth,<br/>  Would like (I think) to trust all to desire,<br/>Which is but a precarious bond, in sooth,<br/>  And apt to be transferred to the first buyer<br/>At a sad discount: while your over chilly<br/>Women, on t' other hand, seem somewhat silly.<br/><br/>That is, we cannot pardon their bad taste,<br/>  For so it seems to lovers swift or slow,<br/>Who fain would have a mutual flame confessed,<br/>  And see a sentimental passion glow,<br/>Even were St. Francis' paramour their guest,<br/>  In his monastic concubine of snow;--<br/>In short, the maxim for the amorous tribe is<br/>Horatian, "_Medio tu tutissimus ibis_"<br/><br/>The "tu" 's _too_ much,--but let it stand,--the verse<br/>  Requires it, that's to say, the English rhyme,<br/>And not the pink of old hexameters;<br/>  But, after all, there's neither tune nor time<br/>In the last line, which cannot well be worse,<br/>  And was thrust in to close the octave's chime:<br/>I own no prosody can ever rate it<br/>As a rule, but _Truth_ may, if you translate it.<br/><br/>If fair Gulbeyaz overdid her part,<br/>  I know not--it succeeded, and success<br/>Is much in most things, not less in the heart<br/>  Than other articles of female dress.<br/>Self-love in Man, too, beats all female art;<br/>  They lie, we lie, all lie, but love no less:<br/>And no one virtue yet, except starvation,<br/>Could stop that worst of vices--propagation.<br/><br/>We leave this royal couple to repose:<br/>  A bed is not a throne, and they may sleep,<br/>Whate'er their dreams be, if of joys or woes:<br/>  Yet disappointed joys are woes as deep<br/>As any man's clay mixture undergoes.<br/>  Our least of sorrows are such as we _weep_;<br/>'T is the vile daily drop on drop which wears<br/>The soul out (like the stone) with petty cares.<br/><br/>A scolding wife, a sullen son, a bill<br/>  To pay, unpaid, protested, or discounted<br/>At a per-centage; a child cross, dog ill,<br/>  A favourite horse fallen lame just as he's mounted,<br/>A bad old woman making a worse will,<br/>  Which leaves you minus of the cash you counted<br/>As certain;--these are paltry things, and yet<br/>I've rarely seen the man they did not fret.<br/><br/>I'm a philosopher; confound them all!<br/>  Bills, beasts, and men, and--no! not womankind!<br/>With one good hearty curse I vent my gall,<br/>  And then my Stoicism leaves nought behind<br/>Which it can either pain or evil call,<br/>  And I can give my whole soul up to mind;<br/>Though what _is_ soul, or mind, their birth or growth,<br/>Is more than I know--the deuce take them both!<br/><br/>So now all things are damned one feels at ease,<br/>  As after reading Athanasius' curse,<br/>Which doth your true believer so much please:<br/>  I doubt if any now could make it worse<br/>O'er his worst enemy when at his knees,<br/>  'T is so sententious, positive, and terse,<br/>And decorates the Book of Common Prayer,<br/>As doth a rainbow the just clearing air.<br/><br/>Gulbeyaz and her lord were sleeping, or<br/>  At least one of them!--Oh, the heavy night,<br/>When wicked wives, who love some bachelor,<br/>  Lie down in dudgeon to sigh for the light<br/>Of the grey morning, and look vainly for<br/>  Its twinkle through the lattice dusky quite--<br/>To toss, to tumble, doze, revive, and quake<br/>Lest their too lawful bed-fellow should wake!<br/><br/>These are beneath the canopy of heaven,<br/>  Also beneath the canopy of beds<br/>Four-posted and silk-curtained, which are given<br/>  For rich men and their brides to lay their heads<br/>Upon, in sheets white as what bards call "driven<br/>  Snow," Well! 't is all hap-hazard when one weds.<br/>Gulbeyaz was an empress, but had been<br/>Perhaps as wretched if a _peasants quean_.<br/><br/>Don Juan in his feminine disguise,<br/>  With all the damsels in their long array,<br/>Had bowed themselves before th' imperial eyes,<br/>  And at the usual signal ta'en their way<br/>Back to their chambers, those long galleries<br/>  In the seraglio, where the ladies lay<br/>Their delicate limbs; a thousand bosoms there<br/>Beating for Love, as the caged bird's for air.<br/><br/>I love the sex, and sometimes would reverse<br/>  The Tyrant's wish, "that Mankind only had<br/>One neck, which he with one fell stroke might pierce:"<br/>  My wish is quite as wide, but not so bad,<br/>And much more tender on the whole than fierce;<br/>  It being (not _now_, but only while a lad)<br/>That Womankind had but one rosy mouth,<br/>To kiss them all at once from North to South.<br/><br/>Oh, enviable Briareus! with thy hands<br/>  And heads, if thou hadst all things multiplied<br/>In such proportion!--But my Muse withstands<br/>  The giant thought of being a Titan's bride,<br/>Or travelling in Patagonian lands;<br/>  So let us back to Lilliput, and guide<br/>Our hero through the labyrinth of Love<br/>In which we left him several lines above.<br/><br/>He went forth with the lovely Odalisques,<br/>  At the given signal joined to their array;<br/>And though he certainly ran many risks,<br/>  Yet he could not at times keep, by the way,<br/>(Although the consequences of such frisks<br/>  Are worse than the worst damages men pay<br/>In moral England, where the thing's a tax,)<br/>From ogling all their charms from breasts to backs.<br/><br/>Still he forgot not his disguise:--along<br/>  The galleries from room to room they walked,<br/>A virgin-like and edifying throng,<br/>  By eunuchs flanked; while at their head there stalked<br/>A dame who kept up discipline among<br/>  The female ranks, so that none stirred or talked,<br/>Without her sanction on their she-parades:<br/>Her title was "the Mother of the Maids."<br/><br/>Whether she was a "Mother," I know not,<br/>  Or whether they were "Maids" who called her Mother;<br/>But this is her Seraglio title, got<br/>  I know not how, but good as any other;<br/>So Cantemir can tell you, or De Tott:<br/>  Her office was to keep aloof or smother<br/>All bad propensities in fifteen hundred<br/>Young women, and correct them when they blundered.<br/><br/>A goodly sinecure, no doubt! but made<br/>  More easy by the absence of all men--<br/>Except his Majesty,--who, with her aid,<br/>  And guards, and bolts, and walls, and now and then<br/>A slight example, just to cast a shade<br/>  Along the rest, contrived to keep this den<br/>Of beauties cool as an Italian convent,<br/>Where all the passions have, alas! but one vent.<br/><br/>And what is that? Devotion, doubtless--how<br/>  Could you ask such a question?--but we will<br/>Continue. As I said, this goodly row<br/>  Of ladies of all countries at the will<br/>Of one good man, with stately march and slow,<br/>  Like water-lilies floating down a rill--<br/>Or rather lake--for _rills_ do _not_ run _slowly_,--<br/>Paced on most maiden-like and melancholy.<br/><br/>But when they reached their own apartments, there,<br/>  Like birds, or boys, or bedlamites broke loose,<br/>Waves at spring-tide, or women anywhere<br/>  When freed from bonds (which are of no great use<br/>After all), or like Irish at a fair,<br/>  Their guards being gone, and as it were a truce<br/>Established between them and bondage, they<br/>Began to sing, dance, chatter, smile, and play.<br/><br/>Their talk, of course, ran most on the new comer;<br/>  Her shape, her hair, her air, her everything:<br/>Some thought her dress did not so much become her,<br/>  Or wondered at her ears without a ring;<br/>Some said her years were getting nigh their summer,<br/>  Others contended they were but in spring;<br/>Some thought her rather masculine in height,<br/>While others wished that she had been so quite.<br/><br/>But no one doubted on the whole, that she<br/>  Was what her dress bespoke, a damsel fair,<br/>And fresh, and "beautiful exceedingly,"<br/>  Who with the brightest Georgians might compare:<br/>They wondered how Gulbeyaz, too, could be<br/>  So silly as to buy slaves who might share<br/>(If that his Highness wearied of his bride)<br/>Her Throne and Power, and everything beside.<br/><br/>But what was strangest in this virgin crew,<br/>  Although her beauty was enough to vex,<br/>After the first investigating view,<br/>  They all found out as few, or fewer, specks<br/>In the fair form of their companion new,<br/>  Than is the custom of the gentle sex,<br/>When they survey, with Christian eyes or Heathen,<br/>In a new face "the ugliest creature breathing."<br/><br/>And yet they had their little jealousies,<br/>  Like all the rest; but upon this occasion,<br/>Whether there are such things as sympathies<br/>  Without our knowledge or our approbation,<br/>Although they could not see through his disguise,<br/>  All felt a soft kind of concatenation,<br/>Like Magnetism, or Devilism, or what<br/>You please--we will not quarrel about that:<br/><br/>But certain 't is they all felt for their new<br/>  Companion something newer still, as 't were<br/>A sentimental friendship through and through,<br/>  Extremely pure, which made them all concur<br/>In wishing her their sister, save a few<br/>  Who wished they had a brother just like her,<br/>Whom, if they were at home in sweet Circassia,<br/>They would prefer to Padisha or Pacha.<br/><br/>Of those who had most genius for this sort<br/>  Of sentimental friendship, there were three,<br/>Lolah, Katinka, and Dud√π--in short<br/>  (To save description), fair as fair can be<br/>Were they, according to the best report,<br/>  Though differing in stature and degree,<br/>And clime and time, and country and complexion--<br/>They all alike admired their new connection.<br/><br/>Lolah was dusk as India and as warm;<br/>  Katinka was a Georgian, white and red,<br/>With great blue eyes, a lovely hand and arm,<br/>  And feet so small they scarce seemed made to tread,<br/>But rather skim the earth; while Dud√π's form<br/>  Looked more adapted to be put to bed,<br/>Being somewhat large, and languishing, and lazy,<br/>Yet of a beauty that would drive you crazy.<br/><br/>A kind of sleepy Venus seemed Dud√π,<br/>  Yet very fit to "murder sleep" in those<br/>Who gazed upon her cheek's transcendent hue,<br/>  Her Attic forehead, and her Phidian nose:<br/>Few angles were there in her form, 't is true,<br/>  Thinner she might have been, and yet scarce lose;<br/>Yet, after all, 't would puzzle to say where<br/>It would not spoil some separate charm to _pare_.<br/><br/>She was not violently lively, but<br/>  Stole on your spirit like a May-day breaking;<br/>Her eyes were not too sparkling, yet, half-shut,<br/>  They put beholders in a tender taking;<br/>She looked (this simile's quite new) just cut<br/>  From marble, like Pygmalion's statue waking,<br/>The mortal and the marble still at strife,<br/>And timidly expanding into Life.<br/><br/>Lolah demanded the new damsel's name--<br/>  "Juanna."--Well, a pretty name enough.<br/>Katinka asked her also whence she came--<br/>  "From Spain."--"But where _is_ Spain?"--"Don't ask such stuff,<br/>Nor show your Georgian ignorance--for shame!"<br/>  Said Lolah, with an accent rather rough,<br/>To poor Katinka: "Spain's an island near<br/>Morocco, betwixt Egypt and Tangier."<br/><br/>Dud√π said nothing, but sat down beside<br/>  Juanna, playing with her veil or hair;<br/>And, looking at her steadfastly, she sighed,<br/>  As if she pitied her for being there,<br/>A pretty stranger without friend or guide,<br/>  And all abashed, too, at the general stare<br/>Which welcomes hapless strangers in all places,<br/>With kind remarks upon their mien and faces.<br/><br/>But here the Mother of the Maids drew near,<br/>  With "Ladies, it is time to go to rest.<br/>I'm puzzled what to do with _you_, my dear!"<br/>  She added to Juanna, their new guest:<br/>"Your coming has been unexpected here,<br/>  And every couch is occupied; you had best<br/>Partake of mine; but by to-morrow early<br/>We will have all things settled for you fairly."<br/><br/>Here Lolah interposed--"Mamma, you know<br/>  You don't sleep soundly, and I cannot bear<br/>That anybody should disturb you so;<br/>  I'll take Juanna; we're a slenderer pair<br/>Than you would make the half of;--don't say no;<br/>  And I of your young charge will take due care."<br/>But here Katinka interfered, and said,<br/>"She also had compassion and a bed."<br/><br/>"Besides, I hate to sleep alone," quoth she.<br/>  The matron frowned: "Why so?"--"For fear of ghosts,"<br/>Replied Katinka; "I am sure I see<br/>  A phantom upon each of the four posts;<br/>And then I have the worst dreams that can be,<br/>  Of Guebres, Giaours, and Ginns, and Gouls in hosts."<br/>The dame replied, "Between your dreams and you,<br/>I fear Juanna's dreams would be but few.<br/><br/>"You, Lolah, must continue still to lie<br/>  Alone, for reasons which don't matter; you<br/>The same, Katinka, until by and by:<br/>  And I shall place Juanna with Dud√π,<br/>Who's quiet, inoffensive, silent, shy,<br/>  And will not toss and chatter the night through.<br/>What say you, child?"--Dud√π said nothing, as<br/>Her talents were of the more silent class;<br/><br/>But she rose up, and kissed the matron's brow<br/>  Between the eyes, and Lolah on both cheeks,<br/>Katinka too; and with a gentle bow<br/>  (Curt'sies are neither used by Turks nor Greeks)<br/>She took Juanna by the hand to show<br/>  Their place of rest, and left to both their piques,<br/>The others pouting at the matron's preference<br/>Of Dud√π, though they held their tongues from deference.<br/><br/>It was a spacious chamber (Oda is<br/>  The Turkish title), and ranged round the wall<br/>Were couches, toilets--and much more than this<br/>  I might describe, as I have seen it all,<br/>But it suffices--little was amiss;<br/>  'T was on the whole a nobly furnished hall,<br/>With all things ladies want, save one or two,<br/>And even those were nearer than they knew.<br/><br/>Dud√π, as has been said, was a sweet creature,<br/>  Not very dashing, but extremely winning,<br/>With the most regulated charms of feature,<br/>  Which painters cannot catch like faces sinning<br/>Against proportion--the wild strokes of nature<br/>  Which they hit off at once in the beginning,<br/>Full of expression, right or wrong, that strike,<br/>And pleasing, or unpleasing, still are like.<br/><br/>But she was a soft landscape of mild earth,<br/>  Where all was harmony, and calm, and quiet,<br/>Luxuriant, budding; cheerful without mirth,<br/>  Which, if not happiness, is much more nigh it<br/>Than are your mighty passions and so forth,<br/>  Which, some call "the Sublime:" I wish they'd try it:<br/>I've seen your stormy seas and stormy women,<br/>And pity lovers rather more than seamen.<br/><br/>But she was pensive more than melancholy,<br/>  And serious more than pensive, and serene,<br/>It may be, more than either--not unholy<br/>  Her thoughts, at least till now, appear to have been.<br/>The strangest thing was, beauteous, she was wholly<br/>  Unconscious, albeit turned of quick seventeen,<br/>That she was fair, or dark, or short, or tall;<br/>She never thought about herself at all.<br/><br/>And therefore was she kind and gentle as<br/>  The Age of Gold (when gold was yet unknown,<br/>By which its nomenclature came to pass;<br/>  Thus most appropriately has been shown<br/>"Lucus √† _non_ lucendo," _not_ what _was_,<br/>  But what _was not_; a sort of style that's grown<br/>Extremely common in this age, whose metal<br/>The Devil may decompose, but never settle:<br/><br/>I think it may be of "Corinthian Brass,"<br/>  Which was a mixture of all metals, but<br/>The brazen uppermost). Kind reader! pass<br/>  This long parenthesis: I could not shut<br/>It sooner for the soul of me, and class<br/>  My faults even with your own! which meaneth, Put<br/>A kind construction upon them and me:<br/>But _that_ you won't--then don't--I am not less free.<br/><br/>'T is time we should return to plain narration,<br/>  And thus my narrative proceeds:--Dud√π,<br/>With every kindness short of ostentation,<br/>  Showed Juan, or Juanna, through and through<br/>This labyrinth of females, and each station<br/>  Described--what's strange--in words extremely few:<br/>I have but one simile, and that's a blunder,<br/>For wordless woman, which is _silent_ thunder.<br/><br/>And next she gave her (I say _her_, because<br/>  The gender still was epicene, at least<br/>In outward show, which is a saving clause)<br/>  An outline of the customs of the East,<br/>With all their chaste integrity of laws,<br/>  By which the more a Harem is increased,<br/>The stricter doubtless grow the vestal duties<br/>Of any supernumerary beauties.<br/><br/>And then she gave Juanna a chaste kiss:<br/>  Dud√∫ was fond of kissing--which I'm sure<br/>That nobody can ever take amiss,<br/>  Because 't is pleasant, so that it be pure,<br/>And between females means no more than this--<br/>  That they have nothing better near, or newer.<br/>"Kiss" rhymes to "bliss" in fact as well as verse--<br/>I wish it never led to something worse.<br/><br/>In perfect innocence she then unmade<br/>  Her toilet, which cost little, for she was<br/>A child of Nature, carelessly arrayed:<br/>  If fond of a chance ogle at her glass,<br/>'T was like the fawn, which, in the lake displayed,<br/>  Beholds her own shy, shadowy image pass,<br/>When first she starts, and then returns to peep,<br/>Admiring this new native of the deep.<br/><br/>And one by one her articles of dress<br/>  Were laid aside; but not before she offered<br/>Her aid to fair Juanna, whose excess<br/>  Of modesty declined the assistance proffered:<br/>Which passed well off--as she could do no less;<br/>  Though by this _politesse_ she rather suffered,<br/>Pricking her fingers with those cursed pins,<br/>Which surely were invented for our sins,--<br/><br/>Making a woman like a porcupine,<br/>  Not to be rashly touched. But still more dread,<br/>Oh ye! whose fate it is, as once 't was mine,<br/>  In early youth, to turn a lady's maid;--<br/>I did my very boyish best to shine<br/>  In tricking her out for a masquerade:<br/>The pins were placed sufficiently, but not<br/>Stuck all exactly in the proper spot.<br/><br/>But these are foolish things to all the wise,<br/>  And I love Wisdom more than she loves me;<br/>My tendency is to philosophise<br/>  On most things, from a tyrant to a tree;<br/>But still the spouseless virgin _Knowledge_ flies.<br/>  What are we? and whence came we? what shall be<br/>Our _ultimate_ existence? what's our present?<br/>Are questions answerless, and yet incessant.<br/><br/>There was deep silence in the chamber: dim<br/>  And distant from each other burned the lights,<br/>And slumber hovered o'er each lovely limb<br/>  Of the fair occupants: if there be sprites,<br/>They should have walked there in their sprightliest trim,<br/>  By way of change from their sepulchral sites,<br/>And shown themselves as ghosts of better taste<br/>Than haunting some old ruin or wild waste.<br/><br/>Many and beautiful lay those around,<br/>  Like flowers of different hue, and clime, and root,<br/>In some exotic garden sometimes found,<br/>  With cost, and care, and warmth induced to shoot.<br/>One with her auburn tresses lightly bound,<br/>  And fair brows gently drooping, as the fruit<br/>Nods from the tree, was slumbering with soft breath,<br/>And lips apart, which showed the pearls beneath.<br/><br/>One with her flushed cheek laid on her white arm,<br/>  And raven ringlets gathered in dark crowd<br/>Above her brow, lay dreaming soft and warm;<br/>  And smiling through her dream, as through a cloud<br/>The moon breaks, half unveiled each further charm,<br/>  As, slightly stirring in her snowy shroud,<br/>Her beauties seized the unconscious hour of night<br/>All bashfully to struggle into light.<br/><br/>This is no bull, although it sounds so; for<br/>  'T was night, but there were lamps, as hath been said.<br/>A third's all pallid aspect offered more<br/>  The traits of sleeping sorrow, and betrayed<br/>Through the heaved breast the dream of some far shore<br/>  Belov√©d and deplored; while slowly strayed<br/>(As night-dew, on a cypress glittering, tinges<br/>The black bough) tear-drops through her eyes' dark fringes.<br/><br/>A fourth as marble, statue-like and still,<br/>  Lay in a breathless, hushed, and stony sleep;<br/>White, cold, and pure, as looks a frozen rill,<br/>  Or the snow minaret on an Alpine steep,<br/>Or Lot's wife done in salt,--or what you will;--<br/>  My similes are gathered in a heap,<br/>So pick and choose--perhaps you'll be content<br/>With a carved lady on a monument.<br/><br/>And lo! a fifth appears;--and what is she?<br/>  A lady of a "certain age," which means<br/>Certainly ag√©d--what her years might be<br/>  I know not, never counting past their teens;<br/>But there she slept, not quite so fair to see,<br/>  As ere that awful period intervenes<br/>Which lays both men and women on the shelf,<br/>To meditate upon their sins and self.<br/><br/>But all this time how slept, or dreamed, Dud√∫?<br/>  With strict inquiry I could ne'er discover,<br/>And scorn to add a syllable untrue;<br/>  But ere the middle watch was hardly over,<br/>Just when the fading lamps waned dim and blue,<br/>  And phantoms hovered, or might seem to hover,<br/>To those who like their company, about<br/>The apartment, on a sudden she screamed out:<br/><br/>And that so loudly, that upstarted all<br/>  The Oda, in a general commotion:<br/>Matron and maids, and those whom you may call<br/>  Neither, came crowding like the waves of Ocean,<br/>One on the other, throughout the whole hall,<br/>  All trembling, wondering, without the least notion<br/>More than I have myself of what could make<br/>The calm Dud√π so turbulently wake.<br/><br/>But wide awake she was, and round her bed.<br/>  With floating draperies and with flying hair,<br/>With eager eyes, and light but hurried tread,<br/>  And bosoms, arms, and ankles glancing bare,<br/>And bright as any meteor ever bred<br/>  By the North Pole,--they sought her cause of care,<br/>For she seemed agitated, flushed, and frightened,<br/>Her eye dilated, and her colour heightened.<br/><br/>But what is strange--and a strong proof how great<br/>  A blessing is sound sleep--Juanna lay<br/>As fast as ever husband by his mate<br/>  In holy matrimony snores away.<br/>Not all the clamour broke her happy state<br/>  Of slumber, ere they shook her,--so they say<br/>At least,--and then she, too, unclosed her eyes,<br/>And yawned a good deal with discreet surprise.<br/><br/>And now commenced a strict investigation,<br/>  Which, as all spoke at once, and more than once<br/>Conjecturing, wondering, asking a narration,<br/>  Alike might puzzle either wit or dunce<br/>To answer in a very clear oration.<br/>  Dud√∫ had never passed for wanting sense,<br/>But being "no orator as Brutus is,"<br/>Could not at first expound what was amiss.<br/><br/>At length she said, that in a slumber sound<br/>  She dreamed a dream, of walking in a wood--<br/>A "wood obscure," like that where Dante found<br/>  Himself in at the age when all grow good;<br/>Life's half-way house, where dames with virtue crowned<br/>  Run much less risk of lovers turning rude;<br/>And that this wood was full of pleasant fruits,<br/>And trees of goodly growth and spreading roots;<br/><br/>And in the midst a golden apple grew,--<br/>  A most prodigious pippin--but it hung<br/>Rather too high and distant; that she threw<br/>  Her glances on it, and then, longing, flung<br/>Stones and whatever she could pick up, to<br/>  Bring down the fruit, which still perversely clung<br/>To its own bough, and dangled yet in sight,<br/>But always at a most provoking height;<br/><br/>That on a sudden, when she least had hope,<br/>  It fell down of its own accord before<br/>Her feet; that her first movement was to stoop<br/>  And pick it up, and bite it to the core;<br/>That just as her young lip began to ope<br/>  Upon the golden fruit the vision bore,<br/>A bee flew out, and stung her to the heart,<br/>And so--she woke with a great scream and start.<br/><br/>All this she told with some confusion and<br/>  Dismay, the usual consequence of dreams<br/>Of the unpleasant kind, with none at hand<br/>  To expound their vain and visionary gleams.<br/>I've known some odd ones which seemed really planned<br/>  Prophetically, or that which one deems<br/>A "strange coincidence," to use a phrase<br/>By which such things are settled now-a-days.<br/><br/>The damsels, who had thoughts of some great harm,<br/>  Began, as is the consequence of fear,<br/>To scold a little at the false alarm<br/>  That broke for nothing on their sleeping ear.<br/>The matron, too, was wroth to leave her warm<br/>  Bed for the dream she had been obliged to hear,<br/>And chafed at poor Dud√π, who only sighed,<br/>And said, that she was sorry she had cried.<br/><br/>"I've heard of stories of a cock and bull;<br/>  But visions of an apple and a bee,<br/>To take us from our natural rest, and pull<br/>  The whole Oda from their beds at half-past three,<br/>Would make us think the moon is at its full.<br/>  You surely are unwell, child! we must see,<br/>To-morrow, what his Highness's physician<br/>Will say to this hysteric of a vision.<br/><br/>"And poor Juanna, too, the child's first night<br/>  Within these walls, to be broke in upon<br/>With such a clamour--I had thought it right<br/>  That the young stranger should not lie alone,<br/>And, as the quietest of all, she might<br/>  With you, Dud√π, a good night's rest have known:<br/>But now I must transfer her to the charge<br/>Of Lolah--though her couch is not so large."<br/><br/>Lolah's eyes sparkled at the proposition;<br/>  But poor Dud√π, with large drops in her own,<br/>Resulting from the scolding or the vision,<br/>  Implored that present pardon might be shown<br/>For this first fault, and that on no condition<br/>  (She added in a soft and piteous tone)<br/>Juanna should be taken from her, and<br/>Her future dreams should be all kept in hand.<br/><br/>She promised never more to have a dream,<br/>  At least to dream so loudly as just now;<br/>She wondered at herself how she could scream--<br/>  'T was foolish, nervous, as she must allow,<br/>A fond hallucination, and a theme<br/>  For laughter--but she felt her spirits low,<br/>And begged they would excuse her; she'd get over<br/>This weakness in a few hours, and recover.<br/><br/>And here Juanna kindly interposed,<br/>  And said she felt herself extremely well<br/>Where she then was, as her sound sleep disclosed,<br/>  When all around rang like a tocsin bell;<br/>She did not find herself the least disposed<br/>  To quit her gentle partner, and to dwell<br/>Apart from one who had no sin to show,<br/>Save that of dreaming once "mal-√†-propos."<br/><br/>As thus Juanna spoke, Dud√π turned round<br/>  And hid her face within Juanna's breast:<br/>Her neck alone was seen, but that was found<br/>  The colour of a budding rose's crest.<br/>I can't tell why she blushed, nor can expound<br/>  The mystery of this rupture of their test;<br/>All that I know is, that the facts I state<br/>Are true as Truth has ever been of late,<br/><br/>And so good night to them,--or, if you will,<br/>  Good morrow--for the cock had crown, and light<br/>Began to clothe each Asiatic hill,<br/>  And the mosque crescent struggled into sight<br/>Of the long caravan, which in the chill<br/>  Of dewy dawn wound slowly round each height<br/>That stretches to the stony belt, which girds<br/>Asia, where Kaff looks down upon the Kurds.<br/><br/>With the first ray, or rather grey of morn,<br/>  Gulbeyaz rose from restlessness; and pale<br/>As Passion rises, with its bosom worn,<br/>  Arrayed herself with mantle, gem, and veil.<br/>The Nightingale that sings with the deep thorn,<br/>  Which fable places in her breast of wail,<br/>Is lighter far of heart and voice than those<br/>Whose headlong passions form their proper woes.<br/><br/>And that's the moral of this composition,<br/>  If people would but see its real drift;--<br/>But _that_ they will not do without suspicion,<br/>  Because all gentle readers have the gift<br/>Of closing 'gainst the light their orbs of vision:<br/>  While gentle writers also love to lift<br/>Their voices 'gainst each other, which is natural,<br/>The numbers are too great for them to flatter all.<br/><br/>Rose the Sultana from a bed of splendour,<br/>  Softer than the soft Sybarite's, who cried<br/>Aloud because his feelings were too tender<br/>  To brook a ruffled rose-leaf by his side,--<br/>So beautiful that Art could little mend her,<br/>  Though pale with conflicts between Love and Pride;--<br/>So agitated was she with her error,<br/>She did not even look into the mirror.<br/><br/>Also arose about the self-same time,<br/>  Perhaps a little later, her great Lord,<br/>Master of thirty kingdoms so sublime,<br/>  And of a wife by whom he was abhorred;<br/>A thing of much less import in that clime--<br/>  At least to those of incomes which afford<br/>The filling up their whole connubial cargo--<br/>Than where two wives are under an embargo.<br/><br/>He did not think much on the matter, nor<br/>  Indeed on any other: as a man<br/>He liked to have a handsome paramour<br/>  At hand, as one may like to have a fan,<br/>And therefore of Circassians had good store,<br/>  As an amusement after the Divan;<br/>Though an unusual fit of love, or duty,<br/>Had made him lately bask in his bride's beauty.<br/><br/>And now he rose; and after due ablutions<br/>  Exacted by the customs of the East,<br/>And prayers and other pious evolutions,<br/>  He drank six cups of coffee at the least,<br/>And then withdrew to hear about the Russians,<br/>  Whose victories had recently increased<br/>In Catherine's reign, whom Glory still adores,<br/>As greatest of all sovereigns and w----s.<br/><br/>But oh, thou grand legitimate Alexander!<br/>  Her son's son, let not this last phrase offend<br/>Thine ear, if it should reach--and now rhymes wander<br/>  Almost as far as Petersburgh, and lend<br/>A dreadful impulse to each loud meander<br/>  Of murmuring Liberty's wide waves, which blend<br/>Their roar even with the Baltic's--so you be<br/>Your father's son, 't is quite enough for me.<br/><br/>To call men love-begotten, or proclaim<br/>  Their mothers as the antipodes of Timon,<br/>That hater of Mankind, would be a shame,<br/>  A libel, or whate'er you please to rhyme on:<br/>But people's ancestors are History's game;<br/>  And if one Lady's slip could leave a crime on<br/>All generations, I should like to know<br/>What pedigree the best would have to show?<br/><br/>Had Catherine and the Sultan understood<br/>  Their own true interests, which Kings rarely know,<br/>Until 't is taught by lessons rather rude,<br/>  There was a way to end their strife, although<br/>Perhaps precarious, had they but thought good,<br/>  Without the aid of Prince or Plenipo:<br/>She to dismiss her guards and he his Harem,<br/>And for their other matters, meet and share 'em.<br/><br/>But as it was, his Highness had to hold<br/>  His daily council upon ways and means<br/>How to encounter with this martial scold,<br/>  This modern Amazon and Queen of queans;<br/>And the perplexity could not be told<br/>  Of all the pillars of the State, which leans<br/>Sometimes a little heavy on the backs<br/>Of those who cannot lay on a new tax.<br/><br/>Meantime Gulbeyaz when her King was gone,<br/>  Retired into her boudoir, a sweet place<br/>For love or breakfast; private, pleasing, lone,<br/>  And rich with all contrivances which grace<br/>Those gay recesses:--many a precious stone<br/>  Sparkled along its roof, and many a vase<br/>Of porcelain held in the fettered flowers,<br/>Those captive soothers of a captive's hours.<br/><br/>Mother of pearl, and porphyry, and marble,<br/>  Vied with each other on this costly spot;<br/>And singing birds without were heard to warble;<br/>  And the stained glass which lighted this fair grot<br/>Varied each ray;--but all descriptions garble<br/>  The true effect, and so we had better not<br/>Be too minute; an outline is the best,--<br/>A lively reader's fancy does the rest.<br/><br/>And here she summoned Baba, and required<br/>  Don Juan at his hands, and information<br/>Of what had passed since all the slaves retired,<br/>  And whether he had occupied their station:<br/>If matters had been managed as desired,<br/>  And his disguise with due consideration<br/>Kept up; and above all, the where and how<br/>He had passed the night, was what she wished to know.<br/><br/>Baba, with some embarrassment, replied<br/>  To this long catechism of questions, asked<br/>More easily than answered,--that he had tried<br/>  His best to obey in what he had been tasked;<br/>But there seemed something that he wished to hide,<br/>  Which Hesitation more betrayed than masked;<br/>He scratched his ear, the infallible resource<br/>To which embarrassed people have recourse.<br/><br/>Gulbeyaz was no model of true patience,<br/>  Nor much disposed to wait in word or deed;<br/>She liked quick answers in all conversations;<br/>  And when she saw him stumbling like a steed<br/>In his replies, she puzzled him for fresh ones;<br/>  And as his speech grew still more broken-kneed,<br/>Her cheek began to flush, her eyes to sparkle,<br/>And her proud brow's blue veins to swell and darkle.<br/><br/>When Baba saw these symptoms, which he knew<br/>  To bode him no great good, he deprecated<br/>Her anger, and beseeched she'd hear him through--<br/>  He could not help the thing which he related:<br/>Then out it came at length, that to Dud√π<br/>  Juan was given in charge, as hath been stated;<br/>But not by Baba's fault, he said, and swore on<br/>The holy camel's hump, besides the Koran.<br/><br/>The chief dame of the Oda, upon whom<br/>  The discipline of the whole Harem bore,<br/>As soon as they re-entered their own room,<br/>  For Baba's function stopped short at the door,<br/>Had settled all; nor could he then presume<br/>  (The aforesaid Baba) just then to do more,<br/>Without exciting such suspicion as<br/>Might make the matter still worse than it was.<br/><br/>He hoped, indeed he thought, he could be sure,<br/>  Juan had not betrayed himself; in fact<br/>'T was certain that his conduct had been pure,<br/>  Because a foolish or imprudent act<br/>Would not alone have made him insecure,<br/>  But ended in his being found out and _sacked,_<br/>And thrown into the sea.--Thus Baba spoke<br/>Of all save Dud√π's dream, which was no joke.<br/><br/>This he discreetly kept in the back ground,<br/>  And talked away--and might have talked till now,<br/>For any further answer that he found,<br/>  So deep an anguish wrung Gulbeyaz' brow:<br/>Her cheek turned ashes, ears rung, brain whirled round,<br/>  As if she had received a sudden blow,<br/>And the heart's dew of pain sprang fast and chilly<br/>O'er her fair front, like Morning's on a lily.<br/><br/>Although she was not of the fainting sort,<br/>  Baba thought she would faint, but there he erred--<br/>It was but a convulsion, which though short<br/>  Can never be described; we all have heard,<br/>And some of us have felt thus "_all amort_"<br/>  When things beyond the common have occurred;--<br/>Gulbeyaz proved in that brief agony<br/>What she could ne'er express--then how should I?<br/><br/>She stood a moment as a Pythoness<br/>  Stands on her tripod, agonized, and full<br/>Of inspiration gathered from distress,<br/>  When all the heart-strings like wild horses pull<br/>The heart asunder;--then, as more or less<br/>  Their speed abated or their strength grew dull,<br/>She sunk down on her seat by slow degrees,<br/>And bowed her throbbing head o'er trembling knees.<br/><br/>Her face declined and was unseen; her hair<br/>  Fell in long tresses like the weeping willow,<br/>Sweeping the marble underneath her chair,<br/>  Or rather sofa (for it was all pillow,<br/>A low, soft ottoman), and black Despair<br/>  Stirred up and down her bosom like a billow,<br/>Which rushes to some shore whose shingles check<br/>Its farther course, but must receive its wreck.<br/><br/>Her head hung down, and her long hair in stooping<br/>  Concealed her features better than a veil;<br/>And one hand o'er the ottoman lay drooping,<br/>  White, waxen, and as alabaster pale:<br/>Would that I were a painter! to be grouping<br/>  All that a poet drags into detail!<br/>Oh that my words were colours! but their tints<br/>May serve perhaps as outlines or slight hints.<br/><br/>Baba, who knew by experience when to talk<br/>  And when to hold his tongue, now held it till<br/>This passion might blow o'er, nor dared to balk<br/>  Gulbeyaz' taciturn or speaking will.<br/>At length she rose up, and began to walk<br/>  Slowly along the room, but silent still,<br/>And her brow cleared, but not her troubled eye;<br/>The wind was down, but still the sea ran high.<br/><br/>She stopped, and raised her head to speak-but paused<br/>  And then moved on again with rapid pace;<br/>Then slackened it, which is the march most caused<br/>  By deep emotion:--you may sometimes trace<br/>A feeling in each footstep, as disclosed<br/>  By Sallust in his Catiline, who, chased<br/>By all the demons of all passions, showed<br/>Their work even by the way in which he trode.<br/><br/>Gulbeyaz stopped and beckoned Baba:--"Slave!<br/>  Bring the two slaves!" she said in a low tone,<br/>But one which Baba did not like to brave,<br/>  And yet he shuddered, and seemed rather prone<br/>To prove reluctant, and begged leave to crave<br/>  (Though he well knew the meaning) to be shown<br/>What slaves her Highness wished to indicate,<br/>For fear of any error, like the late.<br/><br/>"The Georgian and her paramour," replied<br/>  The Imperial Bride--and added, "Let the boat<br/>Be ready by the secret portal's side:<br/>  You know the rest." The words stuck in her throat,<br/>Despite her injured love and fiery pride;<br/>  And of this Baba willingly took note,<br/>And begged by every hair of Mahomet's beard,<br/>She would revoke the order he had heard.<br/><br/>"To hear is to obey," he said; "but still,<br/>  Sultana, think upon the consequence:<br/>It is not that I shall not all fulfil<br/>  Your orders, even in their severest sense;<br/>But such precipitation may end ill,<br/>  Even at your own imperative expense:<br/>I do not mean destruction and exposure,<br/>In case of any premature disclosure;<br/><br/>"But your own feelings. Even should all the rest<br/>  Be hidden by the rolling waves, which hide<br/>Already many a once love-beaten breast<br/>  Deep in the caverns of the deadly tide--<br/>You love this boyish, new, Seraglio guest,<br/>  And if this violent remedy be tried--<br/>Excuse my freedom, when I here assure you,<br/>That killing him is not the way to cure you."<br/><br/>"What dost thou know of Love or feeling?--Wretch!<br/>  Begone!" she cried, with kindling eyes--"and do<br/>My bidding!" Baba vanished, for to stretch<br/>  His own remonstrance further he well knew<br/>Might end in acting as his own "Jack Ketch;"<br/>  And though he wished extremely to get through<br/>This awkward business without harm to others,<br/>He still preferred his own neck to another's.<br/><br/>Away he went then upon his commission,<br/>  Growling and grumbling in good Turkish phrase<br/>Against all women of whate'er condition,<br/>  Especially Sultanas and their ways;<br/>Their obstinacy, pride, and indecision,<br/>  Their never knowing their own mind two days,<br/>The trouble that they gave, their immorality,<br/>Which made him daily bless his own neutrality.<br/><br/>And then he called his brethren to his aid,<br/>  And sent one on a summons to the pair,<br/>That they must instantly be well arrayed,<br/>  And above all be combed even to a hair,<br/>And brought before the Empress, who had made<br/>  Inquiries after them with kindest care:<br/>At which Dud√π looked strange, and Juan silly;<br/>But go they must at once, and will I--nill I.<br/><br/>And here I leave them at their preparation<br/>  For the imperial presence, wherein whether<br/>Gulbeyaz showed them both commiseration,<br/>  Or got rid of the parties altogether,<br/>Like other angry ladies of her nation,--<br/>  Are things the turning of a hair or feather<br/>May settle; but far be 't from me to anticipate<br/>In what way feminine caprice may dissipate.<br/><br/>I leave them for the present with good wishes,<br/>  Though doubts of their well doing, to arrange<br/>Another part of History; for the dishes<br/>  Of this our banquet we must sometimes change;<br/>And trusting Juan may escape the fishes,<br/>  (Although his situation now seems strange,<br/>And scarce secure),--as such digressions _are_ fair,<br/>The Muse will take a little touch at warfare.<br/><br/>CANTO THE SEVENTH.<br/><br/>O LOVE! O Glory! what are ye who fly<br/>  Around us ever, rarely to alight?<br/>There's not a meteor in the polar sky<br/>  Of such transcendent and more fleeting flight.<br/>Chill, and chained to cold earth, we lift on high<br/>  Our eyes in search of either lovely light;<br/>A thousand and a thousand colours they<br/>Assume, then leave us on our freezing way.<br/><br/>And such as they are, such my present tale is,<br/>  A nondescript and ever-varying rhyme,<br/>A versified Aurora Borealis,<br/>  Which flashes o'er a waste and icy clime.<br/>When we know what all are, we must bewail us,<br/>  But ne'ertheless I hope it is no crime<br/>To laugh at _all_ things--for I wish to know<br/>_What_, after _all_, are _all_ things--but a _show_?<br/><br/>They accuse me--_Me_--the present writer of<br/>  The present poem--of--I know not what--A<br/>tendency to under-rate and scoff<br/>  At human power and virtue, and all that;<br/>And this they say in language rather rough.<br/>  Good God! I wonder what they would be at!<br/>I say no more than hath been said in Dant√©'s<br/>Verse, and by Solomon and by Cervantes;<br/><br/>By Swift, by Machiavel, by Rochefoucault,<br/>  By F√©n√©lon, by Luther, and by Plato;<br/>By Tillotson, and Wesley, and Rousseau,<br/>  Who knew this life was not worth a potato.<br/>'T is not their fault, nor mine, if this be so,--<br/>  For my part, I pretend not to be Cato,<br/>Nor even Diogenes.--We live and die,<br/>But which is best, _you_ know no more than I.<br/><br/>Socrates said, our only knowledge was<br/>  "To know that nothing could be known;" a pleasant<br/>Science enough, which levels to an ass<br/>  Each man of wisdom, future, past, or present.<br/>Newton (that proverb of the mind), alas!<br/>  Declared, with all his grand discoveries recent,<br/>That he himself felt only "like a youth<br/>Picking up shells by the great ocean--Truth."<br/><br/>Ecclesiastes said, "that all is vanity"--<br/>  Most modern preachers say the same, or show it<br/>By their examples of true Christianity:<br/>  In short, all know, or very soon may know it;<br/>And in this scene of all-confessed inanity,<br/>  By Saint, by Sage, by Preacher, and by Poet,<br/>Must I restrain me, through the fear of strife,<br/>From holding up the nothingness of Life?<br/><br/>Dogs, or men!--for I flatter you in saying<br/>  That ye are dogs--your betters far--ye may<br/>Read, or read not, what I am now essaying<br/>  To show ye what ye are in every way.<br/>As little as the moon stops for the baying<br/>  Of wolves, will the bright Muse withdraw one ray<br/>From out her skies--then howl your idle wrath!<br/>While she still silvers o'er your gloomy path.<br/><br/>"Fierce loves and faithless wars"--I am not sure<br/>  If this be the right reading--'t is no matter;<br/>The fact's about the same, I am secure;<br/>  I sing them both, and am about to batter<br/>A town which did a famous siege endure,<br/>  And was beleaguered both by land and water<br/>By Souvaroff, or Anglic√® Suwarrow,<br/>Who loved blood as an alderman loves marrow.<br/><br/>The fortress is called Ismail, and is placed<br/>  Upon the Danube's left branch and left bank,<br/>With buildings in the Oriental taste,<br/>  But still a fortress of the foremost rank,<br/>Or was at least, unless 't is since defaced,<br/>  Which with your conquerors is a common prank:<br/>It stands some eighty versts from the high sea,<br/>And measures round of toises thousands three.<br/><br/>Within the extent of this fortification<br/>  A borough is comprised along the height<br/>Upon the left, which from its loftier station<br/>  Commands the city, and upon its site<br/>A Greek had raised around this elevation<br/>  A quantity of palisades _upright_,<br/>So placed as to _impede_ the fire of those<br/>Who held the place, and to _assist_ the foe's.<br/><br/>This circumstance may serve to give a notion<br/>  Of the high talents of this new Vauban:<br/>But the town ditch below was deep as Ocean,<br/>  The rampart higher than you'd wish to hang:<br/>But then there was a great want of precaution<br/>  (Prithee, excuse this engineering slang),<br/>Nor work advanced, nor covered way was there,<br/>To hint, at least, "Here is no thoroughfare."<br/><br/>But a stone bastion, with a narrow gorge,<br/>  And walls as thick as most skulls born as yet;<br/>Two batteries, cap-√†-pie, as our St. George,<br/>  Casemated one, and t' other "a barbette,"<br/>Of Danube's bank took formidable charge;<br/>  While two-and-twenty cannon duly set<br/>Rose over the town's right side, in bristling tier,<br/>Forty feet high, upon a cavalier.<br/><br/>But from the river the town's open quite,<br/>  Because the Turks could never be persuaded<br/>A Russian vessel e'er would heave in sight;<br/>  And such their creed was till they were invaded,<br/>When it grew rather late to set things right:<br/>  But as the Danube could not well be waded,<br/>They looked upon the Muscovite flotilla,<br/>And only shouted, "Allah!" and "Bis Millah!"<br/><br/>The Russians now were ready to attack;<br/>  But oh, ye goddesses of War and Glory!<br/>How shall I spell the name of each Cossacque<br/>  Who were immortal, could one tell their story?<br/>Alas! what to their memory can lack?<br/>  Achilles' self was not more grim and gory<br/>Than thousands of this new and polished nation,<br/>Whose names want nothing but--pronunciation.<br/><br/>Still I'll record a few, if but to increase<br/>  Our euphony: there was Strongenoff, and Strokonoff,<br/>Meknop, Serge Lwow, Ars√©niew of modern Greece,<br/>  And Tschitsshakoff, and Roguenoff, and Chokenoff,<br/>And others of twelve consonants apiece;<br/>  And more might be found out, if I could poke enough<br/>Into gazettes; but Fame (capricious strumpet),<br/>It seems, has got an ear as well as trumpet,<br/><br/>And cannot tune those discords of narration,<br/>  Which may be names at Moscow, into rhyme;<br/>Yet there were several worth commemoration,<br/>  As e'er was virgin of a nuptial chime;<br/>Soft words, too, fitted for the peroration<br/>  Of Londonderry drawling against time,<br/>Ending in "ischskin," "ousckin," "iffskchy," "ouski,"<br/>Of whom we can insert but Rousamouski,<br/><br/>Scherematoff and Chrematoff, Koklophti,<br/>  Koclobski, Kourakin, and Mouskin Pouskin,<br/>All proper men of weapons, as e'er scoffed high<br/>  Against a foe, or ran a sabre through skin:<br/>Little cared they for Mahomet or Mufti,<br/>  Unless to make their kettle-drums a new skin<br/>Out of their hides, if parchment had grown dear,<br/>And no more handy substitute been near.<br/><br/>Then there were foreigners of much renown,<br/>  Of various nations, and all volunteers;<br/>Not fighting for their country or its crown,<br/>  But wishing to be one day brigadiers;<br/>Also to have the sacking of a town;--<br/>  A pleasant thing to young men at their years.<br/>'Mongst them were several Englishmen of pith,<br/>Sixteen called Thomson, and nineteen named Smith.<br/><br/>Jack Thomson and Bill Thomson;--all the rest<br/>  Had been called _"Jemmy,"_ after the great bard;<br/>I don't know whether they had arms or crest,<br/>  But such a godfather's as good a card.<br/>Three of the Smiths were Peters; but the best<br/>  Amongst them all, hard blows to inflict or ward,<br/>Was _he_, since so renowned "in country quarters<br/>At Halifax;" but now he served the Tartars.<br/><br/>The rest were Jacks and Gills and Wills and Bills,<br/>  But when I've added that the elder Jack Smith<br/>Was born in Cumberland among the hills,<br/>  And that his father was an honest blacksmith,<br/>I've said all _I_ know of a name that fills<br/>  Three lines of the despatch in taking "Schmacksmith,"<br/>A village of Moldavia's waste, wherein<br/>He fell, immortal in a bulletin.<br/><br/>I wonder (although Mars no doubt's a god I<br/>  Praise) if a man's name in a _bulletin_<br/>May make up for a _bullet in_ his body?<br/>  I hope this little question is no sin,<br/>Because, though I am but a simple noddy,<br/>  I think one Shakespeare puts the same thought in<br/>The mouth of some one in his plays so doting,<br/>Which many people pass for wits by quoting.<br/><br/>Then there were Frenchmen, gallant, young, and gay;<br/>  But I'm too great a patriot to record<br/>Their Gallic names upon a glorious day;<br/>  I'd rather tell ten lies than say a word<br/>Of truth;--such truths are treason; they betray<br/>  Their country; and as traitors are abhorred,<br/>Who name the French in English, save to show<br/>How Peace should make John Bull the Frenchman's foe.<br/><br/>The Russians, having built two batteries on<br/>  An isle near Ismail, had two ends in view;<br/>The first was to bombard it, and knock down<br/>  The public buildings and the private too,<br/>No matter what poor souls might be undone:<br/>  The city's shape suggested this, 't is true,<br/>Formed like an amphitheatre--each dwelling<br/>Presented a fine mark to throw a shell in.<br/><br/>The second object was to profit by<br/>  The moment of the general consternation,<br/>To attack the Turk's flotilla, which lay nigh<br/>  Extremely tranquil, anchored at its station:<br/>But a third motive was as probably<br/>  To frighten them into capitulation;<br/>A phantasy which sometimes seizes warriors,<br/>Unless they are game as bull-dogs and fox-terriers.<br/><br/>A habit rather blameable, which is<br/>  That of despising those we combat with,<br/>Common in many cases, was in this<br/>  The cause of killing Tchitchitzkoff and Smith--<br/>One of the valorous "Smiths" whom we shall miss<br/>  Out of those nineteen who late rhymed to "pith;"<br/>But 't is a name so spread o'er "Sir" and "Madam,"<br/>That one would think the _first_ who bore it _"Adam."_<br/><br/>The Russian batteries were incomplete,<br/>  Because they were constructed in a hurry;<br/>Thus the same cause which makes a verse want feet,<br/>  And throws a cloud o'er Longman and John Murray,<br/>When the sale of new books is not so fleet<br/>  As they who print them think is necessary,<br/>May likewise put off for a time what story<br/>Sometimes calls "Murder," and at others "Glory."<br/><br/>Whether it was their engineer's stupidity,<br/>  Their haste or waste, I neither know nor care,<br/>Or some contractor's personal cupidity,<br/>  Saving his soul by cheating in the ware<br/>Of homicide, but there was no solidity<br/>  In the new batteries erected there;<br/>They either missed, or they were never missed,<br/>And added greatly to the missing list.<br/><br/>A sad miscalculation about distance<br/>  Made all their naval matters incorrect;<br/>Three fireships lost their amiable existence<br/>  Before they reached a spot to take effect;<br/>The match was lit too soon, and no assistance<br/>  Could remedy this lubberly defect;<br/>They blew up in the middle of the river,<br/>While, though 't was dawn, the Turks slept fast as ever.<br/><br/>At seven they rose, however, and surveyed<br/>  The Russ flotilla getting under way;<br/>'T was nine, when still advancing undismayed,<br/>  Within a cable's length their vessels lay<br/>Off Ismail, and commenced a cannonade,<br/>  Which was returned with interest, I may say,<br/>And by a fire of musketry and grape,<br/>And shells and shot of every size and shape.<br/><br/>For six hours bore they without intermission<br/>  The Turkish fire, and, aided by their own<br/>Land batteries, worked their guns with great precision;<br/>  At length they found mere cannonade alone<br/>By no means would produce the town's submission,<br/>  And made a signal to retreat at one.<br/>One bark blew up, a second near the works<br/>Running aground, was taken by the Turks.<br/><br/>The Moslem, too, had lost both ships and men;<br/>  But when they saw the enemy retire,<br/>Their Delhis manned some boats, and sailed again,<br/>  And galled the Russians with a heavy fire,<br/>And tried to make a landing on the main;<br/>  But here the effect fell short of their desire:<br/>Count Damas drove them back into the water<br/>Pell-mell, and with a whole gazette of slaughter.<br/><br/>"If" (says the historian here) "I could report<br/>  All that the Russians did upon this day,<br/>I think that several volumes would fall short,<br/>  And I should still have many things to say;"<br/>And so he says no more--but pays his court<br/>  To some distinguished strangers in that fray;<br/>The Prince de Ligne, and Langeron, and Damas,<br/>Names great as any that the roll of Fame has.<br/><br/>This being the case, may show us what Fame _is_:<br/>  For out of these three "_preux Chevaliers_," how<br/>Many of common readers give a guess<br/>  That such existed? (and they may live now<br/>For aught we know.) Renown's all hit or miss;<br/>  There's fortune even in Fame, we must allow.<br/>'T is true, the Memoirs of the Prince de Ligne<br/>Have half withdrawn from _him_ Oblivion's screen.<br/><br/>But here are men who fought in gallant actions<br/>  As gallantly as ever heroes fought,<br/>But buried in the heap of such transactions<br/>  Their names are rarely found, nor often sought.<br/>Thus even good fame may suffer sad contractions,<br/>  And is extinguished sooner than she ought:<br/>Of all our modern battles, I will bet<br/>You can't repeat nine names from each Gazette.<br/><br/>In short, this last attack, though rich in glory,<br/>  Showed that _somewhere, somehow_, there was a fault,<br/>And Admiral Ribas (known in Russian story)<br/>  Most strongly recommended an assault;<br/>In which he was opposed by young and hoary,<br/>  Which made a long debate; but I must halt,<br/>For if I wrote down every warrior's speech,<br/>I doubt few readers e'er would mount the breach.<br/><br/>There was a man, if that he was a man,<br/>  Not that his manhood could be called in question,<br/>For had he not been Hercules, his span<br/>  Had been as short in youth as indigestion<br/>Made his last illness, when, all worn and wan,<br/>  He died beneath a tree, as much unblest on<br/>The soil of the green province he had wasted,<br/>As e'er was locust on the land it blasted.<br/><br/>This was Potemkin--a great thing in days<br/>  When homicide and harlotry made great;<br/>If stars and titles could entail long praise,<br/>  His glory might half equal his estate.<br/>This fellow, being six foot high, could raise<br/>  A kind of phantasy proportionate<br/>In the then Sovereign of the Russian people,<br/>Who measured men as you would do a steeple.<br/><br/>While things were in abeyance, Ribas sent<br/>  A courier to the Prince, and he succeeded<br/>In ordering matters after his own bent;<br/>  I cannot tell the way in which he pleaded,<br/>But shortly he had cause to be content.<br/>  In the mean time, the batteries proceeded,<br/>And fourscore cannon on the Danube's border<br/>Were briskly fired and answered in due order.<br/><br/>But on the thirteenth, when already part<br/>  Of the troops were embarked, the siege to raise,<br/>A courier on the spur inspired new heart<br/>  Into all panters for newspaper praise,<br/>As well as dilettanti in War's art,<br/>  By his despatches (couched in pithy phrase)<br/>Announcing the appointment of that lover of<br/>Battles to the command, Field-Marshal Souvaroff.<br/><br/>The letter of the Prince to the same Marshal<br/>  Was worthy of a Spartan, had the cause<br/>Been one to which a good heart could be partial--<br/>  Defence of freedom, country, or of laws;<br/>But as it was mere lust of Power to o'er-arch all<br/>  With its proud brow, it merits slight applause,<br/>Save for its style, which said, all in a trice,<br/>"You will take Ismail at whatever price."<br/><br/>"Let there be Light! said God, and there was Light!"<br/>  "Let there be Blood!" says man, and there's a sea!<br/>The fiat of this spoiled child of the Night<br/>  (For Day ne'er saw his merits) could decree<br/>More evil in an hour, than thirty bright<br/>  Summers could renovate, though they should be<br/>Lovely as those which ripened Eden's fruit;<br/>For War cuts up not only branch, but root.<br/><br/>Our friends, the Turks, who with loud "Allahs" now<br/>  Began to signalise the Russ retreat,<br/>Were damnably mistaken; few are slow<br/>  In thinking that their enemy is beat,<br/>(Or _beaten_, if you insist on grammar, though<br/>  I never think about it in a heat,)<br/>But here I say the Turks were much mistaken,<br/>Who hating hogs, yet wished to save their bacon.<br/><br/>For, on the sixteenth, at full gallop, drew<br/>  In sight two horsemen, who were deemed Cossacques<br/>For some time, till they came in nearer view:<br/>  They had but little baggage at their backs,<br/>For there were but _three_ shirts between the two;<br/>
