But on they rode upon two Ukraine hacks,<br/>Till, in approaching, were at length descried<br/>In this plain pair, Suwarrow and his guide.<br/><br/>"Great joy to London now!" says some great fool,<br/>  When London had a grand illumination,<br/>Which to that bottle-conjuror, John Bull,<br/>  Is of all dreams the first hallucination;<br/>So that the streets of coloured lamps are full,<br/>  That sage (said John) surrenders at discretion<br/>His purse, his soul, his sense, and even his nonsense,<br/>To gratify, like a huge moth, this _one_ sense.<br/><br/>'T is strange that he should further "Damn his eyes,"<br/>  For they are damned; that once all-famous oath<br/>Is to the Devil now no further prize,<br/>  Since John has lately lost the use of both.<br/>Debt he calls Wealth, and taxes Paradise;<br/>  And Famine, with her gaunt and bony growth,<br/>Which stare him in the face, he won't examine,<br/>Or swears that Ceres hath begotten Famine.<br/><br/>But to the tale;--great joy unto the camp!<br/>  To Russian, Tartar, English, French, Cossacque,<br/>O'er whom Suwarrow shone like a gas lamp,<br/>  Presaging a most luminous attack;<br/>Or like a wisp along the marsh so damp,<br/>  Which leads beholders on a boggy walk,<br/>He flitted to and fro a dancing light,<br/>Which all who saw it followed, wrong or right.<br/><br/>But, certes, matters took a different face;<br/>  There was enthusiasm and much applause,<br/>The fleet and camp saluted with great grace,<br/>  And all presaged good fortune to their cause.<br/>Within a cannot-shot length of the place<br/>  They drew, constructed ladders, repaired flaws<br/>In former works, made new, prepared fascines,<br/>And all kinds of benevolent machines.<br/><br/>'T is thus the spirit of a single mind<br/>  Makes that of multitudes take one direction,<br/>As roll the waters to the breathing wind,<br/>  Or roams the herd beneath the bull's protection;<br/>Or as a little dog will lead the blind,<br/>  Or a bell-wether form the flock's connection<br/>By tinkling sounds, when they go forth to victual;<br/>Such is the sway of your great men o'er little.<br/><br/>The whole camp rung with joy; you would have thought<br/>  That they were going to a marriage feast<br/>(This metaphor, I think, holds good as aught,<br/>  Since there is discord after both at least):<br/>There was not now a luggage boy but sought<br/>  Danger and spoil with ardour much increased;<br/>And why? because a little--odd--old man,<br/>Stripped to his shirt, was come to lead the van.<br/><br/>But so it was; and every preparation<br/>  Was made with all alacrity: the first<br/>Detachment of three columns took its station,<br/>  And waited but the signal's voice to burst<br/>Upon the foe: the second's ordination<br/>  Was also in three columns, with a thirst<br/>For Glory gaping o'er a sea of Slaughter:<br/>The third, in columns two, attacked by water.<br/><br/>New batteries were erected, and was held<br/>  A general council, in which Unanimity,<br/>That stranger to most councils, here prevailed,<br/>  As sometimes happens in a great extremity;<br/>And every difficulty being dispelled,<br/>  Glory began to dawn with due sublimity,<br/>While Souvaroff, determined to obtain it,<br/>Was teaching his recruits to use the bayonet.<br/><br/>It is an actual fact, that he, commander<br/>  In chief, in proper person deigned to drill<br/>The awkward squad, and could afford to squander<br/>  His time, a corporal's duty to fulfil;<br/>Just as you'd break a sucking salamander<br/>  To swallow flame, and never take it ill:<br/>He showed them how to mount a ladder (which<br/>Was not like Jacob's) or to cross a ditch.<br/><br/>Also he dressed up, for the nonce, fascines<br/>  Like men with turbans, scimitars, and dirks,<br/>And made them charge with bayonet these machines,<br/>  By way of lesson against actual Turks;<br/>And when well practised in these mimic scenes,<br/>  He judged them proper to assail the works,--<br/>(At which your wise men sneered in phrases witty),<br/>He made no answer--but he took the city.<br/><br/>Most things were in this posture on the eve<br/>  Of the assault, and all the camp was in<br/>A stern repose; which you would scarce conceive;<br/>  Yet men resolved to dash through thick and thin<br/>Are very silent when they once believe<br/>  That all is settled:--there was little din,<br/>For some were thinking of their home and friends,<br/>And others of themselves and latter ends.<br/><br/>Suwarrow chiefly was on the alert,<br/>  Surveying, drilling, ordering, jesting, pondering;<br/>For the man was, we safely may assert,<br/>  A thing to wonder at beyond most wondering;<br/>Hero, buffoon, half-demon, and half-dirt,<br/>  Praying, instructing, desolating, plundering--Now<br/>Mars, now Momus--and when bent to storm<br/>A fortress, Harlequin in uniform.<br/><br/>The day before the assault, while upon drill--<br/>  For this great conqueror played the corporal--<br/>Some Cossacques, hovering like hawks round a hill,<br/>  Had met a party towards the Twilight's fall,<br/>One of whom spoke their tongue--or well or ill,<br/>  'T was much that he was understood at all;<br/>But whether from his voice, or speech, or manner,<br/>They found that he had fought beneath their banner.<br/><br/>Whereon immediately at his request<br/>  They brought him and his comrades to head-quarters;<br/>Their dress was Moslem, but you might have guessed<br/>  That these were merely masquerading Tartars,<br/>And that beneath each Turkish-fashioned vest<br/>  Lurked Christianity--which sometimes barters<br/>Her inward grace for outward show, and makes<br/>It difficult to shun some strange mistakes.<br/><br/>Suwarrow, who was standing in his shirt<br/>  Before a company of Calmucks, drilling,<br/>Exclaiming, fooling, swearing at the inert,<br/>  And lecturing on the noble art of killing,--<br/>For deeming human clay but common dirt<br/>  This great philosopher was thus instilling<br/>His maxims, which to martial comprehension<br/>Proved death in battle equal to a pension;--<br/><br/>Suwarrow, when he saw this company<br/>  Of Cossacques and their prey, turned round and cast<br/>Upon them his slow brow and piercing eye:--<br/>  "Whence come ye?"--"From Constantinople last,<br/>Captives just now escaped," was the reply.<br/>  "What are ye?"--"What you see us." Briefly passed<br/>This dialogue; for he who answered knew<br/>To whom he spoke, and made his words but few.<br/><br/>"Your names?"--"Mine's Johnson, and my comrade's Juan;<br/>  The other two are women, and the third<br/>Is neither man nor woman." The Chief threw on<br/>  The party a slight glance, then said," I have heard<br/>_Your_ name before, the second is a new one:<br/>  To bring the other three here was absurd:<br/>But let that pass:--I think I have heard your name<br/>In the Nikolaiew regiment?"--"The same."<br/><br/>"You served at Widdin?"--"Yes."--"You led the attack?"<br/>  "I did."--"What next?"--"I really hardly know"--<br/>"You were the first i' the breach?"--"I was not slack<br/>  At least to follow those who might be so"--"What<br/>followed?"--"A shot laid me on my back,<br/>  And I became a prisoner to the foe"--<br/>"You shall have vengeance, for the town surrounded<br/>Is twice as strong as that where you were wounded.<br/><br/>"Where will you serve?"--"Where'er you please."--"I know<br/>  You like to be the hope of the forlorn,<br/>And doubtless would be foremost on the foe<br/>  After the hardships you've already borne.<br/>And this young fellow--say what can he do?<br/>  He with the beardless chin and garments torn?"--<br/>"Why, General, if he hath no greater fault<br/>In War than Love, he had better lead the assault"--<br/><br/>"He shall if that he dare." Here Juan bowed<br/>  Low as the compliment deserved. Suwarrow<br/>Continued: "Your old regiment's allowed,<br/>  By special providence, to lead to-morrow,<br/>Or, it may be, to-night, the assault: I have vowed<br/>  To several Saints, that shortly plough or harrow<br/>Shall pass o'er what was Ismail, and its tusk<br/>Be unimpeded by the proudest mosque.<br/><br/>"So now, my lads, for Glory!"--Here he turned<br/>  And drilled away in the most classic Russian,<br/>Until each high heroic bosom burned<br/>  For cash and conquest, as if from a cushion<br/>A preacher had held forth (who nobly spurned<br/>  All earthly goods save tithes) and bade them push on<br/>To slay the Pagans who resisted, battering<br/>The armies of the Christian Empress Catherine.<br/><br/>Johnson, who knew by this long colloquy<br/>  Himself a favourite, ventured to address<br/>Suwarrow, though engaged with accents high<br/>  In his resumed amusement. "I confess<br/>My debt in being thus allowed to die<br/>  Among the foremost; but if you'd express<br/>Explicitly our several posts, my friend<br/>And self would know what duty to attend."<br/><br/>"Right! I was busy, and forgot. Why, you<br/>  Will join your former regiment, which should be<br/>Now under arms. Ho! Katskoff, take him to"--<br/>  (Here he called up a Polish orderly)<br/>"His post, I mean the regiment Nikolaiew:<br/>  The stranger stripling may remain with me;<br/>He's a fine boy. The women may be sent<br/>To the other baggage, or to the sick tent."<br/><br/>But here a sort of scene began to ensue:<br/>  The ladies,--who by no means had been bred<br/>To be disposed of in a way so new,<br/>  Although their Harem education led,<br/>Doubtless, to that of doctrines the most true,<br/>  Passive obedience,--now raised up the head<br/>With flashing eyes and starting tears, and flung<br/>Their arms, as hens their wings about their young,<br/><br/>O'er the promoted couple of brave men<br/>  Who were thus honoured by the greatest Chief<br/>That ever peopled Hell with heroes slain,<br/>  Or plunged a province or a realm in grief.<br/>Oh, foolish mortals! Always taught in vain!<br/>  Oh, glorious Laurel! since for one sole leaf<br/>Of thine imaginary deathless tree,<br/>Of blood and tears must flow the unebbing sea.<br/><br/>Suwarrow, who had small regard for tears,<br/>  And not much sympathy for blood, surveyed<br/>The women with their hair about their ears<br/>  And natural agonies, with a slight shade<br/>Of feeling: for however Habit sears<br/>  Men's hearts against whole millions, when their trade<br/>Is butchery, sometimes a single sorrow<br/>Will touch even heroes--and such was Suwarrow.<br/><br/>He said,--and in the kindest Calmuck tone,--<br/>  "Why, Johnson, what the devil do you mean<br/>By bringing women here? They shall be shown<br/>  All the attention possible, and seen<br/>In safety to the waggons, where alone<br/>  In fact they can be safe. You should have been<br/>Aware this kind of baggage never thrives;<br/>Save wed a year, I hate recruits with wives"--<br/><br/>"May it please your Excellency," thus replied<br/>  Our British friend, "these are the wives of others,<br/>And not our own. I am too qualified<br/>  By service with my military brothers<br/>To break the rules by bringing one's own bride<br/>  Into a camp: I know that nought so bothers<br/>The hearts of the heroic on a charge,<br/>As leaving a small family at large.<br/><br/>"But these are but two Turkish ladies, who<br/>  With their attendant aided our escape,<br/>And afterwards accompanied us through<br/>  A thousand perils in this dubious shape.<br/>To me this kind of life is not so new;<br/>  To them, poor things, it is an awkward scrape:<br/>I therefore, if you wish me to fight freely,<br/>Request that they may both be used genteelly."<br/><br/>Meantime these two poor girls, with swimming eyes,<br/>  Looked on as if in doubt if they could trust<br/>Their own protectors; nor was their surprise<br/>  Less than their grief (and truly not less just)<br/>To see an old man, rather wild than wise<br/>  In aspect, plainly clad, besmeared with dust,<br/>Stripped to his waistcoat, and that not too clean,<br/>More feared than all the Sultans ever seen.<br/><br/>For everything seemed resting on his nod,<br/>  As they could read in all eyes. Now to them,<br/>Who were accustomed, as a sort of god,<br/>  To see the Sultan, rich in many a gem,<br/>Like an imperial peacock stalk abroad<br/>  (That royal bird, whose tail's a diadem,)<br/>With all the pomp of Power, it was a doubt<br/>How Power could condescend to do without.<br/><br/>John Johnson, seeing their extreme dismay,<br/>  Though little versed in feelings oriental,<br/>Suggested some slight comfort in his way:<br/>  Don Juan, who was much more sentimental,<br/>Swore they should see him by the dawn of day,<br/>  Or that the Russian army should repent all:<br/>And, strange to say, they found some consolation<br/>In this--for females like exaggeration.<br/><br/>And then with tears, and sighs, and some slight kisses,<br/>  They parted for the present--these to await,<br/>According to the artillery's hits or misses,<br/>  What sages call Chance, Providence, or Fate--<br/>(Uncertainty is one of many blisses,<br/>  A mortgage on Humanity's estate;)<br/>While their belov√©d friends began to arm,<br/>To burn a town which never did them harm.<br/><br/>Suwarrow,--who but saw things in the gross.<br/>  Being much too gross to see them in detail,<br/>Who calculated life as so much dross,<br/>  And as the wind a widowed nation's wail,<br/>And cared as little for his army's loss<br/>  (So that their efforts should at length prevail)<br/>As wife and friends did for the boils of Job,--<br/>What was 't to him to hear two women sob?<br/><br/>Nothing.--The work of Glory still went on<br/>  In preparations for a cannonade<br/>As terrible as that of Ilion,<br/>  If Homer had found mortars ready made;<br/>But now, instead of slaying Priam's son,<br/>  We only can but talk of escalade,<br/>Bombs, drums, guns, bastions, batteries, bayonets, bullets--<br/>Hard words, which stick in the soft Muses' gullets.<br/><br/>Oh, thou eternal Homer! who couldst charm<br/>  All ears, though long; all ages, though so short,<br/>By merely wielding with poetic arm<br/>  Arms to which men will never more resort,<br/>Unless gunpowder should be found to harm<br/>  Much less than is the hope of every court,<br/>Which now is leagued young Freedom to annoy;<br/>But they will not find Liberty a Troy:--<br/><br/>Oh, thou eternal Homer! I have now<br/>  To paint a siege, wherein more men were slain,<br/>With deadlier engines and a speedier blow,<br/>  Than in thy Greek gazette of that campaign;<br/>And yet, like all men else, I must allow,<br/>  To vie with thee would be about as vain<br/>As for a brook to cope with Ocean's flood,--<br/>But still we moderns equal you in blood:<br/><br/>If not in poetry, at least in fact;<br/>  And fact is Truth, the grand desideratum!<br/>Of which, howe'er the Muse describes each act,<br/>  There should be ne'ertheless a slight substratum.<br/>But now the town is going to be attacked;<br/>  Great deeds are doing--how shall I relate 'em?<br/>Souls of immortal Generals! Phoebus watches<br/>To colour up his rays from your despatches.<br/><br/>Oh, ye great bulletins of Bonaparte!<br/>  Oh, ye less grand long lists of killed and wounded!<br/>Shade of Leonidas, who fought so hearty,<br/>  When my poor Greece was once, as now, surrounded!<br/>Oh, C√¶sar's Commentaries! now impart, ye<br/>  Shadows of Glory! (lest I be confounded),<br/>A portion of your fading twilight hues--<br/>So beautiful, so fleeting--to the Muse.<br/><br/>When I call "fading" martial immortality,<br/>  I mean, that every age and every year,<br/>And almost every day, in sad reality,<br/>  Some sucking hero is compelled to rear,<br/>Who, when we come to sum up the totality<br/>  Of deeds to human happiness most dear,<br/>Turns out to be a butcher in great business,<br/>Afflicting young folks with a sort of dizziness.<br/><br/>Medals, rank, ribands, lace, embroidery, scarlet,<br/>  Are things immortal to immortal man,<br/>As purple to the Babylonian harlot;<br/>  An uniform to boys is like a fan<br/>To women; there is scarce a crimson varlet<br/>  But deems himself the first in Glory's van.<br/>But Glory's glory; and if you would find<br/>What _that_ is--ask the pig who sees the wind!<br/><br/>At least _he feels it_, and some say he _sees_,<br/>  Because he runs before it like a pig;<br/>Or, if that simple sentence should displease,<br/>  Say, that he scuds before it like a brig,<br/>A schooner, or--but it is time to ease<br/>  This Canto, ere my Muse perceives fatigue.<br/>The next shall ring a peal to shake all people,<br/>Like a bob-major from a village steeple.<br/><br/>Hark! through the silence of the cold, dull night,<br/>  The hum of armies gathering rank on rank!<br/>Lo! dusky masses steal in dubious sight<br/>  Along the leaguered wall and bristling bank<br/>Of the armed river, while with straggling light<br/>  The stars peep through the vapours dim and dank,<br/>Which curl in various wreaths:--how soon the smoke<br/>Of Hell shall pall them in a deeper cloak!<br/><br/>Here pause we for the present--as even then<br/>  That awful pause, dividing Life from Death,<br/>Struck for an instant on the hearts of men,--<br/>  Thousands of whom were drawing their last breath!<br/>A moment--and all will be Life again!<br/>  The march! the charge! the shouts of either faith,<br/>Hurrah! and Allah! and one moment more--<br/>The death-cry drowning in the Battle's roar.<br/><br/>CANTO THE EIGHTH.<br/><br/>Oh, blood and thunder! and oh, blood and wounds!<br/>  These are but vulgar oaths, as you may deem,<br/>Too gentle reader! and most shocking sounds:--<br/>  And so they are; yet thus is Glory's dream<br/>Unriddled, and as my true Muse expounds<br/>  At present such things, since they are her theme,<br/>So be they her inspirers! Call them Mars,<br/>Bellona, what you will--they mean but wars.<br/><br/>All was prepared--the fire, the sword, the men<br/>  To wield them in their terrible array,--<br/>The army, like a lion from his den,<br/>  Marched forth with nerve and sinews bent to slay,--<br/>A human Hydra, issuing from its fen<br/>  To breathe destruction on its winding way,<br/>Whose heads were heroes, which cut off in vain<br/>Immediately in others grew again.<br/><br/>History can only take things in the gross;<br/>  But could we know them in detail, perchance<br/>In balancing the profit and the loss,<br/>  War's merit it by no means might enhance,<br/>To waste so much gold for a little dross,<br/>  As hath been done, mere conquest to advance.<br/>The drying up a single tear has more<br/>Of honest fame, than shedding seas of gore.<br/><br/>And why?--because it brings self-approbation;<br/>  Whereas the other, after all its glare,<br/>Shouts, bridges, arches, pensions from a nation,<br/>  Which (it may be) has not much left to spare,<br/>A higher title, or a loftier station,<br/>  Though they may make Corruption gape or stare,<br/>Yet, in the end, except in Freedom's battles,<br/>Are nothing but a child of Murder's rattles.<br/><br/>And such they are--and such they will be found:<br/>  Not so Leonidas and Washington,<br/>Whose every battle-field is holy ground,<br/>  Which breathes of nations saved, not worlds undone.<br/>How sweetly on the ear such echoes sound!<br/>  While the mere victor's may appal or stun<br/>The servile and the vain--such names will be<br/>A watchword till the Future shall be free.<br/><br/>The night was dark, and the thick mist allowed<br/>  Nought to be seen save the artillery's flame,<br/>Which arched the horizon like a fiery cloud,<br/>  And in the Danube's waters shone the same--<br/>A mirrored Hell! the volleying roar, and loud<br/>  Long booming of each peal on peal, o'ercame<br/>The ear far more than thunder; for Heaven's flashes<br/>Spare, or smite rarely--Man's make millions ashes!<br/><br/>The column ordered on the assault scarce passed<br/>  Beyond the Russian batteries a few toises,<br/>When up the bristling Moslem rose at last,<br/>  Answering the Christian thunders with like voices:<br/>Then one vast fire, air, earth, and stream embraced,<br/>  Which rocked as 't were beneath the mighty noises;<br/>While the whole rampart blazed like Etna, when<br/>The restless Titan hiccups in his den;<br/><br/>And one enormous shout of "Allah!" rose<br/>  In the same moment, loud as even the roar<br/>Of War's most mortal engines, to their foes<br/>  Hurling defiance: city, stream, and shore<br/>Resounded "Allah!" and the clouds which close<br/>  With thickening canopy the conflict o'er,<br/>Vibrate to the Eternal name. Hark! through<br/>All sounds it pierceth--"Allah! Allah Hu!"<br/><br/>The columns were in movement one and all,<br/>  But of the portion which attacked by water,<br/>Thicker than leaves the lives began to fall,<br/>  Though led by Arseniew, that great son of slaughter,<br/>As brave as ever faced both bomb and ball.<br/>  "Carnage" (so Wordsworth tells you) "is God's daughter:"<br/>If _he_ speak truth, she is Christ's sister, and<br/>Just now behaved as in the Holy Land.<br/><br/>The Prince de Ligne was wounded in the knee;<br/>  Count Chapeau-Bras,--too, had a ball between<br/>His cap and head, which proves the head to be<br/>  Aristocratic as was ever seen,<br/>Because it then received no injury<br/>  More than the cap; in fact, the ball could mean<br/>No harm unto a right legitimate head;<br/>"Ashes to ashes"--why not lead to lead?<br/><br/>Also the General Markow, Brigadier,<br/>  Insisting on removal of _the Prince_<br/>Amidst some groaning thousands dying near,--<br/>  All common fellows, who might writhe and wince,<br/>And shriek for water into a deaf ear,--<br/>  The General Markow, who could thus evince<br/>His sympathy for rank, by the same token,<br/>To teach him greater, had his own leg broken.<br/><br/>Three hundred cannon threw up their emetic,<br/>  And thirty thousand muskets flung their pills<br/>Like hail, to make a bloody Diuretic.<br/>  Mortality! thou hast thy monthly bills:<br/>Thy plagues--thy famines--thy physicians--yet tick,<br/>  Like the death-watch, within our ears the ills<br/>Past, present, and to come;--but all may yield<br/>To the true portrait of one battle-field;<br/><br/>There the still varying pangs, which multiply<br/>  Until their very number makes men hard<br/>By the infinities of agony,<br/>  Which meet the gaze, whate'er it may regard--<br/>The groan, the roll in dust, the all-_white_ eye<br/>  Turned back within its socket,--these reward<br/>Your rank and file by thousands, while the rest<br/>May win perhaps a riband at the breast!<br/><br/>Yet I love Glory;--Glory's a great thing:--<br/>  Think what it is to be in your old age<br/>Maintained at the expense of your good King:<br/>  A moderate pension shakes full many a sage,<br/>And Heroes are but made for bards to sing,<br/>  Which is still better--thus, in verse, to wage<br/>Your wars eternally, besides enjoying<br/>Half-pay for life, make Mankind worth destroying.<br/><br/>The troops, already disembarked, pushed on<br/>  To take a battery on the right: the others,<br/>Who landed lower down, their landing done,<br/>  Had set to work as briskly as their brothers:<br/>Being grenadiers, they mounted one by one,<br/>  Cheerful as children climb the breasts of mothers,<br/>O'er the intrenchment and the palisade,<br/>Quite orderly, as if upon parade.<br/><br/>And this was admirable: for so hot<br/>  The fire was, that were red Vesuvius loaded,<br/>Besides its lava, with all sorts of shot<br/>  And shells or hells, it could not more have goaded.<br/>Of officers a third fell on the spot,<br/>  A thing which Victory by no means boded<br/>To gentlemen engaged in the assault:<br/>Hounds, when the huntsman tumbles, are at fault.<br/><br/>But here I leave the general concern<br/>  To track our Hero on his path of Fame:<br/>He must his laurels separately earn--<br/>  For fifty thousand heroes, name by name,<br/>Though all deserving equally to turn<br/>  A couplet, or an elegy to claim,<br/>Would form a lengthy lexicon of Glory,<br/>And, what is worse still, a much longer story:<br/><br/>And therefore we must give the greater number<br/>  To the Gazette--which doubtless fairly dealt<br/>By the deceased, who lie in famous slumber<br/>  In ditches, fields, or wheresoe'er they felt<br/>Their clay for the last time their souls encumber;--<br/>  Thrice happy he whose name has been well spelt<br/>In the despatch: I knew a man whose loss<br/>Was printed _Grove_, although his name was Grose.<br/><br/>Juan and Johnson joined a certain corps,<br/>  And fought away with might and main, not knowing<br/>The way which they had never trod before,<br/>  And still less guessing where they might be going;<br/>But on they marched, dead bodies trampling o'er,<br/>  Firing, and thrusting, slashing, sweating, glowing,<br/>But fighting thoughtlessly enough to win,<br/>To their _two_ selves, _one_ whole bright bulletin.<br/><br/>Thus on they wallowed in the bloody mire<br/>  Of dead and dying thousands,--sometimes gaining<br/>A yard or two of ground, which brought them nigher<br/>  To some odd angle for which all were straining;<br/>At other times, repulsed by the close fire,<br/>  Which really poured as if all Hell were raining<br/>Instead of Heaven, they stumbled backwards o'er<br/>A wounded comrade, sprawling in his gore.<br/><br/>Though 't was Don Juan's first of fields, and though<br/>  The nightly muster and the silent march<br/>In the chill dark, when Courage does not glow<br/>  So much as under a triumphal arch,<br/>Perhaps might make him shiver, yawn, or throw<br/>  A glance on the dull clouds (as thick as starch,<br/>Which stiffened Heaven) as if he wished for day;--<br/>Yet for all this he did not run away.<br/><br/>Indeed he could not. But what if he had?<br/>  There _have been_ and _are_ heroes who begun<br/>With something not much better, or as bad:<br/>  Frederick the Great from Molwitz deigned to run,<br/>For the first and last time; for, like a pad,<br/>  Or hawk, or bride, most mortals after one<br/>Warm bout are broken in to their new tricks,<br/>And fight like fiends for pay or politics.<br/><br/>He was what Erin calls, in her sublime<br/>  Old Erse or Irish, or it may be _Punic_;--<br/>(The antiquarians--who can settle Time,<br/>  Which settles all things, Roman, Greek, or Runic--<br/>Swear that Pat's language sprung from the same clime<br/>  With Hannibal, and wears the Tyrian tunic<br/>Of Dido's alphabet--and this is rational<br/>As any other notion, and not national;)--<br/><br/>But Juan was quite "a broth of a boy,"<br/>  A thing of impulse and a child of song;<br/>Now swimming in the sentiment of joy,<br/>  Or the _sensation_ (if that phrase seem wrong),<br/>And afterward, if he must needs destroy,<br/>  In such good company as always throng<br/>To battles, sieges, and that kind of pleasure,<br/>No less delighted to employ his leisure;<br/><br/>But always without malice: if he warred<br/>  Or loved, it was with what we call "the best<br/>Intentions," which form all Mankind's _trump card_,<br/>  To be produced when brought up to the test.<br/>The statesman--hero--harlot--lawyer--ward<br/>  Off each attack, when people are in quest<br/>Of their designs, by saying they _meant well_;<br/>'T is pity "that such meaning should pave Hell."<br/><br/>I almost lately have begun to doubt<br/>  Whether Hell's pavement--if it be so _paved_--<br/>Must not have latterly been quite worn out,<br/>  Not by the numbers good intent hath saved,<br/>But by the mass who go below without<br/>  Those ancient good intentions, which once shaved<br/>And smoothed the brimstone of that street of Hell<br/>Which bears the greatest likeness to Pall Mall.<br/><br/>Juan, by some strange chance, which oft divides<br/>  Warrior from warrior in their grim career,<br/>Like chastest wives from constant husbands' sides<br/>  Just at the close of the first bridal year,<br/>By one of those odd turns of Fortune's tides,<br/>  Was on a sudden rather puzzled here,<br/>When, after a good deal of heavy firing,<br/>He found himself alone, and friends retiring.<br/><br/>I don't know how the thing occurred--it might<br/>  Be that the greater part were killed or wounded,<br/>And that the rest had faced unto the right<br/>  About; a circumstance which has confounded<br/>C√¶sar himself, who, in the very sight<br/>  Of his whole army, which so much abounded<br/>In courage, was obliged to snatch a shield,<br/>And rally back his Romans to the field.<br/><br/>Juan, who had no shield to snatch, and was<br/>  No C√¶sar, but a fine young lad, who fought<br/>He knew not why, arriving at this pass,<br/>  Stopped for a minute, as perhaps he ought<br/>For a much longer time; then, like an ass<br/>  (Start not, kind reader, since great Homer thought<br/>This simile enough for Ajax, Juan<br/>Perhaps may find it better than a new one);<br/><br/>Then, like an ass, he went upon his way,<br/>  And, what was stranger, never looked behind;<br/>But seeing, flashing forward, like the day<br/>  Over the hills, a fire enough to blind<br/>Those who dislike to look upon a fray,<br/>  He stumbled on, to try if he could find<br/>A path, to add his own slight arm and forces<br/>To corps, the greater part of which were corses.<br/><br/>Perceiving then no more the commandant<br/>  Of his own corps, nor even the corps, which had<br/>Quite disappeared--the gods know how! (I can't<br/>  Account for everything which may look bad<br/>In history; but we at least may grant<br/>  It was not marvellous that a mere lad,<br/>In search of Glory, should look on before,<br/>Nor care a pinch of snuff about his corps:)--<br/><br/>Perceiving nor commander nor commanded,<br/>  And left at large, like a young heir, to make<br/>His way to--where he knew not--single handed;<br/>  As travellers follow over bog and brake<br/>An "ignis fatuus;" or as sailors stranded<br/>  Unto the nearest hut themselves betake;<br/>So Juan, following Honour and his nose,<br/>Rushed where the thickest fire announced most foes.<br/><br/>He knew not where he was, nor greatly cared,<br/>  For he was dizzy, busy, and his veins<br/>Filled as with lightning--for his spirit shared<br/>  The hour, as is the case with lively brains;<br/>And where the hottest fire was seen and heard,<br/>  And the loud cannon pealed his hoarsest strains,<br/>He rushed, while earth and air were sadly shaken<br/>By thy humane discovery, Friar Bacon!<br/><br/>And as he rushed along, it came to pass he<br/>  Fell in with what was late the second column,<br/>Under the orders of the General Lascy,<br/>  But now reduced, as is a bulky volume<br/>Into an elegant extract (much less massy)<br/>  Of heroism, and took his place with solemn<br/>Air 'midst the rest, who kept their valiant faces<br/>And levelled weapons still against the Glacis.<br/><br/>Just at this crisis up came Johnson too,<br/>  Who had "retreated," as the phrase is when<br/>Men run away much rather than go through<br/>  Destruction's jaws into the Devil's den;<br/>But Johnson was a clever fellow, who<br/>  Knew when and how "to cut and come again,"<br/>And never ran away, except when running<br/>Was nothing but a valorous kind of cunning.<br/><br/>And so, when all his corps were dead or dying,<br/>  Except Don Juan, a mere novice, whose<br/>More virgin valour never dreamt of flying,<br/>  From ignorance of danger, which indues<br/>Its votaries, like Innocence relying<br/>  On its own strength, with careless nerves and thews,--<br/>Johnson retired a little, just to rally<br/>Those who catch cold in "shadows of Death's valley."<br/><br/>And there, a little sheltered from the shot,<br/>  Which rained from bastion, battery, parapet,<br/>Rampart, wall, casement, house--for there was not<br/>  In this extensive city, sore beset<br/>By Christian soldiery, a single spot<br/>  Which did not combat like the Devil, as yet,--<br/>He found a number of Chasseurs, all scattered<br/>By the resistance of the chase they battered.<br/><br/>And these he called on; and, what 's strange, they came<br/>  Unto his call, unlike "the spirits from<br/>The vasty deep," to whom you may exclaim,<br/>  Says Hotspur, long ere they will leave their home:--<br/>Their reasons were uncertainty, or shame<br/>  At shrinking from a bullet or a bomb,<br/>And that odd impulse, which in wars or creeds<br/>Makes men, like cattle, follow him who leads.<br/><br/>By Jove! he was a noble fellow, Johnson,<br/>  And though his name, than Ajax or Achilles,<br/>Sounds less harmonious, underneath the sun soon<br/>  We shall not see his likeness: he could kill his<br/>Man quite as quietly as blows the Monsoon<br/>  Her steady breath (which some months the same _still_ is):<br/>Seldom he varied feature, hue, or muscle,<br/>And could be very busy without bustle;<br/><br/>And therefore, when he ran away, he did so<br/>  Upon reflection, knowing that behind<br/>He would find others who would fain be rid so<br/>  Of idle apprehensions, which like wind<br/>Trouble heroic stomachs. Though their lids so<br/>  Oft are soon closed, all heroes are not blind,<br/>But when they light upon immediate death,<br/>Retire a little, merely to take breath.<br/><br/>But Johnson only ran off, to return<br/>  With many other warriors, as we said,<br/>Unto that rather somewhat misty bourne,<br/>  Which Hamlet tells us is a pass of dread.<br/>To Jack, howe'er, this gave but slight concern:<br/>  His soul (like galvanism upon the dead)<br/>Acted upon the living as on wire,<br/>And led them back into the heaviest fire.<br/><br/>Egad! they found the second time what they<br/>  The first time thought quite terrible enough<br/>To fly from, malgr√© all which people say<br/>  Of Glory, and all that immortal stuff<br/>Which fills a regiment (besides their pay,<br/>  That daily shilling which makes warriors tough)--<br/>They found on their return the self-same welcome,<br/>Which made some _think_, and others _know_, a _hell_ come.<br/><br/>They fell as thick as harvests beneath hail,<br/>  Grass before scythes, or corn below the sickle,<br/>Proving that trite old truth, that Life's as frail<br/>  As any other boon for which men stickle.<br/>The Turkish batteries thrashed them like a flail,<br/>  Or a good boxer, into a sad pickle<br/>Putting the very bravest, who were knocked<br/>Upon the head before their guns were cocked.<br/><br/>The Turks behind the traverses and flanks<br/>  Of the next bastion, fired away like devils,<br/>And swept, as gales sweep foam away, whole ranks:<br/>  However, Heaven knows how, the Fate who levels<br/>Towns--nations--worlds, in her revolving pranks,<br/>  So ordered it, amidst these sulphury revels,<br/>That Johnson, and some few who had not scampered,<br/>Reached the interior "talus" of the rampart.<br/><br/>First one or two, then five, six, and a dozen<br/>  Came mounting quickly up, for it was now<br/>All neck or nothing, as, like pitch or rosin,<br/>  Flame was showered forth above, as well 's below,<br/>So that you scarce could say who best had chosen,<br/>  The gentlemen that were the first to show<br/>Their martial faces on the parapet,<br/>Or those who thought it brave to wait as yet.<br/><br/>But those who scaled, found out that their advance<br/>  Was favoured by an accident or blunder:<br/>The Greek or Turkish Cohorn's ignorance<br/>  Had pallisadoed in a way you'd wonder<br/>To see in forts of Netherlands or France--<br/>  (Though these to our Gibraltar must knock under)--<br/>Right in the middle of the parapet<br/>Just named, these palisades were primly set:<br/><br/>So that on either side some nine or ten<br/>  Paces were left, whereon you could contrive<br/>To march; a great convenience to our men,<br/>  At least to all those who were left alive,<br/>Who thus could form a line and fight again;<br/>  And that which farther aided them to strive<br/>Was, that they could kick down the palisades,<br/>Which scarcely rose much higher than grass blades.<br/><br/>Among the first,--I will not say _the first_,<br/>  For such precedence upon such occasions<br/>Will oftentimes make deadly quarrels burst<br/>  Out between friends as well as allied nations:<br/>The Briton must be bold who really durst<br/>  Put to such trial John Bull's partial patience,<br/>As say that Wellington at Waterloo<br/>Was beaten,--though the Prussians say so too;--<br/><br/>And that if Blucher, Bulow, Gneisenau,<br/>  And God knows who besides in "au" and "ow,"<br/>Had not come up in time to cast an awe<br/>  Into the hearts of those who fought till now<br/>As tigers combat with an empty craw,<br/>  The Duke of Wellington had ceased to show<br/>His Orders--also to receive his pensions,<br/>Which are the heaviest that our history mentions.<br/><br/>But never mind;--"God save the King!" and _Kings!_<br/>  For if _he_ don't, I doubt if _men_ will longer--<br/>I think I hear a little bird, who sings<br/>  The people by and by will be the stronger:<br/>The veriest jade will wince whose harness wrings<br/>  So much into the raw as quite to wrong her<br/>Beyond the rules of posting,--and the mob<br/>At last fall sick of imitating Job.<br/><br/>At first it grumbles, then it swears, and then,<br/>  Like David, flings smooth pebbles 'gainst a Giant;<br/>At last it takes to weapons such as men<br/>  Snatch when Despair makes human hearts less pliant.<br/>Then comes "the tug of war;"--'t will come again,<br/>  I rather doubt; and I would fain say "fie on 't,"<br/>If I had not perceived that Revolution<br/>Alone can save the earth from Hell's pollution.<br/><br/>But to continue:--I say not _the_ first,<br/>  But of the first, our little friend Don Juan<br/>Walked o'er the walls of Ismail, as if nursed<br/>  Amidst such scenes--though this was quite a new one<br/>To him, and I should hope to _most_. The thirst<br/>  Of Glory, which so pierces through and through one,<br/>Pervaded him--although a generous creature,<br/>As warm in heart as feminine in feature.<br/><br/>And here he was--who upon Woman's breast,<br/>  Even from a child, felt like a child; howe'er<br/>The Man in all the rest might be confessed,<br/>  To him it was Elysium to be there;<br/>And he could even withstand that awkward test<br/>  Which Rousseau points out to the dubious fair,<br/>"Observe your lover when he _leaves_ your arms;"<br/>But Juan never _left_ them--while they had charms,<br/><br/>Unless compelled by Fate, or wave, or wind,<br/>  Or near relations--who are much the same.<br/>But _here_ he was!--where each tie that can bind<br/>  Humanity must yield to steel and flame:<br/>And _he_ whose very body was all mind,<br/>  Flung here by Fate or Circumstance, which tame<br/>The loftiest, hurried by the time and place,<br/>Dashed on like a spurred blood-horse in a race.<br/><br/>So was his blood stirred while he found resistance,<br/>  As is the hunter's at the five-bar gate,<br/>Or double post and rail, where the existence<br/>  Of Britain's youth depends upon their weight--The<br/>lightest being the safest: at a distance<br/>  He hated cruelty, as all men hate<br/>Blood, until heated--and even then his own<br/>At times would curdle o'er some heavy groan.<br/><br/>The General Lascy, who had been hard pressed,<br/>  Seeing arrive an aid so opportune<br/>As were some hundred youngsters all abreast,<br/>  Who came as if just dropped down from the moon<br/>To Juan, who was nearest him, addressed<br/>  His thanks, and hopes to take the city soon,<br/>Not reckoning him to be a "base Bezonian"<br/>(As Pistol calls it), but a young Livonian.<br/><br/>Juan, to whom he spoke in German, knew<br/>  As much of German as of Sanscrit, and<br/>In answer made an inclination to<br/>  The General who held him in command;<br/>For seeing one with ribands, black and blue,<br/>  Stars, medals, and a bloody sword in hand,<br/>Addressing him in tones which seemed to thank,<br/>He recognised an officer of rank.<br/><br/>Short speeches pass between two men who speak<br/>  No common language; and besides, in time<br/>Of war and taking towns, when many a shriek<br/>  Rings o'er the dialogue, and many a crime<br/>Is perpetrated ere a word can break<br/>  Upon the ear, and sounds of horror chime<br/>In like church-bells, with sigh, howl, groan, yell, prayer,<br/>There cannot be much conversation there.<br/><br/>And therefore all we have related in<br/>  Two long octaves, passed in a little minute;<br/>But in the same small minute, every sin<br/>  Contrived to get itself comprised within it.<br/>The very cannon, deafened by the din,<br/>  Grew dumb, for you might almost hear a linnet,<br/>As soon as thunder, 'midst the general noise<br/>Of Human Nature's agonizing voice!<br/><br/>The town was entered. Oh Eternity!--<br/>  "God made the country, and man made the town,"<br/>So Cowper says--and I begin to be<br/>  Of his opinion, when I see cast down<br/>Rome--Babylon-Tyre-Carthage--Nineveh--<br/>  All walls men know, and many never known;<br/>And pondering on the present and the past,<br/>To deem the woods shall be our home at last:--<br/><br/>Of all men, saving Sylla, the man-slayer,<br/>  Who passes for in life and death most lucky,<br/>Of the great names which in our faces stare,<br/>  The General Boon, back-woodsman of Kentucky,<br/>Was happiest amongst mortals anywhere;<br/>  For killing nothing but a bear or buck, he<br/>Enjoyed the lonely, vigorous, harmless days<br/>Of his old age in wilds of deepest maze.<br/><br/>Crime came not near him--she is not the child<br/>  Of solitude; Health shrank not from him--for<br/>Her home is in the rarely trodden wild,<br/>  Where if men seek her not, and death be more<br/>Their choice than life, forgive them, as beguiled<br/>  By habit to what their own hearts abhor--<br/>In cities caged. The present case in point I<br/>Cite is, that Boon lived hunting up to ninety;<br/><br/>And, what's still stranger, left behind a name<br/>  For which men vainly decimate the throng,<br/>Not only famous, but of that _good_ fame,<br/>  Without which Glory's but a tavern song--<br/>Simple, serene, the _antipodes_ of Shame,<br/>  Which Hate nor Envy e'er could tinge with wrong;<br/>An active hermit, even in age the child<br/>Of Nature--or the Man of Ross run wild.<br/><br/>'T is true he shrank from men even of his nation,<br/>  When they built up unto his darling trees,--<br/>He moved some hundred miles off, for a station<br/>  Where there were fewer houses and more ease;<br/>The inconvenience of civilisation<br/>  Is, that you neither can be pleased nor please;<br/>But where he met the individual man,<br/>He showed himself as kind as mortal can.<br/><br/>He was not all alone: around him grew<br/>  A sylvan tribe of children of the chase,<br/>Whose young, unwakened world was ever new,<br/>  Nor sword nor sorrow yet had left a trace<br/>On her unwrinkled brow, nor could you view<br/>  A frown on Nature's or on human face;<br/>The free-born forest found and kept them free,<br/>And fresh as is a torrent or a tree.<br/><br/>And tall, and strong, and swift of foot were they,<br/>  Beyond the dwarfing city's pale abortions,<br/>Because their thoughts had never been the prey<br/>  Of care or gain: the green woods were their portions;<br/>No sinking spirits told them they grew grey,<br/>  No fashion made them apes of her distortions;<br/>Simple they were, not savage--and their rifles,<br/>Though very true, were not yet used for trifles.<br/><br/>Motion was in their days, Rest in their slumbers,<br/>  And Cheerfulness the handmaid of their toil;<br/>Nor yet too many nor too few their numbers;<br/>  Corruption could not make their hearts her soil;<br/>The lust which stings, the splendour which encumbers,<br/>  With the free foresters divide no spoil;<br/>Serene, not sullen, were the solitudes<br/>Of this unsighing people of the woods.<br/><br/>So much for Nature:--by way of variety,<br/>  Now back to thy great joys, Civilisation!<br/>And the sweet consequence of large society,<br/>  War--pestilence--the despot's desolation,<br/>The kingly scourge, the lust of notoriety,<br/>  The millions slain by soldiers for their ration,<br/>The scenes like Catherine's boudoir at threescore,<br/>With Ismail's storm to soften it the more.<br/><br/>The town was entered: first one column made<br/>  Its sanguinary way good--then another;<br/>The reeking bayonet and the flashing blade<br/>  Clashed 'gainst the scimitar, and babe and mother<br/>With distant shrieks were heard Heaven to upbraid:--<br/>  Still closer sulphury clouds began to smother<br/>The breath of morn and man, where foot by foot<br/>The maddened Turks their city still dispute.<br/><br/>Koutousow, he who afterwards beat back<br/>  (With some assistance from the frost and snow)<br/>Napoleon on his bold and bloody track,<br/>  It happened was himself beat back just now:<br/>He was a jolly fellow, and could crack<br/>  His jest alike in face of friend or foe,<br/>Though Life, and Death, and Victory were at stake;<br/>But here it seemed his jokes had ceased to take:<br/><br/>For having thrown himself into a ditch,<br/>  Followed in haste by various grenadiers,<br/>Whose blood the puddle greatly did enrich,<br/>  He climbed to where the parapet appears;<br/>But there his project reached its utmost pitch<br/>  ('Mongst other deaths the General Ribaupierre's<br/>Was much regretted), for the Moslem men<br/>Threw them all down into the ditch again.<br/><br/>And had it not been for some stray troops landing<br/>  They knew not where, being carried by the stream<br/>To some spot, where they lost their understanding,<br/>  And wandered up and down as in a dream,<br/>Until they reached, as daybreak was expanding,<br/>  That which a portal to their eyes did seem,--<br/>The great and gay Koutousow might have lain<br/>Where three parts of his column yet remain.<br/><br/>And scrambling round the rampart, these same troops,<br/>  After the taking of the "Cavalier,"<br/>Just as Koutousow's most "forlorn" of "hopes"<br/>  Took, like chameleons, some slight tinge of fear,<br/>Opened the gate called "Kilia," to the groups<br/>  Of baffled heroes, who stood shyly near,<br/>Sliding knee-deep in lately frozen mud,<br/>Now thawed into a marsh of human blood.<br/><br/>The Kozacks, or, if so you please, Cossacques--<br/>  (I don't much pique myself upon orthography,<br/>So that I do not grossly err in facts,<br/>  Statistics, tactics, politics, and geography)--<br/>Having been used to serve on horses' backs,<br/>  And no great dilettanti in topography<br/>Of fortresses, but fighting where it pleases<br/>Their chiefs to order,--were all cut to pieces.<br/><br/>Their column, though the Turkish batteries thundered<br/>  Upon them, ne'ertheless had reached the rampart,<br/>And naturally thought they could have plundered<br/>  The city, without being farther hampered;<br/>But as it happens to brave men, they blundered--<br/>  The Turks at first pretended to have scampered,<br/>Only to draw them 'twixt two bastion corners,<br/>From whence they sallied on those Christian scorners.<br/><br/>Then being taken by the tail--a taking<br/>  Fatal to bishops as to soldiers--these<br/>Cossacques were all cut off as day was breaking,<br/>  And found their lives were let at a short lease--But<br/>perished without shivering or shaking,<br/>  Leaving as ladders their heaped carcasses,<br/>O'er which Lieutenant-Colonel Yesouskoi<br/>Marched with the brave battalion of Polouzki:--<br/><br/>This valiant man killed all the Turks he met,<br/>  But could not eat them, being in his turn<br/>Slain by some Mussulmans, who would not yet,<br/>  Without resistance, see their city burn.<br/>The walls were won, but 't was an even bet<br/>  Which of the armies would have cause to mourn:<br/>'T was blow for blow, disputing inch by inch,<br/>For one would not retreat, nor 't other flinch.<br/><br/>Another column also suffered much:--<br/>  And here we may remark with the historian,<br/>You should but give few cartridges to such<br/>  Troops as are meant to march with greatest glory on:<br/>When matters must be carried by the touch<br/>  Of the bright bayonet, and they all should hurry on;<br/>They sometimes, with a hankering for existence,<br/>Keep merely firing at a foolish distance.<br/><br/>A junction of the General Meknop's men<br/>  (Without the General, who had fallen some time<br/>Before, being badly seconded just then)<br/>  Was made at length with those who dared to climb<br/>The death-disgorging rampart once again;<br/>  And, though the Turk's resistance was sublime,<br/>They took the bastion, which the Seraskier<br/>Defended at a price extremely dear.<br/><br/>Juan and Johnson, and some volunteers,<br/>  Among the foremost, offered him good quarter,<br/>A word which little suits with Seraskiers,<br/>  Or at least suited not this valiant Tartar.<br/>He died, deserving well his country's tears,<br/>  A savage sort of military martyr:<br/>An English naval officer, who wished<br/>To make him prisoner, was also dished:<br/><br/>For all the answer to his proposition<br/>  Was from a pistol-shot that laid him dead;<br/>On which the rest, without more intermission,<br/>  Began to lay about with steel and lead--<br/>The pious metals most in requisition<br/>  On such occasions: not a single head<br/>Was spared;--three thousand Moslems perished here,<br/>And sixteen bayonets pierced the Seraskier.<br/><br/>The city's taken--only part by part--<br/>  And Death is drunk with gore: there's not a street<br/>Where fights not to the last some desperate heart<br/>  For those for whom it soon shall cease to beat.<br/>Here War forgot his own destructive art<br/>  In more destroying Nature; and the heat<br/>Of Carnage, like the Nile's sun-sodden slime,<br/>Engendered monstrous shapes of every crime.<br/><br/>A Russian officer, in martial tread<br/>  Over a heap of bodies, felt his heel<br/>Seized fast, as if 't were by the serpent's head<br/>  Whose fangs Eve taught her human seed to feel;<br/>In vain he kicked, and swore, and writhed, and bled,<br/>  And howled for help as wolves do for a meal--<br/>The teeth still kept their gratifying hold,<br/>As do the subtle snakes described of old.<br/><br/>A dying Moslem, who had felt the foot<br/>  Of a foe o'er him, snatched at it, and bit<br/>The very tendon which is most acute--<br/>  (That which some ancient Muse or modern wit<br/>Named after thee, Achilles!) and quite through 't<br/>  He made the teeth meet, nor relinquished it<br/>Even with his life--for (but they lie) 't is said<br/>To the live leg still clung the severed head.<br/><br/>However this may be, 't is pretty sure<br/>  The Russian officer for life was lamed,<br/>For the Turk's teeth stuck faster than a skewer,<br/>  And left him 'midst the invalid and maimed:<br/>The regimental surgeon could not cure<br/>  His patient, and, perhaps, was to be blamed<br/>More than the head of the inveterate foe,<br/>Which was cut off, and scarce even then let go.<br/><br/>But then the fact's a fact--and 't is the part<br/>  Of a true poet to escape from fiction<br/>Whene'er he can; for there is little art<br/>  in leaving verse more free from the restriction<br/>Of Truth than prose, unless to suit the mart<br/>  For what is sometimes called poetic diction,<br/>And that outrageous appetite for lies<br/>Which Satan angles with for souls, like flies.<br/><br/>The city's taken, but not rendered!--No!<br/>  There's not a Moslem that hath yielded sword:<br/>The blood may gush out, as the Danube's flow<br/>  Rolls by the city wall; but deed nor word<br/>Acknowledge aught of dread of Death or foe:<br/>  In vain the yell of victory is roared<br/>By the advancing Muscovite--the groan<br/>Of the last foe is echoed by his own.<br/><br/>The bayonet pierces and the sabre cleaves,<br/>  And human lives are lavished everywhere,<br/>As the year closing whirls the scarlet leaves<br/>  When the stripped forest bows to the bleak air,<br/>And groans; and thus the peopled city grieves,<br/>  Shorn of its best and loveliest, and left bare;<br/>But still it falls in vast and awful splinters,<br/>As oaks blown down with all their thousand winters.<br/><br/>It is an awful topic--but 't is not<br/>  My cue for any time to be terrific:<br/>For checkered as is seen our human lot<br/>  With good, and bad, and worse, alike prolific<br/>Of melancholy merriment, to quote<br/>  Too much of one sort would be soporific;--<br/>Without, or with, offence to friends or foes,<br/>I sketch your world exactly as it goes.<br/><br/>And one good action in the midst of crimes<br/>  Is "quite refreshing," in the affected phrase<br/>Of these ambrosial, Pharisaic times,<br/>  With all their pretty milk-and-water ways,<br/>And may serve therefore to bedew these rhymes,<br/>  A little scorched at present with the blaze<br/>Of conquest and its consequences, which<br/>Make Epic poesy so rare and rich.<br/><br/>Upon a taken bastion, where there lay<br/>  Thousands of slaughtered men, a yet warm group<br/>Of murdered women, who had found their way<br/>  To this vain refuge, made the good heart droop<br/>And shudder;--while, as beautiful as May,<br/>  A female child of ten years tried to stoop<br/>And hide her little palpitating breast<br/>Amidst the bodies lulled in bloody rest.<br/><br/>Two villanous Cossacques pursued the child<br/>  With flashing eyes and weapons: matched with _them_,<br/>The rudest brute that roams Siberia's wild<br/>  Has feelings pure and polished as a gem,--<br/>The bear is civilised, the wolf is mild;<br/>  And whom for this at last must we condemn?<br/>Their natures? or their sovereigns, who employ<br/>All arts to teach their subjects to destroy?<br/><br/>Their sabres glittered o'er her little head,<br/>  Whence her fair hair rose twining with affright,<br/>Her hidden face was plunged amidst the dead:<br/>  When Juan caught a glimpse of this sad sight,<br/>I shall not say exactly what he _said_,<br/>  Because it might not solace "ears polite;"<br/>But what he _did_, was to lay on their backs,<br/>The readiest way of reasoning with Cossacques.<br/><br/>One's hip he slashed, and split the other's shoulder,<br/>  And drove them with their brutal yells to seek<br/>If there might be chirurgeons who could solder<br/>  The wounds they richly merited, and shriek<br/>Their baffled rage and pain; while waxing colder<br/>  As he turned o'er each pale and gory cheek,<br/>Don Juan raised his little captive from<br/>The heap a moment more had made her tomb.<br/><br/>And she was chill as they, and on her face<br/>  A slender streak of blood announced how near<br/>Her fate had been to that of all her race;<br/>  For the same blow which laid her mother here<br/>Had scarred her brow, and left its crimson trace,<br/>  As the last link with all she had held dear;<br/>But else unhurt, she opened her large eyes,<br/>And gazed on Juan with a wild surprise.<br/><br/>Just at this instant, while their eyes were fixed<br/>  Upon each other, with dilated glance,<br/>In Juan's look, pain, pleasure, hope, fear, mixed<br/>  With joy to save, and dread of some mischance<br/>Unto his prot√©g√©e; while hers, transfixed<br/>  With infant terrors, glared as from a trance,<br/>A pure, transparent, pale, yet radiant face,<br/>Like to a lighted alabaster vase:--<br/><br/>Up came John Johnson (I will not say _"Jack,"_<br/>  For that were vulgar, cold, and common-place<br/>On great occasions, such as an attack<br/>  On cities, as hath been the present case):<br/>Up Johnson came, with hundreds at his back,<br/>  Exclaiming--"Juan! Juan! On, boy! brace<br/>Your arm, and I'll bet Moscow to a dollar,<br/>That you and I will win St. George's collar.<br/><br/>"The Seraskier is knocked upon the head,<br/>  But the stone bastion still remains, wherein<br/>The old Pacha sits among some hundreds dead,<br/>  Smoking his pipe quite calmly 'midst the din<br/>Of our artillery and his own: 't is said<br/>  Our killed, already piled up to the chin,<br/>Lie round the battery; but still it batters,<br/>And grape in volleys, like a vineyard, scatters.<br/><br/>"Then up with me!"--But Juan answered, "Look<br/>  Upon this child--I saved her--must not leave<br/>Her life to chance; but point me out some nook<br/>  Of safety, where she less may shrink and grieve,<br/>And I am with you."--Whereon Johnson took<br/>  A glance around--and shrugged--and twitched his sleeve<br/>And black silk neckcloth--and replied, "You're right;<br/>Poor thing! what's to be done? I'm puzzled quite."<br/><br/>Said Juan--"Whatsoever is to be<br/>  Done, I'll not quit her till she seems secure<br/>Of present life a good deal more than we."--<br/>  Quoth Johnson--"_Neither_ will I quite insure;<br/>But at the least _you_ may die gloriously."--<br/>  Juan replied--" At least I will endure<br/>Whate'er is to be borne--but not resign<br/>This child, who is parentless, and therefore mine."<br/><br/>Johnson said--"Juan, we've no time to lose;<br/>  The child's a pretty child--a very pretty--<br/>I never saw such eyes--but hark! now choose<br/>  Between your fame and feelings, pride and pity:--<br/>Hark! how the roar increases!--no excuse<br/>  Will serve when there is plunder in a city;--<br/>I should be loath to march without you, but,<br/>By God! we'll be too late for the first cut."<br/><br/>But Juan was immovable; until<br/>  Johnson, who really loved him in his way,<br/>Picked out amongst his followers with some skill<br/>  Such as he thought the least given up to prey,<br/>And, swearing, if the infant came to ill<br/>  That they should all be shot on the next day,--<br/>But if she were delivered safe and sound,<br/>They should at least have fifty rubles round,<br/><br/>And all allowances besides of plunder<br/>  In fair proportion with their comrades;--then<br/>Juan consented to march on through thunder,<br/>  Which thinned at every step their ranks of men:<br/>And yet the rest rushed eagerly--no wonder,<br/>  For they were heated by the hope of gain,<br/>A thing which happens everywhere each day--<br/>No hero trusteth wholly to half pay.<br/><br/>And such is Victory, and such is Man!<br/>  At least nine tenths of what we call so:--God<br/>May have another name for half we scan<br/>  As human beings, or his ways are odd.<br/>But to our subject: a brave Tartar Khan--<br/>  Or "Sultan," as the author (to whose nod<br/>In prose I bend my humble verse) doth call<br/>This chieftain--somehow would not yield at all:<br/><br/>But flanked by _five_ brave sons (such is polygamy,<br/>  That she spawns warriors by the score, where none<br/>Are prosecuted for that false crime bigamy),<br/>  He never would believe the city won<br/>While Courage clung but to a single twig.--Am I<br/>  Describing Priam's, Peleus', or Jove's son?<br/>Neither--but a good, plain, old, temperate man,<br/>Who fought with his five children in the van.<br/><br/>To _take_ him was the point.--The truly brave,<br/>  When they behold the brave oppressed with odds,<br/>Are touched with a desire to shield and save;--<br/>  A mixture of wild beasts and demi-gods<br/>Are they--now furious as the sweeping wave,<br/>  Now moved with pity: even as sometimes nods<br/>The rugged tree unto the summer wind,<br/>Compassion breathes along the savage mind.<br/><br/>But he would _not_ be _taken_, and replied<br/>  To all the propositions of surrender<br/>By mowing Christians down on every side,<br/>  As obstinate as Swedish Charles at Bender.<br/>His five brave boys no less the foe defied;<br/>  Whereon the Russian pathos grew less tender<br/>As being a virtue, like terrestrial patience,<br/>Apt to wear out on trifling provocations.<br/><br/>And spite of Johnson and of Juan, who<br/>  Expended all their Eastern phraseology<br/>In begging him, for God's sake, just to show<br/>  So much less fight as might form an apology<br/>For _them_ in saving such a desperate foe--<br/>  He hewed away, like Doctors of Theology<br/>When they dispute with sceptics; and with curses<br/>Struck at his friends, as babies beat their nurses.<br/><br/>Nay, he had wounded, though but slightly, both<br/>  Juan and Johnson; whereupon they fell,<br/>The first with sighs, the second with an oath,<br/>  Upon his angry Sultanship, pell-mell,<br/>And all around were grown exceeding wroth<br/>  At such a pertinacious infidel,<br/>And poured upon him and his sons like rain,<br/>Which they resisted like a sandy plain<br/><br/>That drinks and still is dry. At last they perished--<br/>  His second son was levelled by a shot;<br/>His third was sabred; and the fourth, most cherished<br/>  Of all the five, on bayonets met his lot;<br/>The fifth, who, by a Christian mother nourished,<br/>  Had been neglected, ill-used, and what not,<br/>Because deformed, yet died all game and bottom,<br/>To save a Sire who blushed that he begot him.<br/><br/>The eldest was a true and tameless Tartar,<br/>  As great a scorner of the Nazarene<br/>As ever Mahomet picked out for a martyr,<br/>  Who only saw the black-eyed girls in green,<br/>Who make the beds of those who won't take quarter<br/>  On earth, in Paradise; and when once seen,<br/>Those houris, like all other pretty creatures,<br/>Do just whate'er they please, by dint of features.<br/><br/>And what they pleased to do with the young Khan<br/>  In Heaven I know not, nor pretend to guess;<br/>But doubtless they prefer a fine young man<br/>  To tough old heroes, and can do no less;<br/>And that's the cause no doubt why, if we scan<br/>  A field of battle's ghastly wilderness,<br/>For one rough, weather-beaten, veteran body,<br/>You'll find ten thousand handsome coxcombs bloody.<br/><br/>Your houris also have a natural pleasure<br/>  In lopping off your lately married men,<br/>Before the bridal hours have danced their measure<br/>  And the sad, second moon grows dim again,<br/>Or dull Repentance hath had dreary leisure<br/>  To wish him back a bachelor now and then:<br/>And thus your Houri (it may be) disputes<br/>Of these brief blossoms the immediate fruits.<br/><br/>Thus the young Khan, with Houris in his sight,<br/>  Thought not upon the charms of four young brides,<br/>But bravely rushed on his first heavenly night.<br/>  In short, howe'er _our_ better faith derides,<br/>These black-eyed virgins make the Moslems fight,<br/>  As though there were one Heaven and none besides--<br/>Whereas, if all be true we hear of Heaven<br/>And Hell, there must at least be six or seven.<br/><br/>So fully flashed the phantom on his eyes,<br/>  That when the very lance was in his heart,<br/>He shouted "Allah!" and saw Paradise<br/>  With all its veil of mystery drawn apart,<br/>And bright Eternity without disguise<br/>  On his soul, like a ceaseless sunrise, dart:--<br/>With Prophets--Houris--Angels--Saints, descried<br/>In one voluptuous blaze,--and then he died,--<br/><br/>But with a heavenly rapture on his face.<br/>  The good old Khan, who long had ceased to see<br/>Houris, or aught except his florid race,<br/>  Who grew like cedars round him gloriously--<br/>When he beheld his latest hero grace<br/>  The earth, which he became like a felled tree,<br/>Paused for a moment from the fight, and cast<br/>A glance on that slain son, his first and last.<br/><br/>The soldiers, who beheld him drop his point,<br/>  Stopped as if once more willing to concede<br/>Quarter, in case he bade them not "aroynt!"<br/>  As he before had done. He did not heed<br/>Their pause nor signs: his heart was out of joint,<br/>  And shook (till now unshaken) like a reed,<br/>As he looked down upon his children gone,<br/>And felt--though done with life--he was alone.<br/><br/>But 't was a transient tremor:--with a spring<br/>  Upon the Russian steel his breast he flung,<br/>As carelessly as hurls the moth her wing<br/>  Against the light wherein she dies: he clung<br/>Closer, that all the deadlier they might wring,<br/>  Unto the bayonets which had pierced his young;<br/>And throwing back a dim look on his sons,<br/>In one wide wound poured forth his soul at once.<br/><br/>'T is strange enough--the rough, tough soldiers, who<br/>  Spared neither sex nor age in their career<br/>Of carnage, when this old man was pierced through,<br/>  And lay before them with his children near,<br/>Touched by the heroism of him they slew,<br/>  Were melted for a moment; though no tear<br/>Flowed from their bloodshot eyes, all red with strife,<br/>They honoured such determined scorn of Life.<br/><br/>But the stone bastion still kept up its fire,<br/>  Where the chief Pacha calmly held his post:<br/>Some twenty times he made the Russ retire,<br/>  And baffled the assaults of all their host;<br/>At length he condescended to inquire<br/>  If yet the city's rest were won or lost;<br/>And being told the latter, sent a Bey<br/>To answer Ribas' summons to give way.<br/><br/>In the mean time, cross-legged, with great sang-froid,<br/>  Among the scorching ruins he sat smoking<br/>Tobacco on a little carpet;--Troy<br/>  Saw nothing like the scene around;--yet looking<br/>With martial Stoicism, nought seemed to annoy<br/>  His stern philosophy; but gently stroking<br/>His beard, he puffed his pipe's ambrosial gales,<br/>As if he had three lives, as well as tails.<br/><br/>The town was taken--whether he might yield<br/>  Himself or bastion, little mattered now:<br/>His stubborn valour was no future shield.<br/>  Ismail's no more! The Crescent's silver bow<br/>Sunk, and the crimson Cross glared o'er the field,<br/>  But red with no _redeeming_ gore: the glow<br/>Of burning streets, like moonlight on the water,<br/>Was imaged back in blood, the sea of slaughter.<br/><br/>All that the mind would shrink from of excesses--<br/>  All that the body perpetrates of bad;<br/>All that we read--hear--dream, of man's distresses--<br/>  All that the Devil would do if run stark mad;<br/>All that defies the worst which pen expresses,--<br/>  All by which Hell is peopled, or as sad<br/>As Hell--mere mortals who their power abuse--<br/>Was here (as heretofore and since) let loose.<br/><br/>If here and there some transient trait of pity<br/>  Was shown, and some more noble heart broke through<br/>Its bloody bond, and saved, perhaps, some pretty<br/>  Child, or an ag√©d, helpless man or two--<br/>What's this in one annihilated city,<br/>  Where thousand loves, and ties, and duties grew?<br/>Cockneys of London! Muscadins of Paris!<br/>Just ponder what a pious pastime War is.<br/><br/>Think how the joys of reading a Gazette<br/>  Are purchased by all agonies and crimes:<br/>Or if these do not move you, don't forget<br/>  Such doom may be your own in after-times.<br/>Meantime the Taxes, Castlereagh, and Debt,<br/>  Are hints as good as sermons, or as rhymes.<br/>Read your own hearts and Ireland's present story,<br/>Then feed her famine fat with Wellesley's glory.<br/><br/>But still there is unto a patriot nation,<br/>  Which loves so well its country and its King,<br/>A subject of sublimest exultation--<br/>  Bear it, ye Muses, on your brightest wing!<br/>Howe'er the mighty locust, Desolation,<br/>  Strip your green fields, and to your harvests cling,<br/>Gaunt famine never shall approach the throne--<br/>Though Ireland starve, great George weighs twenty stone.<br/><br/>But let me put an end unto my theme:<br/>  There was an end of Ismail--hapless town!<br/>Far flashed her burning towers o'er Danube's stream,<br/>  And redly ran his blushing waters down.<br/>The horrid war-whoop and the shriller scream<br/>  Rose still; but fainter were the thunders grown:<br/>Of forty thousand who had manned the wall,<br/>Some hundreds breathed--the rest were silent all!<br/><br/>In one thing ne'ertheless 't is fit to praise<br/>  The Russian army upon this occasion,<br/>A virtue much in fashion now-a-days,<br/>  And therefore worthy of commemoration:<br/>The topic's tender, so shall be my phrase--<br/>  Perhaps the season's chill, and their long station<br/>In Winter's depth, or want of rest and victual,<br/>Had made them chaste;--they ravished very little.<br/><br/>Much did they slay, more plunder, and no less<br/>  Might here and there occur some violation<br/>In the other line;--but not to such excess<br/>  As when the French, that dissipated nation,<br/>Take towns by storm: no causes can I guess,<br/>  Except cold weather and commiseration;<br/>But all the ladies, save some twenty score,<br/>Were almost as much virgins as before.<br/><br/>Some odd mistakes, too, happened in the dark,<br/>  Which showed a want of lanterns, or of taste--<br/>Indeed the smoke was such they scarce could mark<br/>  Their friends from foes,--besides such things from haste<br/>Occur, though rarely, when there is a spark<br/>  Of light to save the venerably chaste:<br/>But six old damsels, each of seventy years,<br/>Were all deflowered by different grenadiers.<br/><br/>But on the whole their continence was great;<br/>  So that some disappointment there ensued<br/>To those who had felt the inconvenient state<br/>  Of "single blessedness," and thought it good<br/>(Since it was not their fault, but only fate,<br/>  To bear these crosses) for each waning prude<br/>To make a Roman sort of Sabine wedding,<br/>Without the expense and the suspense of bedding.<br/><br/>Some voices of the buxom middle-aged<br/>  Were also heard to wonder in the din<br/>(Widows of forty were these birds long caged)<br/>  "Wherefore the ravishing did not begin!"<br/>But while the thirst for gore and plunder raged,<br/>  There was small leisure for superfluous sin;<br/>But whether they escaped or no, lies hid<br/>In darkness--I can only hope they did.<br/><br/>Suwarrow now was conqueror--a match<br/>  For Timour or for Zinghis in his trade.<br/>While mosques and streets, beneath his eyes, like thatch<br/>  Blazed, and the cannon's roar was scarce allayed,<br/>With bloody hands he wrote his first despatch;<br/>  And here exactly follows what he said:--<br/>"Glory to _God_ and to the Empress!" (_Powers<br/>Eternal! such names mingled!_) "Ismail's ours."<br/><br/>Methinks these are the most tremendous words,<br/>  Since "MENE, MENE, TEKEL," and "UPHARSIN,"<br/>Which hands or pens have ever traced of swords.<br/>  Heaven help me! I'm but little of a parson:<br/>What Daniel read was short-hand of the Lord's,<br/>  Severe, sublime; the prophet wrote no farce on<br/>The fate of nations;--but this Russ so witty<br/>Could rhyme, like Nero, o'er a burning city.<br/><br/>He wrote this Polar melody, and set it,<br/>  Duly accompanied by shrieks and groans,<br/>Which few will sing, I trust, but none forget it--<br/>  For I will teach, if possible, the stones<br/>To rise against Earth's tyrants. Never let it<br/>  Be said that we still truckle unto thrones;--<br/>But ye--our children's children! think how we<br/>Showed _what things were_ before the World was free!<br/><br/>That hour is not for us, but 't is for you:<br/>  And as, in the great joy of your Millennium,<br/>You hardly will believe such things were true<br/>  As now occur, I thought that I would pen you 'em;<br/>But may their very memory perish too!--<br/>  Yet if perchance remembered, still disdain you 'em<br/>More than you scorn the savages of yore,<br/>Who _painted_ their _bare_ limbs, but _not_ with gore.<br/><br/>And when you hear historians talk of thrones,<br/>  And those that sate upon them, let it be<br/>As we now gaze upon the mammoth's bones,<br/>  And wonder what old world such things could see,<br/>Or hieroglyphics on Egyptian stones,<br/>  The pleasant riddles of futurity--<br/>Guessing at what shall happily be hid,<br/>As the real purpose of a pyramid.<br/><br/>Reader! I have kept my word,--at least so far<br/>  As the first Canto promised. You have now<br/>Had sketches of Love--Tempest--Travel--War,--<br/>  All very accurate, you must allow,<br/>And _Epic_, if plain truth should prove no bar;<br/>  For I have drawn much less with a long bow<br/>Than my forerunners. Carelessly I sing,<br/>But Phoebus lends me now and then a string,<br/><br/>With which I still can harp, and carp, and fiddle.<br/>  What further hath befallen or may befall<br/>The hero of this grand poetic riddle,<br/>  I by and by may tell you, if at all:<br/>But now I choose to break off in the middle,<br/>  Worn out with battering Ismail's stubborn wall,<br/>While Juan is sent off with the despatch,<br/>For which all Petersburgh is on the watch.<br/><br/>This special honour was conferred, because<br/>  He had behaved with courage and humanity--<br/>Which last men like, when they have time to pause<br/>  From their ferocities produced by vanity.<br/>His little captive gained him some applause<br/>  For saving her amidst the wild insanity<br/>Of carnage,--and I think he was more glad in her<br/>Safety, than his new order of St. Vladimir.<br/><br/>The Moslem orphan went with her protector,<br/>  For she was homeless, houseless, helpless; all<br/>Her friends, like the sad family of Hector,<br/>  Had perished in the field or by the wall:<br/>Her very place of birth was but a spectre<br/>  Of what it had been; there the Muezzin's call<br/>To prayer was heard no more!--and Juan wept,<br/>And made a vow to shield her, which he kept.<br/><br/>CANTO THE NINTH.<br/><br/>Oh, Wellington! (or "Villainton"--for Fame<br/>  Sounds the heroic syllables both ways;<br/>France could not even conquer your great name,<br/>  But punned it down to this facetious phrase--<br/>Beating or beaten she will laugh the same,)<br/>  You have obtained great pensions and much praise:<br/>Glory like yours should any dare gainsay,<br/>Humanity would rise, and thunder "Nay!"<br/><br/>I don't think that you used Kinnaird quite well<br/>  In Marin√®t's affair--in fact, 't was shabby,<br/>And like some other things won't do to tell<br/>  Upon your tomb in Westminster's old Abbey.<br/>Upon the rest 't is not worth while to dwell,<br/>  Such tales being for the tea-hours of some tabby;<br/>But though your years as _man_ tend fast to zero,<br/>In fact your Grace is still but a _young Hero_.<br/><br/>Though Britain owes (and pays you too) so much,<br/>  Yet Europe doubtless owes you greatly more:<br/>You have repaired Legitimacy's crutch,<br/>  A prop not quite so certain as before:<br/>The Spanish, and the French, as well as Dutch,<br/>  Have seen, and felt, how strongly you _restore_;<br/>And Waterloo has made the world your debtor<br/>(I wish your bards would sing it rather better).<br/><br/>You are "the best of cut-throats:"--do not start;<br/>  The phrase is Shakespeare's, and not misapplied:--<br/>War's a brain-spattering, windpipe-slitting art,<br/>  Unless her cause by right be sanctified.<br/>If you have acted _once_ a generous part,<br/>  The World, not the World's masters, will decide,<br/>And I shall be delighted to learn who,<br/>Save you and yours, have gained by Waterloo?<br/><br/>I am no flatterer--you've supped full of flattery:<br/>  They say you like it too--'t is no great wonder.<br/>He whose whole life has been assault and battery,<br/>  At last may get a little tired of thunder;<br/>And swallowing eulogy much more than satire, he<br/>  May like being praised for every lucky blunder,<br/>Called "Saviour of the Nations"--not yet saved,--<br/>And "Europe's Liberator"--still enslaved.<br/><br/>I've done. Now go and dine from off the plate<br/>  Presented by the Prince of the Brazils,<br/>And send the sentinel before your gate<br/>  A slice or two from your luxurious meals:<br/>He fought, but has not fed so well of late.<br/>  Some hunger, too, they say the people feels:--<br/>There is no doubt that you deserve your ration,<br/>But pray give back a little to the nation.<br/><br/>I don't mean to reflect--a man so great as<br/>  You, my lord Duke! is far above reflection:<br/>The high Roman fashion, too, of Cincinnatus,<br/>  With modern history has but small connection:<br/>Though as an Irishman you love potatoes,<br/>  You need not take them under your direction;<br/>And half a million for your Sabine farm<br/>Is rather dear!--I'm sure I mean no harm.<br/><br/>Great men have always scorned great recompenses:<br/>  Epaminondas saved his Thebes, and died,<br/>Not leaving even his funeral expenses:<br/>  George Washington had thanks, and nought beside,<br/>Except the all-cloudless glory (which few men's is)<br/>  To free his country: Pitt too had his pride,<br/>And as a high-souled Minister of state is<br/>Renowned for ruining Great Britain gratis.<br/><br/>Never had mortal man such opportunity,<br/>  Except Napoleon, or abused it more:<br/>You might have freed fallen Europe from the unity<br/>  Of Tyrants, and been blest from shore to shore:<br/>And _now_--what is your fame? Shall the Muse tune it ye?<br/>  _Now_--that the rabble's first vain shouts are o'er?<br/>Go! hear it in your famished country's cries!<br/>Behold the World! and curse your victories!<br/><br/>As these new cantos touch on warlike feats,<br/>  To _you_ the unflattering Muse deigns to inscribe<br/>Truths, that you will not read in the Gazettes,<br/>  But which 't is time to teach the hireling tribe<br/>Who fatten on their country's gore, and debts,<br/>  Must be recited--and without a bribe.<br/>You _did great_ things, but not being _great_ in mind,<br/>Have left _undone_ the _greatest_--and mankind.<br/><br/>Death laughs--Go ponder o'er the skeleton<br/>  With which men image out the unknown thing<br/>That hides the past world, like to a set sun<br/>  Which still elsewhere may rouse a brighter spring--<br/>Death laughs at all you weep for!--look upon<br/>  This hourly dread of all! whose _threatened sting_<br/>Turns Life to terror, even though in its sheath:<br/>Mark! how its lipless mouth grins without breath!<br/><br/>Mark! how it laughs and scorns at all you are!<br/>  And yet _was_ what you are; from _ear_ to _ear_<br/>It _laughs not_--there is now no fleshy bar<br/> So called; the Antic long hath ceased to _hear_,<br/>But still he _smiles_; and whether near or far,<br/>  He strips from man that mantle (far more dear<br/>Than even the tailor's), his incarnate skin,<br/>White, black, or copper--the dead bones will grin.<br/><br/>And thus Death laughs,--it is sad merriment,<br/>  But still it _is_ so; and with such example<br/>Why should not Life be equally content<br/>  With his Superior, in a smile to trample<br/>Upon the nothings which are daily spent<br/>  Like bubbles on an Ocean much less ample<br/>Than the Eternal Deluge, which devours<br/>Suns as rays--worlds like atoms--years like hours?<br/><br/>"To be, or not to be? _that_ is the question,"<br/>  Says Shakespeare, who just now is much in fashion.<br/>I am neither Alexander nor Heph√¶stion,<br/>  Nor ever had for _abstract_ fame much passion;<br/>But would much rather have a sound digestion<br/>  Than Buonaparte's cancer:--could I dash on<br/>Through fifty victories to shame or fame--<br/>Without a stomach what were a good name?<br/><br/>_"O dura ilia messorum!"_--"Oh<br/>  Ye rigid guts of reapers!"
