I translate<br/>For the great benefit of those who know<br/>  What indigestion is--that inward fate<br/>Which makes all Styx through one small liver flow.<br/>  A peasant's sweat is worth his lord's estate:<br/>Let _this_ one toil for bread--_that_ rack for rent,<br/>He who sleeps best may be the most content.<br/><br/>"To be, or not to be?"--Ere I decide,<br/>  I should be glad to know that which _is being_.<br/>'T is true we speculate both far and wide,<br/>  And deem, because we _see_, we are _all-seeing_:<br/>For my part, I'll enlist on neither side,<br/>  Until I see both sides for once agreeing.<br/>For me, I sometimes think that Life is Death,<br/>Rather than Life a mere affair of breath.<br/><br/>_"Que scais-je"_ was the motto of Montaigne,<br/>  As also of the first academicians:<br/>That all is dubious which man may attain,<br/>  Was one of their most favourite positions.<br/>There's no such thing as certainty, that's plain<br/>  As any of Mortality's conditions;<br/>So little do we know what we're about in<br/>This world, I doubt if doubt itself be doubting.<br/><br/>It is a pleasant voyage perhaps to float,<br/>  Like Pyrrho, on a sea of speculation;<br/>But what if carrying sail capsize the boat?<br/>  Your wise men don't know much of navigation;<br/>And swimming long in the abyss of thought<br/>  Is apt to tire: a calm and shallow station<br/>Well nigh the shore, where one stoops down and gathers<br/>Some pretty shell, is best for moderate bathers.<br/><br/>"But Heaven," as Cassio says, "is above all--<br/>  No more of this, then, let us pray!" We have<br/>Souls to save, since Eve's slip and Adam's fall,<br/>  Which tumbled all mankind into the grave,<br/>Besides fish, beasts, and birds. "The sparrow's fall<br/>  Is special providence," though how _it_ gave<br/>Offence, we know not; probably it perched<br/>Upon the tree which Eve so fondly searched.<br/><br/>Oh! ye immortal Gods! what is Theogony?<br/>  Oh! thou, too, mortal man! what is Philanthropy?<br/>Oh! World, which was and is, what is Cosmogony?<br/>  Some people have accused me of Misanthropy;<br/>And yet I know no more than the mahogany<br/>  That forms this desk, of what they mean;--_Lykanthropy_<br/>I comprehend, for without transformation<br/>Men become wolves on any slight occasion.<br/><br/>But I, the mildest, meekest of mankind,<br/>  Like Moses, or Melancthon, who have ne'er<br/>Done anything exceedingly unkind,--<br/>  And (though I could not now and then forbear<br/>Following the bent of body or of mind)<br/>  Have always had a tendency to spare,--<br/>Why do they call me Misanthrope? Because<br/>_They hate me, not I them:_--and here we'll pause.<br/><br/>'T is time we should proceed with our good poem,--<br/>  For I maintain that it is really good,<br/>Not only in the body but the proem,<br/>  However little both are understood<br/>Just now,--but by and by the Truth will show 'em<br/>  Herself in her sublimest attitude:<br/>And till she doth, I fain must be content<br/>To share her beauty and her banishment.<br/><br/>Our hero (and, I trust, kind reader! yours)<br/>  Was left upon his way to the chief city<br/>Of the immortal Peter's polished boors,<br/>  Who still have shown themselves more brave than witty.<br/>I know its mighty Empire now allures<br/>  Much flattery--even Voltaire's, and that's a pity.<br/>For me, I deem an absolute autocrat<br/>_Not_ a barbarian, but much worse than that.<br/><br/>And I will war, at least in words (and--should<br/>  My chance so happen--deeds), with all who war<br/>With Thought;--and of Thought's foes by far most rude,<br/>  Tyrants and sycophants have been and are.<br/>I know not who may conquer: if I could<br/>  Have such a prescience, it should be no bar<br/>To this my plain, sworn, downright detestation<br/>Of every despotism in every nation.<br/><br/>It is not that I adulate the people:<br/>  Without _me_, there are demagogues enough,<br/>And infidels, to pull down every steeple,<br/>  And set up in their stead some proper stuff.<br/>Whether they may sow scepticism to reap Hell,<br/>  As is the Christian dogma rather rough,<br/>I do not know;--I wish men to be free<br/>As much from mobs as kings--from you as me.<br/><br/>The consequence is, being of no party,<br/>  I shall offend all parties:--never mind!<br/>My words, at least, are more sincere and hearty<br/>  Than if I sought to sail before the wind.<br/>He who has nought to gain can have small art: he<br/>  Who neither wishes to be bound nor bind,<br/>May still expatiate freely, as will I,<br/>Nor give my voice to slavery's jackal cry.<br/><br/>_That's_ an appropriate simile, _that jackal;_--<br/>  I've heard them in the Ephesian ruins howl<br/>By night, as do that mercenary pack all,<br/>  Power's base purveyors, who for pickings prowl,<br/>And scent the prey their masters would attack all.<br/>  However, the poor jackals are less foul<br/>(As being the brave lions' keen providers)<br/>Than human insects, catering for spiders.<br/><br/>Raise but an arm! 't will brush their web away,<br/>  And without _that_, their poison and their claws<br/>Are useless. Mind, good people! what I say--<br/>  (Or rather Peoples)--_go on_ without pause!<br/>The web of these Tarantulas each day<br/>  Increases, till you shall make common cause:<br/>None, save the Spanish Fly and Attic Bee,<br/>As yet are strongly stinging to be free.<br/><br/>Don Juan, who had shone in the late slaughter,<br/>  Was left upon his way with the despatch,<br/>Where blood was talked of as we would of water;<br/>  And carcasses that lay as thick as thatch<br/>O'er silenced cities, merely served to flatter<br/>  Fair Catherine's pastime--who looked on the match<br/>Between these nations as a main of cocks,<br/>Wherein she liked her own to stand like rocks.<br/><br/>And there in a _kibitka_ he rolled on,<br/>  (A cursed sort of carriage without springs,<br/>Which on rough roads leaves scarcely a whole bone,)<br/>  Pondering on Glory, Chivalry, and Kings,<br/>And Orders, and on all that he had done--<br/>  And wishing that post-horses had the wings<br/>Of Pegasus, or at the least post-chaises<br/>Had feathers, when a traveller on deep ways is.<br/><br/>At every jolt--and they were many--still<br/>  He turned his eyes upon his little charge,<br/>As if he wished that she should fare less ill<br/>  Than he, in these sad highways left at large<br/>To ruts, and flints, and lovely Nature's skill,<br/>  Who is no paviour, nor admits a barge<br/>On _her_ canals, where God takes sea and land,<br/>Fishery and farm, both into his own hand.<br/><br/>At least he pays no rent, and has best right<br/>  To be the first of what we used to call<br/>"Gentlemen farmers"--a race worn out quite,<br/>  Since lately there have been no rents at all,<br/>And "gentlemen" are in a piteous plight,<br/>  And "farmers" can't raise Ceres from her fall:<br/>She fell with Buonaparte,--What strange thoughts<br/>Arise, when we see Emperors fall with oats!<br/><br/>But Juan turned his eyes on the sweet child<br/>  Whom he had saved from slaughter--what a trophy<br/>Oh! ye who build up monuments, defiled<br/>  With gore, like Nadir Shah, that costive Sophy,<br/>Who, after leaving Hindostan a wild,<br/>  And scarce to the Mogul a cup of coffee<br/>To soothe his woes withal, was slain, the sinner!<br/>Because he could no more digest his dinner;--<br/><br/>Oh ye! or we! or he! or she! reflect,<br/>  That _one_ life saved, especially if young<br/>Or pretty, is a thing to recollect<br/>  Far sweeter than the greenest laurels sprung<br/>From the manure of human clay, though decked<br/>  With all the praises ever said or sung:<br/>Though hymned by every harp, unless within<br/>Your heart joins chorus, Fame is but a din.<br/><br/>Oh! ye great authors luminous, voluminous!<br/>  Ye twice ten hundred thousand daily scribes!<br/>Whose pamphlets, volumes, newspapers, illumine us!<br/>  Whether you're paid by government in bribes,<br/>To prove the public debt is not consuming us--<br/>  Or, roughly treading on the "courtier's kibes"<br/>With clownish heel your popular circulation<br/>Feeds you by printing half the realm's starvation;--<br/><br/>Oh, ye great authors!--_A propos des bottes,_--<br/>  I have forgotten what I meant to say,<br/>As sometimes have been greater sages' lots;--<br/>  'T was something calculated to allay<br/>All wrath in barracks, palaces, or cots:<br/>  Certes it would have been but thrown away,<br/>And that's one comfort for my lost advice,<br/>Although no doubt it was beyond all price.<br/><br/>But let it go:--it will one day be found<br/>  With other relics of "a former World,"<br/>When this World shall be _former,_ underground,<br/>  Thrown topsy-turvy, twisted, crisped, and curled,<br/>Baked, fried, or burnt, turned inside-out, or drowned,<br/>  Like all the worlds before, which have been hurled<br/>First out of, and then back again to chaos--<br/>The superstratum which will overlay us.<br/><br/>So Cuvier says:--and then shall come again<br/>  Unto the new creation, rising out<br/>From our old crash, some mystic, ancient strain<br/>  Of things destroyed and left in airy doubt;<br/>Like to the notions we now entertain<br/>  Of Titans, giants, fellows of about<br/>Some hundred feet in height, _not_ to say _miles,_<br/>And mammoths, and your winged crocodiles.<br/><br/>Think if then George the Fourth should be dug up!<br/>  How the new worldlings of the then new East<br/>Will wonder where such animals could sup!<br/>  (For they themselves will be but of the least:<br/>Even worlds miscarry, when too oft they pup,<br/>  And every new creation hath decreased<br/>In size, from overworking the material--<br/>Men are but maggots of some huge Earth's burial.)<br/><br/>_How_ will--to these young people, just thrust out<br/>  From some fresh Paradise, and set to plough,<br/>And dig, and sweat, and turn themselves about,<br/>  And plant, and reap, and spin, and grind, and sow,<br/>Till all the arts at length are brought about,<br/>  Especially of War and taxing,--_how_,<br/>I say, will these great relics, when they see 'em,<br/>Look like the monsters of a new Museum!<br/><br/>But I am apt to grow too metaphysical:<br/>  "The time is out of joint,"--and so am I;<br/>I quite forget this poem's merely quizzical,<br/>  And deviate into matters rather dry.<br/>I ne'er decide what I shall say, and this I call<br/>  Much too poetical: men should know why<br/>They write, and for what end; but, note or text,<br/>I never know the word which will come next.<br/><br/>So on I ramble, now and then narrating,<br/>  Now pondering:--it is time we should narrate.<br/>I left Don Juan with his horses baiting--<br/>  Now we'll get o'er the ground at a great rate:<br/>I shall not be particular in stating<br/>  His journey, we've so many tours of late:<br/>Suppose him then at Petersburgh; suppose<br/>That pleasant capital of painted snows;<br/><br/>Suppose him in a handsome uniform--<br/>  A scarlet coat, black facings, a long plume,<br/>Waving, like sails new shivered in a storm,<br/>  Over a cocked hat in a crowded room,<br/>And brilliant breeches, bright as a Cairn Gorme,<br/>  Of yellow casimire we may presume,<br/>White stockings drawn uncurdled as new milk<br/>O'er limbs whose symmetry set off the silk;<br/><br/>Suppose him sword by side, and hat in hand,<br/>  Made up by Youth, Fame, and an army tailor--<br/>That great enchanter, at whose rod's command<br/>  Beauty springs forth, and Nature's self turns paler,<br/>Seeing how Art can make her work more grand<br/>  (When she don't pin men's limbs in like a gaoler),--<br/>Behold him placed as if upon a pillar! He<br/>Seems Love turned a Lieutenant of Artillery!<br/><br/>His bandage slipped down into a cravat--<br/>  His wings subdued to epaulettes--his quiver<br/>Shrunk to a scabbard, with his arrows at<br/>  His side as a small sword, but sharp as ever--<br/>His bow converted into a cocked hat--<br/>  But still so like, that Psyche were more clever<br/>Than some wives (who make blunders no less stupid),<br/>If she had not mistaken him for Cupid.<br/><br/>The courtiers stared, the ladies whispered, and<br/>  The Empress smiled: the reigning favourite frowned--<br/>I quite forget which of them was in hand<br/>  Just then, as they are rather numerous found,<br/>Who took, by turns, that difficult command<br/>  Since first her Majesty was singly crowned:<br/>But they were mostly nervous six-foot fellows,<br/>All fit to make a Patagonian jealous.<br/><br/>Juan was none of these, but slight and slim,<br/>  Blushing and beardless; and, yet, ne'ertheless,<br/>There was a something in his turn of limb,<br/>  And still more in his eye, which seemed to express,<br/>That, though he looked one of the Seraphim,<br/>  There lurked a man beneath the Spirit's dress.<br/>Besides, the Empress sometimes liked a boy,<br/>And had just buried the fair-faced Lanskoi.<br/><br/>No wonder then that Yermoloff, or Momonoff,<br/>  Or Scherbatoff, or any other _off_<br/>Or _on_, might dread her Majesty had not room enough<br/>  Within her bosom (which was not too tough),<br/>For a new flame; a thought to cast of gloom enough<br/>  Along the aspect, whether smooth or rough,<br/>Of him who, in the language of his station,<br/>Then held that "high official situation."<br/><br/>O gentle ladies! should you seek to know<br/>  The import of this diplomatic phrase,<br/>Bid Ireland's Londonderry's Marquess show<br/>  His parts of speech, and in the strange displays<br/>Of that odd string of words, all in a row,<br/>  Which none divine, and every one obeys,<br/>Perhaps you may pick out some queer _no_ meaning,--<br/>Of that weak wordy harvest the sole gleaning.<br/><br/>I think I can explain myself without<br/>  That sad inexplicable beast of prey--<br/>That Sphinx, whose words would ever be a doubt,<br/>  Did not his deeds unriddle them each day--<br/>That monstrous hieroglyphic--that long spout<br/>  Of blood and water--leaden Castlereagh!<br/>And here I must an anecdote relate,<br/>But luckily of no great length or weight.<br/><br/>An English lady asked of an Italian,<br/>  What were the actual and official duties<br/>Of the strange thing some women set a value on,<br/>  Which hovers oft about some married beauties,<br/>Called "Cavalier Servente?"--a Pygmalion<br/>  Whose statues warm (I fear, alas! too true 't is)<br/>Beneath his art:--the dame, pressed to disclose them,<br/>Said--"Lady, I beseech you to _suppose them_."<br/><br/>And thus I supplicate your supposition,<br/>  And mildest, matron-like interpretation,<br/>Of the imperial favourite's condition.<br/>  'T was a high place, the highest in the nation<br/>In fact, if not in rank; and the suspicion<br/>  Of any one's attaining to his station,<br/>No doubt gave pain, where each new pair of shoulders,<br/>If rather broad, made stocks rise--and their holders.<br/><br/>Juan, I said, was a most beauteous boy,<br/>  And had retained his boyish look beyond<br/>The usual hirsute seasons which destroy,<br/>  With beards and whiskers, and the like, the fond<br/>_Parisian_ aspect, which upset old Troy<br/>  And founded Doctors' Commons:--I have conned<br/>The history of divorces, which, though chequered,<br/>Calls Ilion's the first damages on record.<br/><br/>And Catherine, who loved all things (save her Lord,<br/>  Who was gone to his place), and passed for much,<br/>Admiring those (by dainty dames abhorred)<br/>  Gigantic gentlemen, yet had a touch<br/>Of sentiment: and he she most adored<br/>  Was the lamented Lanskoi, who was such<br/>A lover as had cost her many a tear,<br/>And yet but made a middling grenadier.<br/><br/>Oh thou "_teterrima causa_" of all "_belli_"--<br/>  Thou gate of Life and Death--thou nondescript!<br/>Whence is our exit and our entrance,--well I<br/>  May pause in pondering how all souls are dipped<br/>In thy perennial fountain:--how man _fell_ I<br/>  Know not, since Knowledge saw her branches stripped<br/>Of her first fruit; but how he _falls_ and rises<br/>Since,--_thou_ hast settled beyond all surmises.<br/><br/>Some call thee "the _worst_ cause of War," but I<br/>  Maintain thou art the _best_.--for after all,<br/>From thee we come, to thee we go, and why<br/>  To get at thee not batter down a wall,<br/>Or waste a World? since no one can deny<br/>  Thou dost replenish worlds both great and small:<br/>With--or without thee--all things at a stand<br/>Are, or would be, thou sea of Life's dry land!<br/><br/>Catherine, who was the grand Epitome<br/>  Of that great cause of War, or Peace, or what<br/>You please (it causes all the things which be,<br/>  So you may take your choice of this or that)--<br/>Catherine, I say, was very glad to see<br/>  The handsome herald, on whose plumage sat<br/>Victory; and, pausing as she saw him kneel<br/>With his despatch, forgot to break the seal.<br/><br/>Then recollecting the whole Empress, nor<br/>  Forgetting quite the Woman (which composed<br/>At least three parts of this great whole), she tore<br/>  The letter open with an air which posed<br/>The Court, that watched each look her visage wore,<br/>  Until a royal smile at length disclosed<br/>Fair weather for the day. Though rather spacious,<br/>Her face was noble, her eyes fine, mouth gracious.<br/><br/>Great joy was hers, or rather joys: the first<br/>  Was a ta'en city, thirty thousand slain:<br/>Glory and triumph o'er her aspect burst,<br/>  As an East Indian sunrise on the main:--<br/>These quenched a moment her Ambition's thirst--<br/>  So Arab deserts drink in Summer's rain:<br/>In vain!--As fall the dews on quenchless sands,<br/>Blood only serves to wash Ambition's hands!<br/><br/>Her next amusement was more fanciful;<br/>  She smiled at mad Suwarrow's rhymes, who threw<br/>Into a Russian couplet rather dull<br/>  The whole gazette of thousands whom he slew:<br/>Her third was feminine enough to annul<br/>  The shudder which runs naturally through<br/>Our veins, when things called Sovereigns think it best<br/>To kill, and Generals turn it into jest.<br/><br/>The two first feelings ran their course complete,<br/>  And lighted first her eye, and then her mouth:<br/>The whole court looked immediately most sweet,<br/>  Like flowers well watered after a long drouth:--<br/>But when on the Lieutenant at her feet<br/>  Her Majesty, who liked to gaze on youth<br/>Almost as much as on a new despatch,<br/>Glanced mildly,--all the world was on the watch.<br/><br/>Though somewhat large, exuberant, and truculent,<br/>  When _wroth_--while _pleased_, she was as fine a figure<br/>As those who like things rosy, ripe, and succulent,<br/>  Would wish to look on, while they are in vigour.<br/>She could repay each amatory look you lent<br/>  With interest, and, in turn, was wont with rigour<br/>To exact of Cupid's bills the full amount<br/>At sight, nor would permit you to discount.<br/><br/>With her the latter, though at times convenient,<br/>  Was not so necessary; for they tell<br/>That she was handsome, and though fierce _looked_ lenient,<br/>  And always used her favourites too well.<br/>If once beyond her boudoir's precincts in ye went,<br/>  Your "fortune" was in a fair way "to swell<br/>A man" (as Giles says); for though she would widow all<br/>Nations, she liked Man as an individual.<br/><br/>What a strange thing is Man! and what a stranger<br/>  Is Woman! What a whirlwind is her head,<br/>And what a whirlpool full of depth and danger<br/>  Is all the rest about her! Whether wed,<br/>Or widow--maid--or mother, she can change her<br/>  Mind like the wind: whatever she has said<br/>Or done, is light to what she'll say or do;--<br/>The oldest thing on record, and yet new!<br/><br/>Oh Catherine! (for of all interjections,<br/>  To thee both _oh!_ and _ah!_ belong, of right,<br/>In Love and War) how odd are the connections<br/>  Of human thoughts, which jostle in their flight!<br/>Just now _yours_ were cut out in different sections:<br/>  _First_ Ismail's capture caught your fancy quite;<br/>_Next_ of new knights, the fresh and glorious batch:<br/>And _thirdly_ he who brought you the despatch!<br/><br/>Shakespeare talks of "the herald Mercury<br/>  New lighted on a heaven-kissing hill:"<br/>And some such visions crossed her Majesty,<br/>  While her young herald knelt before her still.<br/>'T is very true the hill seemed rather high,<br/>  For a Lieutenant to climb up; but skill<br/>Smoothed even the Simplon's steep, and by God's blessing,<br/>With Youth and Health all kisses are "Heaven-kissing."<br/><br/>Her Majesty looked down, the youth looked up--<br/>  And so they fell in love;--she with his face,<br/>His grace, his God-knows-what: for Cupid's cup<br/>  With the first draught intoxicates apace,<br/>A quintessential laudanum or "Black Drop,"<br/>  Which makes one drunk at once, without the base<br/>Expedient of full bumpers; for the eye<br/>In love drinks all Life's fountains (save tears) dry.<br/><br/>He, on the other hand, if not in love,<br/>  Fell into that no less imperious passion,<br/>Self-love--which, when some sort of thing above<br/>  Ourselves, a singer, dancer, much in fashion,<br/>Or Duchess--Princess--Empress, "deigns to prove"<br/>  ('T is Pope's phrase) a great longing, though a rash one,<br/>For one especial person out of many,<br/>Make us believe ourselves as good as any.<br/><br/>Besides, he was of that delighted age<br/>  Which makes all female ages equal--when<br/>We don't much care with whom we may engage,<br/>  As bold as Daniel in the lions' den,<br/>So that we can our native sun assuage<br/>  In the next ocean, which may flow just then--<br/>To make a _twilight_ in, just as Sol's heat is<br/>Quenched in the lap of the salt sea, or Thetis.<br/><br/>And Catherine (we must say thus much for Catherine),<br/>  Though bold and bloody, was the kind of thing<br/>Whose temporary passion was quite flattering,<br/>  Because each lover looked a sort of King,<br/>Made up upon an amatory pattern,<br/>  A royal husband in all save the _ring_--<br/>Which, (being the damnedest part of matrimony,)<br/>Seemed taking out the sting to leave the honey:<br/><br/>And when you add to this, her Womanhood<br/>  In its meridian, her blue eyes or gray--<br/>(The last, if they have soul, are quite as good,<br/>  Or better, as the best examples say:<br/>Napoleon's, Mary's (Queen of Scotland), should<br/>  Lend to that colour a transcendent ray;<br/>And Pallas also sanctions the same hue,<br/>Too wise to look through optics black or blue)--<br/><br/>Her sweet smile, and her then majestic figure,<br/>  Her plumpness, her imperial condescension,<br/>Her preference of a boy to men much bigger<br/>  (Fellows whom Messalina's self would pension),<br/>Her prime of life, just now in juicy vigour,<br/>  With other _extras_, which we need not mention,--<br/>All these, or any one of these, explain<br/>Enough to make a stripling very vain.<br/><br/>And that's enough, for Love is vanity,<br/>  Selfish in its beginning as its end,<br/>Except where 't is a mere insanity,<br/>  A maddening spirit which would strive to blend<br/>Itself with Beauty's frail inanity,<br/>  On which the Passion's self seems to depend;<br/>And hence some heathenish philosophers<br/>Make Love the main-spring of the Universe.<br/><br/>Besides Platonic love, besides the love<br/>  Of God, the love of sentiment, the loving<br/>Of faithful pairs--(I needs must rhyme with dove,<br/>  That good old steam-boat which keeps verses moving<br/>'Gainst reason--Reason ne'er was hand-and-glove<br/>  With rhyme, but always leant less to improving<br/>The sound than sense)--besides all these pretences<br/>To Love, there are those things which words name senses;<br/><br/>Those movements, those improvements in our bodies<br/>  Which make all bodies anxious to get out<br/>Of their own sand-pits, to mix with a goddess,<br/>  For such all women are at first no doubt.<br/>How beautiful that moment! and how odd is<br/>  That fever which precedes the languid rout<br/>Of our sensations! What a curious way<br/>The whole thing is of clothing souls in clay!<br/><br/>The noblest kind of love is love Platonical,<br/>  To end or to begin with; the next grand<br/>Is that which may be christened love canonical,<br/>  Because the clergy take the thing in hand;<br/>The third sort to be noted in our chronicle<br/>  As flourishing in every Christian land,<br/>Is when chaste matrons to their other ties<br/>Add what may be called _marriage in disguise_.<br/><br/>Well, we won't analyse--our story must<br/>  Tell for itself: the Sovereign was smitten,<br/>Juan much flattered by her love, or lust;--<br/>  I cannot stop to alter words once written,<br/>And the _two_ are so mixed with human dust,<br/>  That he who _names one_, both perchance may hit on:<br/>But in such matters Russia's mighty Empress<br/>Behaved no better than a common sempstress.<br/><br/>The whole court melted into one wide whisper,<br/>  And all lips were applied unto all ears!<br/>The elder ladies' wrinkles curled much crisper<br/>  As they beheld; the younger cast some leers<br/>On one another, and each lovely lisper<br/>  Smiled as she talked the matter o'er; but tears<br/>Of rivalship rose in each clouded eye<br/>Of all the standing army who stood by.<br/><br/>All the ambassadors of all the powers<br/>  Inquired, Who was this very new young man,<br/>Who promised to be great in some few hours?<br/>  Which is full soon (though Life is but a span).<br/>Already they beheld the silver showers<br/>  Of rubles rain, as fast as specie can,<br/>Upon his cabinet, besides the presents<br/>Of several ribands, and some thousand peasants.<br/><br/>Catherine was generous,--all such ladies are:<br/>  Love--that great opener of the heart and all<br/>The ways that lead there, be they near or far,<br/>  Above, below, by turnpikes great or small,--<br/>Love--(though she had a cursed taste for War,<br/>  And was not the best wife unless we call<br/>Such Clytemnestra, though perhaps 't is better<br/>That one should die--than two drag on the fetter)--<br/><br/>Love had made Catherine make each lover's fortune,<br/>  Unlike our own half-chaste Elizabeth,<br/>Whose avarice all disbursements did importune,<br/>  If History, the grand liar, ever saith<br/>The truth; and though grief her old age might shorten,<br/>  Because she put a favourite to death,<br/>Her vile, ambiguous method of flirtation,<br/>And stinginess, disgrace her sex and station.<br/><br/>But when the lev√©e rose, and all was bustle<br/>  In the dissolving circle, all the nations'<br/>Ambassadors began as 't were to hustle<br/>  Round the young man with their congratulations.<br/>Also the softer silks were heard to rustle<br/>  Of gentle dames, among whose recreations<br/>It is to speculate on handsome faces,<br/>Especially when such lead to high places.<br/><br/>Juan, who found himself, he knew not how,<br/>  A general object of attention, made<br/>His answers with a very graceful bow,<br/>  As if born for the ministerial trade.<br/>Though modest, on his unembarrassed brow<br/>  Nature had written "Gentleman!" He said<br/>Little, but to the purpose; and his manner<br/>Flung hovering graces o'er him like a banner.<br/><br/>An order from her Majesty consigned<br/>  Our young Lieutenant to the genial care<br/>Of those in office: all the world looked kind,<br/>  (As it will look sometimes with the first stare,<br/>Which Youth would not act ill to keep in mind,)<br/>  As also did Miss Protasoff then there,<br/>Named from her mystic office "l'Eprouveuse,"<br/>A term inexplicable to the Muse.<br/><br/>With _her_ then, as in humble duty bound,<br/>  Juan retired,--and so will I, until<br/>My Pegasus shall tire of touching ground.<br/>  We have just lit on a "heaven-kissing hill,"<br/>So lofty that I feel my brain turn round,<br/>  And all my fancies whirling like a mill;<br/>Which is a signal to my nerves and brain,<br/>To take a quiet ride in some green lane.<br/><br/>CANTO THE TENTH.<br/><br/>When Newton saw an apple fall, he found<br/>  In that slight startle from his contemplation--<br/>'T is _said_ (for I'll not answer above ground<br/>  For any sage's creed or calculation)--<br/>A mode of proving that the Earth turned round<br/>  In a most natural whirl, called "gravitation;"<br/>And this is the sole mortal who could grapple,<br/>Since Adam--with a fall--or with an apple.<br/><br/>Man fell with apples, and with apples rose,<br/>  If this be true; for we must deem the mode<br/>In which Sir Isaac Newton could disclose<br/>  Through the then unpaved stars the turnpike road,<br/>A thing to counterbalance human woes:<br/>  For ever since immortal man hath glowed<br/>With all kinds of mechanics, and full soon<br/>Steam-engines will conduct him to the moon.<br/><br/>And wherefore this exordium?--Why, just now,<br/>  In taking up this paltry sheet of paper,<br/>My bosom underwent a glorious glow,<br/>  And my internal spirit cut a caper:<br/>And though so much inferior, as I know,<br/>  To those who, by the dint of glass and vapour,<br/>Discover stars, and sail in the wind's eye,<br/>I wish to do as much by Poesy.<br/><br/>In the wind's eye I have sailed, and sail; but for<br/>  The stars, I own my telescope is dim;<br/>But at the least I have shunned the common shore,<br/>  And leaving land far out of sight, would skim<br/>The Ocean of Eternity: the roar<br/>  Of breakers has not daunted my slight, trim,<br/>But _still_ sea-worthy skiff; and she may float<br/>Where ships have foundered, as doth many a boat.<br/><br/>We left our hero, Juan, in the _bloom_<br/>  Of favouritism, but not yet in the _blush;--_<br/>And far be it from my _Muses_ to presume<br/>  (For I have more than one Muse at a push),<br/>To follow him beyond the drawing-room:<br/>  It is enough that Fortune found him flush<br/>Of Youth, and Vigour, Beauty, and those things<br/>Which for an instant clip Enjoyment's wings.<br/><br/>But soon they grow again and leave their nest.<br/>  "Oh!" saith the Psalmist, "that I had a dove's<br/>Pinions to flee away, and be at rest!"<br/>  And who that recollects young years and loves,--<br/>Though hoary now, and with a withering breast,<br/>  And palsied Fancy, which no longer roves<br/>Beyond its dimmed eye's sphere,--but would much rather<br/>Sigh like his son, than cough like his grandfather?<br/><br/>But sighs subside, and tears (even widows') shrink,<br/>  Like Arno in the summer, to a shallow,<br/>So narrow as to shame their wintry brink,<br/>  Which threatens inundations deep and yellow!<br/>Such difference doth a few months make. You'd think<br/>  Grief a rich field which never would lie fallow;<br/>No more it doth--its ploughs but change their boys,<br/>Who furrow some new soil to sow for joys.<br/><br/>But coughs will come when sighs depart--and now<br/>  And then before sighs cease; for oft the one<br/>Will bring the other, ere the lake-like brow<br/>  Is ruffled by a wrinkle, or the Sun<br/>Of Life reached ten o'clock: and while a glow,<br/>  Hectic and brief as summer's day nigh done,<br/>O'erspreads the cheek which seems too pure for clay,<br/>Thousands blaze, love, hope, die,--how happy they!--<br/><br/>But Juan was not meant to die so soon:--<br/>  We left him in the focus of such glory<br/>As may be won by favour of the moon<br/>  Or ladies' fancies--rather transitory<br/>Perhaps; but who would scorn the month of June,<br/>  Because December, with his breath so hoary,<br/>Must come? Much rather should he court the ray,<br/>To hoard up warmth against a wintry day.<br/><br/>Besides, he had some qualities which fix<br/>  Middle-aged ladies even more than young:<br/>The former know what's what; while new-fledged chicks<br/>  Know little more of Love than what is sung<br/>In rhymes, or dreamt (for Fancy will play tricks)<br/>  In visions of those skies from whence Love sprung.<br/>Some reckon women by their suns or years,<br/>I rather think the Moon should date the dears.<br/><br/>And why? because she's changeable and chaste:<br/>  I know no other reason, whatsoe'er<br/>Suspicious people, who find fault in haste,<br/>  May choose to tax me with; which is not fair,<br/>Nor flattering to "their temper or their taste,"<br/>  As my friend Jeffrey writes with such an air:<br/>However, I forgive him, and I trust<br/>He will forgive himself;--if not, I must.<br/><br/>Old enemies who have become new friends<br/>  Should so continue--'t is a point of honour;<br/>And I know nothing which could make amends<br/>  For a return to Hatred: I would shun her<br/>Like garlic, howsoever she extends<br/>  Her hundred arms and legs, and fain outrun her.<br/>Old flames, new wives, become our bitterest foes--<br/>Converted foes should scorn to join with those.<br/><br/>This were the worst desertion:--renegadoes,<br/>  Even shuffling Southey, that incarnate lie,<br/>Would scarcely join again the "reformadoes,"<br/>  Whom he forsook to fill the Laureate's sty;<br/>And honest men from Iceland to Barbadoes,<br/>  Whether in Caledon or Italy,<br/>Should not veer round with every breath, nor seize<br/>To pain, the moment when you cease to please.<br/><br/>The lawyer and the critic but behold<br/>  The baser sides of literature and life,<br/>And nought remains unseen, but much untold,<br/>  By those who scour those double vales of strife.<br/>While common men grow ignorantly old,<br/>  The lawyer's brief is like the surgeon's knife,<br/>Dissecting the whole inside of a question,<br/>And with it all the process of digestion.<br/><br/>A legal broom's a moral chimney-sweeper,<br/>  And that's the reason he himself's so dirty;<br/>The endless soot bestows a tint far deeper<br/>  Than can be hid by altering his shirt; he<br/>Retains the sable stains of the dark creeper,<br/>  At least some twenty-nine do out of thirty,<br/>In all their habits;--not so _you_, I own;<br/>As C√¶sar wore his robe you wear your gown.<br/><br/>And all our little feuds, at least all _mine_,<br/>  Dear Jeffrey, once my most redoubted foe<br/>(As far as rhyme and criticism combine<br/>  To make such puppets of us things below),<br/>Are over: Here's a health to "Auld Lang Syne!"<br/>  I do not know you, and may never know<br/>Your face--but you have acted on the whole<br/>Most nobly, and I own it from my soul.<br/><br/>And when I use the phrase of "Auld Lang Syne!"<br/>  'T is not addressed to you--the more's the pity<br/>For me, for I would rather take my wine<br/>  With you, than aught (save Scott) in your proud city:<br/>But somehow--it may seem a schoolboy's whine,<br/>  And yet I seek not to be grand nor witty,<br/>But I am half a Scot by birth, and bred<br/>A whole one, and my heart flies to my head,--<br/><br/>As "Auld Lang Syne" brings Scotland, one and all,<br/>  Scotch plaids, Scotch snoods, the blue hills, and clear streams,<br/>The Dee--the Don--Balgounie's brig's _black wall_--<br/>  All my boy feelings, all my gentler dreams<br/>Of what I _then dreamt_, clothed in their own pall,--<br/>  Like Banquo's offspring--floating past me seems<br/>My childhood, in this childishness of mine:--<br/>I care not--'t is a glimpse of "_Auld Lang Syne_."<br/><br/>And though, as you remember, in a fit<br/>  Of wrath and rhyme, when juvenile and curly,<br/>I railed at Scots to show my wrath and wit,<br/>  Which must be owned was sensitive and surly,<br/>Yet 't is in vain such sallies to permit,<br/>  They cannot quench young feelings fresh and early:<br/>I "_scotched_ not killed" the Scotchman in my blood,<br/>And love the land of "mountain and of flood."<br/><br/>Don Juan, who was real, or ideal,--<br/>  For both are much the same, since what men think<br/>Exists when the once thinkers are less real<br/>  Than what they thought, for Mind can never sink,<br/>And 'gainst the Body makes a strong appeal;<br/>  And yet 't is very puzzling on the brink<br/>Of what is called Eternity to stare,<br/>And know no more of what is _here_, than _there_;--<br/><br/>Don Juan grew a very polished Russian--<br/>  _How_ we won't mention, _why_ we need not say:<br/>Few youthful minds can stand the strong concussion<br/>  Of any slight temptation in their way;<br/>But _his_ just now were spread as is a cushion<br/>  Smoothed for a Monarch's seat of honour: gay<br/>Damsels, and dances, revels, ready money,<br/>Made ice seem Paradise, and winter sunny.<br/><br/>The favour of the Empress was agreeable;<br/>  And though the duty waxed a little hard,<br/>Young people at his time of life should be able<br/>  To come off handsomely in that regard.<br/>He was now growing up like a green tree, able<br/>  For Love, War, or Ambition, which reward<br/>Their luckier votaries, till old Age's tedium<br/>Make some prefer the circulating medium.<br/><br/>About this time, as might have been anticipated,<br/>  Seduced by Youth and dangerous examples,<br/>Don Juan grew, I fear, a little dissipated;<br/>  Which is a sad thing, and not only tramples<br/>On our fresh feelings, but--as being participated<br/>  With all kinds of incorrigible samples<br/>Of frail humanity--must make us selfish,<br/>And shut our souls up in us like a shell-fish.<br/><br/>This we pass over. We will also pass<br/>  The usual progress of intrigues between<br/>Unequal matches, such as are, alas!<br/>  A young Lieutenant's with a _not old_ Queen,<br/>But one who is not so youthful as she was<br/>  In all the royalty of sweet seventeen.<br/>Sovereigns may sway materials, but not matter,<br/>And wrinkles, the d----d democrats! won't flatter.<br/><br/>And Death, the Sovereign's Sovereign, though the great<br/>  Gracchus of all mortality, who levels,<br/>With his _Agrarian_ laws, the high estate<br/>  Of him who feasts, and fights, and roars, and revels,<br/>To one small grass-grown patch (which must await<br/>  Corruption for its crop) with the poor devils<br/>Who never had a foot of land till now,--<br/>Death's a reformer--all men must allow.<br/><br/>He lived (not Death, but Juan) in a hurry<br/>  Of waste, and haste, and glare, and gloss, and glitter,<br/>In this gay clime of bear-skins black and furry--<br/>  Which (though I hate to say a thing that's bitter)<br/>Peep out sometimes, when things are in a flurry,<br/>  Through all the "purple and fine linen," fitter<br/>For Babylon's than Russia's royal harlot--<br/>And neutralise her outward show of scarlet.<br/><br/>And this same state we won't describe: we would<br/>  Perhaps from hearsay, or from recollection:<br/>But getting nigh grim Dante's "obscure wood,"<br/>  That horrid equinox, that hateful section<br/>Of human years--that half-way house--that rude<br/>  Hut, whence wise travellers drive with circumspection<br/>Life's sad post-horses o'er the dreary frontier<br/>Of Age, and looking back to Youth, give _one_ tear;--<br/><br/>I won't describe,--that is, if I can help<br/>  Description; and I won't reflect,--that is,<br/>If I can stave off thought, which--as a whelp<br/>  Clings to its teat--sticks to me through the abyss<br/>Of this odd labyrinth; or as the kelp<br/>  Holds by the rock; or as a lover's kiss<br/>Drains its first draught of lips:--but, as I said,<br/>I _won't_ philosophise, and _will_ be read.<br/><br/>Juan, instead of courting courts, was courted,--<br/>  A thing which happens rarely: this he owed<br/>Much to his youth, and much to his reported<br/>  Valour; much also to the blood he showed,<br/>Like a race-horse; much to each dress he sported,<br/>  Which set the beauty off in which he glowed,<br/>As purple clouds befringe the sun; but most<br/>He owed to an old woman and his post.<br/><br/>He wrote to Spain;--and all his near relations,<br/>  Perceiving he was in a handsome way<br/>Of getting on himself, and finding stations<br/>  For cousins also, answered the same day.<br/>Several prepared themselves for emigrations;<br/>  And eating ices, were o'erheard to say,<br/>That with the addition of a slight pelisse,<br/>Madrid's and Moscow's climes were of a piece.<br/><br/>His mother, Donna Inez, finding, too,<br/>  That in the lieu of drawing on his banker,<br/>Where his assets were waxing rather few,<br/>  He had brought his spending to a handsome anchor,--<br/>Replied, "that she was glad to see him through<br/>  Those pleasures after which wild youth will hanker;<br/>As the sole sign of Man's being in his senses<br/>Is--learning to reduce his past expenses.<br/><br/>"She also recommended him to God,<br/>  And no less to God's Son, as well as Mother,<br/>Warned him against Greek worship, which looks odd<br/>  In Catholic eyes; but told him, too, to smother<br/>_Outward_ dislike, which don't look well abroad;<br/>  Informed him that he had a little brother<br/>Born in a second wedlock; and above<br/>All, praised the Empress's _maternal_ love.<br/><br/>"She could not too much give her approbation<br/>  Unto an Empress, who preferred young men<br/>Whose age, and what was better still, whose nation<br/>  And climate, stopped all scandal (now and then);--<br/>At home it might have given her some vexation;<br/>  But where thermometers sink down to ten,<br/>Or five, or one, or zero, she could never<br/>Believe that Virtue thawed before the river."<br/><br/>Oh for a _forty-parson power_--to chant<br/>  Thy praise, Hypocrisy! Oh for a hymn<br/>Loud as the virtues thou dost loudly vaunt,<br/>  Not practise! Oh for trump of Cherubim!<br/>Or the ear-trumpet of my good old aunt,<br/>  Who, though her spectacles at last grew dim,<br/>Drew quiet consolation through its hint,<br/>When she no more could read the pious print.<br/><br/>She was no Hypocrite at least, poor soul,<br/>  But went to heaven in as sincere a way<br/>As anybody on the elected roll,<br/>  Which portions out upon the Judgment Day<br/>Heaven's freeholds, in a sort of Doomsday scroll,<br/>  Such as the conqueror William did repay<br/>His knights with, lotting others' properties<br/>Into some sixty thousand new knights' fees.<br/><br/>I can't complain, whose ancestors are there,<br/>  Erneis, Radulphus--eight-and-forty manors<br/>(If that my memory doth not greatly err)<br/>  Were _their_ reward for following Billy's banners:<br/>And though I can't help thinking 't was scarce fair<br/>  To strip the Saxons of their _hydes_ like tanners;<br/>Yet as they founded churches with the produce,<br/>You'll deem, no doubt, they put it to a good use.<br/><br/>The gentle Juan flourished, though at times<br/>  He felt like other plants called sensitive,<br/>Which shrink from touch, as Monarchs do from rhymes,<br/>  Save such as Southey can afford to give.<br/>Perhaps he longed in bitter frosts for climes<br/>  In which the Neva's ice would cease to live<br/>Before May-day: perhaps, despite his duty,<br/>In Royalty's vast arms he sighed for Beauty:<br/><br/>Perhaps--but, sans perhaps, we need not seek<br/>  For causes young or old: the canker-worm<br/>Will feed upon the fairest, freshest cheek,<br/>  As well as further drain the withered form:<br/>Care, like a housekeeper, brings every week<br/>  His bills in, and however we may storm,<br/>They must be paid: though six days smoothly run,<br/>The seventh will bring blue devils or a dun.<br/><br/>I don't know how it was, but he grew sick:<br/>  The Empress was alarmed, and her physician<br/>(The same who physicked Peter) found the tick<br/>  Of his fierce pulse betoken a condition<br/>Which augured of the dead, however _quick_<br/>  Itself, and showed a feverish disposition;<br/>At which the whole Court was extremely troubled,<br/>The Sovereign shocked, and all his medicines doubled.<br/><br/>Low were the whispers, manifold the rumours:<br/>  Some said he had been poisoned by Potemkin;<br/>Others talked learnedly of certain tumours,<br/>  Exhaustion, or disorders of the same kin;<br/>Some said 't was a concoction of the humours,<br/>  Which with the blood too readily will claim kin:<br/>Others again were ready to maintain,<br/>"'T was only the fatigue of last campaign."<br/><br/>But here is one prescription out of many:<br/>  "_Sodae sulphat_.  ívj.  ífs. _Mannae optim._<br/>_Aq. fervent._ f. ‚Ñ• ifs.  íij. _tinct. Sennae_<br/>  _Haustus_" (And here the surgeon came and cupped him)<br/>"‚Ñû _Pulv. Com._ gr. iij. _Ipecacuanh√¶_"<br/>  (With more beside if Juan had not stopped 'em).<br/>"_Bolus Potassae Sulphuret. sumendus_,<br/>_Et haustus ter in die capiendus._"<br/><br/>This is the way physicians mend or end us,<br/>  _Secundum artem_: but although we sneer<br/>In health--when ill, we call them to attend us,<br/>  Without the least propensity to jeer;<br/>While that "_hiatus maxime deflendus_"<br/>  To be filled up by spade or mattock's near,<br/>Instead of gliding graciously down Lethe,<br/>We tease mild Baillie, or soft Abernethy.<br/><br/>Juan demurred at this first notice to<br/>  Quit; and though Death had threatened an ejection,<br/>His youth and constitution bore him through,<br/>  And sent the doctors in a new direction.<br/>But still his state was delicate: the hue<br/>  Of health but flickered with a faint reflection<br/>Along his wasted cheek, and seemed to gravel<br/>The faculty--who said that he must travel.<br/><br/>The climate was too cold, they said, for him,<br/>  Meridian-born, to bloom in. This opinion<br/>Made the chaste Catherine look a little grim,<br/>  Who did not like at first to lose her minion:<br/>But when she saw his dazzling eye wax dim,<br/>  And drooping like an eagle's with clipt pinion,<br/>She then resolved to send him on a mission,<br/>But in a style becoming his condition.<br/><br/>There was just then a kind of a discussion,<br/>  A sort of treaty or negotiation,<br/>Between the British cabinet and Russian,<br/>  Maintained with all the due prevarication<br/>With which great states such things are apt to push on;<br/>  Something about the Baltic's navigation,<br/>Hides, train-oil, tallow, and the rights of Thetis,<br/>Which Britons deem their _uti possidetis_.<br/><br/>So Catherine, who had a handsome way<br/>  Of fitting out her favourites, conferred<br/>This secret charge on Juan, to display<br/>  At once her royal splendour, and reward<br/>His services. He kissed hands the next day,<br/>  Received instructions how to play his card,<br/>Was laden with all kinds of gifts and honours,<br/>Which showed what great discernment was the donor's.<br/><br/>But she was lucky, and luck's all. Your Queens<br/>  Are generally prosperous in reigning--<br/>Which puzzles us to know what Fortune means:--<br/>  But to continue--though her years were waning,<br/>Her climacteric teased her like her teens;<br/>  And though her dignity brooked no complaining,<br/>So much did Juan's setting off distress her,<br/>She could not find at first a fit successor.<br/><br/>But Time, the comforter, will come at last;<br/>  And four-and-twenty hours, and twice that number<br/>Of candidates requesting to be placed,<br/>  Made Catherine taste next night a quiet slumber:--<br/>Not that she meant to fix again in haste,<br/>  Nor did she find the quantity encumber,<br/>But always choosing with deliberation,<br/>Kept the place open for their emulation.<br/><br/>While this high post of honour's in abeyance,<br/>  For one or two days, reader, we request<br/>You'll mount with our young hero the conveyance<br/>  Which wafted him from Petersburgh: the best<br/>Barouche, which had the glory to display once<br/>  The fair Czarina's autocratic crest,<br/>When, a new Iphigene, she went to Tauris,<br/>Was given to her favourite, and now _bore his_.<br/><br/>A bull-dog, and a bullfinch, and an ermine,<br/>  All private favourites of Don Juan;--for<br/>(Let deeper sages the true cause determine)<br/>  He had a kind of inclination, or<br/>Weakness, for what most people deem mere vermin,<br/>  Live animals: an old maid of threescore<br/>For cats and birds more penchant ne'er displayed,<br/>Although he was not old, nor even a maid;--<br/><br/>The animals aforesaid occupied<br/>  Their station: there were valets, secretaries,<br/>In other vehicles; but at his side<br/>  Sat little Leila, who survived the parries<br/>He made 'gainst Cossacque sabres in the wide<br/>  Slaughter of Ismail. Though my wild Muse varies<br/>Her note, she don't forget the infant girl<br/>Whom he preserved, a pure and living pearl.<br/><br/>Poor little thing! She was as fair as docile,<br/>  And with that gentle, serious character,<br/>As rare in living beings as a fossile<br/>  Man, 'midst thy mouldy mammoths, "grand Cuvier!"<br/>Ill fitted was her ignorance to jostle<br/>  With this o'erwhelming world, where all must err:<br/>But she was yet but ten years old, and therefore<br/>Was tranquil, though she knew not why or wherefore.<br/><br/>Don Juan loved her, and she loved him, as<br/>  Nor brother, father, sister, daughter love.--I<br/>cannot tell exactly what it was;<br/>  He was not yet quite old enough to prove<br/>Parental feelings, and the other class,<br/>  Called brotherly affection, could not move<br/>His bosom,--for he never had a sister:<br/>Ah! if he had--how much he would have missed her!<br/><br/>And still less was it sensual; for besides<br/>  That he was not an ancient debauchee,<br/>(Who like sour fruit, to stir their veins' salt tides,<br/>  As acids rouse a dormant alkali,)<br/>Although (_'t will_ happen as our planet guides)<br/>  His youth was not the chastest that might be,<br/>There was the purest Platonism at bottom<br/>Of all his feelings--only he forgot 'em.<br/><br/>Just now there was no peril of temptation;<br/>  He loved the infant orphan he had saved,<br/>As patriots (now and then) may love a nation;<br/>  His pride, too, felt that she was not enslaved<br/>Owing to him;--as also her salvation<br/>  Through his means and the Church's might be paved.<br/>But one thing's odd, which here must be inserted,<br/>The little Turk refused to be converted.<br/><br/>'T was strange enough she should retain the impression<br/>  Through such a scene of change, and dread, and slaughter;<br/>But though three Bishops told her the transgression,<br/>  She showed a great dislike to holy water;<br/>She also had no passion for confession;<br/>  Perhaps she had nothing to confess:--no matter,<br/>Whate'er the cause, the Church made little of it--<br/>She still held out that Mahomet was a prophet.<br/><br/>In fact, the only Christian she could bear<br/>  Was Juan; whom she seemed to have selected<br/>In place of what her home and friends once _were_.<br/>  _He_ naturally loved what he protected:<br/>And thus they formed a rather curious pair,<br/>  A guardian green in years, a ward connected<br/>In neither clime, time, blood, with her defender;<br/>And yet this want of ties made theirs more tender.<br/><br/>They journeyed on through Poland and through Warsaw,<br/>  Famous for mines of salt and yokes of iron:<br/>Through Courland also, which that famous farce saw<br/>  Which gave her dukes the graceless name of "Biron."<br/>'T is the same landscape which the modern Mars saw,<br/>  Who marched to Moscow, led by Fame, the Siren!<br/>To lose by one month's frost some twenty years<br/>Of conquest, and his guard of Grenadiers.<br/><br/>Let this not seem an anti-climax:--"Oh!<br/>  My guard! my old guard!" exclaimed that god of clay.<br/>Think of the Thunderer's falling down below<br/>  Carotid-artery-cutting Castlereagh!<br/>Alas! that glory should be chilled by snow!<br/>  But should we wish to warm us on our way<br/>Through Poland, there is Kosciusko's name<br/>Might scatter fire through ice, like Hecla's flame.<br/><br/>From Poland they came on through Prussia Proper,<br/>  And K√∂nigsberg, the capital, whose vaunt,<br/>Besides some veins of iron, lead, or copper,<br/>  Has lately been the great Professor Kant.<br/>Juan, who cared not a tobacco-stopper<br/>  About philosophy, pursued his jaunt<br/>To Germany, whose somewhat tardy millions<br/>Have princes who spur more than their postilions.<br/><br/>And thence through Berlin, Dresden, and the like,<br/>  Until he reached the castellated Rhine:--<br/>Ye glorious Gothic scenes! how much ye strike<br/>  All phantasies, not even excepting mine!<br/>A grey wall, a green ruin, rusty pike,<br/>  Make my soul pass the equinoctial line<br/>Between the present and past worlds, and hover<br/>Upon their airy confines, half-seas-over.<br/><br/>But Juan posted on through Mannheim, Bonn,<br/>  Which Drachenfels frowns over like a spectre<br/>Of the good feudal times for ever gone,<br/>  On which I have not time just now to lecture.<br/>From thence he was drawn onwards to Cologne,<br/>  A city which presents to the inspector<br/>Eleven thousand maiden heads of bone.<br/>The greatest number flesh hath ever known.<br/><br/>From thence to Holland's Hague and Helvoetsluys,<br/>  That water-land of Dutchmen and of ditches,<br/>Where juniper expresses its best juice,<br/>  The poor man's sparkling substitute for riches.<br/>Senates and sages have condemned its use--<br/>  But to deny the mob a cordial, which is<br/>Too often all the clothing, meat, or fuel,<br/>Good government has left them, seems but cruel.<br/><br/>Here he embarked, and with a flowing sail<br/>  Went bounding for the Island of the free,<br/>Towards which the impatient wind blew half a gale;<br/>  High dashed the spray, the bows dipped in the sea,<br/>And sea-sick passengers turned somewhat pale;<br/>  But Juan, seasoned, as he well might be,<br/>By former voyages, stood to watch the skiffs<br/>Which passed, or catch the first glimpse of the cliffs.<br/><br/>At length they rose, like a white wall along<br/>  The blue sea's border; and Don Juan felt--<br/>What even young strangers feel a little strong<br/>  At the first sight of Albion's chalky belt--A<br/>kind of pride that he should be among<br/>  Those haughty shopkeepers, who sternly dealt<br/>Their goods and edicts out from pole to pole,<br/>And made the very billows pay them toll.<br/><br/>I've no great cause to love that spot of earth,<br/>  Which holds what _might have been_ the noblest nation;<br/>But though I owe it little but my birth,<br/>  I feel a mixed regret and veneration<br/>For its decaying fame and former worth.<br/>  Seven years (the usual term of transportation)<br/>Of absence lay one's old resentments level,<br/>When a man's country's going to the devil.<br/><br/>Alas! could she but fully, truly, know<br/>  How her great name is now throughout abhorred;<br/>How eager all the Earth is for the blow<br/>  Which shall lay bare her bosom to the sword;<br/>How all the nations deem her their worst foe,<br/>  That worse than _worst of foes_, the once adored<br/>False friend, who held out Freedom to Mankind,<br/>And now would chain them--to the very _mind_;--<br/><br/>Would she be proud, or boast herself the free,<br/>  Who is but first of slaves? The nations are<br/>In prison,--but the gaoler, what is he?<br/>  No less a victim to the bolt and bar.<br/>Is the poor privilege to turn the key<br/>  Upon the captive, Freedom? He's as far<br/>From the enjoyment of the earth and air<br/>Who watches o'er the chain, as they who wear.<br/><br/>Don Juan now saw Albion's earliest beauties,<br/>  Thy cliffs, _dear_ Dover! harbour, and hotel;<br/>Thy custom-house, with all its delicate duties;<br/>  Thy waiters running mucks at every bell;<br/>Thy packets, all whose passengers are booties<br/>  To those who upon land or water dwell;<br/>And last, not least, to strangers uninstructed,<br/>Thy long, long bills, whence nothing is deducted.<br/><br/>Juan, though careless, young, and _magnifique_,<br/>  And rich in rubles, diamonds, cash, and credit,<br/>Who did not limit much his bills per week,<br/>  Yet stared at this a little, though he paid it,--<br/>(His Maggior Duomo, a smart, subtle Greek,<br/>  Before him summed the awful scroll and read it):<br/>But, doubtless, as the air--though seldom sunny--<br/>Is free, the respiration's worth the money.<br/><br/>On with the horses! Off to Canterbury!<br/>  Tramp, tramp o'er pebble, and splash, splash through puddle;<br/>Hurrah! how swiftly speeds the post so merry!<br/>  Not like slow Germany, wherein they muddle<br/>Along the road, as if they went to bury<br/>  Their fare; and also pause besides, to fuddle<br/>With "schnapps"--sad dogs! whom "Hundsfot," or "Verflucter,"<br/>Affect no more than lightning a conductor.<br/><br/>Now there is nothing gives a man such spirits,<br/>  Leavening his blood as cayenne doth a curry,<br/>As going at full speed--no matter where its<br/>  Direction be, so 't is but in a hurry,<br/>And merely for the sake of its own merits;<br/>  For the less cause there is for all this flurry,<br/>The greater is the pleasure in arriving<br/>At the great _end_ of travel--which is driving.<br/><br/>They saw at Canterbury the cathedral;<br/>  Black Edward's helm, and Becket's bloody stone,<br/>Were pointed out as usual by the bedral,<br/>  In the same quaint, uninterested tone:--<br/>There's glory again for you, gentle reader! All<br/>  Ends in a rusty casque and dubious bone,<br/>Half-solved into these sodas or magnesias,<br/>Which form that bitter draught, the human species.<br/><br/>The effect on Juan was of course sublime:<br/>  He breathed a thousand Cressys, as he saw<br/>That casque, which never stooped except to Time.<br/>  Even the bold Churchman's tomb excited awe,<br/>Who died in the then great attempt to climb<br/>  O'er Kings, who _now_ at least _must talk_ of Law<br/>Before they butcher. Little Leila gazed,<br/>And asked why such a structure had been raised:<br/><br/>And being told it was "God's House," she said<br/>  He was well lodged, but only wondered how<br/>He suffered Infidels in his homestead,<br/>  The cruel Nazarenes, who had laid low<br/>His holy temples in the lands which bred<br/>  The True Believers;--and her infant brow<br/>Was bent with grief that Mahomet should resign<br/>A mosque so noble, flung like pearls to swine.<br/><br/>On! on! through meadows, managed like a garden,<br/>  A paradise of hops and high production;<br/>For, after years of travel by a bard in<br/>  Countries of greater heat, but lesser suction,<br/>A green field is a sight which makes him pardon<br/>  The absence of that more sublime construction,<br/>Which mixes up vines--olives--precipices--<br/>Glaciers--volcanoes--oranges and ices.<br/><br/>And when I think upon a pot of beer----<br/>  But I won't weep!--and so drive on, postilions!<br/>As the smart boys spurred fast in their career,<br/>  Juan admired these highways of free millions--<br/>A country in all senses the most dear<br/>  To foreigner or native, save some silly ones,<br/>Who "kick against the pricks" just at this juncture,<br/>And for their pains get only a fresh puncture.<br/><br/>What a delightful thing's a turnpike road!<br/>  So smooth, so level, such a mode of shaving<br/>The Earth, as scarce the eagle in the broad<br/>  Air can accomplish, with his wide wings waving.<br/>Had such been cut in Phaeton's time, the god<br/>  Had told his son to satisfy his craving<br/>With the York mail;--but onward as we roll,<br/>_Surgit amari aliquid_--the toll!<br/><br/>Alas! how deeply painful is all payment!<br/>  Take lives--take wives--take aught except men's purses:<br/>As Machiavel shows those in purple raiment,<br/>  Such is the shortest way to general curses.<br/>They hate a murderer much less than a claimant<br/>  On that sweet ore which everybody nurses.--<br/>Kill a man's family, and he may brook it,<br/>But keep your hands out of his breeches' pocket:<br/><br/>So said the Florentine: ye monarchs, hearken<br/>  To your instructor. Juan now was borne,<br/>Just as the day began to wane and darken,<br/>  O'er the high hill, which looks with pride or scorn<br/>Toward the great city.--Ye who have a spark in<br/>  Your veins of Cockney spirit, smile or mourn<br/>According as you take things well or ill;--<br/>Bold Britons, we are now on Shooter's Hill!<br/><br/>The Sun went down, the smoke rose up, as from<br/>  A half-unquenched volcano, o'er a space<br/>Which well beseemed the "Devil's drawing-room,"<br/>  As some have qualified that wondrous place:<br/>But Juan felt, though not approaching _Home_,<br/>  As one who, though he were not of the race,<br/>Revered the soil, of those true sons the mother,<br/>Who butchered half the earth, and bullied t' other.<br/><br/>A mighty mass of brick, and smoke, and shipping,<br/>  Dirty and dusky, but as wide as eye<br/>Could reach, with here and there a sail just skipping<br/>  In sight, then lost amidst the forestry<br/>Of masts; a wilderness of steeples peeping<br/>  On tiptoe through their sea-coal canopy;<br/>A huge, dun Cupola, like a foolscap crown<br/>On a fool's head--and there is London Town!<br/><br/>But Juan saw not this: each wreath of smoke<br/>  Appeared to him but as the magic vapour<br/>Of some alchymic furnace, from whence broke<br/>  The wealth of worlds (a wealth of tax and paper):<br/>The gloomy clouds, which o'er it as a yoke<br/>  Are bowed, and put the Sun out like a taper,<br/>Were nothing but the natural atmosphere,<br/>Extremely wholesome, though but rarely clear.<br/><br/>He paused--and so will I; as doth a crew<br/>  Before they give their broadside. By and by,<br/>My gentle countrymen, we will renew<br/>  Our old acquaintance; and at least I'll try<br/>To tell you truths _you_ will not take as true,<br/>  Because they are so;--a male Mrs. Fry,<br/>With a soft besom will I sweep your halls,<br/>And brush a web or two from off the walls.<br/><br/>Oh Mrs. Fry! Why go to Newgate? Why<br/>  Preach to _poor_ rogues? And wherefore not begin<br/>With Carlton, or with other houses? Try<br/>  Your hand at hardened and imperial Sin.<br/>To mend the People's an absurdity,<br/>  A jargon, a mere philanthropic din,<br/>Unless you make their betters better:--Fie!<br/>I thought you had more religion, Mrs. Fry.<br/><br/>Teach _them_ the decencies of good threescore;<br/>  Cure _them_ of tours, hussar and highland dresses;<br/>Tell _them_ that youth once gone returns no more,<br/>  That hired huzzas redeem no land's distresses;<br/>Tell them Sir William Curtis is a bore,<br/>  Too dull even for the dullest of excesses--<br/>The witless Falstaff of a hoary Hal,<br/>A fool whose bells have ceased to ring at all.<br/><br/>Tell them, though it may be, perhaps, too late--<br/>  On Life's worn confine, jaded, bloated, sated--<br/>To set up vain pretence of being _great_,<br/>  'T is not so to be _good_; and, be it stated,<br/>The worthiest kings have ever loved least state:<br/>  And tell them--But you won't, and I have prated<br/>Just now enough; but, by and by, I'll prattle<br/>Like Roland's horn in Roncesvalles' battle.<br/><br/>CANTO THE ELEVENTH.<br/><br/>WHEN Bishop Berkeley said "there was no matter,"<br/>  And proved it--'t was no matter what he said:<br/>They say his system 't is in vain to batter,<br/>  Too subtle for the airiest human head;<br/>And yet who can believe it? I would shatter<br/>  Gladly all matters down to stone or lead,<br/>Or adamant, to find the World a spirit,<br/>And wear my head, denying that I wear it.<br/><br/>What a sublime discovery 't was to make the<br/>  Universe universal egotism,<br/>That all's ideal--_all ourselves!_--I'll stake the<br/>  World (be it what you will) that _that's_ no schism.<br/>Oh Doubt!--if thou be'st Doubt, for which some take thee,<br/>  But which I doubt extremely--thou sole prism<br/>Of the Truth's rays, spoil not my draught of spirit!<br/>Heaven's brandy, though our brain can hardly bear it.<br/><br/>For ever and anon comes Indigestion<br/>  (Not the most "dainty Ariel"), and perplexes<br/>Our soarings with another sort of question:<br/>  And that which after all my spirit vexes,<br/>Is, that I find no spot where Man can rest eye on,<br/>  Without confusion of the sorts and sexes,<br/>Of Beings, Stars, and this unriddled wonder,<br/>The World, which at the worst's a _glorious_ blunder--<br/><br/>If it be chance--or, if it be according<br/>  To the old text, still better:--lest it should<br/>Turn out so, we 'll say nothing 'gainst the wording,<br/>  As several people think such hazards rude.<br/>They're right; our days are too brief for affording<br/>  Space to dispute what _no one_ ever could<br/>Decide, and _everybody one day_ will<br/>Know very clearly--or at least lie still.<br/><br/>And therefore will I leave off metaphysical<br/>  Discussion, which is neither here nor there:<br/>If I agree that what is, is; then this I call<br/>  Being quite perspicuous and extremely fair;<br/>The truth is, I've grown lately rather phthisical:<br/>  I don't know what the reason is--the air<br/>Perhaps; but as I suffer from the shocks<br/>Of illness, I grow much more orthodox.<br/><br/>The first attack at once proved the Divinity<br/>  (But that I never doubted, nor the Devil);<br/>The next, the Virgin's mystical virginity;<br/>  The third, the usual Origin of Evil;<br/>The fourth at once established the whole Trinity<br/>  On so uncontrovertible a level,<br/>That I devoutly wished the three were four--<br/>On purpose to believe so much the more.<br/><br/>To our theme.--The man who has stood on the Acropolis,<br/>  And looked down over Attica; or he<br/>Who has sailed where picturesque Constantinople is,<br/>  Or seen Timbuctoo, or hath taken tea<br/>In small-eyed China's crockery-ware metropolis,<br/>  Or sat amidst the bricks of Nineveh,<br/>May not think much of London's first appearance--<br/>But ask him what he thinks of it a year hence!<br/><br/>Don Juan had got out on Shooter's Hill;<br/>  Sunset the time, the place the same declivity<br/>Which looks along that vale of Good and Ill<br/>  Where London streets ferment in full activity,<br/>While everything around was calm and still,<br/>  Except the creak of wheels, which on their pivot he<br/>Heard,--and that bee-like, bubbling, busy hum<br/>Of cities, that boil over with their scum:--<br/><br/>I say, Don Juan, wrapped in contemplation,<br/>  Walked on behind his carriage, o'er the summit,<br/>And lost in wonder of so great a nation,<br/>  Gave way to 't, since he could not overcome it.<br/>"And here," he cried, "is Freedom's chosen station;<br/>  Here peals the People's voice, nor can entomb it<br/>Racks--prisons--inquisitions; Resurrection<br/>Awaits it, each new meeting or election.<br/><br/>"Here are chaste wives, pure lives; here people pay<br/>  But what they please; and if that things be dear,<br/>'T is only that they love to throw away<br/>  Their cash, to show how much they have a-year.<br/>Here laws are all inviolate--none lay<br/>  Traps for the traveller--every highway's clear--<br/>Here"--he was interrupted by a knife,<br/>With--"Damn your eyes! your money or your life!"--<br/><br/>These free-born sounds proceeded from four pads<br/>  In ambush laid, who had perceived him loiter<br/>Behind his carriage; and, like handy lads,<br/>  Had seized the lucky hour to reconnoitre,<br/>In which the heedless gentleman who gads<br/>  Upon the road, unless he prove a fighter,<br/>May find himself within that isle of riches<br/>Exposed to lose his life as well as breeches.<br/><br/>Juan, who did not understand a word<br/>  Of English, save their shibboleth, "God damn!"<br/>And even that he had so rarely heard,<br/>  He sometimes thought 't was only their "SalƒÅm,"<br/>Or "God be with you!"--and 't is not absurd<br/>  To think so,--for half English as I am<br/>(To my misfortune), never can I say<br/>I heard them wish "God with you," save that way;--<br/><br/>Juan yet quickly understood their gesture,<br/>  And being somewhat choleric and sudden,<br/>Drew forth a pocket pistol from his vesture,<br/>  And fired it into one assailant's pudding--<br/>Who fell, as rolls an ox o'er in his pasture,<br/>  And roared out, as he writhed his native mud in,<br/>Unto his nearest follower or henchman,<br/>"Oh Jack! I'm floored by that 'ere bloody Frenchman!"<br/><br/>On which Jack and his train set off at speed,<br/>  And Juan's suite, late scattered at a distance,<br/>Came up, all marvelling at such a deed,<br/>  And offering, as usual, late assistance.<br/>Juan, who saw the moon's late minion bleed<br/>  As if his veins would pour out his existence,<br/>Stood calling out for bandages and lint,<br/>And wished he had been less hasty with his flint.<br/><br/>"Perhaps," thought he, "it is the country's wont<br/>  To welcome foreigners in this way: now<br/>I recollect some innkeepers who don't<br/>  Differ, except in robbing with a bow,<br/>In lieu of a bare blade and brazen front--<br/>  But what is to be done? I can't allow<br/>The fellow to lie groaning on the road:<br/>So take him up--I'll help you with the load."<br/><br/>But ere they could perform this pious duty,<br/>  The dying man cried, "Hold! I've got my gruel!<br/>Oh! for a glass of _max_! We've missed our booty;<br/>  Let me die where I am!" And as the fuel<br/>Of Life shrunk in his heart, and thick and sooty<br/>  The drops fell from his death-wound, and he drew ill<br/>His breath,--he from his swelling throat untied<br/>A kerchief, crying, "Give Sal that!"--and died.<br/><br/>The cravat stained with bloody drops fell down<br/>  Before Don Juan's feet: he could not tell<br/>Exactly why it was before him thrown,<br/>  Nor what the meaning of the man's farewell.<br/>Poor Tom was once a kiddy upon town,<br/>  A thorough varmint, and a _real_ swell,<br/>Full flash, all fancy, until fairly diddled,<br/>His pockets first and then his body riddled.<br/><br/>Don Juan, having done the best he could<br/>  In all the circumstances of the case,<br/>As soon as "Crowner's quest" allowed, pursued<br/>  His travels to the capital apace;--<br/>Esteeming it a little hard he should<br/>  In twelve hours' time, and very little space,<br/>Have been obliged to slay a free-born native<br/>In self-defence: this made him meditative.<br/><br/>He from the world had cut off a great man,<br/>  Who in his time had made heroic bustle.<br/>Who in a row like Tom could lead the van,<br/>  Booze in the ken, or at the spellken hustle?<br/>Who queer a flat? Who (spite of Bow-street's ban)<br/>  On the high toby-spice so flash the muzzle?<br/>Who on a lark with black-eyed Sal (his blowing),<br/>So prime--so swell--so nutty--and so knowing?<br/><br/>But Tom's no more--and so no more of Tom.<br/>  Heroes must die; and by God's blessing 't is<br/>Not long before the most of them go home.<br/>  Hail! Thamis, hail! Upon thy verge it is<br/>That Juan's chariot, rolling like a drum<br/>  In thunder, holds the way it can't well miss,<br/>Through Kennington and all the other "tons,"<br/>Which make us wish ourselves in town at once;--<br/><br/>Through Groves, so called as being void of trees,<br/>  (Like _lucus_ from _no_ light); through prospects named<br/>Mount Pleasant, as containing nought to please,<br/>  Nor much to climb; through little boxes framed<br/>Of bricks, to let the dust in at your ease,<br/>  With "To be let," upon their doors proclaimed;<br/>Through "Rows" most modestly called "Paradise,"<br/>Which Eve might quit without much sacrifice;--<br/><br/>Through coaches, drays, choked turnpikes, and a whirl<br/>  Of wheels, and roar of voices, and confusion;<br/>Here taverns wooing to a pint of "purl,"<br/>  There mails fast flying off like a delusion;<br/>There barbers' blocks with periwigs in curl<br/>  In windows; here the lamplighter's infusion<br/>Slowly distilled into the glimmering glass<br/>(For in those days we had not got to gas--);<br/><br/>Through this, and much, and more, is the approach<br/>  Of travellers to mighty Babylon:<br/>Whether they come by horse, or chaise, or coach,<br/>  With slight exceptions, all the ways seem one.<br/>I could say more, but do not choose to encroach<br/>  Upon the Guide-book's privilege. The Sun<br/>Had set some time, and night was on the ridge<br/>Of twilight, as the party crossed the bridge.<br/><br/>That's rather fine, the gentle sound of Thamis--<br/>  Who vindicates a moment, too, his stream--<br/>Though hardly heard through multifarious "damme's:"<br/>  The lamps of Westminster's more regular gleam,<br/>The breadth of pavement, and yon shrine where Fame is<br/>  A spectral resident--whose pallid beam<br/>In shape of moonshine hovers o'er the pile--<br/>Make this a sacred part of Albion's isle.<br/><br/>The Druids' groves are gone--so much the better:<br/>  Stonehenge is not--but what the devil is it?--But<br/>Bedlam still exists with its sage fetter,<br/>  That madmen may not bite you on a visit;<br/>The Bench too seats or suits full many a debtor;<br/>  The Mansion House, too (though some people quiz it),<br/>To me appears a stiff yet grand erection;<br/>But then the Abbey's worth the whole collection.<br/><br/>The line of lights, too, up to Charing Cross,<br/>  Pall Mall, and so forth, have a coruscation<br/>Like gold as in comparison to dross,<br/>  Matched with the Continent's illumination,<br/>Whose cities Night by no means deigns to gloss.<br/>  The French were not yet a lamp-lighting nation,<br/>And when they grew so--on their new-found lantern,<br/>Instead of wicks, they made a wicked man turn.<br/><br/>A row of Gentlemen along the streets<br/>  Suspended may illuminate mankind,<br/>As also bonfires made of country seats;<br/>  But the old way is best for the purblind:<br/>The other looks like phosphorus on sheets,<br/>  A sort of _ignis fatuus_ to the mind,<br/>Which, though 't is certain to perplex and frighten,<br/>Must burn more mildly ere it can enlighten.<br/><br/>But London's so well lit, that if Diogenes<br/>  Could recommence to hunt his _honest man_,<br/>And found him not amidst the various progenies<br/>  Of this enormous City's spreading span,<br/>'T were not for want of lamps to aid his dodging his<br/>  Yet undiscovered treasure. What _I_ can,<br/>I've done to find the same throughout Life's journey,<br/>But see the World is only one attorney.<br/><br/>Over the stones still rattling, up Pall Mall,<br/>  Through crowds and carriages, but waxing thinner<br/>As thundered knockers broke the long sealed spell<br/>  Of doors 'gainst duns, and to an early dinner<br/>Admitted a small party as night fell,--<br/>  Don Juan, our young diplomatic sinner,<br/>Pursued his path, and drove past some hotels,<br/>St. James's Palace, and St. James's "Hells."<br/><br/>They reached the hotel: forth streamed from the front door<br/>  A tide of well-clad waiters, and around<br/>The mob stood, and as usual several score<br/>  Of those pedestrian Paphians who abound<br/>In decent London when the daylight's o'er;<br/>  Commodious but immoral, they are found<br/>Useful, like Malthus, in promoting marriage.--<br/>But Juan now is stepping from his carriage<br/><br/>Into one of the sweetest of hotels,<br/>  Especially for foreigners--and mostly<br/>For those whom favour or whom Fortune swells,<br/>  And cannot find a bill's small items costly.<br/>There many an envoy either dwelt or dwells<br/>  (The den of many a diplomatic lost lie),<br/>Until to some conspicuous square they pass,<br/>And blazon o'er the door their names in brass.<br/><br/>Juan, whose was a delicate commission,<br/>  Private, though publicly important, bore<br/>No title to point out with due precision<br/>  The exact affair on which he was sent o'er.<br/>'T was merely known, that on a secret mission<br/>  A foreigner of rank had graced our shore,<br/>Young, handsome, and accomplished, who was said<br/>(In whispers) to have turned his Sovereign's head.<br/><br/>Some rumour also of some strange adventures<br/>  Had gone before him, and his wars and loves;<br/>And as romantic heads are pretty painters,<br/>  And, above all, an Englishwoman's roves<br/>Into the excursive, breaking the indentures<br/>  Of sober reason, wheresoe'er it moves,<br/>He found himself extremely in the fashion,<br/>Which serves our thinking people for a passion.<br/><br/>I don't mean that they are passionless, but quite<br/>  The contrary; but then 't is in the head;<br/>Yet as the consequences are as bright<br/>  As if they acted with the heart instead,<br/>What after all can signify the site<br/>  Of ladies' lucubrations? So they lead<br/>In safety to the place for which you start,<br/>What matters if the road be head or heart?<br/><br/>Juan presented in the proper place,<br/>  To proper placemen, every Russ credential;<br/>And was received with all the due grimace<br/>  By those who govern in the mood potential,<br/>Who, seeing a handsome stripling with smooth face,<br/>  Thought (what in state affairs is most essential),<br/>That they as easily might _do_ the youngster,<br/>As hawks may pounce upon a woodland songster.<br/><br/>They erred, as ag√©d men will do; but by<br/>  And by we'll talk of that; and if we don't,<br/>'T will be because our notion is not high<br/>  Of politicians and their double front,<br/>Who live by lies, yet dare not boldly lie:--<br/>  Now what I love in women is, they won't<br/>Or can't do otherwise than lie--but do it<br/>So well, the very Truth seems falsehood to it.<br/><br/>And, after all, what is a lie? 'T is but<br/>  The truth in masquerade; and I defy<br/>Historians--heroes--lawyers--priests, to put<br/>  A fact without some leaven of a lie.<br/>The very shadow of true Truth would shut<br/>  Up annals--revelations--poesy,<br/>And prophecy--except it should be dated<br/>Some years before the incidents related.<br/><br/>Praised be all liars and all lies! Who now<br/>  Can tax my mild Muse with misanthropy?<br/>She rings the World's "Te Deum," and her brow<br/>  Blushes for those who will not:--but to sigh<br/>Is idle; let us like most others bow,<br/>  Kiss hands--feet--any part of Majesty,<br/>After the good example of "Green Erin,"<br/>Whose shamrock now seems rather worse for wearing.<br/><br/>Don Juan was presented, and his dress<br/>  And mien excited general admiration--<br/>I don't know which was more admired or less:<br/>  One monstrous diamond drew much observation,<br/>Which Catherine in a moment of _"ivresse"_<br/>  (In Love or Brandy's fervent fermentation),<br/>Bestowed upon him, as the public learned;<br/>And, to say truth, it had been fairly earned.<br/><br/>Besides the ministers and underlings,<br/>  Who must be courteous to the accredited<br/>Diplomatists of rather wavering Kings,<br/>  Until their royal riddle's fully read,<br/>The very clerks,--those somewhat dirty springs<br/>  Of Office, or the House of Office, fed<br/>By foul corruption into streams,--even they<br/>Were hardly rude enough to earn their pay:<br/><br/>And insolence no doubt is what they are<br/>  Employed for, since it is their daily labour,<br/>In the dear offices of Peace or War;<br/>  And should you doubt, pray ask of your next neighbour,<br/>When for a passport, or some other bar<br/>  To freedom, he applied (a grief and a bore),<br/>If he found not this spawn of tax-born riches,<br/>Like lap-dogs, the least civil sons of b-----s.<br/><br/>But Juan was received with much _"empressement:"_--<br/>  These phrases of refinement I must borrow<br/>From our next neighbours' land, where, like a chessman,<br/>  There is a move set down for joy or sorrow,<br/>Not only in mere talking, but the press. Man<br/>  In Islands is, it seems, downright and thorough,<br/>More than on Continents--as if the Sea<br/>(See Billingsgate) made even the tongue more free.<br/><br/>And yet the British "Damme"'s rather Attic,<br/>  Your continental oaths are but incontinent,<br/>And turn on things which no aristocratic<br/>  Spirit would name, and therefore even I won't anent<br/>This subject quote; as it would be schismatic<br/>  In _politesse_, and have a sound affronting in 't;--<br/>But "Damme"'s quite ethereal, though too daring--<br/>Platonic blasphemy--the soul of swearing.<br/><br/>For downright rudeness, ye may stay at home;<br/>  For true or false politeness (and scarce _that<br/>Now_) you may cross the blue deep and white foam--<br/>  The first the emblem (rarely though) of what<br/>You leave behind, the next of much you come<br/>  To meet.
