However, 't is no time to chat<br/>On general topics: poems must confine<br/>Themselves to unity, like this of mine.<br/><br/>In the great world,--which, being interpreted,<br/>  Meaneth the West or worst end of a city,<br/>And about twice two thousand people bred<br/>  By no means to be very wise or witty,<br/>But to sit up while others lie in bed,<br/>  And look down on the Universe with pity,--<br/>Juan, as an inveterate patrician,<br/>Was well received by persons of condition.<br/><br/>He was a bachelor, which is a matter<br/>  Of import both to virgin and to bride,<br/>The former's hymeneal hopes to flatter;<br/>  And (should she not hold fast by Love or Pride)<br/>'T is also of some moment to the latter:<br/>  A rib's a thorn in a wed gallant's side,<br/>Requires decorum, and is apt to double<br/>The horrid sin--and what's still worse, the trouble.<br/><br/>But Juan was a bachelor--of arts,<br/>  And parts, and hearts: he danced and sung, and had<br/>An air as sentimental as Mozart's<br/>  Softest of melodies; and could be sad<br/>Or cheerful, without any "flaws or starts,"<br/>  Just at the proper time: and though a lad,<br/>Had seen the world--which is a curious sight,<br/>And very much unlike what people write.<br/><br/>Fair virgins blushed upon him; wedded dames<br/>  Bloomed also in less transitory hues;<br/>For both commodities dwell by the Thames,<br/>  The painting and the painted; Youth, Ceruse,<br/>Against his heart preferred their usual claims,<br/>  Such as no gentleman can quite refuse:<br/>Daughters admired his dress, and pious mothers<br/>Inquired his income, and if he had brothers.<br/><br/>The milliners who furnish "drapery Misses"<br/>  Throughout the season, upon speculation<br/>Of payment ere the Honeymoon's last kisses<br/>  Have waned into a crescent's coruscation,<br/>Thought such an opportunity as this is,<br/>  Of a rich foreigner's initiation,<br/>Not to be overlooked--and gave such credit,<br/>That future bridegrooms swore, and sighed, and paid it.<br/><br/>The Blues, that tender tribe, who sigh o'er sonnets,<br/>  And with the pages of the last Review<br/>Line the interior of their heads or bonnets,<br/>  Advanced in all their azure's highest hue:<br/>They talked bad French or Spanish, and upon its<br/>  Late authors asked him for a hint or two;<br/>And which was softest, Russian or Castilian?<br/>And whether in his travels he saw Ilion?<br/><br/>Juan, who was a little superficial,<br/>  And not in literature a great Drawcansir,<br/>Examined by this learn√©d and especial<br/>  Jury of matrons, scarce knew what to answer:<br/>His duties warlike, loving or official,<br/>  His steady application as a dancer,<br/>Had kept him from the brink of Hippocrene,<br/>Which now he found was blue instead of green.<br/><br/>However, he replied at hazard, with<br/>  A modest confidence and calm assurance,<br/>Which lent his learn√©d lucubrations pith,<br/>  And passed for arguments of good endurance.<br/>That prodigy, Miss Araminta Smith<br/>  (Who at sixteen translated "Hercules Furens"<br/>Into as furious English), with her best look,<br/>Set down his sayings in her common-place book.<br/><br/>Juan knew several languages--as well<br/>  He might--and brought them up with skill, in time<br/>To save his fame with each accomplished belle,<br/>  Who still regretted that he did not rhyme.<br/>There wanted but this requisite to swell<br/>  His qualities (with them) into sublime:<br/>Lady Fitz-Frisky, and Miss Maevia Mannish,<br/>Both longed extremely to be sung in Spanish.<br/><br/>However, he did pretty well, and was<br/>  Admitted as an aspirant to all<br/>The coteries, and, as in Banquo's glass,<br/>  At great assemblies or in parties small,<br/>He saw ten thousand living authors pass,<br/>  That being about their average numeral;<br/>Also the eighty "greatest living poets,"<br/>As every paltry magazine can show _it's_.<br/><br/>In twice five years the "greatest living poet,"<br/>  Like to the champion in the fisty ring,<br/>Is called on to support his claim, or show it,<br/>  Although 't is an imaginary thing.<br/>Even I--albeit I'm sure I did not know it,<br/>  Nor sought of foolscap subjects to be king,--<br/>Was reckoned, a considerable time,<br/>The grand Napoleon of the realms of rhyme.<br/><br/>But Juan was my Moscow, and Faliero<br/>  My Leipsic, and my Mont Saint Jean seems Cain:<br/>_La Belle Alliance_ of dunces down at zero,<br/>  Now that the Lion's fallen, may rise again:<br/>But I will fall at least as fell my Hero;<br/>  Nor reign at all, or as a _monarch_ reign;<br/>Or to some lonely isle of gaolers go,<br/>With turncoat Southey for my turnkey Lowe.<br/><br/>Sir Walter reigned before me; Moore and Campbell<br/>  Before and after; but now grown more holy,<br/>The Muses upon Sion's hill must ramble<br/>  With poets almost clergymen, or wholly;<br/>And Pegasus has a psalmodic amble<br/>  Beneath the very Reverend Rowley Powley,<br/>Who shoes the glorious animal with stilts,<br/>A modern Ancient Pistol--"by these hilts!"<br/><br/>Still he excels that artificial hard<br/>  Labourer in the same vineyard, though the vine<br/>Yields him but vinegar for his reward.--<br/>  That neutralised dull Dorus of the Nine;<br/>That swarthy Sporus, neither man nor bard;<br/>  That ox of verse, who _ploughs_ for every line:--<br/>Cambyses' roaring Romans beat at least<br/>The howling Hebrews of Cybele's priest.--<br/><br/>Then there's my gentle Euphues,--who, they say,<br/>  Sets up for being a sort of _moral me_;<br/>He'll find it rather difficult some day<br/>  To turn out both, or either, it may be.<br/>Some persons think that Coleridge hath the sway;<br/>  And Wordsworth has supporters, two or three;<br/>And that deep-mouthed Boeotian "Savage Landor"<br/>Has taken for a swan rogue Southey's gander.<br/><br/>John Keats, who was killed off by one critique,<br/>  Just as he really promised something great,<br/>If not intelligible, without Greek<br/>  Contrived to talk about the gods of late,<br/>Much as they might have been supposed to speak.<br/>  Poor fellow! His was an untoward fate;<br/>'T is strange the mind, that very fiery particle,<br/>Should let itself be snuffed out by an article.<br/><br/>The list grows long of live and dead pretenders<br/>  To that which none will gain--or none will know<br/>The conqueror at least; who, ere Time renders<br/>  His last award, will have the long grass grow<br/>Above his burnt-out brain, and sapless cinders.<br/>  If I might augur, I should rate but low<br/>Their chances;--they're too numerous, like the thirty<br/>Mock tyrants, when Rome's annals waxed but dirty.<br/><br/>This is the literary _lower_ empire,<br/>  Where the praetorian bands take up the matter;--<br/>A "dreadful trade," like his who "gathers samphire,"<br/>  The insolent soldiery to soothe and flatter,<br/>With the same feelings as you'd coax a vampire.<br/>  Now, were I once at home, and in good satire,<br/>I'd try conclusions with those Janizaries,<br/>And show them _what_ an intellectual war is.<br/><br/>I think I know a trick or two, would turn<br/>  Their flanks;--but it is hardly worth my while,<br/>With such small gear to give myself concern:<br/>  Indeed I've not the necessary bile;<br/>My natural temper's really aught but stern,<br/>  And even my Muse's worst reproof's a smile;<br/>And then she drops a brief and modern curtsy,<br/>And glides away, assured she never hurts ye.<br/><br/>My Juan, whom I left in deadly peril<br/>  Amongst live poets and _blue_ ladies, passed<br/>With some small profit through that field so sterile,<br/>  Being tired in time--and, neither least nor last,<br/>Left it before he had been treated very ill;<br/>  And henceforth found himself more gaily classed<br/>Amongst the higher spirits of the day,<br/>The Sun's true son, no vapour, but a ray.<br/><br/>His morns he passed in business--which dissected,<br/>  Was, like all business, a laborious nothing<br/>That leads to lassitude, the most infected<br/>  And Centaur Nessus garb of mortal clothing,<br/>And on our sofas makes us lie dejected,<br/>  And talk in tender horrors of our loathing<br/>All kinds of toil, save for our country's good--<br/>Which grows no better, though 't is time it should.<br/><br/>His afternoons he passed in visits, luncheons,<br/>  Lounging and boxing; and the twilight hour<br/>In riding round those vegetable puncheons<br/>  Called "Parks," where there is neither fruit nor flower<br/>Enough to gratify a bee's slight munchings;<br/>  But after all it is the only "bower"<br/>(In Moore's phrase) where the fashionable fair<br/>Can form a slight acquaintance with fresh air.<br/><br/>Then dress, then dinner, then awakes the world!<br/>  Then glare the lamps, then whirl the wheels, then roar<br/>Through street and square fast flashing chariots hurled<br/>  Like harnessed meteors; then along the floor<br/>Chalk mimics painting; then festoons are twirled;<br/>  Then roll the brazen thunders of the door,<br/>Which opens to the thousand happy few<br/>An earthly Paradise of _Or Molu_.<br/><br/>There stands the noble hostess, nor shall sink<br/>  With the three-thousandth curtsy; there the waltz,<br/>The only dance which teaches girls to think,<br/>  Makes one in love even with its very faults.<br/>Saloon, room, hall, o'erflow beyond their brink,<br/>  And long the latest of arrivals halts,<br/>'Midst royal dukes and dames condemned to climb,<br/>And gain an inch of staircase at a time.<br/><br/>Thrice happy he who, after a survey<br/>  Of the good company, can win a corner,<br/>A door that's _in_ or boudoir _out_ of the way,<br/>  Where he may fix himself like small "Jack Horner,"<br/>And let the Babel round run as it may,<br/>  And look on as a mourner, or a scorner,<br/>Or an approver, or a mere spectator,<br/>Yawning a little as the night grows later.<br/><br/>But this won't do, save by and by; and he<br/>  Who, like Don Juan, takes an active share,<br/>Must steer with care through all that glittering sea<br/>  Of gems and plumes and pearls and silks, to where<br/>He deems it is his proper place to be;<br/>  Dissolving in the waltz to some soft air,<br/>Or proudlier prancing with mercurial skill,<br/>Where Science marshals forth her own quadrille.<br/><br/>Or, if he dance not, but hath higher views<br/>  Upon an heiress or his neighbour's bride,<br/>Let him take care that that which he pursues<br/>  Is not at once too palpably descried:<br/>Full many an eager gentleman oft rues<br/>  His haste; Impatience is a blundering guide<br/>Amongst a people famous for reflection,<br/>Who like to play the fool with circumspection.<br/><br/>But, if you can contrive, get next at supper;<br/>  Or, if forestalled, get opposite and ogle:--<br/>Oh, ye ambrosial moments! always upper<br/>  In mind, a sort of sentimental bogle,<br/>Which sits for ever upon Memory's crupper,<br/>  The ghost of vanished pleasures once in vogue! Ill<br/>Can tender souls relate the rise and fall<br/>Of hopes and fears which shake a single ball.<br/><br/>But these precautionary hints can touch<br/>  Only the common run, who must pursue,<br/>And watch and ward; whose plans a word too much<br/>  Or little overturns; and not the few<br/>Or many (for the number's sometimes such)<br/>  Whom a good mien, especially if new,<br/>Or fame--or name--for Wit, War, Sense, or Nonsense,<br/>Permits whate'er they please,--or _did_ not long since.<br/><br/>Our Hero--as a hero--young and handsome,<br/>  Noble, rich, celebrated, and a stranger,<br/>Like other slaves of course must pay his ransom,<br/>  Before he can escape from so much danger<br/>As will environ a conspicuous man. Some<br/>  Talk about poetry, and "rack and manger,"<br/>And ugliness, disease, as toil and trouble;--<br/>I wish they knew the life of a young noble.<br/><br/>They are young, but know not Youth--it is anticipated;<br/> Handsome but wasted, rich without a sou;<br/>Their vigour in a thousand arms is dissipated;<br/>  Their cash comes _from_, their wealth goes _to_ a Jew;<br/>Both senates see their nightly votes participated<br/>  Between the Tyrant's and the Tribunes' crew;<br/>And having voted, dined, drunk, gamed, and whored,<br/>The family vault receives another Lord.<br/><br/>"Where is the World?" cries Young, at _eighty_--"Where<br/>  The World in which a man was born?" Alas!<br/>Where is the world of _eight_ years past? _'T was there_--<br/>  I look for it--'t is gone, a globe of glass!<br/>Cracked, shivered, vanished, scarcely gazed on, ere<br/>  A silent change dissolves the glittering mass.<br/>Statesmen, Chiefs, Orators, Queens, Patriots, Kings,<br/>And Dandies--all are gone on the Wind's wings.<br/><br/>Where is Napoleon the Grand? God knows!<br/>  Where little Castlereagh? The devil can tell!<br/>Where Grattan, Curran, Sheridan--all those<br/>  Who bound the Bar or Senate in their spell?<br/>Where is the unhappy Queen, with all her woes?<br/>  And where the Daughter, whom the Isles loved well?<br/>Where are those martyred saints the Five per Cents?<br/>And where--oh, where the devil are the Rents?<br/><br/>Where's Brummell? Dished. Where's Long Pole Wellesley? Diddled.<br/>  Where's Whitbread? Romilly? Where's George the Third?<br/>Where is his will? (That's not so soon unriddled.)<br/>  And where is "Fum" the Fourth, our "royal bird?"<br/>Gone down, it seems, to Scotland to be fiddled<br/>  Unto by Sawney's violin, we have heard:<br/>"Caw me, caw thee"--for six months hath been hatching<br/>This scene of royal itch and loyal scratching.<br/><br/>Where is Lord This? And where my Lady That?<br/>  The Honourable Mistresses and Misses?<br/>Some laid aside like an old Opera hat,<br/>  Married, unmarried, and remarried: (this is<br/>An evolution oft performed of late).<br/>  Where are the Dublin shouts--and London hisses?<br/>Where are the Grenvilles? Turned as usual. Where<br/>My friends the Whigs? Exactly where they were.<br/><br/>Where are the Lady Carolines and Franceses?<br/>  Divorced or doing thereanent. Ye annals<br/>So brilliant, where the list of routs and dances is,--<br/>  Thou Morning Post, sole record of the panels<br/>Broken in carriages, and all the phantasies<br/>  Of fashion,--say what streams now fill those channels?<br/>Some die, some fly, some languish on the Continent,<br/>Because the times have hardly left them _one_ tenant.<br/><br/>Some who once set their caps at cautious dukes,<br/>  Have taken up at length with younger brothers:<br/>Some heiresses have bit at sharpers' hooks:<br/>  Some maids have been made wives, some merely mothers:<br/>Others have lost their fresh and fairy looks:<br/>  In short, the list of alterations bothers.<br/>There's little strange in this, but something strange is<br/>The unusual quickness of these common changes.<br/><br/>Talk not of seventy years as age; in seven<br/>  I have seen more changes, down from monarchs to<br/>The humblest individuals under Heaven,<br/>  Than might suffice a moderate century through.<br/>I knew that nought was lasting, but now even<br/>  Change grows too changeable, without being new:<br/>Nought's permanent among the human race,<br/>Except the Whigs _not_ getting into place.<br/><br/>I have seen Napoleon, who seemed quite a Jupiter,<br/>  Shrink to a Saturn. I have seen a Duke<br/>(No matter which) turn politician stupider,<br/>  If that can well be, than his wooden look.<br/>But it is time that I should hoist my "blue Peter,"<br/>  And sail for a new theme:--I have seen--and shook<br/>To see it--the King hissed, and then caressed;<br/>But don't pretend to settle which was best.<br/><br/>I have seen the Landholders without a rap--<br/>  I have seen Joanna Southcote--I have seen<br/>The House of Commons turned to a tax-trap--<br/>  I have seen that sad affair of the late Queen--<br/>I have seen crowns worn instead of a fool's cap--<br/>  I have seen a Congress doing all that's mean--<br/>I have seen some nations, like o'erloaded asses,<br/>Kick off their burthens--meaning the high classes.<br/><br/>I have seen small poets, and great prosers, and<br/>  Interminable--_not eternal_--speakers--<br/>I have seen the funds at war with house and land--<br/>  I have seen the country gentlemen turn squeakers--<br/>I have seen the people ridden o'er like sand<br/>  By slaves on horseback--I have seen malt liquors<br/>Exchanged for "thin potations" by John Bull--<br/>I have seen John half detect himself a fool.--<br/><br/>But _"carpe diem,"_ Juan, _"carpe, carpe!"_<br/>  To-morrow sees another race as gay<br/>And transient, and devoured by the same harpy.<br/>  "Life's a poor player,"--then "play out the play,<br/>Ye villains!" and above all keep a sharp eye<br/>  Much less on what you do than what you say:<br/>Be hypocritical, be cautious, be<br/>Not what you _seem_, but always what you _see_.<br/><br/>But how shall I relate in other cantos<br/>  Of what befell our hero in the land,<br/>Which 't is the common cry and lie to vaunt as<br/>  A moral country? But I hold my hand--<br/>For I disdain to write an Atalantis;<br/>  But 't is as well at once to understand,<br/>You are _not_ a moral people, and you know it,<br/>Without the aid of too sincere a poet.<br/><br/>What Juan saw and underwent shall be<br/>  My topic, with of course the due restriction<br/>Which is required by proper courtesy;<br/>  And recollect the work is only fiction,<br/>And that I sing of neither mine nor me,<br/>  Though every scribe, in some slight turn of diction,<br/>Will hint allusions never _meant_. Ne'er doubt<br/>_This_--when I speak, I _don't hint_, but _speak out_.<br/><br/>Whether he married with the third or fourth<br/>  Offspring of some sage husband-hunting countess,<br/>Or whether with some virgin of more worth<br/>  (I mean in Fortune's matrimonial bounties),<br/>He took to regularly peopling Earth,<br/>  Of which your lawful, awful wedlock fount is,--<br/>Or whether he was taken in for damages,<br/>For being too excursive in his homages,--<br/><br/>Is yet within the unread events of Time.<br/>  Thus far, go forth, thou Lay, which I will back<br/>Against the same given quantity of rhyme,<br/>  For being as much the subject of attack<br/>As ever yet was any work sublime,<br/>  By those who love to say that white is black.<br/>So much the better!--I may stand alone,<br/>But would not change my free thoughts for a throne.<br/><br/>CANTO THE TWELFTH.<br/><br/>Of all the barbarous middle ages, that<br/>  Which is most barbarous is the middle age<br/>Of man! it is--I really scarce know what;<br/>  But when we hover between fool and sage,<br/>And don't know justly what we would be at--<br/>  A period something like a printed page,<br/>Black letter upon foolscap, while our hair<br/>Grows grizzled, and we are not what we were;--<br/><br/>Too old for Youth,--too young, at thirty-five,<br/>  To herd with boys, or hoard with good threescore,--<br/>I wonder people should be left alive;<br/>  But since they are, that epoch is a bore:<br/>Love lingers still, although 't were late to wive:<br/>  And as for other love, the illusion's o'er;<br/>And Money, that most pure imagination,<br/>Gleams only through the dawn of its creation.<br/><br/>O Gold! Why call we misers miserable?<br/>  Theirs is the pleasure that can never pall;<br/>Theirs is the best bower anchor, the chain cable<br/>  Which holds fast other pleasures great and small.<br/>Ye who but see the saving man at table,<br/>  And scorn his temperate board, as none at all,<br/>And wonder how the wealthy can be sparing,<br/>Know not what visions spring from each cheese-paring.<br/><br/>Love or lust makes Man sick, and wine much sicker;<br/>  Ambition rends, and gaming gains a loss;<br/>But making money, slowly first, then quicker,<br/>  And adding still a little through each cross<br/>(Which _will_ come over things), beats Love or liquor,<br/>  The gamester's counter, or the statesman's _dross_.<br/>O Gold! I still prefer thee unto paper,<br/>Which makes bank credit like a bank of _vapour_.<br/><br/>Who hold the balance of the World? Who reign<br/>  O'er congress, whether royalist or liberal?<br/>Who rouse the shirtless patriots of Spain?<br/>  (That make old Europe's journals "squeak and gibber" all)<br/>Who keep the World, both old and new, in pain<br/>  Or pleasure? Who make politics run glibber all?<br/>The shade of Buonaparte's noble daring?--<br/>Jew Rothschild, and his fellow-Christian, Baring.<br/><br/>Those, and the truly liberal Lafitte,<br/>  Are the true Lords of Europe. Every loan<br/>Is not a merely speculative hit,<br/>  But seats a Nation or upsets a Throne.<br/>Republics also get involved a bit;<br/>  Columbia's stock hath holders not unknown<br/>On 'Change; and even thy silver soil, Peru,<br/>Must get itself discounted by a Jew.<br/><br/>Why call the miser miserable? as<br/>  I said before: the frugal life is his,<br/>Which in a saint or cynic ever was<br/>  The theme of praise: a hermit would not miss<br/>Canonization for the self-same cause,<br/>  And wherefore blame gaunt Wealth's austerities?<br/>Because, you 'll say, nought calls for such a trial;--<br/>Then there's more merit in his self-denial.<br/><br/>He is your only poet;--Passion, pure<br/>  And sparkling on from heap to heap, displays,<br/>_Possessed_, the ore, of which _mere hopes_ allure<br/>  Nations athwart the deep: the golden rays<br/>Flash up in ingots from the mine obscure:<br/>  On him the Diamond pours its brilliant blaze,<br/>While the mild Emerald's beam shades down the dies<br/>Of other stones, to soothe the miser's eyes.<br/><br/>The lands on either side are his; the ship<br/>  From Ceylon, Inde, or far Cathay, unloads<br/>For him the fragrant produce of each trip;<br/>  Beneath his cars of Ceres groan the roads,<br/>And the vine blushes like Aurora's lip;<br/>  His very cellars might be Kings' abodes;<br/>While he, despising every sensual call,<br/>Commands--the intellectual Lord of _all_.<br/><br/>Perhaps he hath great projects in his mind,<br/>  To build a college, or to found a race,<br/>A hospital, a church,--and leave behind<br/>  Some dome surmounted by his meagre face:<br/>Perhaps he fain would liberate Mankind<br/>  Even with the very ore which makes them base;<br/>Perhaps he would be wealthiest of his nation,<br/>Or revel in the joys of calculation.<br/><br/>But whether all, or each, or none of these<br/>  May be the hoarder's principle of action,<br/>The fool will call such mania a disease:--<br/>  What is his _own?_ Go--look at each transaction,<br/>Wars, revels, loves--do these bring men more ease<br/>  Than the mere plodding through each "vulgar fraction?"<br/>Or do they benefit Mankind? Lean Miser!<br/>Let spendthrifts' heirs inquire of yours--who's wiser?<br/><br/>How beauteous are rouleaus! how charming chests<br/>  Containing ingots, bags of dollars, coins<br/>(Not of old victors, all whose heads and crests<br/>  Weigh not the thin ore where their visage shines,<br/>But) of fine unclipped gold, where dully rests<br/>  Some likeness, which the glittering cirque confines,<br/>Of modern, reigning, sterling, stupid stamp!--<br/>Yes! ready money _is_ Aladdin's lamp.<br/><br/>"Love rules the Camp, the Court, the Grove,--for Love<br/>  Is Heaven, and Heaven is Love:"--so sings the bard;<br/>Which it were rather difficult to prove<br/>  (A thing with poetry in general hard).<br/>Perhaps there may be something in "the Grove,"<br/>  At least it rhymes to "Love:" but I'm prepared<br/>To doubt (no less than landlords of their rental)<br/>If "Courts" and "Camps" be quite so sentimental.<br/><br/>But if Love don't, _Cash_ does, and Cash alone:<br/>  Cash rules the Grove, and fells it too besides;<br/>Without cash, camps were thin, and courts were none;<br/>  Without cash, Malthus tells you--"take no brides."<br/>So Cash rules Love the ruler, on his own<br/>  High ground, as virgin Cynthia sways the tides:<br/>And as for "Heaven being Love," why not say honey<br/>Is wax? Heaven is not Love, 't is Matrimony.<br/><br/>Is not all Love prohibited whatever,<br/>  Excepting Marriage? which is Love, no doubt,<br/>After a sort; but somehow people never<br/>  With the same thought the two words have helped out.<br/>Love may exist _with_ Marriage, and _should_ ever,<br/>  And Marriage also may exist without;<br/>But Love _sans_ banns is both a sin and shame,<br/>And ought to go by quite another name.<br/><br/>Now if the "Court," and "Camp," and "Grove," be not<br/>  Recruited all with constant married men,<br/>Who never coveted their neighbour's lot,<br/>  I say _that_ line's a lapsus of the pen;--<br/>Strange too in my _buon camerado_ Scott,<br/>  So celebrated for his morals, when<br/>My Jeffrey held him up as an example<br/>To me;--of whom these morals are a sample.<br/><br/>Well, if I don't succeed, I _have_ succeeded,<br/>  And that's enough; succeeded in my youth,<br/>The only time when much success is needed:<br/>  And my success produced what I, in sooth,<br/>Cared most about; it need not now be pleaded--<br/>  Whate'er it was, 'twas mine; I've paid, in truth,<br/>Of late, the penalty of such success,<br/>But have not learned to wish it any less.<br/><br/>That suit in Chancery,--which some persons plead<br/>  In an appeal to the unborn, whom they,<br/>In the faith of their procreative creed,<br/>  Baptize Posterity, or future clay,--<br/>To me seems but a dubious kind of reed<br/>  To lean on for support in any way;<br/>Since odds are that Posterity will know<br/>No more of them, than they of her, I trow.<br/><br/>Why, I'm Posterity--and so are you;<br/>  And whom do we remember? Not a hundred.<br/>Were every memory written down all true,<br/>  The tenth or twentieth name would be but blundered;<br/>Even Plutarch's Lives have but picked out a few,<br/>  And 'gainst those few your annalists have thundered;<br/>And Mitford in the nineteenth century<br/>Gives, with Greek truth, the good old Greek the lie.<br/><br/>Good people all, of every degree,<br/>  Ye gentle readers and ungentle writers,<br/>In this twelfth Canto 't is my wish to be<br/>  As serious as if I had for inditers<br/>Malthus and Wilberforce:--the last set free<br/>  The Negroes, and is worth a million fighters;<br/>While Wellington has but enslaved the Whites,<br/>And Malthus does the thing 'gainst which he writes.<br/><br/>I'm serious--so are all men upon paper;<br/>  And why should I not form my speculation,<br/>And hold up to the Sun my little taper?<br/>  Mankind just now seem wrapped in meditation<br/>On constitutions and steam-boats of vapour;<br/>  While sages write against all procreation,<br/>Unless a man can calculate his means<br/>Of feeding brats the moment his wife weans.<br/><br/>That's noble! That's romantic! For my part,<br/>  I think that "Philo-genitiveness" is--<br/>(Now here's a word quite after my own heart,<br/>  Though there's a shorter a good deal than this,<br/>If that politeness set it not apart;<br/>  But I'm resolved to say nought that's amiss)--<br/>I say, methinks that "Philo-genitiveness"<br/>Might meet from men a little more forgiveness.<br/><br/>And now to business.--O my gentle Juan!<br/>  Thou art in London--in that pleasant place,<br/>Where every kind of mischief's daily brewing,<br/>  Which can await warm Youth in its wild race.<br/>'T is true, that thy career is not a new one;<br/>  Thou art no novice in the headlong chase<br/>Of early life; but this is a new land,<br/>Which foreigners can never understand.<br/><br/>What with a small diversity of climate,<br/>  Of hot or cold, mercurial or sedate,<br/>I could send forth my mandate like a Primate<br/>  Upon the rest of Europe's social state;<br/>But thou art the most difficult to rhyme at,<br/>  Great Britain, which the Muse may penetrate.<br/>All countries have their "Lions," but in thee<br/>There is but one superb menagerie.<br/><br/>But I am sick of politics. Begin--<br/>  _"Paulo Majora."_ Juan, undecided<br/>Amongst the paths of being "taken in,"<br/>  Above the ice had like a skater glided:<br/>When tired of play, he flirted without sin<br/>  With some of those fair creatures who have prided<br/>Themselves on innocent tantalisation,<br/>And hate all vice except its reputation.<br/><br/>But these are few, and in the end they make<br/>  Some devilish escapade or stir, which shows<br/>That even the purest people may mistake<br/>  Their way through Virtue's primrose paths of snows;<br/>And then men stare, as if a new ass spake<br/>  To Balaam, and from tongue to ear o'erflows<br/>Quicksilver small talk, ending (if you note it)<br/>With the kind World's Amen--"Who would have thought it?"<br/><br/>The little Leila, with her Orient eyes,<br/>  And taciturn Asiatic disposition,<br/>(Which saw all Western things with small surprise,<br/>  To the surprise of people of condition,<br/>Who think that novelties are butterflies<br/>  To be pursued as food for inanition,)<br/>Her charming figure and romantic history<br/>Became a kind of fashionable mystery.<br/><br/>The women much divided--as is usual<br/>  Amongst the sex in little things or great--<br/>Think not, fair creatures, that I mean to abuse you all,<br/>  I have always liked you better than I state--<br/>Since I've grown moral, still I must accuse you all<br/>  Of being apt to talk at a great rate;<br/>And now there was a general sensation<br/>Amongst you, about Leila's education.<br/><br/>In one point only were you settled--and<br/>  You had reason; 't was that a young child of grace,<br/>As beautiful as her own native land,<br/>  And far away, the last bud of her race,<br/>Howe'er our friend Don Juan might command<br/>  Himself for five, four, three, or two years' space,<br/>Would be much better taught beneath the eye<br/>Of peeresses whose follies had run dry.<br/><br/>So first there was a generous emulation,<br/>  And then there was a general competition,<br/>To undertake the orphan's education:<br/>  As Juan was a person of condition,<br/>It had been an affront on this occasion<br/>  To talk of a subscription or petition;<br/>But sixteen dowagers, ten unwed she sages<br/>Whose tale belongs to "Hallam's Middle Ages,"<br/><br/>And one or two sad, separate wives, without<br/>  A fruit to bloom upon their withering bough--<br/>Begged to bring _up_ the little girl, and _"out"_--<br/>  For that's the phrase that settles all things now,<br/>Meaning a virgin's first blush at a rout,<br/>  And all her points as thorough-bred to show:<br/>And I assure you, that like virgin honey<br/>Tastes their first season (mostly if they have money).<br/><br/>How all the needy honourable misters,<br/>  Each out-at-elbow peer, or desperate dandy,<br/>The watchful mothers, and the careful sisters,<br/>  (Who, by the by, when clever, are more handy<br/>At making matches, where "'t is gold that glisters,"<br/>  Than their _he_ relatives), like flies o'er candy<br/>Buzz round "the Fortune" with their busy battery,<br/>To turn her head with waltzing and with flattery!<br/><br/>Each aunt, each cousin, hath her speculation;<br/>  Nay, married dames will now and then discover<br/>Such pure disinterestedness of passion,<br/>  I've known them court an heiress for their lover.<br/>"_Tantoene!_" Such the virtues of high station,<br/>  Even in the hopeful Isle, whose outlet's "Dover!"<br/>While the poor rich wretch, object of these cares,<br/>Has cause to wish her sire had had male heirs.<br/><br/>Some are soon bagged, and some reject three dozen:<br/>  'T is fine to see them scattering refusals<br/>And wild dismay o'er every angry cousin<br/>  (Friends of the party), who begin accusals,<br/>Such as--"Unless Miss Blank meant to have chosen<br/>  Poor Frederick, why did she accord perusals<br/>To his billets? _Why_ waltz with him? Why, I pray,<br/>Look _'Yes'_ last night, and yet say _'No'_ to-day?<br/><br/>"Why?--Why?--Besides, Fred really was _attached_;<br/>  'T was not her fortune--he has enough without;<br/>The time will come she'll wish that she had snatched<br/>  So good an opportunity, no doubt:--<br/>But the old Marchioness some plan had hatched,<br/>  As I'll tell Aurea at to-morrow's rout:<br/>And after all poor Frederick may do better--<br/>Pray did you see her answer to his letter?"<br/><br/>Smart uniforms and sparkling coronets<br/>  Are spurned in turn, until her turn arrives,<br/>After male loss of time, and hearts, and bets<br/>  Upon the sweepstakes for substantial wives;<br/>And when at last the pretty creature gets<br/>  Some gentleman, who fights, or writes, or drives,<br/>It soothes the awkward squad of the rejected<br/>To find how very badly she selected.<br/><br/>For sometimes they accept some long pursuer,<br/>  Worn out with importunity; or fall<br/>(But here perhaps the instances are fewer)<br/>  To the lot of him who scarce pursued at all.<br/>A hazy widower turned of forty 's sure<br/>  (If 't is not vain examples to recall)<br/>To draw a high prize: now, howe'er he got her, I<br/>See nought more strange in this than t' other lottery.<br/><br/>I, for my part--(one "modern instance" more,<br/>  "True,'t is a pity--pity 't is, 't is true")--<br/>Was chosen from out an amatory score,<br/>  Albeit my years were less discreet than few;<br/>But though I also had reformed before<br/>  Those became one who soon were to be two,<br/>I'll not gainsay the generous public's voice,<br/>That the young lady made a monstrous choice.<br/><br/>Oh, pardon my digression--or at least<br/>  Peruse! 'T is always with a moral end<br/>That I dissert, like grace before a feast:<br/>  For like an ag√©d aunt, or tiresome friend,<br/>A rigid guardian, or a zealous priest,<br/>  My Muse by exhortation means to mend<br/>All people, at all times, and in most places,<br/>Which puts my Pegasus to these grave paces.<br/><br/>But now I'm going to be immoral; now<br/>  I mean to show things really as they are,<br/>Not as they ought to be: for I avow,<br/>  That till we see what's what in fact, we're far<br/>From much improvement with that virtuous plough<br/>  Which skims the surface, leaving scarce a scar<br/>Upon the black loam long manured by Vice,<br/>Only to keep its corn at the old price.<br/><br/>But first of little Leila we'll dispose,<br/>  For like a day-dawn she was young and pure--<br/>Or like the old comparison of snows,<br/>  (Which are more pure than pleasant, to be sure,<br/>Like many people everybody knows),--<br/>  Don Juan was delighted to secure<br/>A goodly guardian for his infant charge,<br/>Who might not profit much by being at large.<br/><br/>Besides, he had found out he was no tutor<br/>  (I wish that others would find out the same),<br/>And rather wished in such things to stand neuter,<br/>  For silly wards will bring their guardians blame:<br/>So when he saw each ancient dame a suitor<br/>  To make his little wild Asiatic tame,<br/>Consulting "the Society for Vice<br/>Suppression," Lady Pinchbeck was his choice.<br/><br/>Olden she was--but had been very young;<br/>  Virtuous she was--and had been, I believe;<br/>Although the World has such an evil tongue<br/>  That--but my chaster ear will not receive<br/>An echo of a syllable that's wrong:<br/>  In fact, there's nothing makes me so much grieve,<br/>As that abominable tittle-tattle,<br/>Which is the cud eschewed by human cattle.<br/><br/>Moreover I've remarked (and I was once<br/>  A slight observer in a modest way),<br/>And so may every one except a dunce,<br/>  That ladies in their youth a little gay,<br/>Besides their knowledge of the World, and sense<br/>  Of the sad consequence of going astray,<br/>Are wiser in their warnings 'gainst the woe<br/>Which the mere passionless can never know.<br/><br/>While the harsh prude indemnifies her virtue<br/>  By railing at the unknown and envied passion,<br/>Seeking far less to save you than to hurt you,<br/>  Or, what's still worse, to put you out of fashion,--<br/>The kinder veteran with calm words will court you,<br/>  Entreating you to pause before you dash on;<br/>Expounding and illustrating the riddle<br/>Of epic Love's beginning--end--and middle.<br/><br/>Now whether it be thus, or that they are stricter,<br/>  As better knowing why they should be so,<br/>I think you'll find from many a family picture,<br/>  That daughters of such mothers as may know<br/>The World by experience rather than by lecture,<br/>  Turn out much better for the Smithfield Show<br/>Of vestals brought into the marriage mart,<br/>Than those bred up by prudes without a heart.<br/><br/>I said that Lady Pinchbeck had been talked about--<br/>  As who has not, if female, young, and pretty?<br/>But now no more the ghost of Scandal stalked about;<br/>  She merely was deemed amiable and witty,<br/>And several of her best _bons-mots_ were hawked about:<br/>  Then she was given to charity and pity,<br/>And passed (at least the latter years of life)<br/>For being a most exemplary wife.<br/><br/>High in high circles, gentle in her own,<br/>  She was the mild reprover of the young,<br/>Whenever--which means every day--they'd shown<br/>  An awkward inclination to go wrong.<br/>The quantity of good she did 's unknown,<br/>  Or at the least would lengthen out my song:<br/>In brief, the little orphan of the East<br/>Had raised an interest in her,--which increased.<br/><br/>Juan, too, was a sort of favourite with her,<br/>  Because she thought him a good heart at bottom,<br/>A little spoiled, but not so altogether;<br/>  Which was a wonder, if you think who got him,<br/>And how he had been tossed, he scarce knew whither:<br/>  Though this might ruin others, it did _not_ him,<br/>At least entirely--for he had seen too many<br/>Changes in Youth, to be surprised at any.<br/><br/>And these vicissitudes tell best in youth;<br/>  For when they happen at a riper age,<br/>People are apt to blame the Fates, forsooth,<br/>  And wonder Providence is not more sage.<br/>Adversity is the first path to Truth:<br/>  He who hath proved War--Storm--or Woman's rage,<br/>Whether his winters be eighteen or eighty,<br/>Hath won the experience which is deemed so weighty.<br/><br/>How far it profits is another matter.--<br/>  Our hero gladly saw his little charge<br/>Safe with a lady, whose last grown-up daughter<br/>  Being long married, and thus set at large,<br/>Had left all the accomplishments she taught her<br/>  To be transmitted, like the Lord Mayor's barge,<br/>To the next comer; or--as it will tell<br/>More Muse-like--like to Cytherea's shell.<br/><br/>I call such things transmission; for there is<br/>  A floating balance of accomplishment,<br/>Which forms a pedigree from Miss to Miss,<br/>  According as their minds or backs are bent.<br/>Some waltz--some draw--some fathom the abyss<br/>  Of Metaphysics; others are content<br/>With Music; the most moderate shine as wits;--<br/>While others have a genius turned for fits.<br/><br/>But whether fits, or wits, or harpsichords--<br/>  Theology--fine arts--or finer stays,<br/>May be the baits for Gentlemen or Lords<br/>  With regular descent, in these our days,<br/>The last year to the new transfers its hoards;<br/>  New vestals claim men's eyes with the same praise<br/>Of "elegant" _et c√¶tera_, in fresh batches--<br/>All matchless creatures--and yet bent on matches.<br/><br/>But now I will begin my poem. 'Tis<br/>  Perhaps a little strange, if not quite new,<br/>That from the first of Cantos up to this<br/>  I've not begun what we have to go through.<br/>These first twelve books are merely flourishes,<br/>  _Preludios_, trying just a string or two<br/>Upon my lyre, or making the pegs sure;<br/>And when so, you shall have the overture.<br/><br/>My Muses do not care a pinch of rosin<br/>  About what's called success, or not succeeding:<br/>Such thoughts are quite below the strain they have chosen;<br/>  'T is a "great moral lesson" they are reading.<br/>I thought, at setting off, about two dozen<br/>  Cantos would do; but at Apollo's pleading,<br/>If that my Pegasus should not be foundered,<br/>I think to canter gently through a hundred.<br/><br/>Don Juan saw that Microcosm on stilts,<br/>  Yclept the Great World; for it is the least,<br/>Although the highest: but as swords have hilts<br/>  By which their power of mischief is increased,<br/>When Man in battle or in quarrel tilts,<br/>  Thus the low world, north, south, or west, or east,<br/>Must still obey the high--which is their handle,<br/>Their Moon, their Sun, their gas, their farthing candle.<br/><br/>He had many friends who had many wives, and was<br/>  Well looked upon by both, to that extent<br/>Of friendship which you may accept or pass,<br/>  It does nor good nor harm; being merely meant<br/>To keep the wheels going of the higher class,<br/>  And draw them nightly when a ticket's sent;<br/>And what with masquerades, and f√™tes, and balls,<br/>For the first season such a life scarce palls.<br/><br/>A young unmarried man, with a good name<br/>  And fortune, has an awkward part to play;<br/>For good society is but a game,<br/>  "The royal game of Goose," as I may say,<br/>Where everybody has some separate aim,<br/>  An end to answer, or a plan to lay--<br/>The single ladies wishing to be double,<br/>The married ones to save the virgins trouble.<br/><br/>I don't mean this as general, but particular<br/>  Examples may be found of such pursuits:<br/>Though several also keep their perpendicular<br/>  Like poplars, with good principles for roots;<br/>Yet many have a method more _reticular_--<br/>  "Fishers for men," like Sirens with soft lutes:<br/>For talk six times with the same single lady,<br/>And you may get the wedding-dresses ready.<br/><br/>Perhaps you'll have a letter from the mother,<br/>  To say her daughter's feelings are trepanned;<br/>Perhaps you'll have a visit from the brother,<br/>  All strut, and stays, and whiskers, to demand<br/>What "your intentions are?"--One way or other<br/>  It seems the virgin's heart expects your hand:<br/>And between pity for her case and yours,<br/>You'll add to Matrimony's list of cures.<br/><br/>I've known a dozen weddings made even _thus_,<br/>  And some of them high names: I have also known<br/>Young men who--though they hated to discuss<br/>  Pretensions which they never dreamed to have shown--<br/>Yet neither frightened by a female fuss,<br/>  Nor by mustachios moved, were let alone,<br/>And lived, as did the broken-hearted fair,<br/>In happier plight than if they formed a pair.<br/><br/>There's also nightly, to the uninitiated,<br/>  A peril--not indeed like Love or Marriage,<br/>But not the less for this to be depreciated:<br/>  It is--I meant and mean not to disparage<br/>The show of Virtue even in the vitiated--<br/>  It adds an outward grace unto their carriage--<br/>But to denounce the amphibious sort of harlot,<br/>_Couleur de rose_, who's neither white nor scarlet.<br/><br/>Such is your cold coquette, who can't say "No,"<br/>  And won't say "Yes," and keeps you on and off-ing<br/>On a lee-shore, till it begins to blow--<br/>  Then sees your heart wrecked, with an inward scoffing.<br/>This works a world of sentimental woe,<br/>  And sends new Werters yearly to their coffin;<br/>But yet is merely innocent flirtation,<br/>Not quite adultery, but adulteration.<br/><br/>"Ye gods, I grow a talker!" Let us prate.<br/>  The next of perils, though I place it _stern_est,<br/>Is when, without regard to Church or State,<br/>  A wife makes or takes love in upright earnest.<br/>Abroad, such things decide few women's fate--<br/>  (Such, early Traveller! is the truth thou learnest)--<br/>But in old England, when a young bride errs,<br/>Poor thing! Eve's was a trifling case to hers.<br/><br/>For 't is a low, newspaper, humdrum, lawsuit<br/>  Country, where a young couple of the same ages<br/>Can't form a friendship, but the world o'erawes it.<br/>  Then there's the vulgar trick of those d----d damages!<br/>A verdict--grievous foe to those who cause it!--<br/>  Forms a sad climax to romantic homages;<br/>Besides those soothing speeches of the pleaders,<br/>And evidences which regale all readers.<br/><br/>But they who blunder thus are raw beginners;<br/>  A little genial sprinkling of hypocrisy<br/>Has saved the fame of thousand splendid sinners,<br/>  The loveliest oligarchs of our Gynocracy;<br/>You may see such at all the balls and dinners,<br/>  Among the proudest of our aristocracy,<br/>So gentle, charming, charitable, chaste--<br/>And all by having _tact_ as well as taste.<br/><br/>Juan, who did not stand in the predicament<br/>  Of a mere novice, had one safeguard more;<br/>For he was sick--no, 't was not the word _sick_ I meant--<br/>  But he had seen so much good love before,<br/>That he was not in heart so very weak;--I meant<br/>  But thus much, and no sneer against the shore<br/>Of white cliffs, white necks, blue eyes, bluer stockings--<br/>Tithes, taxes, duns--and doors with double knockings.<br/><br/>But coming young from lands and scenes romantic,<br/>  Where lives, not lawsuits, must be risked for Passion<br/>And Passion's self must have a spice of frantic,<br/>  Into a country where 't is half a fashion,<br/>Seemed to him half commercial, half pedantic,<br/> Howe'er he might esteem this moral nation:<br/>Besides (alas! his taste--forgive and pity!)<br/>At _first_ he did not think the women pretty.<br/><br/>I say at _first_--for he found out at _last_,<br/>  But by degrees, that they were fairer far<br/>Than the more glowing dames whose lot is cast<br/>  Beneath the influence of the Eastern Star.<br/>A further proof we should not judge in haste;<br/>  Yet inexperience could not be his bar<br/>To taste:--the truth is, if men would confess,<br/>That novelties _please_ less than they _impress_.<br/><br/>Though travelled, I have never had the luck to<br/>  Trace up those shuffling negroes, Nile or Niger,<br/>To that impracticable place Timbuctoo,<br/>  Where Geography finds no one to oblige her<br/>With such a chart as may be safely stuck to--<br/>  For Europe ploughs in Afric like "_bos piger_:"<br/>But if I _had been_ at Timbuctoo, there<br/>No doubt I should be told that black is fair.<br/><br/>It is. 1 will not swear that black is white,<br/>  But I suspect in fact that white is black,<br/>And the whole matter rests upon eye-sight:--<br/>  Ask a blind man, the best judge. You'll attack<br/>Perhaps this new position--but I'm right;<br/>  Or if I'm wrong, I'll not be ta'en aback:--<br/>He hath no morn nor night, but all is dark<br/>Within--and what seest thou? A dubious spark!<br/><br/>But I'm relapsing into Metaphysics,<br/>  That labyrinth, whose clue is of the same<br/>Construction as your cures for hectic phthisics,<br/>  Those bright moths fluttering round a dying flame:<br/>And this reflection brings me to plain Physics,<br/>  And to the beauties of a foreign dame,<br/>Compared with those of our pure pearls of price,<br/>Those polar summers, _all_ Sun, and some ice.<br/><br/>Or say they are like virtuous mermaids, whose<br/>  Beginnings are fair faces, ends mere fishes;--<br/>Not that there's not a quantity of those<br/>  Who have a due respect for their own wishes.<br/>Like Russians rushing from hot baths to snows<br/>  Are they, at bottom virtuous even when vicious:<br/>They warm into a scrape, but keep of course,<br/>As a reserve, a plunge into remorse.<br/><br/>But this has nought to do with their outsides.<br/>  I said that Juan did not think them pretty<br/>At the first blush; for a fair Briton hides<br/>  Half her attractions--probably from pity--And<br/>rather calmly into the heart glides,<br/>  Than storms it as a foe would take a city;<br/>But once _there_ (if you doubt this, prithee try)<br/>She keeps it for you like a true ally.<br/><br/>She cannot step as does an Arab barb,<br/>  Or Andalusian girl from mass returning,<br/>Nor wear as gracefully as Gauls her garb,<br/>  Nor in her eye Ausonia's glance is burning;<br/>Her voice, though sweet, is not so fit to warb-<br/>  le those _bravuras_ (which I still am learning<br/>To like, though I have been seven years in Italy,<br/>And have, or had, an ear that served me prettily);--<br/><br/>She cannot do these things, nor one or two<br/>  Others, in that off-hand and dashing style<br/>Which takes so much--to give the Devil his due;<br/>  Nor is she quite so ready with her smile,<br/>Nor settles all things in one interview,<br/>  (A thing approved as saving time and toil);--<br/>But though the soil may give you time and trouble,<br/>Well cultivated, it will render double.<br/><br/>And if in fact she takes to a _grande passion_,<br/>  It is a very serious thing indeed:<br/>Nine times in ten 't is but caprice or fashion,<br/>  Coquetry, or a wish to take the lead,<br/>The pride of a mere child with a new sash on,<br/>  Or wish to make a rival's bosom bleed:<br/>But the _tenth_ instance will be a tornado,<br/>For there's no saying what they will or may do.<br/><br/>The reason's obvious: if there's an _√©clat_,<br/>  They lose their caste at once, as do the Parias;<br/>And when the delicacies of the Law<br/>  Have filled their papers with their comments various,<br/>Society, that china without flaw,<br/>  (The Hypocrite!) will banish them like Marius,<br/>To sit amidst the ruins of their guilt:<br/>For Fame's a Carthage not so soon rebuilt.<br/><br/>Perhaps this is as it should be;--it is<br/>  A comment on the Gospel's "Sin no more,<br/>And be thy sins forgiven:"--but upon this<br/>  I leave the Saints to settle their own score.<br/>Abroad, though doubtless they do much amiss,<br/>  An erring woman finds an opener door<br/>For her return to Virtue--as they call<br/>That Lady, who should be at home to all.<br/><br/>For me, I leave the matter where I find it,<br/>  Knowing that such uneasy virtue leads<br/>People some ten times less in fact to mind it,<br/>  And care but for discoveries, and not deeds.<br/>And as for Chastity, you'll never bind it<br/>  By all the laws the strictest lawyer pleads,<br/>But aggravate the crime you have not prevented,<br/>By rendering desperate those who had else repented.<br/><br/>But Juan was no casuist, nor had pondered<br/>  Upon the moral lessons of mankind:<br/>Besides, he had not seen of several hundred<br/>  A lady altogether to his mind.<br/>A little _blas√©_--'t is not to be wondered<br/>  At, that his heart had got a tougher rind:<br/>And though not vainer from his past success,<br/>No doubt his sensibilities were less.<br/><br/>He also had been busy seeing sights--<br/>  The Parliament and all the other houses;<br/>Had sat beneath the Gallery at nights,<br/>  To hear debates whose thunder _roused_ (not _rouses_)<br/>The World to gaze upon those Northern Lights,<br/>  Which flashed as far as where the musk-bull browses;<br/>He had also stood at times behind the Throne--<br/>But Grey was not arrived, and Chatham gone.<br/><br/>He saw, however, at the closing session,<br/>  That noble sight, when _really_ free the nation,<br/>A King in constitutional possession<br/>  Of such a Throne as is the proudest station,<br/>Though Despots know it not--till the progression<br/>  Of Freedom shall complete their education.<br/>'T is not mere Splendour makes the show august<br/>To eye or heart--it is the People's trust.<br/><br/>There, too, he saw (whate'er he may be now)<br/>  A Prince, the prince of Princes at the time,<br/>With fascination in his very bow,<br/>  And full of promise, as the spring of prime.<br/>Though Royalty was written on his brow,<br/>  He had _then_ the grace, too, rare in every clime,<br/>Of being, without alloy of fop or beau,<br/>A finished Gentleman from top to toe.<br/><br/>And Juan was received, as hath been said,<br/>  Into the best society; and there<br/>Occurred what often happens, I'm afraid,<br/>  However disciplined and debonnaire:--<br/>The talent and good humour he displayed,<br/>  Besides the marked distinction of his air,<br/>Exposed him, as was natural, to temptation,<br/>Even though himself avoided the occasion.<br/><br/>But what, and where, with whom, and when, and why,<br/>  Is not to be put hastily together;<br/>And as my object is Morality<br/>  (Whatever people say), I don't know whether<br/>I'll leave a single reader's eyelid dry,<br/>  But harrow up his feelings till they wither,<br/>And hew out a huge monument of pathos,<br/>As Philip's son proposed to do with Athos.<br/><br/>Here the twelfth canto of our Introduction<br/>  Ends. When the body of the Book's begun,<br/>You'll find it of a different construction<br/>  From what some people say 't will be when done;<br/>The plan at present 's simply in concoction.<br/>  I can't oblige you, reader, to read on;<br/>That's your affair, not mine: a real spirit<br/>Should neither court neglect, nor dread to bear it.<br/><br/>And if my thunderbolt not always rattles,<br/>  Remember, reader! you have had before,<br/>The worst of tempests and the best of battles,<br/>  That e'er were brewed from elements or gore,<br/>Besides the most sublime of--Heaven knows what else;<br/>  An usurer could scarce expect much more--<br/>But my best canto--save one on astronomy--<br/>Will turn upon "Political Economy."<br/><br/>_That_ is your present theme for popularity:<br/>  Now that the public hedge hath scarce a stake,<br/>It grows an act of patriotic charity,<br/>  To show the people the best way to break.<br/>_My plan_ (but I, if but for singularity,<br/>  Reserve it) will be very sure to take.<br/>Meantime, read all the National-Debt sinkers,<br/>And tell me what you think of our great thinkers.<br/><br/>CANTO THE THIRTEENTH.<br/><br/>I now mean to be serious;--it is time,<br/>  Since Laughter now-a-days is deemed too serious;<br/>A jest at Vice by Virtue's called a crime,<br/>  And critically held as deleterious:<br/>Besides, the sad's a source of the sublime,<br/>  Although, when long, a little apt to weary us;<br/>And therefore shall my lay soar high and solemn,<br/>As an old temple dwindled to a column.<br/><br/>The Lady Adeline Amundeville<br/>  ('T is an old Norman name, and to be found<br/>In pedigrees, by those who wander still<br/>  Along the last fields of that Gothic ground)<br/>Was high-born, wealthy by her father's will,<br/>  And beauteous, even where beauties most abound,<br/>In Britain--which, of course, true patriots find<br/>The goodliest soil of Body and of Mind.<br/><br/>I'll not gainsay them; it is not my cue;<br/>  I'll leave them to their taste, no doubt the best;<br/>An eye's an eye, and whether black or blue,<br/>  Is no great matter, so 't is in request;<br/>'T is nonsense to dispute about a hue--<br/>  The kindest may be taken as a test.<br/>The fair sex should be always fair; and no man,<br/>Till thirty, should perceive there's a plain woman.<br/><br/>And after that serene and somewhat dull<br/>  Epoch, that awkward corner turned for days<br/>More quiet, when our moon's no more at full,<br/>  We may presume to criticise or praise;<br/>Because Indifference begins to lull<br/>  Our passions, and we walk in Wisdom's ways;<br/>Also because the figure and the face<br/>Hint, that 't is time to give the younger place.<br/><br/>I know that some would fain postpone this era,<br/>  Reluctant as all placemen to resign<br/>Their post; but theirs is merely a chimera,<br/>  For they have passed Life's equinoctial line:<br/>But then they have their claret and Madeira,<br/>  To irrigate the dryness of decline;<br/>And County meetings, and the Parliament,<br/>And debt--and what not, for their solace sent.<br/><br/>And is there not Religion, and Reform,<br/>  Peace, War, the taxes, and what's called the "Nation"?<br/>The struggle to be pilots in a storm?<br/>  The landed and the monied speculation?<br/>The joys of mutual hate to keep them warm,<br/>  Instead of Love, that mere hallucination?<br/>Now Hatred is by far the longest pleasure;<br/>Men love in haste, but they detest at leisure.<br/><br/>Rough Johnson, the great moralist, professed,<br/>  Right honestly, "he liked an honest hater!"--<br/>The only truth that yet has been confessed<br/>  Within these latest thousand years or later.<br/>Perhaps the fine old fellow spoke in jest:--<br/>  For my part, I am but a mere spectator,<br/>And gaze where'er the palace or the hovel is,<br/>Much in the mode of Goethe's Mephistopheles;<br/><br/>But neither love nor hate in much excess;<br/>  Though 't was not once so. If I sneer sometimes,<br/>It is because I cannot well do less,<br/>  And now and then it also suits my rhymes.<br/>I should be very willing to redress<br/>  Men's wrongs, and rather check than punish crimes,<br/>Had not Cervantes, in that too true tale<br/>Of Quixote, shown how all such efforts fail.<br/><br/>Of all tales 't is the saddest--and more sad,<br/>  Because it makes us smile: his hero's right,<br/>And still pursues the right;--to curb the bad<br/>  His only object, and 'gainst odds to fight<br/>His guerdon: 't is his virtue makes him mad!<br/>  But his adventures form a sorry sight;--<br/>A sorrier still is the great moral taught<br/>By that real Epic unto all who have thought.<br/><br/>Redressing injury, revenging wrong,<br/>  To aid the damsel and destroy the caitiff;<br/>Opposing singly the united strong,<br/>  From foreign yoke to free the helpless native:--<br/>Alas! must noblest views, like an old song,<br/>  Be for mere Fancy's sport a theme creative,<br/>A jest, a riddle, Fame through thin and thick sought!<br/>And Socrates himself but Wisdom's Quixote?<br/><br/>Cervantes smiled Spain's chivalry away;<br/>  A single laugh demolished the right arm<br/>Of his own country;--seldom since that day<br/>  Has Spain had heroes. While Romance could charm,<br/>The World gave ground before her bright array;<br/>  And therefore have his volumes done such harm,<br/>That all their glory, as a composition,<br/>Was dearly purchased by his land's perdition.<br/><br/>I'm "at my old lunes"--digression, and forget<br/>  The Lady Adeline Amundeville;<br/>The fair most fatal Juan ever met,<br/>  Although she was not evil nor meant ill;<br/>But Destiny and Passion spread the net<br/>  (Fate is a good excuse for our own will),<br/>And caught them;--what do they _not_ catch, methinks?<br/>But I'm not Oedipus, and Life's a Sphinx.<br/><br/>I tell the tale as it is told, nor dare<br/>  To venture a solution: "_Davus sum!_"<br/>And now I will proceed upon the pair.<br/>  Sweet Adeline, amidst the gay World's hum,<br/>Was the Queen-Bee, the glass of all that's fair;<br/>  Whose charms made all men speak, and women dumb.<br/>The last's a miracle, and such was reckoned,<br/>And since that time there has not been a second.<br/><br/>Chaste was she, to Detraction's desperation,<br/>  And wedded unto one she had loved well--<br/>A man known in the councils of the Nation,<br/>  Cool, and quite English, imperturbable,<br/>Though apt to act with fire upon occasion,<br/>  Proud of himself and her: the World could tell<br/>Nought against either, and both seemed secure--<br/>She in her virtue, he in his hauteur.<br/><br/>It chanced some diplomatical relations,<br/>  Arising out of business, often brought<br/>Himself and Juan in their mutual stations<br/>  Into close contact. Though reserved, nor caught<br/>By specious seeming, Juan's youth, and patience,<br/>  And talent, on his haughty spirit wrought,<br/>And formed a basis of esteem, which ends<br/>In making men what Courtesy calls friends.<br/><br/>And thus Lord Henry, who was cautious as<br/>  Reserve and Pride could make him, and full slow<br/>In judging men--when once his judgment was<br/>  Determined, right or wrong, on friend or foe,<br/>Had all the pertinacity Pride has,<br/>  Which knows no ebb to its imperious flow,<br/>And loves or hates, disdaining to be guided,<br/>Because its own good pleasure hath decided.<br/><br/>His friendships, therefore, and no less aversions,<br/>  Though oft well founded, which confirmed but more<br/>His prepossessions, like the laws of Persians<br/>  And Medes, would ne'er revoke what went before.<br/>His feelings had not those strange fits, like tertians,<br/>  Of common likings, which make some deplore<br/>What they should laugh at--the mere ague still<br/>Of men's regard, the fever or the chill.<br/><br/>"'T is not in mortals to command success:"<br/>  But _do you more_, Sempronius--_don't_ deserve it,<br/>And take my word, you won't have any less.<br/>  Be wary, watch the time, and always serve it;<br/>Give gently way, when there's too great a press;<br/>  And for your conscience, only learn to nerve it;<br/>For, like a racer, or a boxer training,<br/>'T will make, if proved, vast efforts without paining.<br/><br/>Lord Henry also liked to be superior,<br/>  As most men do, the little or the great;<br/>The very lowest find out an inferior,<br/>  At least they think so, to exert their state<br/>Upon: for there are very few things wearier<br/>  Than solitary Pride's oppressive weight,<br/>Which mortals generously would divide,<br/>By bidding others carry while they ride.<br/><br/>In birth, in rank, in fortune likewise equal,<br/>  O'er Juan he could no distinction claim;<br/>In years he had the advantage of Time's sequel;<br/>  And, as he thought, in country much the same--<br/>Because bold Britons have a tongue and free quill,<br/>  At which all modern nations vainly aim;<br/>And the Lord Henry was a great debater,<br/>So that few Members kept the House up later.<br/><br/>These were advantages: and then he thought--<br/>  It was his foible, but by no means sinister--<br/>That few or none more than himself had caught<br/>  Court mysteries, having been himself a minister:<br/>He liked to teach that which he had been taught,<br/>  And greatly shone whenever there had been a stir;<br/>And reconciled all qualities which grace man,<br/>Always a patriot--and, sometimes, a placeman.<br/><br/>He liked the gentle Spaniard for his gravity;<br/>  He almost honoured him for his docility;<br/>Because, though young, he acquiesced with suavity,<br/>  Or contradicted but with proud humility.<br/>He knew the World, and would not see depravity<br/>  In faults which sometimes show the soil's fertility,<br/>If that the weeds o'erlive not the first crop--<br/>For then they are very difficult to stop.<br/><br/>And then he talked with him about Madrid,<br/>  Constantinople, and such distant places;<br/>Where people always did as they were bid,<br/>  Or did what they should not with foreign graces.<br/>Of coursers also spake they: Henry rid<br/>  Well, like most Englishmen, and loved the races;<br/>And Juan, like a true-born Andalusian,<br/>Could back a horse, as Despots ride a Russian.<br/><br/>And thus acquaintance grew, at noble routs,<br/>  And diplomatic dinners, or at other--<br/>For Juan stood well both with Ins and Outs,<br/>  As in freemasonry a higher brother.<br/>Upon his talent Henry had no doubts;<br/>  His manner showed him sprung from a high mother,<br/>And all men like to show their hospitality<br/>To him whose breeding matches with his quality.<br/><br/>At Blank-Blank Square;--for we will break no squares<br/>  By naming streets: since men are so censorious,<br/>And apt to sow an author's wheat with tares,<br/>  Reaping allusions private and inglorious,<br/>Where none were dreamt of, unto Love's affairs,<br/>  Which were, or are, or are to be notorious,<br/>That therefore do I previously declare,<br/>Lord Henry's mansion was in Blank-Blank Square.<br/><br/>Also there bin another pious reason<br/>  For making squares and streets anonymous;<br/>Which is, that there is scarce a single season<br/>  Which doth not shake some very splendid house<br/>With some slight heart-quake of domestic treason--<br/>  A topic Scandal doth delight to rouse:<br/>Such I might stumble over unawares,<br/>Unless I knew the very chastest squares.<br/><br/>'T is true, I might have chosen Piccadilly,<br/>  A place where peccadillos are unknown;<br/>But I have motives, whether wise or silly,<br/>  For letting that pure sanctuary alone.<br/>Therefore I name not square, street, place, until I<br/>  Find one where nothing naughty can be shown,<br/>A vestal shrine of Innocence of Heart:<br/>Such are--but I have lost the London Chart.<br/><br/>At Henry's mansion then, in Blank-Blank Square,<br/>  Was Juan a _recherch√©_, welcome guest,<br/>As many other noble scions were;<br/>  And some who had but Talent for their crest;<br/>Or Wealth, which is a passport everywhere;<br/>  Or even mere Fashion, which indeed's the best<br/>Recommendation; and to be well dressed<br/>Will very often supersede the rest.<br/><br/>And since "there's safety in a multitude<br/>  Of counsellors," as Solomon has said,<br/>Or some one for him, in some sage, grave mood;--<br/>  Indeed we see the daily proof displayed<br/>In Senates, at the Bar, in wordy feud,<br/>  Where'er collective wisdom can parade,<br/>Which is the only cause that we can guess<br/>Of Britain's present wealth and happiness;--<br/><br/>But as "there's safety" grafted in the number<br/>  "Of counsellors," for men,--thus for the sex<br/>A large acquaintance lets not Virtue slumber;<br/>  Or should it shake, the choice will more perplex--<br/>Variety itself will more encumber.<br/>  'Midst many rocks we guard more against wrecks--<br/>And thus with women: howsoe'er it shocks some's<br/>Self-love, there's safety in a crowd of coxcombs.<br/><br/>But Adeline had not the least occasion<br/>  For such a shield, which leaves but little merit<br/>To Virtue proper, or good education.<br/>  Her chief resource was in her own high spirit,<br/>Which judged Mankind at their due estimation;<br/>  And for coquetry, she disdained to wear it--<br/>Secure of admiration: its impression<br/>Was faint--as of an every-day possession.<br/><br/>To all she was polite without parade;<br/>  To some she showed attention of that kind<br/>Which flatters, but is flattery conveyed<br/>  In such a sort as cannot leave behind<br/>A trace unworthy either wife or maid;--<br/>  A gentle, genial courtesy of mind,<br/>To those who were, or passed for meritorious,<br/>Just to console sad Glory for being glorious;<br/><br/>Which is in all respects, save now and then,<br/>  A dull and desolate appendage. Gaze<br/>Upon the shades of those distinguished men<br/>  Who were or are the puppet-shows of praise,<br/>The praise of persecution. Gaze again<br/>  On the most favoured; and amidst the blaze<br/>Of sunset halos o'er the laurel-browed,<br/>What can ye recognise?--a gilded cloud.<br/><br/>There also was of course in Adeline<br/>  That calm patrician polish in the address,<br/>Which ne'er can pass the equinoctial line<br/>  Of anything which Nature would express;<br/>Just as a Mandarin finds nothing fine,--<br/>  At least his manner suffers not to guess,<br/>That anything he views can greatly please:<br/>Perhaps we have borrowed this from the Chinese--<br/><br/>Perhaps from Horace: his _"Nil admirari"_<br/>  Was what he called the "Art of Happiness"--<br/>An art on which the artists greatly vary,<br/>  And have not yet attained to much success.<br/>However, 't is expedient to be wary:<br/>  Indifference, certes, don't produce distress;<br/>And rash Enthusiasm in good society<br/>Were nothing but a moral inebriety.<br/><br/>But Adeline was not indifferent: for<br/>  (_Now_ for a common-place!) beneath the snow,<br/>As a Volcano holds the lava more<br/>  Within--_et c√¶tera_. Shall I go on?--No!<br/>I hate to hunt down a tired metaphor,<br/>  So let the often-used Volcano go.<br/>Poor thing! How frequently, by me and others,<br/>It hath been stirred up till its smoke quite smothers!<br/><br/>I'll have another figure in a trice:--<br/>  What say you to a bottle of champagne?<br/>Frozen into a very vinous ice,<br/>  Which leaves few drops of that immortal rain,<br/>Yet in the very centre, past all price,<br/>  About a liquid glassful will remain;<br/>And this is stronger than the strongest grape<br/>Could e'er express in its expanded shape:<br/><br/>'T is the whole spirit brought to a quintessence;<br/>  And thus the chilliest aspects may concentre<br/>A hidden nectar under a cold presence.<br/>  And such are many--though I only meant her<br/>From whom I now deduce these moral lessons,<br/>  On which the Muse has always sought to enter.<br/>And your cold people are beyond all price,<br/>When once you've broken their confounded ice.<br/><br/>But after all they are a North-West Passage<br/>  Unto the glowing India of the soul;<br/>And as the good ships sent upon that message<br/>  Have not exactly ascertained the Pole<br/>(Though Parry's efforts look a lucky presage),<br/>  Thus gentlemen may run upon a shoal;<br/>For if the Pole's not open, but all frost<br/>(A chance still), 't is a voyage or vessel lost.<br/><br/>And young beginners may as well commence<br/>  With quiet cruising o'er the ocean, Woman;<br/>While those who are not beginners should have sense<br/>  Enough to make for port, ere Time shall summon<br/>With his grey signal-flag; and the past tense,<br/>  The dreary _Fuimus_ of all things human,<br/>Must be declined, while Life's thin thread's spun out<br/>Between the gaping heir and gnawing gout.<br/><br/>But Heaven must be diverted; its diversion<br/>  Is sometimes truculent--but never mind:<br/>The World upon the whole is worth the assertion<br/>  (If but for comfort) that all things are kind:<br/>And that same devilish doctrine of the Persian,<br/>  Of the "Two Principles," but leaves behind<br/>As many doubts as any other doctrine<br/>Has ever puzzled Faith withal, or yoked her in,<br/><br/>The English winter--ending in July,<br/>  To recommence in August--now was done.<br/>'T is the postilion's paradise: wheels fly;<br/>  On roads, East, South, North, West, there is a run.<br/>But for post-horses who finds sympathy?<br/>  Man's pity's for himself, or for his son,<br/>Always premising that said son at college<br/>Has not contracted much more debt than knowledge.<br/><br/>The London winter's ended in July--<br/>  Sometimes a little later. I don't err<br/>In this: whatever other blunders lie<br/>  Upon my shoulders, here I must aver<br/>My Muse a glass of _Weatherology_;<br/>  For Parliament is our barometer:<br/>Let Radicals its other acts attack,<br/>Its sessions form our only almanack.<br/><br/>When its quicksilver's down at zero,--lo!<br/>  Coach, chariot, luggage, baggage, equipage!<br/>Wheels whirl from Carlton Palace to Soho,<br/>  And happiest they who horses can engage;<br/>The turnpikes glow with dust; and Rotten Row<br/>  Sleeps from the chivalry of this bright age;<br/>And tradesmen, with long bills and longer faces,<br/>Sigh--as the postboys fasten on the traces.<br/><br/>They and their bills, "Arcadians both," are left<br/>  To the Greek Kalends of another session.<br/>Alas! to them of ready cash bereft,<br/>  What hope remains? Of _hope_ the full possession,<br/>Or generous draft, conceded as a gift,<br/>  At a long date--till they can get a fresh one--<br/>Hawked about at a discount, small or large;<br/>Also the solace of an overcharge.<br/><br/>But these are trifles. Downward flies my Lord,<br/>  Nodding beside my Lady in his carriage.<br/>Away! away! "Fresh horses!" are the word,<br/>  And changed as quickly as hearts after marriage;<br/>The obsequious landlord hath the change restored;<br/>  The postboys have no reason to disparage<br/>Their fee; but ere the watered wheels may hiss hence,<br/>The ostler pleads too for a reminiscence.<br/><br/>'T is granted; and the valet mounts the dickey--<br/>  That gentleman of Lords and Gentlemen;<br/>Also my Lady's gentlewoman, tricky,<br/>  Tricked out, but modest more than poet's pen<br/>Can paint,--_"Cosi viaggino i Ricchi!"_<br/>  (Excuse a foreign slipslop now and then,<br/>If but to show I've travelled: and what's Travel,<br/>Unless it teaches one to quote and cavil?)<br/><br/>The London winter and the country summer<br/>  Were well nigh over. 'T is perhaps a pity,<br/>When Nature wears the gown that doth become her,<br/>  To lose those best months in a sweaty city,<br/>And wait until the nightingale grows dumber,<br/>  Listening debates not very wise or witty,<br/>Ere patriots their true _country_ can remember;--<br/>But there's no shooting (save grouse) till September.<br/><br/>I've done with my tirade. The World was gone;<br/>  The twice two thousand, for whom Earth was made,<br/>Were vanished to be what they call alone--<br/>  That is, with thirty servants for parade,<br/>As many guests, or more; before whom groan<br/>  As many covers, duly, daily laid.<br/>Let none accuse old England's hospitality--<br/>Its quantity is but condensed to quality.<br/><br/>Lord Henry and the Lady Adeline<br/>  Departed like the rest of their compeers,<br/>The peerage, to a mansion very fine;<br/>  The Gothic Babel of a thousand years.<br/>None than themselves could boast a longer line,<br/>  Where Time through heroes and through beauties steers;<br/>And oaks as olden as their pedigree<br/>Told of their Sires--a tomb in every tree.<br/><br/>A paragraph in every paper told<br/>  Of their departure--such is modern fame:<br/>'T is pity that it takes no further hold<br/>  Than an advertisement, or much the same;<br/>When, ere the ink be dry, the sound grows cold.<br/>  The Morning Post was foremost to proclaim--<br/>"Departure, for his country seat, to-day,<br/>Lord H. Amundeville and Lady A.<br/><br/>"We understand the splendid host intends<br/>  To entertain, this autumn, a select<br/>And numerous party of his noble friends;<br/>  'Midst whom we have heard, from sources quite correct,<br/>The Duke of D---- the shooting season spends,<br/>  With many more by rank and fashion decked;<br/>Also a foreigner of high condition,<br/>The envoy of the secret Russian mission."<br/><br/>And thus we see--who doubts the Morning Post?<br/>  (Whose articles are like the "Thirty-nine,"<br/>Which those most swear to who believe them most)--<br/>  Our gay Russ Spaniard was ordained to shine,<br/>Decked by the rays reflected from his host,<br/>  With those who, Pope says, "greatly daring dine."--<br/>'T is odd, but true,--last war the News abounded<br/>More with these dinners than the killed or wounded;--<br/><br/>As thus: "On Thursday there was a grand dinner;<br/>  Present, Lords A.B.C."--- Earls, dukes, by name<br/>Announced with no less pomp than Victory's winner:<br/>  Then underneath, and in the very same<br/>Column: date, "Falmouth. There has lately been here<br/>  The Slap-dash regiment, so well known to Fame,<br/>Whose loss in the late action we regret:<br/>The vacancies are filled up--see Gazette."<br/><br/>To Norman Abbey whirled the noble pair,--<br/>  An old, old Monastery once, and now<br/>Still older mansion--of a rich and rare<br/>  Mixed Gothic, such as artists all allow<br/>Few specimens yet left us can compare<br/>  Withal: it lies, perhaps, a little low,<br/>Because the monks preferred a hill behind,<br/>To shelter their devotion from the wind.<br/><br/>It stood embosomed in a happy valley,<br/>  Crowned by high woodlands, where the Druid oak<br/>Stood like Caractacus, in act to rally<br/>  His host, with broad arms 'gainst the thunder-stroke;<br/>And from beneath his boughs were seen to sally<br/>  The dappled foresters; as Day awoke,<br/>The branching stag swept down with all his herd,<br/>To quaff a brook which murmured like a bird.<br/><br/>Before the mansion lay a lucid Lake,<br/>  Broad as transparent, deep, and freshly fed<br/>By a river, which its softened way did take<br/>  In currents through the calmer water spread<br/>Around: the wildfowl nestled in the brake<br/>  And sedges, brooding in their liquid bed:<br/>The woods sloped downwards to its brink, and stood<br/>With their green faces fixed upon the flood.<br/><br/>Its outlet dashed into a deep cascade,<br/>  Sparkling with foam, until again subsiding,<br/>Its shriller echoes--like an infant made<br/>  Quiet--sank into softer ripples, gliding<br/>Into a rivulet; and thus allayed,<br/>  Pursued its course, now gleaming, and now hiding<br/>Its windings through the woods; now clear, now blue,<br/>According as the skies their shadows threw.<br/><br/>A glorious remnant of the Gothic pile<br/>  (While yet the Church was Rome's) stood half apart<br/>In a grand Arch, which once screened many an aisle.<br/>  These last had disappeared--a loss to Art:<br/>The first yet frowned superbly o'er the soil,<br/>  And kindled feelings in the roughest heart,<br/>Which mourned the power of Time's or Tempest's march,<br/>In gazing on that venerable Arch.<br/><br/>Within a niche, nigh to its pinnacle,<br/>  Twelve Saints had once stood sanctified in stone;<br/>But these had fallen, not when the friars fell,<br/>  But in the war which struck Charles from his throne,<br/>When each house was a fortalice--as tell<br/>  The annals of full many a line undone,--<br/>The gallant Cavaliers, who fought in vain<br/>For those who knew not to resign or reign.<br/><br/>But in a higher niche, alone, but crowned,<br/>  The Virgin-Mother of the God-born Child,<br/>With her Son in her bless√©d arms, looked round,<br/>  Spared by some chance when all beside was spoiled:<br/>She made the earth below seem holy ground.<br/>  This may be superstition, weak or wild;<br/>But even the faintest relics of a shrine<br/>Of any worship wake some thoughts divine.<br/><br/>A mighty window, hollow in the centre,<br/>  Shorn of its glass of thousand colourings,<br/>Through which the deepened glories once could enter,<br/>  Streaming from off the Sun like Seraph's wings,<br/>Now yawns all desolate: now loud, now fainter,<br/>  The gale sweeps through its fretwork, and oft sings<br/>The owl his anthem, where the silenced quire<br/>Lie with their Hallelujahs quenched like fire.<br/><br/>But in the noontide of the moon, and when<br/>  The wind is wing√©d from one point of heaven,<br/>There moans a strange unearthly sound, which then<br/>  Is musical--a dying accent driven<br/>Through the huge Arch, which soars and sinks again.<br/>  Some deem it but the distant echo given<br/>Back to the night wind by the waterfall,<br/>And harmonised by the old choral wall:<br/><br/>Others, that some original shape, or form<br/>  Shaped by decay perchance, hath given the power<br/>(Though less than that of Memnon's statue, warm<br/>  In Egypt's rays, to harp at a fixed hour)<br/>To this grey ruin: with a voice to charm,<br/>  Sad, but serene, it sweeps o'er tree or tower;<br/>The cause I know not, nor can solve; but such<br/>The fact:--I've heard it,--once perhaps too much.<br/><br/>Amidst the court a Gothic fountain played,<br/>  Symmetrical, but decked with carvings quaint--<br/>Strange faces, like to men in masquerade,<br/>  And here perhaps a monster, there a saint:<br/>The spring gushed through grim mouths of granite made,<br/>  And sparkled into basins, where it spent<br/>Its little torrent in a thousand bubbles,<br/>Like man's vain Glory, and his vainer troubles.<br/><br/>The Mansion's self was vast and venerable,<br/>  With more of the monastic than has been<br/>Elsewhere preserved: the cloisters still were stable,<br/>  The cells, too, and Refectory, I ween:<br/>An exquisite small chapel had been able,<br/>  Still unimpaired, to decorate the scene;<br/>The rest had been reformed, replaced, or sunk,<br/>And spoke more of the baron than the monk.<br/><br/>Huge halls, long galleries, spacious chambers, joined<br/>  By no quite lawful marriage of the arts,<br/>Might shock a connoisseur; but when combined,<br/>  Formed a whole which, irregular in parts,<br/>Yet left a grand impression on the mind,<br/>  At least of those whose eyes are in their hearts:<br/>We gaze upon a giant for his stature,<br/>Nor judge at first if all be true to nature.<br/><br/>Steel Barons, molten the next generation<br/>  To silken rows of gay and gartered Earls,<br/>Glanced from the walls in goodly preservation:<br/>  And Lady Marys
