blooming into girls,<br/>With fair long locks, had also kept their station:<br/>  And Countesses mature in robes and pearls:<br/>Also some beauties of Sir Peter Lely,<br/>Whose drapery hints we may admire them freely.<br/><br/>Judges in very formidable ermine<br/>  Were there, with brows that did not much invite<br/>The accused to think their lordships would determine<br/>  His cause by leaning much from might to right:<br/>Bishops, who had not left a single sermon;<br/>  Attorneys-general, awful to the sight,<br/>As hinting more (unless our judgments warp us)<br/>Of the "Star Chamber" than of "Habeas Corpus."<br/><br/>Generals, some all in armour, of the old<br/>  And iron time, ere lead had ta'en the lead;<br/>Others in wigs of Marlborough's martial fold,<br/>  Huger than twelve of our degenerate breed:<br/>Lordlings, with staves of white or keys of gold:<br/>  Nimrods, whose canvas scarce contained the steed;<br/>And, here and there, some stern high patriot stood,<br/>Who could not get the place for which he sued.<br/><br/>But ever and anon, to soothe your vision,<br/>  Fatigued with these hereditary glories,<br/>There rose a Carlo Dolce or a Titian,<br/>  Or wilder group of savage Salvatore's:<br/>Here danced Albano's boys, and here the sea shone<br/>  In Vernet's ocean lights; and there the stories<br/>Of martyrs awed, as Spagnoletto tainted<br/>His brush with all the blood of all the sainted.<br/><br/>Here sweetly spread a landscape of Lorraine;<br/>  There Rembrandt made his darkness equal light,<br/>Or gloomy Caravaggio's gloomier stain<br/>  Bronzed o'er some lean and stoic anchorite:--<br/>But, lo! a Teniers woos, and not in vain,<br/>  Your eyes to revel in a livelier sight:<br/>His bell-mouthed goblet makes me feel quite Danish<br/>Or Dutch with thirst--What, ho! a flask of Rhenish.<br/><br/>Oh, reader! if that thou canst read,--and know,<br/>  'T is not enough to spell, or even to read,<br/>To constitute a reader--there must go<br/>  Virtues of which both you and I have need;--<br/>Firstly, begin with the beginning--(though<br/>  That clause is hard); and secondly, proceed:<br/>Thirdly, commence not with the end--or, sinning<br/>In this sort, end at last with the beginning.<br/><br/>But, reader, thou hast patient been of late,<br/>  While I, without remorse of rhyme, or fear,<br/>Have built and laid out ground at such a rate,<br/>  Dan Phoebus takes me for an auctioneer.<br/>That Poets were so from their earliest date,<br/>  By Homer's "Catalogue of ships" is clear;<br/>But a mere modern must be moderate--<br/>I spare you then the furniture and plate.<br/><br/>The mellow Autumn came, and with it came<br/>  The promised party, to enjoy its sweets.<br/>The corn is cut, the manor full of game;<br/>  The pointer ranges, and the sportsman beats<br/>In russet jacket:--lynx-like in his aim;<br/>  Full grows his bag, and wonder_ful_ his feats.<br/>Ah, nutbrown partridges! Ah, brilliant pheasants!<br/>And ah, ye poachers!--'T is no sport for peasants.<br/><br/>An English Autumn, though it hath no vines,<br/>  Blushing with Bacchant coronals along<br/>The paths o'er which the far festoon entwines<br/>  The red grape in the sunny lands of song,<br/>Hath yet a purchased choice of choicest wines;<br/>  The Claret light, and the Madeira strong.<br/>If Britain mourn her bleakness, we can tell her,<br/>The very best of vineyards is the cellar.<br/><br/>Then, if she hath not that serene decline<br/>  Which makes the southern Autumn's day appear<br/>As if 't would to a second Spring resign<br/>  The season, rather than to Winter drear,--<br/>Of in-door comforts still she hath a mine,--<br/>  The sea-coal fires, the "earliest of the year;"<br/>Without doors, too, she may compete in mellow,<br/>As what is lost in green is gained in yellow.<br/><br/>And for the effeminate _villeggialura_--<br/>  Rife with more horns than hounds--she hath the chase,<br/>So animated that it might allure a<br/>  Saint from his beads to join the jocund race:<br/>Even Nimrod's self might leave the plains of Dura,<br/>  And wear the Melton jacket for a space:<br/>If she hath no wild boars, she hath a tame<br/>Preserve of bores, who ought to be made game.<br/><br/>The noble guests, assembled at the Abbey,<br/>  Consisted of--we give the sex the _pas_--<br/>The Duchess of Fitz-Fulke; the Countess Crabby;<br/>  The Ladies Scilly, Busey;--Miss Eclat,<br/>Miss Bombazeen, Miss Mackstay, Miss O'Tabby,<br/>  And Mrs. Rabbi, the rich banker's squaw;<br/>Also the honourable Mrs. Sleep,<br/>Who looked a white lamb, yet was a black sheep:<br/><br/>With other Countesses of Blank--but rank;<br/>  At once the "lie" and the _√©lite_ of crowds;<br/>Who pass like water filtered in a tank,<br/>  All purged and pious from their native clouds;<br/>Or paper turned to money by the Bank:<br/>  No matter how or why, the passport shrouds<br/>The _pass√©e_ and the past; for good society<br/>Is no less famed for tolerance than piety,--<br/><br/>That is, up to a certain point; which point<br/>  Forms the most difficult in punctuation.<br/>Appearances appear to form the joint<br/>  On which it hinges in a higher station;<br/>And so that no explosion cry "Aroint<br/>  Thee, witch!" or each Medea has her Jason;<br/>Or (to the point with Horace and with Pulci)<br/>_"Omne tulit punctum,_ qu√¶ _miscuit utile dulci."_<br/><br/>I can't exactly trace their rule of right,<br/>  Which hath a little leaning to a lottery.<br/>I've seen a virtuous woman put down quite<br/>  By the mere combination of a coterie;<br/>Also a so-so matron boldly fight<br/>  Her way back to the world by dint of plottery,<br/>And shine the very _Siria_, of the spheres,<br/>Escaping with a few slight, scarless sneers.<br/><br/>I have seen more than I'll say:--but we will see<br/>  How _our "villeggiatura"_ will get on.<br/>The party might consist of thirty-three<br/>  Of highest caste--the Brahmins of the _ton_.<br/>I have named a few, not foremost in degree,<br/>  But ta'en at hazard as the rhyme may run.<br/>By way of sprinkling, scattered amongst these,<br/>There also were some Irish absentees.<br/><br/>There was Parolles, too, the legal bully,<br/>  Who limits all his battles to the Bar<br/>And Senate: when invited elsewhere, truly,<br/>  He shows more appetite for words than war.<br/>There was the young bard Rackrhyme, who had newly<br/>  Come out and glimmered as a six weeks' star.<br/>There was Lord Pyrrho, too, the great freethinker;<br/>And Sir John Pottledeep, the mighty drinker.<br/><br/>There was the Duke of Dash, who was a--duke,<br/>  "Aye, every inch a" duke; there were twelve peers<br/>Like Charlemagne's--and all such peers in _look_<br/>  And _intellect_, that neither eyes nor ears<br/>For commoners had ever them mistook.<br/>  There were the six Miss Rawbolds--pretty dears!<br/>All song and sentiment; whose hearts were set<br/>Less on a convent than a coronet.<br/><br/>There were four Honourable Misters, whose<br/>  Honour was more before their names than after;<br/>There was the _preux Chevalier de la Ruse_,<br/>  Whom France and Fortune lately deigned to waft here,<br/>Whose chiefly harmless talent was to amuse;<br/>  But the clubs found it rather serious laughter,<br/>Because--such was his magic power to please--<br/>The dice seemed charmed, too, with his repartees.<br/><br/>There was Dick Dubious, the metaphysician,<br/>  Who loved philosophy and a good dinner;<br/>Angle, the _soi-disant_ mathematician;<br/>  Sir Henry Silvercup, the great race-winner.<br/>There was the Reverend Rodomont Precisian,<br/>  Who did not hate so much the sin as sinner:<br/>And Lord Augustus Fitz-Plantagenet,<br/>Good at all things, but better at a bet.<br/><br/>There was Jack Jargon, the gigantic guardsman;<br/>  And General Fireface, famous in the field,<br/>A great tactician, and no less a swordsman,<br/>  Who ate, last war, more Yankees than he killed.<br/>There was the waggish Welsh Judge, Jefferies Hardsman,<br/>  In his grave office so completely skilled,<br/>That when a culprit came for condemnation,<br/>He had his Judge's joke for consolation.<br/><br/>Good company's a chess-board--there are kings,<br/>  Queens, bishops, knights, rooks, pawns; the World's a game;<br/>Save that the puppets pull at their own strings,<br/>  Methinks gay Punch hath something of the same.<br/>My Muse, the butterfly hath but her wings,<br/>  Not stings, and flits through ether without aim,<br/>Alighting rarely:--were she but a hornet,<br/>Perhaps there might be vices which would mourn it.<br/><br/>I had forgotten--but must not forget--<br/>  An orator, the latest of the session,<br/>Who had delivered well a very set<br/>  Smooth speech, his first and maidenly transgression<br/>Upon debate: the papers echoed yet<br/>  With his _d√©but_, which made a strong impression,<br/>And ranked with what is every day displayed--<br/>"The best first speech that ever yet was made."<br/><br/>Proud of his "Hear hims!" proud, too, of his vote,<br/>  And lost virginity of oratory,<br/>Proud of his learning (just enough to quote),<br/>  He revelled in his Ciceronian glory:<br/>With memory excellent to get by rote,<br/>  With wit to hatch a pun or tell a story,<br/>Graced with some merit, and with more effrontery,<br/>"His country's pride," he came down to the country.<br/><br/>There also were two wits by acclamation,<br/>  Longbow from Ireland, Strongbow from the Tweed--Both<br/>lawyers and both men of education--<br/>  But Strongbow's wit was of more polished breed;<br/>Longbow was rich in an imagination<br/>  As beautiful and bounding as a steed,<br/>But sometimes stumbling over a potato,--<br/>While Strongbow's best things might have come from Cato.<br/><br/>Strongbow was like a new-tuned harpsichord;<br/>  But Longbow wild as an √Üolian harp,<br/>With which the Winds of heaven can claim accord,<br/>  And make a music, whether flat or sharp.<br/>Of Strongbow's talk you would not change a word:<br/>  At Longbow's phrases you might sometimes carp:<br/>Both wits--one born so, and the other bred--<br/>This by his heart--his rival by his head.<br/><br/>If all these seem an heterogeneous mass<br/>  To be assembled at a country seat,<br/>Yet think, a specimen of every class<br/>  Is better than a humdrum t√™te-√†-t√™te.<br/>The days of Comedy are gone, alas!<br/>  When Congreve's fool could vie with Moli√®re's _b√™te_:<br/>Society is smoothed to that excess,<br/>That manners hardly differ more than dress.<br/><br/>Our ridicules are kept in the back-ground--<br/>  Ridiculous enough, but also dull;<br/>Professions, too, are no more to be found<br/>  Professional; and there is nought to cull<br/>Of Folly's fruit; for though your fools abound,<br/>  They're barren, and not worth the pains to pull.<br/>Society is now one polished horde,<br/>Formed of two mighty tribes, the _Bores_ and _Bored_.<br/><br/>But from being farmers, we turn gleaners, gleaning<br/>  The scanty but right-well threshed ears of Truth;<br/>And, gentle reader! when you gather meaning,<br/>  You may be Boaz, and I--modest Ruth.<br/>Further I'd quote, but Scripture intervening<br/>  Forbids. A great impression in my youth<br/>Was made by Mrs. Adams, where she cries,<br/>"That Scriptures out of church are blasphemies."<br/><br/>But what we can we glean in this vile age<br/>  Of chaff, although our gleanings be not grist.<br/>I must not quite omit the talking sage,<br/>  Kit-Cat, the famous Conversationist,<br/>Who, in his common-place book, had a page<br/>  Prepared each morn for evenings. "List, oh list!"<br/>"Alas, poor ghost!"--What unexpected woes<br/>Await those who have studied their _bons-mots!_<br/><br/>Firstly, they must allure the conversation,<br/>  By many windings to their clever clinch;<br/>And secondly, must let slip no occasion,<br/>  Nor _bate_ (abate) their hearers of an _inch_,<br/>But take an ell--and make a great sensation,<br/>  If possible; and thirdly, never flinch<br/>When some smart talker puts them to the test,<br/>But seize the last word, which no doubt's the best.<br/><br/>Lord Henry and his lady were the hosts;<br/>  The party we have touched on were the guests.<br/>Their table was a board to tempt even ghosts<br/>  To pass the Styx for more substantial feasts.<br/>I will not dwell upon _rago√ªts_ or roasts,<br/>  Albeit all human history attests<br/>That happiness for Man--the hungry sinner!--<br/>Since Eve ate apples, much depends on dinner.<br/><br/>Witness the lands which "flowed with milk and honey,"<br/>  Held out unto the hungry Israelites:<br/>To this we have added since, the love of money,<br/>  The only sort of pleasure which requites.<br/>Youth fades, and leaves our days no longer sunny;<br/>  We tire of mistresses and parasites;<br/>But oh, ambrosial cash! Ah! who would lose thee?<br/>When we no more can use, or even abuse thee!<br/><br/>The gentlemen got up betimes to shoot,<br/>  Or hunt: the young, because they liked the sport--<br/>The first thing boys like after play and fruit;<br/>  The middle-aged, to make the day more short;<br/>For _ennui_ is a growth of English root,<br/>  Though nameless in our language:--we retort<br/>The fact for words, and let the French translate<br/>That awful yawn which sleep can not abate.<br/><br/>The elderly walked through the library,<br/>  And tumbled books, or criticised the pictures,<br/>Or sauntered through the gardens piteously,<br/>  And made upon the hot-house several strictures,<br/>Or rode a nag which trotted not too high,<br/>  Or on the morning papers read their lectures,<br/>Or on the watch their longing eyes would fix,<br/>Longing at sixty for the hour of six.<br/><br/>But none were _g√™n√©_: the great hour of union<br/>  Was rung by dinner's knell; till then all were<br/>Masters of their own time--or in communion,<br/>  Or solitary, as they chose to bear<br/>The hours, which how to pass is but to few known.<br/>  Each rose up at his own, and had to spare<br/>What time he chose for dress, and broke his fast<br/>When, where, and how he chose for that repast.<br/><br/>The ladies--some rouged, some a little pale--<br/>  Met the morn as they might. If fine, they rode,<br/>Or walked; if foul, they read, or told a tale,<br/>  Sung, or rehearsed the last dance from abroad;<br/>Discussed the fashion which might next prevail,<br/>  And settled bonnets by the newest code,<br/>Or crammed twelve sheets into one little letter,<br/>To make each correspondent a new debtor.<br/><br/>For some had absent lovers, all had friends;<br/>  The earth has nothing like a she epistle,<br/>And hardly Heaven--because it never ends--<br/>  I love the mystery of a female missal,<br/>Which, like a creed, ne'er says all it intends,<br/>  But full of cunning as Ulysses' whistle,<br/>When he allured poor Dolon:--you had better<br/>Take care what you reply to such a letter.<br/><br/>Then there were billiards; cards, too, but _no_ dice;--<br/>  Save in the clubs no man of honour plays;--<br/>Boats when 't was water, skating when 't was ice,<br/>  And the hard frost destroyed the scenting days:<br/>And angling, too, that solitary vice,<br/>  Whatever Izaak Walton sings or says:<br/>The quaint, old, cruel coxcomb, in his gullet<br/>Should have a hook, and a small trout to pull it.<br/><br/>With evening came the banquet and the wine;<br/>  The conversazione--the duet<br/>Attuned by voices more or less divine<br/>  (My heart or head aches with the memory yet).<br/>The four Miss Rawbolds in a glee would shine;<br/>  But the two youngest loved more to be set<br/>Down to the harp--because to Music's charms<br/>They added graceful necks, white hands and arms.<br/><br/>Sometimes a dance (though rarely on field days,<br/>  For then the gentlemen were rather tired)<br/>Displayed some sylph-like figures in its maze;<br/>  Then there was small-talk ready when required;<br/>Flirtation--but decorous; the mere praise<br/>  Of charms that should or should not be admired.<br/>The hunters fought their fox-hunt o'er again,<br/>And then retreated soberly--at ten.<br/><br/>The politicians, in a nook apart,<br/>  Discussed the World, and settled all the spheres:<br/>The wits watched every loophole for their art,<br/>  To introduce a _bon-mot_ head and ears;<br/>Small is the rest of those who would be smart,<br/>  A moment's good thing may have cost them years<br/>Before they find an hour to introduce it;<br/>And then, even _then_, some bore may make them lose it.<br/><br/>But all was gentle and aristocratic<br/>  In this our party; polished, smooth, and cold,<br/>As Phidian forms cut out of marble Attic.<br/>  There now are no Squire Westerns, as of old;<br/>And our Sophias are not so emphatic,<br/>  But fair as then, or fairer to behold:<br/>We have no accomplished blackguards, like Tom Jones,<br/>But gentlemen in stays, as stiff as stones.<br/><br/>They separated at an early hour;<br/>  That is, ere midnight--which is London's noon:<br/>But in the country ladies seek their bower<br/>  A little earlier than the waning moon.<br/>Peace to the slumbers of each folded flower--<br/>  May the rose call back its true colour soon!<br/>Good hours of fair cheeks are the fairest tinters,<br/>And lower the price of rouge--at least some winters.<br/><br/>CANTO THE FOURTEENTH.<br/><br/>IF from great Nature's or our own abyss<br/>  Of Thought we could but snatch a certainty,<br/>Perhaps Mankind might find the path they miss--<br/>  But then 't would spoil much good philosophy.<br/>One system eats another up, and this<br/>  Much as old Saturn ate his progeny;<br/>For when his pious consort gave him stones<br/>In lieu of sons, of these he made no bones.<br/><br/>But System doth reverse the Titan's breakfast,<br/>  And eats her parents, albeit the digestion<br/>Is difficult. Pray tell me, can you make fast,<br/>  After due search, your faith to any question?<br/>Look back o'er ages, ere unto the stake fast<br/>  You bind yourself, and call some mode the best one.<br/>Nothing more true than _not_ to trust your senses;<br/>And yet what are your other evidences?<br/><br/>For me, I know nought; nothing I deny,<br/>  Admit--reject--contemn: and what know _you_,<br/>Except perhaps that you were born to die?<br/>  And both may after all turn out untrue.<br/>An age may come, Font of Eternity,<br/>  When nothing shall be either old or new.<br/>Death, so called, is a thing which makes men weep,<br/>And yet a third of Life is passed in sleep.<br/><br/>A sleep without dreams, after a rough day<br/>  Of toil, is what we covet most; and yet<br/>How clay shrinks back from more quiescent clay!<br/>  The very Suicide that pays his debt<br/>At once without instalments (an old way<br/>  Of paying debts, which creditors regret),<br/>Lets out impatiently his rushing breath,<br/>Less from disgust of Life than dread of Death.<br/><br/>'T is round him--near him--here--there--everywhere--<br/>  And there's a courage which grows out of fear,<br/>Perhaps of all most desperate, which will dare<br/>  The worst to _know_ it:--when the mountains rear<br/>Their peaks beneath your human foot, and there<br/>  You look down o'er the precipice, and drear<br/>The gulf of rock yawns,--you can't gaze a minute,<br/>Without an awful wish to plunge within it.<br/><br/>'T is true, you don't--but, pale and struck with terror,<br/>  Retire: but look into your past impression!<br/>And you will find, though shuddering at the mirror<br/>  Of your own thoughts, in all their self-confession,<br/>The lurking bias, be it truth or error,<br/>  To the _unknown_; a secret prepossession,<br/>To plunge with all your fears--but where? You know not,<br/>And that's the reason why you do--or do not.<br/><br/>But what's this to the purpose? you will say.<br/>  Gent. reader, nothing; a mere speculation,<br/>For which my sole excuse is--'t is my way;<br/>  Sometimes _with_ and sometimes without occasion,<br/>I write what's uppermost, without delay;<br/>  This narrative is not meant for narration,<br/>But a mere airy and fantastic basis,<br/>To build up common things with common places.<br/><br/>You know, or don't know, that great Bacon saith,<br/>  "Fling up a straw, 't will show the way the wind blows;"<br/>And such a straw, borne on by human breath,<br/>  Is Poesy, according as the Mind glows;<br/>A paper kite which flies 'twixt Life and Death,<br/>  A shadow which the onward Soul behind throws:<br/>And mine's a bubble, not blown up for praise,<br/>But just to play with, as an infant plays.<br/><br/>The World is all before me--or behind;<br/>  For I have seen a portion of that same,<br/>And quite enough for me to keep in mind;--<br/>  Of passions, too, I have proved enough to blame,<br/>To the great pleasure of our friends, Mankind,<br/>  Who like to mix some slight alloy with fame;<br/>For I was rather famous in my time,<br/>Until I fairly knocked it up with rhyme.<br/><br/>I have brought this world about my ears, and eke<br/>  The other; that's to say, the Clergy--who<br/>Upon my head have bid their thunders break<br/>  In pious libels by no means a few.<br/>And yet I can't help scribbling once a week,<br/>  Tiring old readers, nor discovering new.<br/>In Youth I wrote because my mind was full,<br/>And _now_ because I feel it growing dull.<br/><br/>But "why then publish?"--There are no rewards<br/>  Of fame or profit when the World grows weary.<br/>I ask in turn,--Why do you play at cards?<br/>  Why drink? Why read?--To make some hour less dreary.<br/>It occupies me to turn back regards<br/>  On what I've seen or pondered, sad or cheery;<br/>And what I write I cast upon the stream,<br/>To swim or sink--I have had at least my dream.<br/><br/>I think that were I _certain_ of success,<br/>  I hardly could compose another line:<br/>So long I've battled either more or less,<br/>  That no defeat can drive me from the Nine.<br/>This feeling 't is not easy to express,<br/>  And yet 't is not affected, I opine.<br/>In play, there are two pleasures for your choosing--<br/>The one is winning, and the other losing.<br/><br/>Besides, my Muse by no means deals in fiction:<br/>  She gathers a repertory of facts,<br/>Of course with some reserve and slight restriction,<br/>  But mostly sings of human things and acts--<br/>And that's one cause she meets with contradiction;<br/>  For too much truth, at first sight, ne'er attracts;<br/>And were her object only what's called Glory,<br/>With more ease too she'd tell a different story.<br/><br/>Love--War--a tempest--surely there's variety;<br/>  Also a seasoning slight of lucubration;<br/>A bird's-eye view, too, of that wild, Society;<br/>  A slight glance thrown on men of every station.<br/>If you have nought else, here's at least satiety,<br/>  Both in performance and in preparation;<br/>And though these lines should only line portmanteaus,<br/>Trade will be all the better for these Cantos.<br/><br/>The portion of this World which I at present<br/>  Have taken up to fill the following sermon,<br/>Is one of which there's no description recent:<br/>  The reason why is easy to determine:<br/>Although it seems both prominent and pleasant,<br/>  There is a sameness in its gems and ermine,<br/>A dull and family likeness through all ages,<br/>Of no great promise for poetic pages.<br/><br/>With much to excite, there's little to exalt;<br/>  Nothing that speaks to all men and all times;<br/>A sort of varnish over every fault;<br/>  A kind of common-place, even in their crimes;<br/>Factitious passions--Wit without much salt--<br/>  A want of that true nature which sublimes<br/>Whate'er it shows with Truth; a smooth monotony<br/>Of character, in those at least who have got any.<br/><br/>Sometimes, indeed, like soldiers off parade,<br/>  They break their ranks and gladly leave the drill;<br/>But then the roll-call draws them back afraid,<br/>  And they must be or seem what they _were_: still<br/>Doubtless it is a brilliant masquerade:<br/>  But when of the first sight you have had your fill,<br/>It palls--at least it did so upon me,<br/>This paradise of Pleasure and _Ennui_.<br/><br/>When we have made our love, and gamed our gaming,<br/>  Dressed, voted, shone, and, may be, something more--<br/>With dandies dined--heard senators declaiming--<br/>  Seen beauties brought to market by the score,<br/>Sad rakes to sadder husbands chastely taming--<br/>  There's little left but to be bored or bore.<br/>Witness those _ci-devant jeunes hommes_ who stem<br/>The stream, nor leave the world which leaveth them.<br/><br/>'T is said--indeed a general complaint--<br/>  That no one has succeeded in describing<br/>The _monde_, exactly as they ought to paint:<br/>  Some say, that authors only snatch, by bribing<br/>The porter, some slight scandals strange and quaint,<br/>  To furnish matter for their moral gibing;<br/>And that their books have but one style in common--<br/>My Lady's prattle, filtered through her woman.<br/><br/>But this can't well be true, just now; for writers<br/>  Are grown of the _beau monde_ a part potential:<br/>I've seen them balance even the scale with fighters,<br/>  Especially when young, for that's essential.<br/>Why do their sketches fail them as inditers<br/>  Of what they deem themselves most consequential,<br/>The _real_ portrait of the highest tribe?<br/>'T is that--in fact--there's little to describe.<br/><br/>_"Haud ignara loquor;"_ these are _Nugae_, "_quarum<br/>  Pars_ parva _fui_," but still art and part.<br/>Now I could much more easily sketch a harem,<br/>  A battle, wreck, or history of the heart,<br/>Than these things; and besides, I wish to spare 'em,<br/>  For reasons which I choose to keep apart.<br/>_"Vetabo Cereris sacrum qui vulgarit"_--<br/>Which means, that vulgar people must not share it.<br/><br/>And therefore what I throw off is ideal--<br/>  Lowered, leavened, like a history of Freemasons,<br/>Which bears the same relation to the real,<br/>  As Captain Parry's Voyage may do to Jason's.<br/>The grand _Arcanum_'s not for men to see all;<br/>  My music has some mystic diapasons;<br/>And there is much which could not be appreciated<br/>In any manner by the uninitiated.<br/><br/>Alas! worlds fall--and Woman, since she felled<br/>  The World (as, since that history, less polite<br/>Than true, hath been a creed so strictly held),<br/>  Has not yet given up the practice quite.<br/>Poor Thing of Usages! coerced, compelled,<br/>  Victim when wrong, and martyr oft when right,<br/>Condemned to child-bed, as men for their sins<br/>Have shaving too entailed upon their chins,--<br/><br/>A daily plague, which in the aggregate<br/>  May average on the whole with parturition.--<br/>But as to women--who can penetrate<br/>  The real sufferings of their she condition?<br/>Man's very sympathy with their estate<br/>  Has much of selfishness, and more suspicion.<br/>Their love, their virtue, beauty, education,<br/>But form good housekeepers--to breed a nation.<br/><br/>All this were very well, and can't be better;<br/>  But even this is difficult, Heaven knows,<br/>So many troubles from her birth beset her,<br/>  Such small distinction between friends and foes;<br/>The gilding wears so soon from off her fetter,<br/>  That--but ask any woman if she'd choose<br/>(Take her at thirty, that is) to have been<br/>Female or male? a schoolboy or a Queen?<br/><br/>"Petticoat Influence" is a great reproach,<br/>  Which even those who obey would fain be thought<br/>To fly from, as from hungry pikes a roach;<br/>  But since beneath it upon earth we are brought,<br/>By various joltings of Life's hackney coach,<br/>  I for one venerate a petticoat--<br/>A garment of a mystical sublimity,<br/>No matter whether russet, silk, or dimity.<br/><br/>Much I respect, and much I have adored,<br/>  In my young days, that chaste and goodly veil,<br/>Which holds a treasure, like a miser's hoard,<br/>  And more attracts by all it doth conceal--<br/>A golden scabbard on a Damasque sword,<br/>  A loving letter with a mystic seal,<br/>A cure for grief--for what can ever rankle<br/>Before a petticoat and peeping ankle?<br/><br/>And when upon a silent, sullen day,<br/>  With a Sirocco, for example, blowing,<br/>When even the sea looks dim with all its spray,<br/>  And sulkily the river's ripple's flowing,<br/>And the sky shows that very ancient gray,<br/>  The sober, sad antithesis to glowing,--<br/>'T is pleasant, if _then_ anything is pleasant,<br/>To catch a glimpse even of a pretty peasant.<br/><br/>We left our heroes and our heroines<br/>  In that fair clime which don't depend on climate,<br/>Quite independent of the Zodiac's signs,<br/>  Though certainly more difficult to rhyme at,<br/>Because the Sun, and stars, and aught that shines,<br/>  Mountains, and all we can be most sublime at,<br/>Are there oft dull and dreary as a _dun_--<br/>Whether a sky's or tradesman's is all one.<br/><br/>An in-door life is less poetical;<br/>  And out-of-door hath showers, and mists, and sleet<br/>With which I could not brew a pastoral:<br/>  But be it as it may, a bard must meet<br/>All difficulties, whether great or small,<br/>  To spoil his undertaking, or complete--<br/>And work away--like Spirit upon Matter--<br/>Embarrassed somewhat both with fire and water.<br/><br/>Juan--in this respect, at least, like saints--<br/>  Was all things unto people of all sorts,<br/>And lived contentedly, without complaints,<br/>  In camps, in ships, in cottages, or courts--<br/>Born with that happy soul which seldom faints,<br/>  And mingling modestly in toils or sports.<br/>He likewise could be most things to all women,<br/>Without the coxcombry of certain _she_ men.<br/><br/>A fox-hunt to a foreigner is strange;<br/>  'T is also subject to the double danger<br/>Of tumbling first, and having in exchange<br/>  Some pleasant jesting at the awkward stranger:<br/>But Juan had been early taught to range<br/>  The wilds, as doth an Arab turned avenger,<br/>So that his horse, or charger, hunter, hack,<br/>Knew that he had a rider on his back.<br/><br/>And now in this new field, with some applause,<br/>  He cleared hedge, ditch, and double post, and rail,<br/>And never _craned_ and made but few _"faux pas,"_<br/>  And only fretted when the scent 'gan fail.<br/>He broke, 't is true, some statutes of the laws<br/>  Of hunting--for the sagest youth is frail;<br/>Rode o'er the hounds, it may be, now and then,<br/>And once o'er several Country Gentlemen.<br/><br/>But on the whole, to general admiration,<br/>  He acquitted both himself and horse: the Squires<br/>Marvelled at merit of another nation;<br/>  The boors cried "Dang it! who'd have thought it?"--Sires,<br/>The Nestors of the sporting generation,<br/>  Swore praises, and recalled their former fires;<br/>The Huntsman's self relented to a grin,<br/>And rated him almost a whipper-in.<br/><br/>Such were his trophies--not of spear and shield,<br/>  But leaps, and bursts, and sometimes foxes' brushes;<br/>Yet I must own,--although in this I yield<br/>  To patriot sympathy a Briton's blushes,--<br/>He thought at heart like courtly Chesterfield,<br/>  Who, after a long chase o'er hills, dales, bushes,<br/>And what not, though he rode beyond all price.<br/>Asked next day, "If men ever hunted _twice_?"<br/><br/>He also had a quality uncommon<br/>  To early risers after a long chase,<br/>Who wake in winter ere the cock can summon<br/>  December's drowsy day to his dull race,--<br/>A quality agreeable to Woman,<br/>  When her soft, liquid words run on apace,<br/>Who likes a listener, whether Saint or Sinner,--<br/>He did not fall asleep just after dinner;<br/><br/>But, light and airy, stood on the alert,<br/>  And shone in the best part of dialogue,<br/>By humouring always what they might assert,<br/>  And listening to the topics most in vogue,<br/>Now grave, now gay, but never dull or pert;<br/>  And smiling but in secret--cunning rogue!<br/>He ne'er presumed to make an error clearer;--<br/>In short, there never was a better hearer.<br/><br/>And then he danced;--all foreigners excel<br/>  The serious Angles in the eloquence<br/>Of pantomime!--he danced, I say, right well,<br/>  With emphasis, and also with good sense--<br/>A thing in footing indispensable;<br/>  He danced without theatrical pretence,<br/>Not like a ballet-master in the van<br/>Of his drilled nymphs, but like a gentleman.<br/><br/>Chaste were his steps, each kept within due bound,<br/>  And Elegance was sprinkled o'er his figure;<br/>Like swift Camilla, he scarce skimmed the ground,<br/>  And rather held in than put forth his vigour;<br/>And then he had an ear for Music's sound,<br/>  Which might defy a crotchet critic's rigour.<br/>Such classic _pas_--sans flaws--set off our hero,<br/>He glanced like a personified Bolero;<br/><br/>Or like a flying Hour before Aurora,<br/>  In Guido's famous fresco (which alone<br/>Is worth a tour to Rome, although no more a<br/>  Remnant were there of the old World's sole throne):<br/>The "_tout ensemble_" of his movements wore a<br/>  Grace of the soft Ideal, seldom shown,<br/>And ne'er to be described; for to the dolour<br/>Of bards and prosers, words are void of colour.<br/><br/>No marvel then he was a favourite;<br/>  A full-grown Cupid, very much admired;<br/>A little spoilt, but by no means so quite;<br/>  At least he kept his vanity retired.<br/>Such was his tact, he could alike delight<br/>  The chaste, and those who are not so much inspired.<br/>The Duchess of Fitz-Fulke, who loved _tracasserie_,<br/>Began to treat him with some small _agacerie_.<br/><br/>She was a fine and somewhat full-blown blonde,<br/>  Desirable, distinguished, celebrated<br/>For several winters in the grand, _grand Monde_:<br/>  I'd rather not say what might be related<br/>Of her exploits, for this were ticklish ground;<br/>  Besides there might be falsehood in what's stated:<br/>Her late performance had been a dead set<br/>At Lord Augustus Fitz-Plantagenet.<br/><br/>This noble personage began to look<br/>  A little black upon this new flirtation;<br/>But such small licences must lovers brook,<br/>  Mere freedoms of the female corporation.<br/>Woe to the man who ventures a rebuke!<br/>  'Twill but precipitate a situation<br/>Extremely disagreeable, but common<br/>To calculators when they count on Woman.<br/><br/>The circle smiled, then whispered, and then sneered;<br/>  The misses bridled, and the matrons frowned;<br/>Some hoped things might not turn out as they feared;<br/>  Some would not deem such women could be found;<br/>Some ne'er believed one half of what they heard;<br/>  Some looked perplexed, and others looked profound:<br/>And several pitied with sincere regret<br/>Poor Lord Augustus Fitz-Plantagenet.<br/><br/>But what is odd, none ever named the Duke,<br/>  Who, one might think, was something in the affair:<br/>True, he was absent, and, 'twas rumoured, took<br/>  But small concern about the when, or where,<br/>Or what his consort did: if he could brook<br/>  Her gaieties, none had a right to stare:<br/>Theirs was that best of unions, past all doubt,<br/>Which never meets, and therefore can't fall out.<br/><br/>But, oh! that I should ever pen so sad a line!<br/>  Fired with an abstract love of Virtue, she,<br/>My Dian of the Ephesians, Lady Adeline,<br/>  Began to think the Duchess' conduct free;<br/>Regretting much that she had chosen so bad a line,<br/>  And waxing chiller in her courtesy,<br/>Looked grave and pale to see her friend's fragility,<br/>For which most friends reserve their sensibility.<br/><br/>There's nought in this bad world like sympathy:<br/>  'Tis so becoming to the soul and face,<br/>Sets to soft music the harmonious sigh,<br/>  And robes sweet Friendship in a Brussels lace.<br/>Without a friend, what were Humanity,<br/>  To hunt our errors up with a good grace?<br/>Consoling us with--"Would you had thought twice!<br/>Ah! if you had but followed my advice!"<br/><br/>O Job! you had two friends: one's quite enough,<br/>  Especially when we are ill at ease;<br/>They're but bad pilots when the weather's rough,<br/>  Doctors less famous for their cures than fees.<br/>Let no man grumble when his friends fall off,<br/>  As they will do like leaves at the first breeze:<br/>When your affairs come round, one way or t' other,<br/>Go to the coffee-house, and take another.<br/><br/>But this is not my maxim: had it been,<br/>  Some heart-aches had been spared me: yet I care not--<br/>I would not be a tortoise in his screen<br/>  Of stubborn shell, which waves and weather wear not:<br/>'Tis better on the whole to have felt and seen<br/>  That which Humanity may bear, or bear not:<br/>'Twill teach discernment to the sensitive,<br/>And not to pour their Ocean in a sieve.<br/><br/>Of all the horrid, hideous notes of woe,<br/>  Sadder than owl-songs or the midnight blast,<br/>Is that portentous phrase, "I told you so,"<br/>  Uttered by friends, those prophets of the _past_,<br/>Who, 'stead of saying what you _now_ should do,<br/>  Own they foresaw that you would fall at last,<br/>And solace your slight lapse 'gainst _bonos mores_,<br/>With a long memorandum of old stories.<br/><br/>The Lady Adeline's serene severity<br/>  Was not confined to feeling for her friend,<br/>Whose fame she rather doubted with posterity,<br/>  Unless her habits should begin to mend:<br/>But Juan also shared in her austerity,<br/>  But mixed with pity, pure as e'er was penned<br/>His Inexperience moved her gentle ruth,<br/>And (as her junior by six weeks) his Youth.<br/><br/>These forty days' advantage of her years--<br/>  And hers were those which can face calculation,<br/>Boldly referring to the list of Peers<br/>  And noble births, nor dread the enumeration--<br/>Gave her a right to have maternal fears<br/>  For a young gentleman's fit education,<br/>Though she was far from that leap year, whose leap,<br/>In female dates, strikes Time all of a heap.<br/><br/>This may be fixed at somewhere before thirty--<br/>  Say seven-and-twenty; for I never knew<br/>The strictest in chronology and virtue<br/>  Advance beyond, while they could pass for new.<br/>O Time! why dost not pause? Thy scythe, so dirty<br/>  With rust, should surely cease to hack and hew:<br/>Reset it--shave more smoothly, also slower,<br/>If but to keep thy credit as a mower.<br/><br/>But Adeline was far from that ripe age,<br/>  Whose ripeness is but bitter at the best:<br/>'Twas rather her Experience made her sage,<br/>  For she had seen the World and stood its test,<br/>As I have said in--I forget what page;<br/>  My Muse despises reference, as you have guessed<br/>By this time;--but strike six from seven-and-twenty,<br/>And you will find her sum of years in plenty.<br/><br/>At sixteen she came out; presented, vaunted,<br/>  She put all coronets into commotion:<br/>At seventeen, too, the World was still enchanted<br/>  With the new Venus of their brilliant Ocean:<br/>At eighteen, though below her feet still panted<br/>  A Hecatomb of suitors with devotion,<br/>She had consented to create again<br/>That Adam, called "The happiest of Men."<br/><br/>Since then she had sparkled through three glowing winters,<br/>  Admired, adored; but also so correct,<br/>That she had puzzled all the acutest hinters,<br/>  Without the apparel of being circumspect:<br/>They could not even glean the slightest splinters<br/>  From off the marble, which had no defect.<br/>She had also snatched a moment since her marriage<br/>To bear a son and heir--and one miscarriage.<br/><br/>Fondly the wheeling fire-flies flew around her,<br/>  Those little glitterers of the London night;<br/>But none of these possessed a sting to wound her--<br/>  She was a pitch beyond a coxcomb's flight.<br/>Perhaps she wished an aspirant profounder;<br/>  But whatsoe'er she wished, she acted right;<br/>And whether Coldness, Pride, or Virtue dignify<br/>A Woman--so she's good--what _does_ it signify?<br/><br/>I hate a motive, like a lingering bottle<br/>  Which with the landlord makes too long a stand,<br/>Leaving all-claretless the unmoistened throttle,<br/>  Especially with politics on hand;<br/>I hate it, as I hate a drove of cattle,<br/>  Who whirl the dust as Simooms whirl the sand;<br/>I hate it as I hate an argument,<br/>A Laureate's Ode, or servile Peer's "Content."<br/><br/>'T is sad to hack into the roots of things,<br/>  They are so much intertwisted with the earth;<br/>So that the branch a goodly verdure flings,<br/>  I reck not if an acorn gave it birth.<br/>To trace all actions to their secret springs<br/>  Would make indeed some melancholy mirth:<br/>But this is not at present my concern,<br/>And I refer you to wise Oxenstiern.<br/><br/>With the kind view of saving an _√©clat_,<br/>  Both to the Duchess and Diplomatist,<br/>The Lady Adeline, as soon's she saw<br/>  That Juan was unlikely to resist--<br/>(For foreigners don't know that a _faux pas_<br/>  In England ranks quite on a different list<br/>From those of other lands unblest with juries,<br/>Whose verdict for such sin a certain cure is;--)<br/><br/>The Lady Adeline resolved to take<br/>  Such measures as she thought might best impede<br/>The farther progress of this sad mistake.<br/>  She thought with some simplicity indeed;<br/>But Innocence is bold even at the stake,<br/>  And simple in the World, and doth not need<br/>Nor use those palisades by dames erected,<br/>Whose virtue lies in never being detected.<br/><br/>It was not that she feared the very worst:<br/>  His Grace was an enduring, married man,<br/>And was not likely all at once to burst<br/>  Into a scene, and swell the clients' clan<br/>Of Doctors' Commons; but she dreaded first<br/>  The magic of her Grace's talisman,<br/>And next a quarrel (as he seemed to fret)<br/>With Lord Augustus Fitz-Plantagenet.<br/><br/>Her Grace, too, passed for being an _intrigante_,<br/>  And somewhat _m√©chante_ in her amorous sphere;<br/>One of those pretty, precious plagues, which haunt<br/>  A lover with caprices soft and dear,<br/>That like to _make_ a quarrel, when they can't<br/>  Find one, each day of the delightful year:<br/>Bewitching, torturing, as they freeze or glow,<br/>And--what is worst of all--won't let you go:<br/><br/>The sort of thing to turn a young man's head,<br/>  Or make a Werter of him in the end.<br/>No wonder then a purer soul should dread<br/>  This sort of chaste _liaison_ for a friend;<br/>It were much better to be wed or dead,<br/>  Than wear a heart a Woman loves to rend.<br/>'T is best to pause, and think, ere you rush on,<br/>If that a _bonne fortune_ be really _bonne_.<br/><br/>And first, in the overflowing of her heart,<br/>  Which really knew or thought it knew no guile,<br/>She called her husband now and then apart,<br/>  And bade him counsel Juan. With a smile<br/>Lord Henry heard her plans of artless art<br/>  To wean Don Juan from the Siren's wile;<br/>And answered, like a statesman or a prophet,<br/>In such guise that she could make nothing of it.<br/><br/>Firstly, he said, "he never interfered<br/>  In anybody's business but the King's:"<br/>Next, that "he never judged from what appeared,<br/>  Without strong reason, of those sort of things:"<br/>Thirdly, that "Juan had more brain than beard,<br/>  And was not to be held in leading strings;"<br/>And fourthly, what need hardly be said twice,<br/>"That good but rarely came from good advice."<br/><br/>And, therefore, doubtless to approve the truth<br/>  Of the last axiom, he advised his spouse<br/>To leave the parties to themselves, forsooth--<br/>  At least as far as _biens√©ance_ allows:<br/>That time would temper Juan's faults of youth;<br/>  That young men rarely made monastic vows;<br/>That Opposition only more attaches--<br/>But here a messenger brought in despatches:<br/><br/>And being of the council called "the Privy,"<br/>  Lord Henry walked into his cabinet,<br/>To furnish matter for some future Livy<br/>  To tell how he reduced the Nation's debt;<br/>And if their full contents I do not give ye,<br/>  It is because I do not know them yet;<br/>But I shall add them in a brief appendix,<br/>To come between mine Epic and its index.<br/><br/>But ere he went, he added a slight hint,<br/>  Another gentle common-place or two,<br/>Such as are coined in Conversation's mint,<br/>  And pass, for want of better, though not new:<br/>Then broke his packet, to see what was in 't,<br/>  And having casually glanced it through,<br/>Retired: and, as he went out, calmly kissed her,<br/>Less like a young wife than an ag√©d sister.<br/><br/>He was a cold, good, honourable man,<br/>  Proud of his birth, and proud of everything;<br/>A goodly spirit for a state Divan,<br/>  A figure fit to walk before a King;<br/>Tall, stately, formed to lead the courtly van<br/>  On birthdays, glorious with a star and string;<br/>The very model of a chamberlain--<br/>And such I mean to make him when I reign.<br/><br/>But there was something wanting on the whole--<br/>  I don't know what, and therefore cannot tell--<br/>Which pretty women--the sweet souls!--call _soul_.<br/>  _Certes_ it was not body; he was well<br/>Proportioned, as a poplar or a pole,<br/>  A handsome man, that human miracle;<br/>And in each circumstance of Love or War<br/>Had still preserved his perpendicular.<br/><br/>Still there was something wanting, as I've said--<br/>  That undefinable "_Je ne s√ßais quoi_"<br/>Which, for what I know, may of yore have led<br/>  To Homer's Iliad, since it drew to Troy<br/>The Greek Eve, Helen, from the Spartan's bed;<br/>  Though on the whole, no doubt, the Dardan boy<br/>Was much inferior to King Menela√ºs:--<br/>But thus it is some women will betray us.<br/><br/>There is an awkward thing which much perplexes,<br/>  Unless like wise Tiresias we had proved<br/>By turns the difference of the several sexes;<br/>  Neither can show quite _how_ they would be loved.<br/>The Sensual for a short time but connects us--<br/>  The Sentimental boasts to be unmoved;<br/>But both together form a kind of Centaur,<br/>Upon whose back 't is better not to venture.<br/><br/>A something all-sufficient for the _heart_<br/>  Is that for which the sex are always seeking:<br/>But how to fill up that same vacant part?<br/>  There lies the rub--and this they are but weak in.<br/>Frail mariners afloat without a chart,<br/>  They run before the wind through high seas breaking;<br/>And when they have made the shore through every shock,<br/>'T is odd--or odds--it may turn out a rock.<br/><br/>There is a flower called "Love in Idleness,"<br/>  For which see Shakespeare's ever-blooming garden;--<br/>I will not make his great description less,<br/>  And beg his British godship's humble pardon,<br/>If, in my extremity of rhyme's distress,<br/>  I touch a single leaf where he is warden;--<br/>But, though the flower is different, with the French<br/>Or Swiss Rousseau--cry _"Voil√† la Pervenche.'"_<br/><br/>Eureka! I have found it! What I mean<br/>  To say is, not that Love is Idleness,<br/>But that in Love such idleness has been<br/>  An accessory, as I have cause to guess.<br/>Hard Labour's an indifferent go-between;<br/>  Your men of business are not apt to express<br/>Much passion, since the merchant-ship, the Argo,<br/>Conveyed Medea as her supercargo.<br/><br/>_"Beatus ille procul!_" from "_negotiis,_"<br/>  Saith Horace; the great little poet's wrong;<br/>His other maxim, _"Noscitur √† sociis,"_<br/>  Is much more to the purpose of his song;<br/>Though even that were sometimes too ferocious,<br/>  Unless good company be kept too long;<br/>But, in his teeth, whate'er their state or station,<br/>Thrice happy they who _have_ an occupation!<br/><br/>Adam exchanged his Paradise for ploughing,<br/>  Eve made up millinery with fig leaves--<br/>The earliest knowledge from the Tree so knowing,<br/>  As far as I know, that the Church receives:<br/>And since that time it need not cost much showing,<br/>  That many of the ills o'er which Man grieves,<br/>And still more Women, spring from not employing<br/>Some hours to make the remnant worth enjoying.<br/><br/>And hence high life is oft a dreary void,<br/>  A rack of pleasures, where we must invent<br/>A something wherewithal to be annoyed.<br/>  Bards may sing what they please about _Content_;<br/>_Contented_, when translated, means but cloyed;<br/>  And hence arise the woes of Sentiment,<br/>Blue-devils--and Blue-stockings--and Romances<br/>Reduced to practice, and performed like dances.<br/><br/>I do declare, upon an affidavit,<br/>  Romances I ne'er read like those I have seen;<br/>Nor, if unto the World I ever gave it,<br/>  Would some believe that such a tale had been:<br/>But such intent I never had, nor have it;<br/>  Some truths are better kept behind a screen,<br/>Especially when they would look like lies;<br/>I therefore deal in generalities.<br/><br/>"An oyster may be crossed in love"--and why?<br/>  Because he mopeth idly in his shell,<br/>And heaves a lonely subterraqueous sigh,<br/>  Much as a monk may do within his cell:<br/>And _√†-propos_ of monks, their Piety<br/>  With Sloth hath found it difficult to dwell:<br/>Those vegetables of the Catholic creed<br/>Are apt exceedingly to run to seed.<br/><br/>O Wilberforce! thou man of black renown,<br/>  Whose merit none enough can sing or say,<br/>Thou hast struck one immense Colossus down,<br/>  Thou moral Washington of Africa!<br/>But there's another little thing, I own,<br/>  Which you should perpetrate some summer's day,<br/>And set the other half of Earth to rights;<br/>You have freed the _blacks_--now pray shut up the whites.<br/><br/>Shut up the bald-coot bully Alexander!<br/>  Ship off the Holy Three to Senegal;<br/>Teach them that "sauce for goose is sauce for gander,"<br/>  And ask them how _they_ like to be in thrall?<br/>Shut up each high heroic Salamander,<br/>  Who eats fire gratis (since the pay's but small);<br/>Shut up--no, _not_ the King, but the Pavilion,<br/>Or else 't will cost us all another million.<br/><br/>Shut up the World at large, let Bedlam out;<br/>  And you will be perhaps surprised to find<br/>All things pursue exactly the same route,<br/>  As now with those of _soi-disant_ sound mind.<br/>This I could prove beyond a single doubt,<br/>  Were there a jot of sense among Mankind;<br/>But till that _point d'appui_ is found, alas!<br/>Like Archimedes, I leave Earth as 't was.<br/><br/>Our gentle Adeline had one defect--<br/>  Her heart was vacant, though a splendid mansion;<br/>Her conduct had been perfectly correct,<br/>  As she had seen nought claiming its expansion.<br/>A wavering spirit may be easier wrecked,<br/>  Because 't is frailer, doubtless, than a staunch one;<br/>But when the latter works its own undoing,<br/>Its inner crash is like an Earthquake's ruin.<br/><br/>She loved her Lord, or thought so; but _that_ love<br/>  Cost her an effort, which is a sad toil,<br/>The stone of Sisyphus, if once we move<br/>  Our feelings 'gainst the nature of the soil.<br/>She had nothing to complain of, or reprove,<br/>  No bickerings, no connubial turmoil:<br/>Their union was a model to behold,<br/>Serene and noble,--conjugal, but cold.<br/><br/>There was no great disparity of years,<br/>  Though much in temper; but they never clashed:<br/>They moved like stars united in their spheres,<br/>  Or like the Rhone by Leman's waters washed,<br/>Where mingled and yet separate appears<br/>  The River from the Lake, all bluely dashed<br/>Through the serene and placid glassy deep,<br/>Which fain would lull its river-child to sleep.<br/><br/>Now when she once had ta'en an interest<br/>  In anything, however she might flatter<br/>Herself that her intentions were the best,<br/>  Intense intentions are a dangerous matter:<br/>Impressions were much stronger than she guessed,<br/>  And gathered as they run like growing water<br/>Upon her mind; the more so, as her breast<br/>Was not at first too readily impressed.<br/><br/>But when it was, she had that lurking Demon<br/>  Of double nature, and thus doubly named--<br/>Firmness yclept in Heroes, Kings, and seamen,<br/>  That is, when they succeed; but greatly blamed<br/>As _Obstinacy_, both in Men and Women,<br/>  Whene'er their triumph pales, or star is tamed:--<br/>And 't will perplex the casuist in morality<br/>To fix the due bounds of this dangerous quality.<br/><br/>Had Buonaparte won at Waterloo,<br/>  It had been firmness; now 't is pertinacity:<br/>Must the event decide between the two?<br/>  I leave it to your people of sagacity<br/>To draw the line between the false and true,<br/>  If such can e'er be drawn by Man's capacity:<br/>My business is with Lady Adeline,<br/>Who in her way too was a heroine.<br/><br/>She knew not her own heart; then how should I?<br/>  I think not she was _then_ in love with Juan:<br/>If so, she would have had the strength to fly<br/>  The wild sensation, unto her a new one:<br/>She merely felt a common sympathy<br/>  (I will not say it was a false or true one)<br/>In him, because she thought he was in danger,--<br/>Her husband's friend--her own--young--and a stranger.<br/><br/>She was, or thought she was, his friend--and this<br/>  Without the farce of Friendship, or romance<br/>Of Platonism, which leads so oft amiss<br/>  Ladies who have studied Friendship but in France<br/>Or Germany, where people _purely_ kiss.<br/>  To thus much Adeline would not advance;<br/>But of such friendship as Man's may to Man be<br/>She was as capable as Woman can be.<br/><br/>No doubt the secret influence of the Sex<br/>  Will there, as also in the ties of blood,<br/>An innocent predominance annex,<br/>  And tune the concord to a finer mood.<br/>If free from Passion, which all Friendship checks,<br/>  And your true feelings fully understood,<br/>No friend like to a woman Earth discovers,<br/>So that you have not been nor will be lovers.<br/><br/>Love bears within its breast the very germ<br/>  Of Change; and how should this be otherwise?<br/>That violent things more quickly find a term<br/>  Is shown through Nature's whole analogies;<br/>And how should the most fierce of all be firm?<br/>  Would you have endless lightning in the skies?<br/>Methinks Love's very title says enough:<br/>How should "the _tender_ passion" e'er be _tough?_<br/><br/>Alas! by all experience, seldom yet<br/>  (I merely quote what I have heard from many)<br/>Had lovers not some reason to regret<br/>  The passion which made Solomon a zany.<br/>I've also seen some wives (not to forget<br/>  The marriage state, the best or worst of any)<br/>Who were the very paragons of wives,<br/>Yet made the misery of at least two lives.<br/><br/>I've also seen some female _friends_ ('t is odd,<br/>  But true--as, if expedient, I could prove)<br/>That faithful were through thick and thin, abroad,<br/>  At home, far more than ever yet was Love--<br/>Who did not quit me when Oppression trod<br/>  Upon me; whom no scandal could remove;<br/>Who fought, and fight, in absence, too, my battles,<br/>Despite the snake Society's loud rattles.<br/><br/>Whether Don Juan and chaste Adeline<br/>  Grew friends in this or any other sense,<br/>Will be discussed hereafter, I opine:<br/>  At present I am glad of a pretence<br/>To leave them hovering, as the effect is fine,<br/>  And keeps the atrocious reader in _suspense_;<br/>The surest way--for ladies and for books--<br/>To bait their tender--or their tenter--hooks.<br/><br/>Whether they rode, or walked, or studied Spanish,<br/>  To read Don Quixote in the original,<br/>A pleasure before which all others vanish;<br/>  Whether their talk was of the kind called "small,"<br/>Or serious, are the topics I must banish<br/>  To the next Canto; where perhaps I shall<br/>Say something to the purpose, and display<br/>Considerable talent in my way.<br/><br/>Above all, I beg all men to forbear<br/>  Anticipating aught about the matter:<br/>They'll only make mistakes about the fair,<br/>  And Juan, too, especially the latter.<br/>And I shall take a much more serious air<br/>  Than I have yet done, in this Epic Satire.<br/>It is not clear that Adeline and Juan<br/>Will fall; but if they do, 't will be their ruin.<br/><br/>But great things spring from little:--Would you think,<br/>  That in our youth, as dangerous a passion<br/>As e'er brought Man and Woman to the brink<br/>  Of ruin, rose from such a slight occasion,<br/>As few would ever dream could form the link<br/>  Of such a sentimental situation?<br/>You'll never guess, I'll bet you millions, milliards--<br/>It all sprung from a harmless game at billiards.<br/><br/>'T is strange,--but true; for Truth is always strange--<br/>  Stranger than fiction: if it could be told,<br/>How much would novels gain by the exchange!<br/>  How differently the World would men behold!<br/>How oft would Vice and Virtue places change!<br/>  The new world would be nothing to the old,<br/>If some Columbus of the moral seas<br/>Would show mankind their Souls' antipodes.<br/><br/>What "antres vast and deserts idle," then,<br/>  Would be discovered in the human soul!<br/>What icebergs in the hearts of mighty men,<br/>  With self-love in the centre as their Pole!<br/>What Anthropophagi are nine of ten<br/>  Of those who hold the kingdoms in control!<br/>Were things but only called by their right name,<br/>C√¶sar himself would be ashamed of Fame.<br/><br/>CANTO THE FIFTEENTH.<br/><br/>AH!--What should follow slips from my reflection;<br/>  Whatever follows ne'ertheless may be<br/>As √†-propos of Hope or Retrospection,<br/>  As though the lurking thought had followed free.<br/>All present life is but an Interjection,<br/>  An "Oh!" or "Ah!" of Joy or Misery,<br/>Or a "Ha! ha!" or "Bah!"--a yawn, or "Pooh!"<br/>Of which perhaps the latter is most true.<br/><br/>But, more or less, the whole's a Syncop√©<br/>  Or a _Singultus_--emblems of Emotion,<br/>The grand Antithesis to great _Ennui_,<br/>  Wherewith we break our bubbles on the Ocean--<br/>That Watery Outline of Eternity,<br/>  Or miniature, at least, as is my notion--<br/>Which ministers unto the Soul's delight,<br/>In seeing matters which are out of sight.<br/><br/>But all are better than the sigh suppressed,<br/>  Corroding in the cavern of the heart,<br/>Making the countenance a masque of rest<br/>  And turning Human Nature to an art.<br/>Few men dare show their thoughts of worst or best;<br/>  Dissimulation always sets apart<br/>A corner for herself; and, therefore, Fiction<br/>Is that which passes with least contradiction.<br/><br/>Ah! who can tell? Or rather, who can not<br/>  Remember, without telling, Passion's errors?<br/>The drainer of Oblivion, even the sot,<br/>  Hath got _blue devils_ for his morning mirrors:<br/>What though on Lethe's stream he seem to float,<br/>  He cannot sink his tremours or his terrors;<br/>The ruby glass that shakes within his hand<br/>Leaves a sad sediment of Time's worst sand.<br/><br/>And as for Love--O Love!--We will proceed:--<br/>  The Lady Adeline Amundeville,<br/>A pretty name as one would wish to read,<br/>  Must perch harmonious on my tuneful quill.<br/>There's Music in the sighing of a reed;<br/>  There's Music in the gushing of a rill;<br/>There's Music in all things, if men had ears:<br/>Their Earth is but an echo of the Spheres.<br/><br/>The Lady Adeline, Right Honourable,<br/>  And honoured, ran a risk of growing less so;<br/>For few of the soft sex are very stable<br/>  In their resolves--alas! that I should say so;<br/>They differ as wine differs from its label,<br/>  When once decanted;--I presume to guess so,<br/>But will not swear: yet both upon occasion,<br/>Till old, may undergo adulteration.<br/><br/>But Adeline was of the purest vintage,<br/>  The unmingled essence of the grape; and yet<br/>Bright as a new napoleon from its mintage,<br/>  Or glorious as a diamond richly set;<br/>A page where Time should hesitate to print age,<br/>  And for which Nature might forego her debt--<br/>Sole creditor whose process doth involve in 't<br/>The luck of finding everybody solvent.<br/><br/>O Death! thou dunnest of all duns! thou daily<br/>  Knockest at doors, at first with modest tap,<br/>Like a meek tradesman when approaching palely<br/>  Some splendid debtor he would take by sap:<br/>But oft denied, as Patience 'gins to fail, he<br/>  Advances with exasperated rap,<br/>And (if let in) insists, in terms unhandsome,<br/>On ready money, or "a draft on Ransom."<br/><br/>Whate'er thou takest, spare awhile poor Beauty!<br/>  She is so rare, and thou hast so much prey.<br/>What though she now and then may slip from duty,<br/>  The more's the reason why you ought to stay;<br/>Gaunt Gourmand! with whole nations for your booty,--<br/>  You should be civil in a modest way:<br/>Suppress, then, some slight feminine diseases,<br/>And take as many heroes as Heaven pleases.<br/><br/>Fair Adeline, the more ingenuous<br/>  Where she was interested (as was said),<br/>Because she was not apt, like some of us,<br/>  To like too readily, or too high bred<br/>To show it--(points we need not now discuss)--<br/>  Would give up artlessly both Heart and Head<br/>Unto such feelings as seemed innocent,<br/>For objects worthy of the sentiment.<br/><br/>Some parts of Juan's history, which Rumour,<br/>  That live Gazette, had scattered to disfigure,<br/>She had heard; but Women hear with more good humour<br/>  Such aberrations than we men of rigour:<br/>Besides, his conduct, since in England, grew more<br/>  Strict, and his mind assumed a manlier vigour:<br/>Because he had, like Alcibiades,<br/>The art of living in all climes with ease.<br/><br/>His manner was perhaps the more seductive,<br/>  Because he ne'er seemed anxious to seduce;<br/>Nothing affected, studied, or constructive<br/>  Of coxcombry or conquest: no abuse<br/>Of his attractions marred the fair perspective,<br/>  To indicate a Cupidon broke loose,<br/>And seem to say, "Resist us if you can"--<br/>Which makes a Dandy while it spoils a Man.<br/><br/>They are wrong--that's not the way to set about it;<br/>  As, if they told the truth, could well be shown.<br/>But, right or wrong, Don Juan was without it;<br/>  In fact, his manner was his own alone:<br/>Sincere he was--at least you could not doubt it,<br/>  In listening merely to his voice's tone.<br/>The Devil hath not in all his quiver's choice<br/>An arrow for the Heart like a sweet voice.<br/><br/>By nature soft, his whole address held off<br/>  Suspicion: though not timid, his regard<br/>Was such as rather seemed to keep aloof,<br/>  To shield himself than put _you_ on your guard:<br/>Perhaps 't was hardly quite assured enough,<br/>  But Modesty's at times its own reward,<br/>Like Virtue; and the absence of pretension<br/>Will go much farther than there's need to mention.<br/><br/>Serene, accomplished, cheerful but not loud;<br/>  Insinuating without insinuation;<br/>Observant of the foibles of the crowd,<br/>  Yet ne'er betraying this in conversation;<br/>Proud with the proud, yet courteously proud,<br/>  So as to make them feel he knew his station<br/>And theirs:--without a struggle for priority,<br/>He neither brooked nor claimed superiority--<br/><br/>That is, with Men: with Women he was what<br/>  They pleased to make or take him for; and their<br/>Imagination's quite enough for that:<br/>  So that the outline's tolerably fair,<br/>They fill the canvas up--and _"verbum sat."_<br/>  If once their phantasies be brought to bear<br/>Upon an object, whether sad or playful,<br/>They can transfigure brighter than a Raphael.<br/><br/>Adeline, no deep judge of character,<br/>  Was apt to add a colouring from her own:<br/>'T is thus the Good will amiably err,<br/>  And eke the Wise, as has been often shown.<br/>Experience is the chief philosopher,<br/>  But saddest when his science is well known:<br/>And persecuted Sages teach the Schools<br/>Their folly in forgetting there are fools.<br/><br/>Was it not so, great Locke? and greater Bacon?<br/>  Great Socrates? And thou, Diviner still,<br/>Whose lot it is by Man to be mistaken,<br/>  And thy pure creed made sanction of all ill?<br/>Redeeming Worlds to be by bigots shaken,<br/>  How was thy toil rewarded? We might fill<br/>Volumes with similar sad illustrations,<br/>But leave them to the conscience of the nations.<br/><br/>I perch upon an humbler promontory,<br/>  Amidst Life's infinite variety:<br/>With no great care for what is nicknamed Glory,<br/>  But speculating as I cast mine eye<br/>On what may suit or may not suit my story,<br/>  And never straining hard to versify,<br/>I rattle on exactly as I'd talk<br/>With anybody in a ride or walk.<br/><br/>I don't know that there may be much ability<br/>  Shown in this sort of desultory rhyme;<br/>But there's a conversational facility,<br/>  Which may round off an hour upon a time.<br/>Of this I'm sure at least, there's no servility<br/>  In mine irregularity of chime,<br/>Which rings what's uppermost of new or hoary,<br/>Just as I feel the _Improvvisatore_.<br/><br/>"_Omnia vult_ belle _Matho dicere_--_dic aliquando_<br/>  _Et_ bene, _dic_ neutrum, _dic aliquando_ male."<br/>The first is rather more than mortal can do;<br/>  The second may be sadly done or gaily;<br/>The third is still more difficult to stand to;<br/>  The fourth we hear, and see, and say too, daily:<br/>The whole together is what I could wish<br/>To serve in this conundrum of a dish.<br/><br/>A modest hope--but Modesty's my forte,<br/>  And Pride my feeble:--let us ramble on.<br/>I meant to make this poem very short,<br/>  But now I can't tell where it may not run.<br/>No doubt, if I had wished to pay my court<br/>  To critics, or to hail the _setting_ sun<br/>Of Tyranny of all kinds, my concision<br/>Were more;--but I was born for opposition.<br/><br/>But then 't is mostly on the weaker side;<br/>  So that I verily believe if they<br/>Who now are basking in their full-blown pride<br/>  Were shaken down, and "dogs had had their day,"<br/>Though at the first I might perchance deride<br/>  Their tumble, I should turn the other way,<br/>And wax an ultra-royalist in Loyalty,<br/>Because I hate even democratic Royalty.<br/><br/>I think I should have made a decent spouse,<br/>  If I had never proved the soft condition;<br/>I think I should have made monastic vows<br/>  But for my own peculiar superstition:<br/>'Gainst rhyme I never should have knocked my brows,<br/>  Nor broken my own head, nor that of Priscian,<br/>Nor worn the motley mantle of a poet,<br/>If some one had not told me to forego it.<br/><br/>But _laissez aller_--Knights and Dames I sing,<br/>  Such as the times may furnish. 'T is a flight<br/>Which seems at first to need no lofty wing,<br/>  Plumed by Longinus or the Stagyrite:<br/>The difficulty lies in colouring<br/>  (Keeping the due proportions still in sight)<br/>With Nature manners which are artificial,<br/>And rend'ring general that which is especial.<br/><br/>The difference is, that in the days of old<br/>  Men made the Manners; Manners now make men--<br/>Pinned like a flock, and fleeced too in their fold,<br/>  At least nine, and a ninth beside of ten.<br/>Now this at all events must render cold<br/>  Your writers, who must either draw again<br/>Days better drawn before, or else assume<br/>The present, with their common-place costume.<br/><br/>We'll do our best to make the best on 't:--March!<br/>  March, my Muse! If you cannot fly, yet flutter;<br/>And when you may not be sublime, be arch,<br/>  Or starch, as are the edicts statesmen utter.<br/>We surely may find something worth research:<br/>  Columbus found a new world in a cutter,<br/>Or brigantine, or pink, of no great tonnage,<br/>While yet America was in her non-age.<br/><br/>When Adeline, in all her growing sense<br/>  Of Juan's merits and his situation,<br/>Felt on the whole an interest intense,--<br/>  Partly perhaps because a fresh sensation,<br/>Or that he had an air of innocence,<br/>  Which is for Innocence a sad temptation,--<br/>As Women hate half measures, on the whole,<br/>She 'gan to ponder how to save his soul.<br/><br/>She had a good opinion of Advice,<br/>  Like all who give and eke receive it gratis,<br/>For which small thanks are still the market price,<br/>  Even where the article at highest rate is:<br/>She thought upon the subject twice or thrice,<br/>  And morally decided--the best state is<br/>For Morals--Marriage; and, this question carried,<br/>She seriously advised him to get married.<br/><br/>Juan replied, with all becoming deference,<br/>  He had a predilection for that tie;<br/>But that, at present, with immediate reference<br/>  To his own circumstances, there might lie<br/>Some difficulties, as in his own preference,<br/>  Or that of her to whom he might apply:<br/>That still he'd wed with such or such a lady,<br/>If that they were not married all already.<br/><br/>Next to the making matches for herself,<br/>  And daughters, brothers, sisters, kith or kin,<br/>Arranging them like books on the same shelf,<br/>  There's nothing women love to dabble in<br/>More (like a stock-holder in growing pelf)<br/>  Than match-making in general: 't is no sin<br/>Certes, but a preventative, and therefore<br/>That is, no doubt, the only reason wherefore.<br/><br/>But never yet (except of course a miss<br/>  Unwed, or mistress never to be wed,<br/>Or wed already, who object to this)<br/>  Was there chaste dame who had not in her head<br/>Some drama of the marriage Unities,<br/>  Observed as strictly both at board and bed,<br/>As those of Aristotle, though sometimes<br/>They turn out Melodrames or Pantomimes.<br/><br/>They generally have some only son,<br/>  Some heir to a large property, some friend<br/>Of an old family, some gay Sir John,<br/>  Or grave Lord George, with whom perhaps might end<br/>A line, and leave Posterity undone,<br/>  Unless a marriage was applied to mend<br/>The prospect and their morals: and besides,<br/>They have at hand a blooming glut of brides.<br/><br/>From these they will be careful to select,<br/>  For this an heiress, and for that a beauty;<br/>For one a songstress who hath no defect,<br/>  For t' other one who promises much duty;<br/>For this a lady no one can reject,<br/>  Whose sole accomplishments were quite a booty;<br/>A second for her excellent connections;<br/>A third, because there can be no objections.<br/><br/>When Rapp the Harmonist embargoed Marriage<br/>  In his harmonious settlement--(which flourishes<br/>Strangely enough as yet without miscarriage,<br/>  Because it breeds no more mouths than it nourishes,<br/>Without those sad expenses which disparage<br/>  What Nature naturally most encourages)--<br/>Why called he "Harmony" a state sans wedlock?<br/>Now here I've got the preacher at a dead lock.<br/><br/>Because he either meant to sneer at Harmony<br/>  Or Marriage, by divorcing them thus oddly.<br/>But whether reverend Rapp learned this in Germany<br/>  Or no, 't is said his sect is rich and godly,<br/>Pious and pure, beyond what I can term any<br/>  Of ours, although they propagate more broadly.<br/>My objection's to his title, not his ritual.<br/>Although I wonder how it grew habitual.<br/><br/>But Rapp is the reverse of zealous matrons,<br/>  Who favour, _malgr√©_ Malthus, Generation--<br/>Professors of that genial art, and patrons<br/>  Of all the modest part of Propagation;<br/>Which after all at such a desperate rate runs,<br/>  That half its produce tends to Emigration,<br/>That sad result of passions and potatoes--<br/>Two weeds which pose our economic Catos.<br/><br/>Had Adeline read Malthus? I can't tell;<br/>  I wish she had: his book's the eleventh commandment,<br/>Which says, "Thou shall not marry," unless _well_:<br/>  This he (as far as I can understand) meant.<br/>'T is not my purpose on his views to dwell,<br/>  Nor canvass what "so eminent a hand" meant;<br/>But, certes, it conducts to lives ascetic,<br/>Or turning Marriage into Arithmetic.<br/><br/>But Adeline, who probably presumed<br/>  That Juan had enough of maintenance,<br/>Or _separate_ maintenance, in case 't was doomed--<br/>  As on the whole it is an even chance<br/>That bridegrooms, after they are fairly _groomed_,<br/>  May retrograde a little in the Dance<br/>Of Marriage--(which might form a painter's fame,<br/>Like Holbein's "Dance of Death"--but 't is the same)--<br/><br/>But Adeline determined Juan's wedding<br/>  In her own mind, and that's enough for Woman:<br/>But then, with whom? There was the sage Miss Reading,<br/>  Miss Raw, Miss Flaw, Miss Showman, and Miss Knowman,<br/>And the two fair co-heiresses Giltbedding.<br/>  She deemed his merits something more than common:<br/>All these were unobjectionable matches,<br/>And might go on, if well wound up, like watches.<br/><br/>There was Miss Millpond, smooth as summer's sea,<br/>  That usual paragon, an only daughter,<br/>Who seemed the cream of Equanimity,<br/>  Till skimmed--and then there was some milk and water,<br/>With a slight shade of blue too, it might be,<br/>  Beneath the surface; but what did it matter?<br/>Love's riotous, but Marriage should have quiet,<br/>And being consumptive, live on a milk diet.<br/><br/>And then there was the Miss Audacia Shoestring,<br/>  A dashing _demoiselle_ of good estate,<br/>Whose heart was fixed upon a star or blue string;<br/>  But whether English Dukes grew rare of late,<br/>Or that she had not harped upon the true string,<br/>  By which such Sirens can attract our great,<br/>She took up with some foreign younger brother,<br/>A Russ or Turk--the one's as good as t' other.<br/><br/>And then there was--but why should I go on,<br/>  Unless the ladies should go off?--there was<br/>Indeed a certain fair and fairy one,<br/>  Of the best class, and better than her class,--<br/>Aurora Raby, a young star who shone<br/>  O'er Life, too sweet an image for such glass,<br/>A lovely being, scarcely formed or moulded,<br/>A rose with all its sweetest leaves yet folded;<br/><br/>Rich, noble, but an orphan--left an only<br/>  Child to the care of guardians good and kind--<br/>But still her aspect had an air so lonely;<br/>  Blood is not water; and where shall we find<br/>Feelings of Youth like those which overthrown lie<br/>  By Death, when we are left, alas! behind,<br/>To feel, in friendless palaces, a home<br/>Is wanting, and our best ties in the tomb?<br/><br/>Early in years, and yet more infantine<br/>  In figure, she had something of Sublime<br/>In eyes which sadly shone, as Seraphs' shine.<br/>  All Youth--but with an aspect beyond Time;<br/>Radiant and grave--as pitying Man's decline;<br/>  Mournful--but mournful of another's crime,<br/>She looked as if she sat by Eden's door,<br/>And grieved for those who could return no more.<br/><br/>She was a Catholic, too, sincere, austere,<br/>  As far as her own gentle heart allowed,<br/>And deemed that fallen worship far more dear<br/>  Perhaps because 't was fallen: her Sires were proud<br/>Of deeds and days when they had filled the ear<br/>  Of nations, and had never bent or bowed<br/>To novel power; and as she was the last,<br/>She held their old faith and old feelings fast.<br/><br/>She gazed upon a World she scarcely knew,<br/>  As seeking not to know it; silent, lone,<br/>As grows a flower, thus quietly she grew,<br/>  And kept her heart serene within its zone.<br/>There was awe in the homage which she drew;<br/>  Her Spirit seemed as seated on a throne<br/>Apart from the surrounding world, and strong<br/>In its own strength--most strange in one so young!<br/><br/>Now it so happened, in the catalogue<br/>  Of Adeline, Aurora was omitted,<br/>Although her birth and wealth had given her vogue,<br/>  Beyond the charmers we have already cited;<br/>Her beauty also seemed to form no clog<br/>  Against her being mentioned as well fitted,<br/>By many virtues, to be worth the trouble<br/>Of single gentlemen who would be double.<br/><br/>And this omission, like that of the bust<br/>  Of Brutus at the pageant of Tiberius,<br/>Made Juan wonder, as no doubt he must.<br/>  This he expressed half smiling and half serious;<br/>When Adeline replied with some disgust,<br/>  And with an air, to say the least, imperious,<br/>She marvelled "what he saw in such a baby<br/>As that prim, silent, cold Aurora Raby?"<br/><br/>Juan rejoined--"She was a Catholic,<br/>  And therefore fittest, as of his persuasion;<br/>Since he was sure his mother would fall sick,<br/>  And the Pope thunder excommunication,<br/>If--" But here Adeline, who seemed to pique<br/>  Herself extremely on the inoculation<br/>Of others with her own opinions, stated--<br/>As usual--the same reason which she late did.<br/><br/>And wherefore not? A reasonable reason,<br/>  If good, is none the worse for repetition;<br/>If bad, the best way's certainly to tease on,<br/>  And amplify: you lose much by concision,<br/>Whereas insisting in or out of season<br/>  Convinces all men, even a politician;<br/>Or--what is just the same--it wearies out.<br/>So the end's gained, what signifies the route?<br/><br/>_Why_ Adeline had this slight prejudice--<br/>  For prejudice it was--against a creature<br/>As pure, as Sanctity itself, from Vice,--<br/>  With all the added charm of form and feature,--<br/>For me appears a question far too nice,<br/>  Since Adeline was liberal by nature;<br/>But Nature's Nature, and has more caprices<br/>Than I have time, or will, to take to pieces.<br/><br/>Perhaps she did not like the quiet way<br/>  With which Aurora on those baubles looked,<br/>Which charm most people in their earlier day:<br/>  For there are few things by Mankind less brooked,<br/>And Womankind too, if we so may say,<br/>  Than finding thus their genius stand rebuked,<br/>Like "Antony's by C√¶sar," by the few<br/>Who look upon them as they ought to do.<br/><br/>It was not envy--Adeline had none;<br/>  Her place was far beyond it, and her mind:<br/>It was not scorn--which could not light on one<br/>  Whose greatest _fault_ was leaving few to find:<br/>It was not jealousy, I think--but shun<br/>  Following the _ignes fatui_ of Mankind:<br/>It was not----but 't is easier far, alas!<br/>To say what it was _not_ than what it was.<br/><br/>Little Aurora deemed she was the theme<br/>  Of such discussion. She was there a guest;<br/>A beauteous ripple of the brilliant stream<br/>  Of Rank and Youth, though purer than the rest,<br/>Which flowed on for a moment in the beam<br/>  Time sheds a moment o'er each sparkling crest.<br/>Had she known this, she would have calmly smiled--<br/>She had so much, or little, of the child.<br/><br/>The dashing and proud air of Adeline<br/>  Imposed not upon her: she saw her blaze<br/>Much as she would have seen a glow-worm shine,<br/>  Then turned unto the stars for loftier rays.<br/>Juan was something she could not divine,<br/>  Being no Sibyl in the new world's ways;<br/>Yet she was nothing dazzled by the meteor,<br/>Because she did not pin her faith on feature.<br/><br/>His fame too,--for he had that kind of fame<br/>  Which sometimes plays the deuce with Womankind,<br/>A heterogeneous mass of glorious blame,<br/>  Half virtues and whole vices being combined;<br/>Faults which attract because they are not tame;<br/>  Follies tricked out so brightly that they blind:--<br/>These seals upon her wax made no impression,<br/>Such was her coldness or her self-possession.<br/><br/>Juan knew nought of such a character--<br/>  High, yet resembling not his lost Haid√©e;<br/>Yet each was radiant in her proper sphere:<br/>  The island girl, bred up by the lone sea,<br/>More warm, as lovely, and not less sincere,<br/>  Was Nature's all: Aurora could not be,<br/>Nor would be thus:--the difference in them<br/>Was such as lies between a flower and gem.<br/><br/>Having wound up with this sublime comparison,<br/>  Methinks we may proceed upon our narrative,<br/>And, as my friend Scott says, "I sound my warison;"<br/>  Scott, the superlative of my comparative--<br/>Scott, who can paint your Christian knight or Saracen,<br/>  Serf--Lord--Man,
