with such skill as none would share it, if<br/>There had not been one Shakespeare and Voltaire,<br/>Of one or both of whom he seems the heir.<br/><br/>I say, in my slight way I may proceed<br/>  To play upon the surface of Humanity.<br/>I write the World, nor care if the World read,<br/>  At least for this I cannot spare its vanity.<br/>My Muse hath bred, and still perhaps may breed<br/>  More foes by this same scroll: when I began it, I<br/>Thought that it might turn out so--_now I know it_,<br/>But still I am, or was, a pretty poet.<br/><br/>The conference or congress (for it ended<br/>  As Congresses of late do) of the Lady<br/>Adeline and Don Juan rather blended<br/>  Some acids with the sweets--for she was heady;<br/>But, ere the matter could be marred or mended,<br/>  The silvery bell rang, not for "dinner ready,"<br/>But for that hour, called half-hour, given to dress,<br/>Though ladies' robes seem scant enough for less.<br/><br/>Great things were now to be achieved at table,<br/>  With massy plate for armour, knives and forks<br/>For weapons; but what Muse since Homer's able<br/>  (His feasts are not the worst part of his works)<br/>To draw up in array a single day-bill<br/>  Of modern dinners? where more mystery lurks,<br/>In soups or sauces, or a sole _rago√∫t_,<br/>Than witches, b--ches, or physicians, brew.<br/><br/>There was a goodly "soupe √† la _bonne femme_"<br/>  Though God knows whence it came from; there was, too,<br/>A turbot for relief of those who cram,<br/>  Relieved with "dindon √† la P√©rigeux;"<br/>There also was----the sinner that I am!<br/>  How shall I get this gourmand stanza through?--<br/>"Soupe √† la Beauveau," whose relief was dory,<br/>Relieved itself by pork, for greater glory.<br/><br/>But I must crowd all into one grand mess<br/>  Or mass; for should I stretch into detail,<br/>My Muse would run much more into excess,<br/>  Than when some squeamish people deem her frail;<br/>But though a _bonne vivante_, I must confess<br/>  Her stomach's not her peccant part; this tale<br/>However doth require some slight refection,<br/>Just to relieve her spirits from dejection.<br/><br/>Fowls "√† la Cond√©," slices eke of salmon,<br/>  With "sauces G√©nevoises," and haunch of venison;<br/>Wines too, which might again have slain young Ammon--<br/>  A man like whom I hope we sha'n't see many soon;<br/>They also set a glazed Westphalian ham on,<br/>  Whereon Apicius would bestow his benison;<br/>And then there was champagne with foaming whirls,<br/>As white as Cleopatra's melted pearls.<br/><br/>Then there was God knows what "√† l'Allemande,"<br/>  "A l'Espagnole," "timballe," and "salpicon"--<br/>With things I can't withstand or understand,<br/>  Though swallowed with much zest upon the whole;<br/>And _"entremets"_ to piddle with at hand,<br/>  Gently to lull down the subsiding soul;<br/>While great Lucullus' _Robe triumphal_ muffles--<br/>(_There's fame_)--young partridge fillets, decked with truffles.<br/><br/>What are the _fillets_ on the Victor's brow<br/>  To these? They are rags or dust. Where is the arch<br/>Which nodded to the nation's spoils below?<br/>  Where the triumphal chariots' haughty march?<br/>Gone to where Victories must like dinners go.<br/>  Farther I shall not follow the research:<br/>But oh! ye modern Heroes with your cartridges,<br/>When will your names lend lustre e'en to partridges?<br/><br/>Those truffles too are no bad accessaries,<br/>  Followed by "petits puits d'amour"--a dish<br/>Of which perhaps the cookery rather varies,<br/>  So every one may dress it to his wish,<br/>According to the best of dictionaries,<br/>  Which encyclopedize both flesh and fish;<br/>But even, sans _confitures_, it no less true is,<br/>There's pretty picking in those _petits puits_.<br/><br/>The mind is lost in mighty contemplation<br/>  Of intellect expanded on two courses;<br/>And Indigestion's grand multiplication<br/>  Requires arithmetic beyond my forces.<br/>Who would suppose, from Adam's simple ration,<br/>  That cookery could have called forth such resources,<br/>As form a science and a nomenclature<br/>From out the commonest demands of Nature?<br/><br/>The glasses jingled, and the palates tingled;<br/>  The diners of celebrity dined well;<br/>The ladies with more moderation mingled<br/>  In the feast, pecking less than I can tell;<br/>Also the younger men too: for a springald<br/>  Can't, like ripe Age, in _gourmandise_ excel,<br/>But thinks less of good eating than the whisper<br/>(When seated next him) of some pretty lisper.<br/><br/>Alas! I must leave undescribed the _gibier_,<br/>  The _salmi_, the _consomm√©_, the _pur√©e_,<br/>All which I use to make my rhymes run glibber<br/>  Than could roast beef in our rough John Bull way:<br/>I must not introduce even a spare rib here,<br/>  "Bubble and squeak" would spoil my liquid lay:<br/>But I have dined, and must forego, alas!<br/>The chaste description even of a "b√©casse;"<br/><br/>And fruits, and ice, and all that Art refines<br/>  From Nature for the service of the _go√ªt_--<br/>_Taste_ or the _gout_,--pronounce it as inclines<br/>  Your stomach! Ere you dine, the French will do;<br/>But _after_, there are sometimes certain signs<br/>  Which prove plain English truer of the two.<br/>Hast ever _had_ the _gout_? I have not had it--<br/>But I may have, and you too, reader, dread it.<br/><br/>The simple olives, best allies of wine,<br/>  Must I pass over in my bill of fare?<br/>I must, although a favourite _plat_ of mine<br/>  In Spain, and Lucca, Athens, everywhere:<br/>On them and bread 'twas oft my luck to dine--<br/>  The grass my table-cloth, in open air,<br/>On Sunium or Hymettus, like Diogenes,<br/>Of whom half my philosophy the progeny is.<br/><br/>Amidst this tumult of fish, flesh, and fowl,<br/>  And vegetables, all in masquerade,<br/>The guests were placed according to their roll,<br/>  But various as the various meats displayed:<br/>Don Juan sat next an "√† l'Espagnole"--<br/>  No damsel, but a dish, as hath been said;<br/>But so far like a lady, that 'twas drest<br/>Superbly, and contained a world of zest.<br/><br/>By some odd chance too, he was placed between<br/>  Aurora and the Lady Adeline--<br/>A situation difficult, I ween,<br/>  For man therein, with eyes and heart, to dine.<br/>Also the conference which we have seen<br/>  Was not such as to encourage him to shine,<br/>For Adeline, addressing few words to him,<br/>With two transcendent eyes seemed to look through him.<br/><br/>I sometimes almost think that eyes have ears:<br/>  This much is sure, that, out of earshot, things<br/>Are somehow echoed to the pretty dears,<br/>  Of which I can't tell whence their knowledge springs.<br/>Like that same mystic music of the spheres,<br/>  Which no one hears, so loudly though it rings,<br/>'Tis wonderful how oft the sex have heard<br/>Long dialogues--which passed without a word!<br/><br/>Aurora sat with that indifference<br/>  Which piques a _preux chevalier_--as it ought:<br/>Of all offences that's the worst offence,<br/>  Which seems to hint you are not worth a thought.<br/>Now Juan, though no coxcomb in pretence,<br/>  Was not exactly pleased to be so caught;<br/>Like a good ship entangled among ice--<br/>And after so much excellent advice.<br/><br/>To his gay nothings, nothing was replied,<br/>  Or something which was nothing, as Urbanity<br/>Required. Aurora scarcely looked aside,<br/>  Nor even smiled enough for any vanity.<br/>The Devil was in the girl! Could it be pride?<br/>  Or modesty, or absence, or inanity?<br/>Heaven knows! But Adeline's malicious eyes<br/>Sparkled with her successful prophecies,<br/><br/>And looked as much as if to say, "I said it;"<br/>  A kind of triumph I'll not recommend,<br/>Because it sometimes, as I have seen or read it,<br/>  Both in the case of lover and of friend,<br/>Will pique a gentleman, for his own credit,<br/>  To bring what was a jest to a serious end:<br/>For all men prophesy what _is_ or _was_,<br/>And hate those who won't let them come to pass.<br/><br/>Juan was drawn thus into some attentions,<br/>  Slight but select, and just enough to express,<br/>To females of perspicuous comprehensions,<br/>  That he would rather make them more than less.<br/>Aurora at the last (so history mentions,<br/>  Though probably much less a fact than guess)<br/>So far relaxed her thoughts from their sweet prison,<br/>As once or twice to smile, if not to listen.<br/><br/>From answering she began to question: this<br/>  With her was rare; and Adeline, who as yet<br/>Thought her predictions went not much amiss,<br/>  Began to dread she'd thaw to a coquette--<br/>So very difficult, they say, it is<br/>  To keep extremes from meeting, when once set<br/>In motion; but she here too much refined--<br/>Aurora's spirit was not of that kind.<br/><br/>But Juan had a sort of winning way,<br/>  A proud humility, if such there be,<br/>Which showed such deference to what females say,<br/>  As if each charming word were a decree.<br/>His tact, too, tempered him from grave to gay,<br/>  And taught him when to be reserved or free:<br/>He had the art of drawing people out,<br/>Without their seeing what he was about.<br/><br/>Aurora, who in her indifference<br/>  Confounded him in common with the crowd<br/>Of flatterers, though she deemed he had more sense<br/>  Than whispering foplings, or than witlings loud--<br/>Commenced (from such slight things will great commence)<br/>  To feel that flattery which attracts the proud<br/>Rather by deference than compliment,<br/>And wins even by a delicate dissent.<br/><br/>And then he had good looks;--that point was carried<br/>  _Nem. con._ amongst the women, which I grieve<br/>To say leads oft to _crim. con._ with the married--<br/>  A case which to the juries we may leave,<br/>Since with digressions we too long have tarried.<br/>  Now though we know of old that looks deceive,<br/>And always have done,--somehow these good looks<br/>Make more impression than the best of books.<br/><br/>Aurora, who looked more on books than faces,<br/>  Was very young, although so very sage,<br/>Admiring more Minerva than the Graces,<br/>  Especially upon a printed page.<br/>But Virtue's self, with all her tightest laces,<br/>  Has not the natural stays of strict old age;<br/>And Socrates, that model of all duty,<br/>Owned to a _penchant_, though discreet, for beauty.<br/><br/>And girls of sixteen are thus far Socratic,<br/>  But innocently so, as Socrates;<br/>And really, if the Sage sublime and Attic<br/>  At seventy years had phantasies like these,<br/>Which Plato in his dialogues dramatic<br/>  Has shown, I know not why they should displease<br/>In virgins--always in a modest way,<br/>Observe,--for that with me's a _sine qu√¢_.<br/><br/>Also observe, that, like the great Lord Coke<br/>  (See Littleton), whene'er I have expressed<br/>Opinions two, which at first sight may look<br/>  Twin opposites, the second is the best.<br/>Perhaps I have a third too, in a nook,<br/>  Or none at all--which seems a sorry jest:<br/>But if a writer should be quite consistent,<br/>How could he possibly show things existent?<br/><br/>If people contradict themselves, can I<br/>  Help contradicting them, and everybody,<br/>Even my veracious self?--But that's a lie:<br/>  I never did so, never will--how should I?<br/>He who doubts all things nothing can deny:<br/>  Truth's fountains may be clear--her streams are muddy,<br/>And cut through such canals of contradiction,<br/>That she must often navigate o'er fiction.<br/><br/>Apologue, Fable, Poesy, and Parable,<br/>  Are false, but may be rendered also true,<br/>By those who sow them in a land that's arable:<br/>  'Tis wonderful what Fable will not do!<br/>'Tis said it makes Reality more bearable:<br/>  But what's Reality? Who has its clue?<br/>Philosophy? No; she too much rejects.<br/>Religion? _Yes_; but which of all her sects?<br/><br/>Some millions must be wrong, that's pretty clear;<br/>  Perhaps it may turn out that all were right.<br/>God help us! Since we have need on our career<br/>  To keep our holy beacons always bright,<br/>'Tis time that some new prophet should appear,<br/>  Or _old_ indulge man with a second sight.<br/>Opinions wear out in some thousand years,<br/>Without a small refreshment from the spheres.<br/><br/>But here again, why will I thus entangle<br/>  Myself with Metaphysics? None can hate<br/>So much as I do any kind of wrangle;<br/>  And yet, such is my folly, or my fate,<br/>I always knock my head against some angle<br/>  About the present, past, or future state:<br/>Yet I wish well to Trojan and to Tyrian,<br/>For I was bred a moderate Presbyterian.<br/><br/>But though I am a temperate theologian,<br/>  And also meek as a metaphysician,<br/>Impartial between Tyrian and Trojan,<br/>  As Eldon on a lunatic commission,--<br/>In politics my duty is to show John<br/>  Bull something of the lower world's condition.<br/>It makes my blood boil like the springs of Hecla,<br/>To see men let these scoundrel Sovereigns break law.<br/><br/>But Politics, and Policy, and Piety,<br/>  Are topics which I sometimes introduce,<br/>Not only for the sake of their variety,<br/>  But as subservient to a moral use;<br/>Because my business is to _dress_ society,<br/>  And stuff with _sage_ that very verdant goose.<br/>And now, that we may furnish with some matter all<br/>Tastes, we are going to try the Supernatural.<br/><br/>And now I will give up all argument;<br/>  And positively, henceforth, no temptation<br/>Shall "fool me to the top up of my bent:"--<br/>  Yes, I'll begin a thorough reformation.<br/>Indeed, I never knew what people meant<br/>  By deeming that my Muse's conversation<br/>Was dangerous;--I think she is as harmless<br/>As some who labour more and yet may charm less.<br/><br/>Grim reader! did you ever see a ghost?<br/>  No; but you have heard--I understand--be dumb!<br/>And don't regret the time you may have lost,<br/>  For you have got that pleasure still to come:<br/>And do not think I mean to sneer at most<br/>  Of these things, or by ridicule benumb<br/>That source of the Sublime and the Mysterious:--<br/>For certain reasons my belief is serious.<br/><br/>Serious? You laugh;--you may: that will I not;<br/>  My smiles must be sincere or not at all.<br/>I say I do believe a haunted spot<br/>  Exists--and where? That shall I not recall,<br/>Because I'd rather it should be forgot,<br/>  "Shadows the soul of Richard" may appal.<br/>In short, upon that subject I've some qualms very<br/>Like those of the philosopher of Malmsbury.<br/><br/>The night--(I sing by night--sometimes an owl,<br/>  And now and then a nightingale)--is dim,<br/>And the loud shriek of sage Minerva's fowl<br/>  Rattles around me her discordant hymn:<br/>Old portraits from old walls upon me scowl--<br/>  I wish to Heaven they would not look so grim;<br/>The dying embers dwindle in the grate--<br/>I think too that I have sat up too late:<br/><br/>And therefore, though 'tis by no means my way<br/>  To rhyme at noon--when I have other things<br/>To think of, if I ever think--I say<br/>  I feel some chilly midnight shudderings,<br/>And prudently postpone, until mid-day,<br/>  Treating a topic which, alas! but brings<br/>Shadows;--but you must be in my condition,<br/>Before you learn to call this superstition.<br/><br/>Between two worlds Life hovers like a star,<br/>  'Twixt Night and Morn, upon the horizon's verge.<br/>How little do we know that which we are!<br/>  How less what we may be! The eternal surge<br/>Of Time and Tide rolls on and bears afar<br/>  Our bubbles; as the old burst, new emerge,<br/>Lashed from the foam of ages; while the graves<br/>Of Empires heave but like some passing waves.<br/><br/>CANTO THE SIXTEENTH.<br/><br/>The antique Persians taught three useful things,<br/>  To draw the bow, to ride, and speak the truth,<br/>This was the mode of Cyrus, best of kings--<br/>  A mode adopted since by modern youth.<br/>Bows have they, generally with two strings;<br/>  Horses they ride without remorse or ruth;<br/>At speaking truth perhaps they are less clever,<br/>But draw the long bow better now than ever.<br/><br/>The cause of this effect, or this defect,--<br/>  "For this effect defective comes by cause,"--<br/>Is what I have not leisure to inspect;<br/>  But this I must say in my own applause,<br/>Of all the Muses that I recollect,<br/>  Whate'er may be her follies or her flaws<br/>In some things, mine's beyond all contradiction<br/>The most sincere that ever dealt in fiction.<br/><br/>And as she treats all things, and ne'er retreats<br/>  From anything, this Epic will contain<br/>A wilderness of the most rare conceits,<br/>  Which you might elsewhere hope to find in vain.<br/>'Tis true there be some bitters with the sweets,<br/>  Yet mixed so slightly, that you can't complain,<br/>But wonder they so few are, since my tale is<br/>"_De rebus cunctis et quibusdam aliis._"<br/><br/>But of all truths which she has told, the most<br/>  True is that which she is about to tell.<br/>I said it was a story of a ghost--<br/>  What then? I only know it so befell.<br/>Have you explored the limits of the coast,<br/>  Where all the dwellers of the earth must dwell?<br/>'Tis time to strike such puny doubters dumb as<br/>The sceptics who would not believe Columbus.<br/><br/>Some people would impose now with authority,<br/>  Turpin's or Monmouth Geoffry's Chronicle;<br/>Men whose historical superiority<br/>  Is always greatest at a miracle.<br/>But Saint Augustine has the great priority,<br/>  Who bids all men believe the impossible,<br/>_Because 'tis so._ Who nibble, scribble, quibble, he<br/>Quiets at once with "_quia impossibile._"<br/><br/>And therefore, mortals, cavil not at all;<br/>  Believe:--if 'tis improbable, you _must_,<br/>And if it is impossible, you _shall_:<br/>  'Tis always best to take things upon trust.<br/>I do not speak profanely to recall<br/>  Those holier Mysteries which the wise and just<br/>Receive as Gospel, and which grow more rooted,<br/>As all truths must, the more they are disputed:<br/><br/>I merely mean to say what Johnson said,<br/>  That in the course of some six thousand years,<br/>All nations have believed that from the dead<br/>  A visitant at intervals appears:<br/>And what is strangest upon this strange head,<br/>  Is, that whatever bar the reason rears<br/>'Gainst such belief, there's something stronger still<br/>In its behalf--let those deny who will.<br/><br/>The dinner and the _soir√©e_ too were done,<br/>  The supper too discussed, the dames admired,<br/>The banqueteers had dropped off one by one--<br/>  The song was silent, and the dance expired:<br/>The last thin petticoats were vanished, gone<br/>  Like fleecy clouds into the sky retired,<br/>And nothing brighter gleamed through the saloon<br/>Than dying tapers--and the peeping moon.<br/><br/>The evaporation of a joyous day<br/>  Is like the last glass of champagne, without<br/>The foam which made its virgin bumper gay;<br/>  Or like a system coupled with a doubt;<br/>Or like a soda bottle when its spray<br/>  Has sparkled and let half its spirit out;<br/>Or like a billow left by storms behind,<br/>Without the animation of the wind;<br/><br/>Or like an opiate, which brings troubled rest,<br/>  Or none; or like--like nothing that I know<br/>Except itself;--such is the human breast;<br/>  A thing, of which similitudes can show<br/>No real likeness,--like the old Tyrian vest<br/>  Dyed purple, none at present can tell how,<br/>If from a shell-fish or from cochineal.<br/>So perish every Tyrant's robe piece-meal!<br/><br/>But next to dressing for a rout or ball,<br/>  Undressing is a woe; our _robe de chambre_<br/>May sit like that of Nessus, and recall<br/>  Thoughts quite as yellow, but less clear than amber.<br/>Titus exclaimed, "I've lost a day!" Of all<br/>  The nights and days most people can remember,<br/>(I have had of both, some not to be disdained,)<br/>I wish they'd state how many they have gained.<br/><br/>And Juan, on retiring for the night,<br/>  Felt restless, and perplexed, and compromised:<br/>He thought Aurora Raby's eyes more bright<br/>  Than Adeline (such is advice) advised;<br/>If he had known exactly his own plight,<br/>  He probably would have philosophised:<br/>A great resource to all, and ne'er denied<br/>Till wanted; therefore Juan only sighed.<br/><br/>He sighed;--the next resource is the full moon,<br/>  Where all sighs are deposited; and now<br/>It happened luckily, the chaste orb shone<br/>  As clear as such a climate will allow;<br/>And Juan's mind was in the proper tone<br/>  To hail her with the apostrophe--"O thou!"<br/>Of amatory egotism the _Tuism_,<br/>Which further to explain would be a truism.<br/><br/>But Lover, Poet, or Astronomer--<br/>  Shepherd, or swain--whoever may behold,<br/>Feel some abstraction when they gaze on her;<br/>  Great thoughts we catch from thence (besides a cold<br/>Sometimes, unless my feelings rather err);<br/>  Deep secrets to her rolling light are told;<br/>The Ocean's tides and mortals' brains she sways,<br/>And also hearts--if there be truth in lays.<br/><br/>Juan felt somewhat pensive, and disposed<br/>  For contemplation rather than his pillow:<br/>The Gothic chamber, where he was enclosed,<br/>  Let in the rippling sound of the lake's billow,<br/>With all the mystery by midnight caused:<br/>  Below his window waved (of course) willow;<br/>And he stood gazing out on the cascade<br/>That flashed and after darkened in the shade.<br/><br/>Upon his table or his toilet,--_which_<br/>  Of these is not exactly ascertained,--<br/>(I state this, for I am cautious to a pitch<br/>  Of nicety, where a fact is to be gained,)<br/>A lamp burned high, while he leant from a niche,<br/>  Where many a Gothic ornament remained,<br/>In chiselled stone and painted glass, and all<br/>That Time has left our fathers of their Hall.<br/><br/>Then, as the night was clear though cold, he threw<br/>  His chamber door wide open--and went forth<br/>Into a gallery of a sombre hue,<br/>  Long, furnished with old pictures of great worth,<br/>Of knights and dames heroic and chaste too,<br/>  As doubtless should be people of high birth;<br/>But by dim lights the portraits of the dead<br/>Have something ghastly, desolate, and dread.<br/><br/>The forms of the grim Knight and pictured Saint<br/>  Look living in the moon; and as you turn<br/>Backward and forward to the echoes faint<br/>  Of your own footsteps--voices from the Urn<br/>Appear to wake, and shadows wild and quaint<br/>  Start from the frames which fence their aspects stern,<br/>As if to ask how you can dare to keep<br/>A vigil there, where all but Death should sleep.<br/><br/>And the pale smile of Beauties in the grave,<br/>  The charms of other days, in starlight gleams,<br/>Glimmer on high; their buried locks still wave<br/>  Along the canvas; their eyes glance like dreams<br/>On ours, or spars within some dusky cave,<br/>  But Death is imaged in their shadowy beams.<br/>A picture is the past; even ere its frame<br/>Be gilt, who sate hath ceased to be the same.<br/><br/>As Juan mused on Mutability,<br/>  Or on his Mistress--terms synonymous--<br/>No sound except the echo of his sigh<br/>  Or step ran sadly through that antique house;<br/>When suddenly he heard, or thought so, nigh,<br/>  A supernatural agent--or a mouse,<br/>Whose little nibbling rustle will embarrass<br/>Most people as it plays along the arras.<br/><br/>It was no mouse--but lo! a monk, arrayed<br/>  In cowl and beads, and dusky garb, appeared,<br/>Now in the moonlight, and now lapsed in shade,<br/>  With steps that trod as heavy, yet unheard;<br/>His garments only a slight murmur made;<br/>  He moved as shadowy as the Sisters weird,<br/>But slowly; and as he passed Juan by,<br/>Glanced, without pausing, on him a bright eye.<br/><br/>Juan was petrified; he had heard a hint<br/>  Of such a Spirit in these halls of old,<br/>But thought, like most men, that there was nothing in 't<br/>  Beyond the rumour which such spots unfold,<br/>Coined from surviving Superstition's mint,<br/>  Which passes ghosts in currency like gold,<br/>But rarely seen, like gold compared with paper.<br/>And did he see this? or was it a vapour?<br/><br/>Once, twice, thrice passed, repassed--the thing of air,<br/>  Or earth beneath, or Heaven, or t' other place;<br/>And Juan gazed upon it with a stare,<br/>  Yet could not speak or move; but, on its base<br/>As stands a statue, stood: he felt his hair<br/>  Twine like a knot of snakes around his face;<br/>He taxed his tongue for words, which were not granted,<br/>To ask the reverend person what he wanted.<br/><br/>The third time, after a still longer pause,<br/>  The shadow passed away--but where? the hall<br/>Was long, and thus far there was no great cause<br/>  To think his vanishing unnatural:<br/>Doors there were many, through which, by the laws<br/>  Of physics, bodies whether short or tall<br/>Might come or go; but Juan could not state<br/>Through which the Spectre seemed to evaporate.<br/><br/>He stood--how long he knew not, but it seemed<br/>  An age--expectant, powerless, with his eyes<br/>Strained on the spot where first the figure gleamed<br/>  Then by degrees recalled his energies,<br/>And would have passed the whole off as a dream,<br/>  But could not wake; he was, he did surmise,<br/>Waking already, and returned at length<br/>Back to his chamber, shorn of half his strength.<br/><br/>All there was as he left it: still his taper<br/>  Burned, and not _blue_, as modest tapers use,<br/>Receiving sprites with sympathetic vapour;<br/>  He rubbed his eyes, and they did not refuse<br/>Their office: he took up an old newspaper;<br/>  The paper was right easy to peruse;<br/>He read an article the King attacking,<br/>And a long eulogy of "Patent Blacking."<br/><br/>This savoured of this world; but his hand shook:<br/>  He shut his door, and after having read<br/>A paragraph, I think about Horne Tooke,<br/>  Undressed, and rather slowly went to bed.<br/>There, couched all snugly on his pillow's nook,<br/>  With what he had seen his phantasy he fed;<br/>And though it was no opiate, slumber crept<br/>Upon him by degrees, and so he slept.<br/><br/>He woke betimes; and, as may be supposed,<br/>  Pondered upon his visitant or vision,<br/>And whether it ought not to be disclosed,<br/>  At risk of being quizzed for superstition.<br/>The more he thought, the more his mind was posed:<br/>  In the mean time, his valet, whose precision<br/>Was great, because his master brooked no less,<br/>Knocked to inform him it was time to dress.<br/><br/>He dressed; and like young people he was wont<br/>  To take some trouble with his toilet, but<br/>This morning rather spent less time upon 't;<br/>  Aside his very mirror soon was put;<br/>His curls fell negligently o'er his front,<br/>  His clothes were not curbed to their usual cut,<br/>His very neckcloth's Gordian knot was tied<br/>Almost an hair's breadth too much on one side.<br/><br/>And when he walked down into the Saloon,<br/>  He sate him pensive o'er a dish of tea,<br/>Which he perhaps had not discovered soon,<br/>  Had it not happened scalding hot to be,<br/>Which made him have recourse unto his spoon;<br/>  So much _distrait_ he was, that all could see<br/>That something was the matter--Adeline<br/>The first--but _what_ she could not well divine.<br/><br/>She looked, and saw him pale, and turned as pale<br/>  Herself; then hastily looked down, and muttered<br/>Something, but what's not stated in my tale.<br/>  Lord Henry said, his muffin was ill buttered;<br/>The Duchess of Fitz-Fulke played with her veil,<br/>  And looked at Juan hard, but nothing uttered.<br/>Aurora Raby with her large dark eyes<br/>Surveyed him with a kind of calm surprise.<br/><br/>But seeing him all cold and silent still,<br/>  And everybody wondering more or less,<br/>Fair Adeline inquired, "If he were ill?"<br/>  He started, and said, "Yes--no--rather--yes."<br/>The family physician had great skill,<br/>  And being present, now began to express<br/>His readiness to feel his pulse and tell<br/>The cause, but Juan said, he was "quite well."<br/><br/>"Quite well; yes,--no."--These answers were mysterious,<br/>  And yet his looks appeared to sanction both,<br/>However they might savour of delirious;<br/>  Something like illness of a sudden growth<br/>Weighed on his spirit, though by no means serious:<br/>  But for the rest, as he himself seemed both<br/>To state the case, it might be ta'en for granted<br/>It was not the physician that he wanted.<br/><br/>Lord Henry, who had now discussed his chocolate,<br/>  Also the muffin whereof he complained,<br/>Said, Juan had not got his usual look elate,<br/>  At which he marvelled, since it had not rained;<br/>Then asked her Grace what news were of the Duke of late?<br/>  _Her_ Grace replied, _his_ Grace was rather pained<br/>With some slight, light, hereditary twinges<br/>Of gout, which rusts aristocratic hinges.<br/><br/>Then Henry turned to Juan, and addressed<br/>  A few words of condolence on his state:<br/>"You look," quoth he, "as if you had had your rest<br/>  Broke in upon by the Black Friar of late."<br/>"What Friar?" said Juan; and he did his best<br/>  To put the question with an air sedate,<br/>Or careless; but the effort was not valid<br/>To hinder him from growing still more pallid.<br/><br/>"Oh! have you never heard of the Black Friar?<br/>  The Spirit of these walls?"--"In truth not I."<br/>"Why Fame--but Fame you know's sometimes a liar--<br/>  Tells an odd story, of which by and by:<br/>Whether with time the Spectre has grown shyer,<br/>  Or that our Sires had a more gifted eye<br/>For such sights, though the tale is half believed,<br/>The Friar of late has not been oft perceived.<br/><br/>"The last time was----"--"I pray," said Adeline--<br/>  (Who watched the changes of Don Juan's brow,<br/>And from its context thought she could divine<br/>  Connections stronger than he chose to avow<br/>With this same legend)--"if you but design<br/>  To jest, you'll choose some other theme just now,<br/>Because the present tale has oft been told,<br/>And is not much improved by growing old."<br/><br/>"Jest!" quoth Milor; "why, Adeline, you know<br/>  That we ourselves--'twas in the honey moon<br/>Saw----"--"Well, no matter, 'twas so long ago;<br/>  But, come, I'll set your story to a tune."<br/>Graceful as Dian when she draws her bow,<br/>  She seized her harp, whose strings were kindled soon<br/>As touched, and plaintively began to play<br/>The air of "'Twas a Friar of Orders Gray."<br/><br/>"But add the words," cried Henry, "which you made;<br/>  For Adeline is half a poetess,"<br/>Turning round to the rest, he smiling said.<br/>  Of course the others could not but express<br/>In courtesy their wish to see displayed<br/>  By one _three_ talents, for there were no less--<br/>The voice, the words, the harper's skill, at once,<br/>Could hardly be united by a dunce.<br/><br/>After some fascinating hesitation,--<br/>  The charming of these charmers, who seem bound,<br/>I can't tell why, to this dissimulation,--<br/>  Fair Adeline, with eyes fixed on the ground<br/>At first, then kindling into animation,<br/>  Added her sweet voice to the lyric sound,<br/>And sang with much simplicity,--a merit<br/>Not the less precious, that we seldom hear it.<br/><br/>Beware! beware! of the Black Friar,<br/>  Who sitteth by Norman stone,<br/>For he mutters his prayer in the midnight air,<br/>  And his mass of the days that are gone.<br/>When the Lord of the Hill, Amundeville,<br/>  Made Norman Church his prey,<br/>And expelled the friars, one friar still<br/>Would not be driven away.<br/><br/>Though he came in his might, with King Henry's right,<br/>  To turn church lands to lay,<br/>With sword in hand, and torch to light<br/>  Their walls, if they said nay;<br/>A monk remained, unchased, unchained,<br/>  And he did not seem formed of clay,<br/>For he's seen in the porch, and he's seen in the church,<br/>Though he is not seen by day.<br/><br/>And whether for good, or whether for ill,<br/>  It is not mine to say;<br/>But still with the house of Amundeville<br/>  He abideth night and day.<br/>By the marriage-bed of their lords, 'tis said,<br/>  He flits on the bridal eve;<br/>And 'tis held as faith, to their bed of Death<br/>He comes--but not to grieve.<br/><br/>When an heir is born, he's heard to mourn,<br/>  And when aught is to befall<br/>That ancient line, in the pale moonshine<br/>  He walks from hall to hall.<br/>His form you may trace, but not his face,<br/>  'Tis shadowed by his cowl;<br/>But his eyes may be seen from the folds between,<br/>And they seem of a parted soul.<br/><br/>But beware! beware! of the Black Friar,<br/>  He still retains his sway,<br/>For he is yet the Church's heir,<br/>  Whoever may be the lay.<br/>Amundeville is Lord by day,<br/>  But the monk is Lord by night;<br/>Nor wine nor wassail could raise a vassal<br/>To question that Friar's right.<br/><br/>Say nought to him as he walks the Hall,<br/>  And he'll say nought to you;<br/>He sweeps along in his dusky pall,<br/>  As o'er the grass the dew.<br/>Then grammercy! for the Black Friar;<br/>  Heaven sain him! fair or foul,--<br/>And whatsoe'er may be his prayer,<br/>Let ours be for his soul.<br/><br/>The lady's voice ceased, and the thrilling wires<br/>  Died from the touch that kindled them to sound;<br/>And the pause followed, which when song expires<br/>  Pervades a moment those who listen round;<br/>And then of course the circle much admires,<br/>  Nor less applauds, as in politeness bound,<br/>The tones, the feeling, and the execution,<br/>To the performer's diffident confusion.<br/><br/>Fair Adeline, though in a careless way,<br/>  As if she rated such accomplishment<br/>As the mere pastime of an idle day,<br/>  Pursued an instant for her own content,<br/>Would now and then as 'twere _without_ display,<br/>  Yet _with_ display in fact, at times relent<br/>To such performances with haughty smile,<br/>To show she _could_, if it were worth her while.<br/><br/>Now this (but we will whisper it aside)<br/>  Was--pardon the pedantic illustration--<br/>Trampling on Plato's pride with greater pride,<br/>  As did the Cynic on some like occasion;<br/>Deeming the sage would be much mortified,<br/>  Or thrown into a philosophic passion,<br/>For a spoilt carpet--but the "Attic Bee"<br/>Was much consoled by his own repartee.<br/><br/>Thus Adeline would throw into the shade<br/>  (By doing easily, whene'er she chose,<br/>What dilettanti do with vast parade)<br/>  Their sort of _half profession_; for it grows<br/>To something like this when too oft displayed;<br/>  And that it is so, everybody knows,<br/>Who have heard Miss That or This, or Lady T'other,<br/>Show off--to please their company or mother.<br/><br/>Oh! the long evenings of duets and trios!<br/>  The admirations and the speculations;<br/>The "Mamma Mia's!" and the "Amor Mio's!"<br/>  The "Tanti palpiti's" on such occasions:<br/>The "Lasciami's," and quavering "Addio's,"<br/>  Amongst our own most musical of nations!<br/>With "Tu mi chamas's" from Portingale,<br/>To soothe our ears, lest Italy should fail.<br/><br/>In Babylon's _bravuras_--as the Home-<br/>  Heart-Ballads of Green Erin or Grey Highlands,<br/>That bring Lochaber back to eyes that roam<br/>  O'er far Atlantic continents or islands,<br/>The calentures of music which o'ercome<br/>  All mountaineers with dreams that they are nigh lands,<br/>No more to be beheld but in such visions--<br/>Was Adeline well versed, as compositions.<br/><br/>She also had a twilight tinge of "_Blue_,"<br/>  Could write rhymes, and compose more than she wrote,<br/>Made epigrams occasionally too<br/>  Upon her friends, as everybody ought.<br/>But still from that sublimer azure hue,<br/>  So much the present dye, she was remote;<br/>Was weak enough to deem Pope a great poet,<br/>And what was worse, was not ashamed to show it.<br/><br/>Aurora--since we are touching upon taste,<br/>  Which now-a-days is the thermometer<br/>By whose degrees all characters are classed--<br/>  Was more Shakespearian, if I do not err.<br/>The worlds beyond this World's perplexing waste<br/>  Had more of her existence, for in her<br/>There was a depth of feeling to embrace<br/>Thoughts, boundless, deep, but silent too as Space.<br/><br/>Not so her gracious, graceful, graceless Grace,<br/>  The full-grown Hebe of Fitz-Fulke, whose mind,<br/>If she had any, was upon her face,<br/>  And that was of a fascinating kind.<br/>A little turn for mischief you might trace<br/>  Also thereon,--but that's not much; we find<br/>Few females without some such gentle leaven,<br/>For fear we should suppose us quite in Heaven.<br/><br/>I have not heard she was at all poetic,<br/>  Though once she was seen reading the _Bath Guide_,<br/>And Hayley's _Triumphs_, which she deemed pathetic,<br/>  Because she said _her temper_ had been tried<br/>So much, the bard had really been prophetic<br/>  Of what she had gone through with--since a bride.<br/>But of all verse, what most ensured her praise<br/>Were sonnets to herself, or _bouts rim√©s_.<br/><br/>'Twere difficult to say what was the object<br/>  Of Adeline, in bringing this same lay<br/>To bear on what appeared to her the subject<br/>  Of Juan's nervous feelings on that day.<br/>Perhaps she merely had the simple project<br/>  To laugh him out of his supposed dismay;<br/>Perhaps she might wish to confirm him in it,<br/>Though why I cannot say--at least this minute.<br/><br/>But so far the immediate effect<br/>  Was to restore him to his self-propriety,<br/>A thing quite necessary to the elect,<br/>  Who wish to take the tone of their society:<br/>In which you cannot be too circumspect,<br/>  Whether the mode be persiflage or piety,<br/>But wear the newest mantle of hypocrisy,<br/>On pain of much displeasing the gynocracy.<br/><br/>And therefore Juan now began to rally<br/>  His spirits, and without more explanation<br/>To jest upon such themes in many a sally.<br/>  Her Grace, too, also seized the same occasion,<br/>With various similar remarks to tally,<br/>  But wished for a still more detailed narration<br/>Of this same mystic friar's curious doings,<br/>About the present family's deaths and wooings.<br/><br/>Of these few could say more than has been said;<br/>  They passed as such things do, for superstition<br/>With some, while others, who had more in dread<br/>  The theme, half credited the strange tradition;<br/>And much was talked on all sides on that head:<br/>  But Juan, when cross-questioned on the vision,<br/>Which some supposed (though he had not avowed it)<br/>Had stirred him, answered in a way to cloud it.<br/><br/>And then, the mid-day having worn to one,<br/>  The company prepared to separate;<br/>Some to their several pastimes, or to none,<br/>  Some wondering 'twas so early, some so late.<br/>There was a goodly match too, to be run<br/>  Between some greyhounds on my Lord's estate,<br/>And a young race-horse of old pedigree,<br/>Matched for the spring, whom several went to see.<br/><br/>There was a picture-dealer who had brought<br/>  A special Titian, warranted original,<br/>So precious that it was not to be bought,<br/>  Though Princes the possessor were besieging all--<br/>The King himself had cheapened it, but thought<br/>  The civil list he deigns to accept (obliging all<br/>His subjects by his gracious acceptation)--<br/>Too scanty, in these times of low taxation.<br/><br/>But as Lord Henry was a connoisseur,--<br/>  The friend of Artists, if not Arts,--the owner,<br/>With motives the most classical and pure,<br/>  So that he would have been the very donor,<br/>Rather than seller, had his wants been fewer,<br/>  So much he deemed his patronage an honour,<br/>Had brought the _capo d'opera_, not for sale,<br/>But for his judgment--never known to fail.<br/><br/>There was a modern Goth, I mean a Gothic<br/>  Bricklayer of Babel, called an architect,<br/>Brought to survey these grey walls which, though so thick,<br/>  Might have from Time acquired some slight defect;<br/>Who, after rummaging the Abbey through thick<br/>  And thin, produced a plan whereby to erect<br/>New buildings of correctest conformation,<br/>And throw down old--which he called _restoration_.<br/><br/>The cost would be a trifle--an "old song,"<br/>  Set to some thousands ('tis the usual burden<br/>Of that same tune, when people hum it long)--<br/>  The price would speedily repay its worth in<br/>An edifice no less sublime than strong,<br/>  By which Lord Henry's good taste would go forth in<br/>Its glory, through all ages shining sunny,<br/>For Gothic daring shown in English money.<br/><br/>There were two lawyers busy on a mortgage<br/>  Lord Henry wished to raise for a new purchase;<br/>Also a lawsuit upon tenures burgage,<br/>  And one on tithes, which sure as Discord's torches,<br/>Kindling Religion till she throws down _her_ gage,<br/>  "Untying" squires "to fight against the churches;"<br/>There was a prize ox, a prize pig, and ploughman,<br/>For Henry was a sort of Sabine showman.<br/><br/>There were two poachers caught in a steel trap,<br/>  Ready for gaol, their place of convalescence;<br/>There was a country girl in a close cap<br/>  And scarlet cloak (I hate the sight to see, since--<br/>Since--since--in youth, I had the sad mishap--<br/>  But luckily I have paid few parish fees since):<br/>That scarlet cloak, alas! unclosed with rigour,<br/>Presents the problem of a double figure.<br/><br/>A reel within a bottle is a mystery,<br/>  One can't tell how it e'er got in or out;<br/>Therefore the present piece of natural history<br/>  I leave to those who are fond of solving doubt;<br/>And merely state, though not for the Consistory,<br/>  Lord Henry was a Justice, and that Scout<br/>The constable, beneath a warrant's banner,<br/>Had bagged this poacher upon Nature's manor.<br/><br/>Now Justices of Peace must judge all pieces<br/>  Of mischief of all kinds, and keep the game<br/>And morals of the country from caprices<br/>  Of those who have not a licence for the same;<br/>And of all things, excepting tithes and leases,<br/>  Perhaps these are most difficult to tame:<br/>Preserving partridges and pretty wenches<br/>Are puzzles to the most precautions benches.<br/><br/>The present culprit was extremely pale,<br/>  Pale as if painted so; her cheek being red<br/>By nature, as in higher dames less hale<br/>  'Tis white, at least when they just rise from bed.<br/>Perhaps she was ashamed of seeming frail,<br/>  Poor soul! for she was country born and bred,<br/>And knew no better in her immorality<br/>Than to wax white--for blushes are for quality.<br/><br/>Her black, bright, downcast, yet _espi√®gle_ eye,<br/>  Had gathered a large tear into its corner,<br/>Which the poor thing at times essayed to dry,<br/>  For she was not a sentimental mourner<br/>Parading all her sensibility,<br/>  Nor insolent enough to scorn the scorner,<br/>But stood in trembling, patient tribulation,<br/>To be called up for her examination.<br/><br/>Of course these groups were scattered here and there,<br/>  Not nigh the gay saloon of ladies gent.<br/>The lawyers in the study; and in air<br/>  The prize pig, ploughman, poachers: the men sent<br/>From town, viz. architect and dealer, were<br/>  Both busy (as a General in his tent<br/>Writing despatches) in their several stations,<br/>Exulting in their brilliant lucubrations.<br/><br/>But this poor girl was left in the great hall,<br/>  While Scout, the parish guardian of the frail,<br/>Discussed (he hated beer yclept the "small")<br/>  A mighty mug of _moral_ double ale.<br/>She waited until Justice could recall<br/>  Its kind attentions to their proper pale,<br/>To name a thing in nomenclature rather<br/>Perplexing for most virgins--a child's father.<br/><br/>You see here was enough of occupation<br/>  For the Lord Henry, linked with dogs and horses.<br/>There was much bustle too, and preparation<br/>  Below stairs on the score of second courses;<br/>Because, as suits their rank and situation,<br/>  Those who in counties have great land resources<br/>Have "public days," when all men may carouse,<br/>Though not exactly what's called "open house."<br/><br/>But once a week or fortnight, _un_invited<br/>  (Thus we translate a _general invitation_)<br/>All country gentlemen, esquired or knighted,<br/>  May drop in without cards, and take their station<br/>At the full board, and sit alike delighted<br/>  With fashionable wines and conversation;<br/>And, as the isthmus of the grand connection,<br/>Talk o'er themselves the past and next election.<br/><br/>Lord Henry was a great electioneerer,<br/>  Burrowing for boroughs like a rat or rabbit.<br/>But county contests cost him rather dearer,<br/>  Because the neighbouring Scotch Earl of Giftgabbit<br/>Had English influence, in the self-same sphere here;<br/>  His son, the Honourable Dick Dicedrabbit,<br/>Was member for the "other interest" (meaning<br/>The same self-interest, with a different leaning).<br/><br/>Courteous and cautious therefore in his county,<br/>  He was all things to all men, and dispensed<br/>To some civility, to others bounty,<br/>  And promises to all--which last commenced<br/>To gather to a somewhat large amount, he<br/>  Not calculating how much they condensed;<br/>But what with keeping some, and breaking others,<br/>His word had the same value as another's.<br/><br/>A friend to Freedom and freeholders--yet<br/>  No less a friend to Government--he held,<br/>That he exactly the just medium hit<br/>  Twixt Place and Patriotism--albeit compelled,<br/>Such was his Sovereign's pleasure, (though unfit,<br/>  He added modestly, when rebels railed,)<br/>To hold some sinecures he wished abolished,<br/>But that with them all Law would be demolished.<br/><br/>He was "free to confess"--(whence comes this phrase?<br/>  Is 't English? No--'tis only parliamentary)<br/>That Innovation's spirit now-a-days<br/>  Had made more progress than for the last century.<br/>He would not tread a factious path to praise,<br/>  Though for the public weal disposed to venture high;<br/>As for his place, he could but say this of it,<br/>That the fatigue was greater than the profit.<br/><br/>Heaven, and his friends, knew that a private life<br/>  Had ever been his sole and whole ambition;<br/>But could he quit his King in times of strife,<br/>  Which threatened the whole country with perdition?<br/>When demagogues would with a butcher's knife<br/>  Cut through and through (oh! damnable incision!)<br/>The Gordian or the G_e_ordi-an knot, whose strings<br/>Have tied together Commons, Lords, and Kings.<br/><br/>Sooner "come Place into the Civil List<br/>  And champion him to the utmost--" he would keep it,<br/>Till duly disappointed or dismissed:<br/>  Profit he cared not for, let others reap it;<br/>But should the day come when Place ceased to exist,<br/>  The country would have far more cause to weep it:<br/>For how could it go on? Explain who can!<br/>_He_ gloried in the name of Englishman.<br/><br/>He was as independent--aye, much more--<br/>  Than those who were not paid for independence,<br/>As common soldiers, or a common----shore,<br/>  Have in their several arts or parts ascendance<br/>O'er the irregulars in lust or gore,<br/>  Who do not give professional attendance.<br/>Thus on the mob all statesmen are as eager<br/>To prove their pride, as footmen to a beggar.<br/><br/>All this (save the last stanza) Henry said,<br/>  And thought. I say no more--I've said too much;<br/>For all of us have either heard or read--<br/>  Off--or _upon_ the hustings--some slight such<br/>Hints from the independent heart or head<br/>  Of the official candidate. I'll touch<br/>No more on this--the dinner-bell hath rung,<br/>And grace is said; the grace I _should_ have _sung_--<br/><br/>But I'm too late, and therefore must make play.<br/>  'Twas a great banquet, such as Albion old<br/>Was wont to boast--as if a glutton's tray<br/>  Were something very glorious to behold.<br/>But 'twas a public feast and public day,--<br/>  Quite full--right dull--guests hot, and dishes cold,--<br/>Great plenty, much formality, small cheer,--<br/>And everybody out of their own sphere.<br/><br/>The squires familiarly formal, and<br/>  My Lords and Ladies proudly condescending;<br/>The very servants puzzling how to hand<br/>  Their plates--without it might be too much bending<br/>From their high places by the sideboard's stand--<br/>  Yet, like their masters, fearful of offending;<br/>For any deviation from the graces<br/>Might cost both man and master too--their _places_.<br/><br/>There were some hunters bold, and coursers keen,<br/>  Whose hounds ne'er erred, nor greyhounds deigned to lurch;<br/>Some deadly shots too, Septembrizers, seen<br/>  Earliest to rise, and last to quit the search<br/>Of the poor partridge through his stubble screen.<br/>  There were some massy members of the church,<br/>Takers of tithes, and makers of good matches,<br/>And several who sung fewer psalms than catches.<br/><br/>There were some country wags too--and, alas!<br/>  Some exiles from the Town, who had been driven<br/>To gaze, instead of pavement, upon grass,<br/>  And rise at nine in lieu of long eleven.<br/>And lo! upon that day it came to pass,<br/>  I sate next that o'erwhelming son of Heaven,<br/>The very powerful parson, Peter Pith,<br/>The loudest wit I e'er was deafened with.<br/><br/>I knew him in his livelier London days,<br/>  A brilliant diner-out, though but a curate,<br/>And not a joke he cut but earned its praise,<br/>  Until Preferment, coming at a sure rate,<br/>(O Providence! how wondrous are thy ways!<br/>  Who would suppose thy gifts sometimes obdurate?)<br/>Gave him, to lay the Devil who looks o'er Lincoln,<br/>A fat fen vicarage, and nought to think on.<br/><br/>His jokes were sermons, and his sermons jokes;<br/>  But both were thrown away amongst the fens;<br/>For Wit hath no great friend in aguish folks.<br/>  No longer ready ears and short-hand pens<br/>Imbibed the gay _bon-mot_, or happy hoax:<br/>  The poor priest was reduced to common sense,<br/>Or to coarse efforts very loud and long,<br/>To hammer a hoarse laugh from the thick throng.<br/><br/>There _is_ a difference, says the song, "between<br/>  A beggar and a Queen," or _was_ (of late<br/>The latter worse used of the two we've seen--<br/>  But we 'll say nothing of affairs of state);<br/>A difference "'twixt a Bishop and a Dean,"<br/>  A difference between crockery ware and plate,<br/>As between English beef and Spartan broth--<br/>And yet great heroes have been bred by both.<br/><br/>But of all Nature's discrepancies, none<br/>  Upon the whole is greater than the difference<br/>Beheld between the Country and the Town,<br/>  Of which the latter merits every preference<br/>From those who have few resources of their own.<br/>  And only think, or act, or feel, with reference<br/>To some small plan of interest or ambition--<br/>Both which are limited to no condition.<br/><br/>But _En avant!_ The light loves languish o'er<br/>  Long banquets and too many guests, although<br/>A slight repast makes people love much more,<br/>  Bacchus and Ceres being, as we know,<br/>Even from our grammar upwards, friends of yore<br/>  With vivifying Venus, who doth owe<br/>To these the invention of champagne and truffles:<br/>Temperance delights her, but long fasting ruffles.<br/><br/>Dully passed o'er the dinner of the day;<br/>  And Juan took his place, he knew not where,<br/>Confused, in the confusion, and _distrait_,<br/>  And sitting as if nailed upon his chair:<br/>Though knives and forks clanked round as in a fray,<br/>  He seemed unconscious of all passing there,<br/>Till some one, with a groan, expressed a wish<br/>(Unheeded twice) to have a fin of fish.<br/><br/>On which, at the _third_ asking of the banns,<br/>  He started; and perceiving smiles around<br/>Broadening to grins, he coloured more than once,<br/>  And hastily--as nothing can confound<br/>A wise man more than laughter from a dunce--<br/>  Inflicted on the dish a deadly wound,<br/>And with such hurry, that, ere he could curb it,<br/>He had paid his neighbour's prayer with half a turbot.<br/><br/>This was no bad mistake, as it occurred,<br/>  The supplicator being an amateur;<br/>But others, who were left with scarce a third,<br/>  Were angry--as they well might, to be sure,<br/>They wondered how a young man so absurd<br/>  Lord Henry at his table should endure;<br/>And this, and his not knowing how much oats<br/>Had fallen last market, cost his host three votes.<br/><br/>They little knew, or might have sympathized,<br/>  That he the night before had seen a ghost,<br/>A prologue which but slightly harmonized<br/>  With the substantial company engrossed<br/>By matter, and so much materialised,<br/>  That one scarce knew at what to marvel most<br/>Of two things--_how_ (the question rather odd is)<br/>Such bodies could have souls, or souls such bodies!<br/><br/>But what confused him more than smile or stare<br/>  From all the 'squires and 'squiresses around,<br/>Who wondered at the abstraction of his air,<br/>  Especially as he had been renowned<br/>For some vivacity among the fair,<br/>  Even in the country circle's narrow bound--<br/>(For little things upon my Lord's estate<br/>Were good small talk for others still less great)--<br/><br/>Was, that he caught Aurora's eye on his,<br/>  And something like a smile upon her cheek.<br/>Now this he really rather took amiss;<br/>  In those who rarely smile, their smile bespeaks<br/>A strong external motive; and in this<br/>  Smile of Aurora's there was nought to pique,<br/>Or Hope, or Love--with any of the wiles<br/>Which some pretend to trace in ladies' smiles.<br/><br/>'Twas a mere quiet smile of contemplation,<br/>  Indicative of some surprise and pity;<br/>And Juan grew carnation with vexation,<br/>  Which was not very wise, and still less witty,<br/>Since he had gained at least her observation,<br/>  A most important outwork of the city--<br/>As Juan should have known, had not his senses<br/>By last night's Ghost been driven from their defences.<br/><br/>But what was bad, she did not blush in turn,<br/>  Nor seem embarrassed--quite the contrary;<br/>Her aspect was as usual, still--_not_ stern--<br/>  And she withdrew, but cast not down, her eye,<br/>Yet grew a little pale--with what? concern?<br/>  I know not; but her colour ne'er was high--<br/>Though sometimes faintly flushed--and always clear,<br/>As deep seas in a sunny atmosphere.<br/><br/>But Adeline was occupied by fame<br/>  This day; and watching, witching, condescending<br/>To the consumers of fish, fowl, and game,<br/>  And dignity with courtesy so blending,<br/>As all must blend whose part it is to aim<br/>  (Especially as the sixth year is ending)<br/>At their lord's, son's, or similar connection's<br/>Safe conduct through the rocks of re-elections.<br/><br/>Though this was most expedient on the whole<br/>  And usual--Juan, when he cast a glance<br/>On Adeline while playing her grand _r√¥le_,<br/>  Which she went through as though it were a dance,<br/>Betraying only now and then her soul<br/>  By a look scarce perceptibly askance<br/>(Of weariness or scorn), began to feel<br/>Some doubt how much of Adeline was _real_;<br/><br/>So well she acted all and every part<br/>  By turns--with that vivacious versatility,<br/>Which many people take for want of heart.<br/>  They err--'tis merely what is called mobility,<br/>A thing of temperament and not of art,<br/>  Though seeming so, from its supposed facility;<br/>And false--though true; for, surely, they're sincerest<br/>Who are strongly acted on by what is nearest.<br/><br/>This makes your actors, artists, and romancers,<br/>  Heroes sometimes, though seldom--sages never:<br/>But speakers, bards, diplomatists, and dancers,<br/>  Little that's great, but much of what is clever;<br/>Most orators, but very few financiers,<br/>  Though all Exchequer Chancellors endeavour,<br/>Of late years, to dispense with Cocker's rigours,<br/>And grow quite figurative with their figures.<br/><br/>The poets of Arithmetic are they<br/>  Who, though they prove not two and two to be<br/>Five, as they might do in a modest way,<br/>  Have plainly made it out that four are three,<br/>Judging by what they take, and what they pay:<br/>  The Sinking Fund's unfathomable sea,<br/>That most unliquidating liquid, leaves<br/>The debt unsunk, yet sinks all it receives.<br/><br/>While Adeline dispensed her airs and graces,<br/>  The fair Fitz-Fulke seemed very much at ease;<br/>Though too well bred to quiz men to their faces,<br/>  Her laughing blue eyes with a glance could seize<br/>The ridicules of people in all places--<br/>  That honey of your fashionable bees--<br/>And store it up for mischievous enjoyment;<br/>And this at present was her kind employment.<br/><br/>However, the day closed, as days must close;<br/>  The evening also waned--and coffee came.<br/>Each carriage was announced, and ladies rose,<br/>  And curtsying off, as curtsies country dame,<br/>Retired: with most unfashionable bows<br/>  Their docile Esquires also did the same,<br/>Delighted with their dinner and their Host,<br/>But with the Lady Adeline the most.<br/><br/>Some praised her beauty: others her great grace;<br/>  The warmth of her politeness, whose sincerity<br/>Was obvious in each feature of her face,<br/>  Whose traits were radiant with the rays of verity.<br/>Yes; _she_ was truly worthy _her_ high place!<br/>  No one could envy her deserved prosperity.<br/>And then her dress--what beautiful simplicity<br/>Draperied her form with curious felicity!<br/><br/>Meanwhile sweet Adeline deserved their praises,<br/>  By an impartial indemnification<br/>For all her past exertion and soft phrases,<br/>  In a most edifying conversation,<br/>Which turned upon their late guests' miens and faces,<br/>  Their families, even to the last relation;<br/>Their hideous wives, their horrid selves and dresses,<br/>And truculent distortion of their tresses.<br/><br/>True, _she_ said little--'twas the rest that broke<br/>  Forth into universal epigram;<br/>But then 'twas to the purpose what she spoke:<br/>  Like Addison's "faint praise," so wont to damn,<br/>Her own but served to set off every joke,<br/>  As music chimes in with a melodrame.<br/>How sweet the task to shield an absent friend!<br/>I ask but this of mine, to----_not_ defend.<br/><br/>There were but two exceptions to this keen<br/>  Skirmish of wits o'er the departed; one,<br/>Aurora, with her pure and placid mien;<br/>  And Juan, too, in general behind none<br/>In gay remark on what he had heard or seen,<br/>  Sate silent now, his usual spirits gone:<br/>In vain he heard the others rail or rally,<br/>He would not join them in a single sally.<br/><br/>'Tis true he saw Aurora look as though<br/>  She approved his silence; she perhaps mistook<br/>Its motive for that charity we owe<br/>  But seldom pay the absent, nor would look<br/>Farther--it might or it might not be so.<br/>  But Juan, sitting silent in his nook,<br/>Observing little in his reverie,<br/>Yet saw this much, which he was glad to see.<br/><br/>The Ghost at least had done him this much good,<br/>  In making him as silent as a ghost,<br/>If in the circumstances which ensued<br/>  He gained esteem where it was worth the most;<br/>And, certainly, Aurora had renewed<br/>  In him some feelings he had lately lost,<br/>Or hardened; feelings which, perhaps ideal,<br/>Are so divine, that I must deem them real:--<br/><br/>The love of higher things and better days;<br/>  The unbounded hope, and heavenly ignorance<br/>Of what is called the World, and the World's ways;<br/>  The moments when we gather from a glance<br/>More joy than from all future pride or praise,<br/>  Which kindle manhood, but can ne'er entrance<br/>The Heart in an existence of its own,<br/>Of which another's bosom is the zone.<br/><br/>Who would not sigh Œë·º¥ Œ±·º¥ œÑ·Ω∞ŒΩ ŒöœÖŒ∏·Ω≥œÅŒµŒπŒ±ŒΩ<br/>  That _hath_ a memory, or that _had_ a heart?<br/>Alas! _her_ star must fade like that of Dian:<br/>  Ray fades on ray, as years on years depart.<br/>Anacreon only had the soul to tie an<br/>  Unwithering myrtle round the unblunted dart<br/>Of Eros: but though thou hast played us many tricks,<br/>Still we respect thee,"_Alma Venus Genetrix!_"<br/><br/>And full of sentiments, sublime as billows<br/>  Heaving between this World and Worlds beyond,<br/>Don Juan, when the midnight hour of pillows<br/>  Arrived, retired to his; but to despond<br/>Rather than rest. Instead of poppies, willows<br/>  Waved o'er his couch; he meditated, fond<br/>Of those sweet bitter thoughts which banish sleep,<br/>And make the worldling sneer, the youngling weep.<br/><br/>The night was as before: he was undrest,<br/>  Saving his night-gown, which is an undress;<br/>Completely _sans culotte_, and without vest;<br/>  In short, he hardly could be clothed with less:<br/>But apprehensive of his spectral guest,<br/>  He sate with feelings awkward to express<br/>(By those who have not had such visitations),<br/>Expectant of the Ghost's fresh operations.<br/><br/>And not in vain he listened;--Hush! what's that?<br/>  I see--I see--Ah, no!--'t is not--yet 't is--<br/>Ye powers! it is the--the--the--Pooh! the cat!<br/>  The Devil may take that stealthy pace of his!<br/>So like a spiritual pit-a-pat,<br/>  Or tiptoe of an amatory Miss,<br/>Gliding the first time to a _rendezvous_,<br/>And dreading the chaste echoes of her shoe.<br/><br/>Again--what is 't? The wind? No, no,--this time<br/>  It is the sable Friar as before,<br/>With awful footsteps regular as rhyme,<br/>  Or (as rhymes may be in these days) much more.<br/>Again through shadows of the night sublime,<br/>  When deep sleep fell on men, and the World wore<br/>The starry darkness round her like a girdle<br/>Spangled with gems--the Monk made his blood curdle.<br/><br/>A noise like to wet fingers drawn on glass,<br/>  Which sets the teeth on edge; and a slight clatter,<br/>Like showers which on the midnight gusts will pass,<br/>  Sounding like very supernatural water,<br/>Came over Juan's ear, which throbbed, alas!<br/>  For Immaterialism's a serious matter;<br/>So that even those whose faith is the most great<br/>In Souls immortal, shun them _t√™te-√†-t√™te_.<br/><br/>Were his eyes open?--Yes! and his mouth too.<br/>  Surprise has this effect--to make one dumb,<br/>Yet leave the gate which Eloquence slips through<br/>  As wide as if a long speech were to come.<br/>Nigh and more nigh the awful echoes drew,<br/>  Tremendous to a mortal tympanum:<br/>His eyes were open, and (as was before<br/>Stated) his mouth. What opened next?--the door.<br/><br/>It opened with a most infernal creak,<br/>  Like that of Hell. "Lasciate ogni speranza,<br/>Voi, ch' entrate!" The hinge seemed to speak,<br/>  Dreadful as Dante's _rima_, or this stanza;<br/>Or--but all words upon such themes are weak:<br/>  A single shade's sufficient to entrance a<br/>Hero--for what is Substance to a Spirit?<br/>Or how is 't _Matter_ trembles to come near it?<br/><br/>The door flew wide, not swiftly,--but, as fly<br/>  The sea-gulls, with a steady, sober flight--<br/>And then swung back; nor close--but stood awry,<br/>  Half letting in long shadows on the light,<br/>Which still in Juan's candlesticks burned high,<br/>  For he had two, both tolerably bright,<br/>And in the doorway, darkening darkness, stood<br/>The sable Friar in his solemn hood.<br/><br/>Don Juan shook, as erst he had been shaken<br/>  The night before; but being sick of shaking,<br/>He first inclined to think he had been mistaken;<br/>  And then to be ashamed of such mistaking;<br/>His own internal ghost began to awaken<br/>  Within him, and to quell his corporal quaking--<br/>Hinting that Soul and Body on the whole<br/>Were odds against a disembodied Soul.<br/><br/>And then his dread grew wrath, and his wrath fierce,<br/>  And he arose, advanced--the Shade retreated;<br/>But Juan, eager now the truth to pierce,<br/>  Followed, his veins no longer cold, but heated,<br/>Resolved to thrust the mystery _carte_ and _tierce_,<br/>  At whatsoever risk of being defeated:<br/>The Ghost stopped, menaced, then retired, until<br/>He reached the ancient wall, then stood stone still.<br/><br/>Juan put forth one arm--Eternal powers!<br/>  It touched no soul, nor body, but the wall,<br/>On which the moonbeams fell in silvery showers,<br/>  Chequered with all the tracery of the Hall;<br/>He shuddered, as no doubt the bravest cowers<br/>  When he can't tell what 'tis that doth appal.<br/>How odd, a single hobgoblin's nonentity<br/>Should cause more fear than a whole host's identity!<br/><br/>But still the Shade remained: the blue eyes glared,<br/>  And rather variably for stony death;<br/>Yet one thing rather good the grave had spared,<br/>  The Ghost had a remarkably sweet breath:<br/>A straggling curl showed he had been fair-haired;<br/>  A red lip, with two rows of pearls beneath,<br/>Gleamed forth, as through the casement's ivy shroud<br/>The Moon peeped, just escaped from a grey cloud.<br/><br/>And Juan, puzzled, but still curious, thrust<br/>  His other arm forth--Wonder upon wonder!<br/>It pressed upon a hard but glowing bust,<br/>  Which beat as if there was a warm heart under.<br/>He found, as people on most trials must,<br/>  That he had made at first a silly blunder,<br/>And that in his confusion he had caught<br/>Only the wall, instead of what he sought.<br/><br/>The Ghost, if Ghost it were, seemed a sweet soul<br/>  As ever lurked beneath a holy hood:<br/>A dimpled chin, a neck of ivory, stole<br/>  Forth into something much like flesh and blood;<br/>Back fell the sable frock and dreary cowl,<br/>  And they revealed--alas! that e'er they should!<br/>In full, voluptuous, but _not o'er_grown bulk,<br/>The phantom of her frolic Grace--Fitz-Fulke!<br/><br/>CANTO THE SEVENTEENTH.<br/><br/>The world is full of orphans: firstly, those<br/>  Who are so in the strict sense of the phrase;<br/>But many a lonely tree the loftier grows<br/>  Than others crowded in the Forest's maze--<br/>The next are such as are not doomed to lose<br/>  Their tender parents, in their budding days,<br/>But, merely, their parental tenderness,<br/>Which leaves them orphans of the heart no less.<br/><br/>The next are "_only_ Children," as they are styled,<br/>  Who grow up _Children_ only, since th' old saw<br/>Pronounces that an "only's" a spoilt child--<br/>  But not to go too far, I hold it law,<br/>That where their education, harsh or mild,<br/>  Transgresses the great bounds of love or awe,<br/>The sufferers--be 't in heart or intellect--<br/>Whate'er the _cause_, are orphans in _effect_.<br/><br/>But to return unto the stricter rule--<br/>  As far as words make rules--our common notion<br/>Of orphan paints at once a parish school,<br/>  A half-starved babe, a wreck upon Life's ocean,<br/>A human (what the Italians nickname) "Mule!"<br/>  A theme for Pity or some worse emotion;<br/>Yet, if examined, it might be admitted<br/>The wealthiest orphans are to be more pitied.<br/><br/>Too soon they are Parents to themselves: for what<br/>  Are Tutors, Guardians, and so forth, compared<br/>With Nature's genial Genitors? so that<br/>  A child of Chancery, that Star-Chamber ward,<br/>(I'll take the likeness I can first come at,)<br/>  Is like--a duckling by Dame Partlett reared,<br/>And frights--especially if 'tis a daughter,<br/>Th' old Hen--by running headlong to the water.<br/><br/>There is a common-place book argument,<br/>  Which glibly glides from every tongue;<br/>When any dare a new light to present,<br/>  "If you are right, then everybody's wrong"!<br/>Suppose the converse of this precedent<br/>  So often urged, so loudly and so long;<br/>"If you are wrong, then everybody's right"!<br/>Was ever everybody yet so quite?<br/><br/>Therefore I would solicit free discussion<br/>  Upon all points--no matter what, or whose--<br/>Because as Ages upon Ages push on,<br/>  The last is apt the former to accuse<br/>Of pillowing its head on a pin-cushion,<br/>  Heedless of pricks because it was obtuse:<br/>What was a paradox becomes a truth or<br/>A something like it--witness Luther!<br/><br/>The Sacraments have been reduced to two,<br/>  And Witches unto none, though somewhat late<br/>Since burning ag√©d women (save a few--<br/>  Not witches only b--ches--who create<br/>Mischief in families, as some know or knew,<br/>  Should still be singed, but lightly, let me state,)<br/>Has been declared an act of inurbanity,<br/>_Malgr√©_ Sir Matthew Hales's great humanity.<br/><br/>Great Galileo was debarred the Sun,<br/>  Because he fixed it; and, to stop his talking,<br/>How Earth could round the solar orbit run,<br/>  Found his own legs embargoed from mere walking:<br/>The man was well-nigh dead, ere men begun<br/>  To think his skull had not some need of caulking;<br/>But now, it seems, he's right--his notion just:<br/>No doubt a consolation to his dust.<br/><br/>Pythagoras, Locke, Socrates--but pages<br/>  Might be filled up, as vainly as before,<br/>With the sad usage of all sorts of sages,<br/>  Who in his life-time, each, was deemed a Bore!<br/>The loftiest minds outrun their tardy ages:<br/>  This they must bear with and, perhaps, much more;<br/>The wise man's sure when he no more can share it, he<br/>Will have a firm Post Obit on posterity.<br/><br/>If such doom waits each intellectual Giant,<br/>  We little people in our lesser way,<br/>In Life's small rubs should surely be more pliant,<br/>  And so for one will I--as well I may--Would<br/>that I were less bilious--but, oh, fie on 't!<br/>    Just as I make my mind up every day,<br/>To be a "_totus, teres_," Stoic, Sage,<br/>The wind shifts and I fly into a rage.<br/><br/>Temperate I am--yet never had a temper;<br/>  Modest I am--yet with some slight assurance;<br/>Changeable too--yet somehow "_Idem semper_:"<br/>  Patient--but not enamoured of endurance;<br/>Cheerful--but, sometimes, rather apt to whimper:<br/>  Mild--but at times a sort of "_Hercules furens_:"<br/>So that I almost think that the same skin<br/>For one without--has two or three within.<br/><br/>Our Hero was, in Canto the Sixteenth,<br/>  Left in a tender moonlight situation,<br/>Such as enables Man to show his strength<br/>  Moral or physical: on this occasion<br/>Whether his virtue triumphed--or, at length,<br/>  His vice--for he was of a kindling nation--<br/>Is more than I shall venture to describe;--<br/>Unless some Beauty with a kiss should bribe.<br/><br/>I leave the thing a problem, like all things:--<br/>  The morning came--and breakfast, tea and toast,<br/>Of which most men partake, but no one sings.<br/>  The company whose birth, wealth, worth, has cost<br/>My trembling Lyre already several strings,<br/>  Assembled with our hostess, and mine host;<br/>The guests dropped in--the last but one, Her Grace,<br/>The latest, Juan, with his virgin face.<br/><br/>Which best it is to encounter--Ghost, or none,<br/>  'Twere difficult to say--but Juan looked<br/>As if he had combated with more than one,<br/>  Being wan and worn, with eyes that hardly brooked<br/>The light, that through the Gothic window shone:<br/>  Her Grace, too, had a sort of air rebuked--<br/>Seemed pale and shivered, as if she had kept<br/>A vigil, or dreamt rather more than slept.
</p>
